# Cross-cutting Abstractions

> This area's 15 designs cover the shared engines (annotation-model, type-conversion, expression-language), the ambient-context family (locale-context, context-propagation), the execution substrate (task-execution, scheduling, retry-resilience, concurrency-contract), the declarative-advice family (transaction-management, caching, validation, exception-translation), and the context services (messages-i18n, resource-loading). Coverage of the CORE intent is genuinely strong and the cross-references between designs are unusually disciplined — the auto-proxy-creator/advisor-chain story, the never-inheritable-tx-resource rule, the cold-path-timer sanction, and the shared ConcurrencyGate primitive are all consistently threaded. The biggest GAP is the missing #[async]/@Async declarative-asynchrony feature itself: every design treats task-execution as the 'run somewhere' seam, but NO design owns the @Async interceptor (the proxy that dispatches proceed() onto an executor, the void/Future return discipline, the AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler, the spring.task.execution.mode=force central-override lever). The application event system (ApplicationEventPublisher / @EventListener / multicaster) is referenced by ~8 designs as 'events'/'event-multicaster' but is not itself a design in this batch — its hot-path SpEL condition, return-value-as-event chaining, generic-event ResolvableType targeting, sync-error-handling, and lazy-listener-loses-registration trap are all unowned here. Smaller but real gaps: the @Async exception-handler routing, the embedded/EmbeddedValueResolver string-resolution split from full expression evaluation, repeatable-annotation merging in annotation-model, and the @Proxyable / global-proxy-default consistency lever that governs every advice feature. Coherence is mostly clean; the main tensions are (1) the cache/validation self-invocation INCONSISTENCY if caching picks R2/R3 (eliminates the caveat) while transaction-management stays proxy-based (keeps it), (2) duplicated cron/duration/timer primitives across scheduling+retry+type-conversion that should be unified, and (3) three designs independently re-deriving the 'silent-DCE self-check' obligation without a single shared owner.

_15 feature designs. Implementation-agnostic — candidate realizations for Phase 2/3, not decisions._

## Meta-Annotation / Merged-Annotation Model  `[annotation-model]`

Spring treats annotations as a composable, user-extensible vocabulary — not a fixed keyword set. Three capabilities define the feature: (1) META-PRESENCE: an annotation declared on another annotation is "meta-present" transitively, so `@RestController` (= `@Controller` = `@Component`) carries `@Component` at meta-distance 2; the framework hard-codes only a few base concepts and everything else composes. (2) ATTRIBUTE FORWARDING via `@AliasFor` in three modes — explicit mirror aliases within one annotation (same type, identical defaults), attribute OVERRIDE punching a value down into a named meta-annotation's attribute (`@SpringBootApplication.scanBasePackages` writes `@ComponentScan.basePackages`), and IMPLICIT transitive alias sets where several attributes overriding the same target become interchangeable. (3) DISTANCE-ORDERED MERGING: `MergedAnnotations` flattens the annotation tree into views ordered by aggregate-index then meta-distance, so the NEAREST/most-specific wins (a directly-present `@Scope` beats an inherited one); reading through this single merge algorithm yields a "synthesized" view that enforces alias semantics (raw reflection does not). Plus two cross-cutting concerns leaf must replicate the INTENT of: metadata must be READABLE WITHOUT LOADING/CONSTRUCTING the target (Spring reads bytecode so a `@ConditionalOnClass` can decide NOT to load a class), and there are two traversal algorithms — "present/get" (element + its meta-annotations only) vs "find/search" (also walks supertypes). This is the shared engine beneath stereotypes (component-stereotypes), conditions (conditions/condition-family), profiles, `@Enable*`/import-composition, qualifiers (candidate-resolver), scopes, and `@Bean` defaults — the one merge algorithm they all consult so semantics never diverge across code paths. The deliberate Spring price (alias enforcement only when read through the API; misconfiguration surfaces at merge-time runtime) is itself a design lever leaf can improve on, since Rust can move much of this to compile time.

### Candidate realizations

#### Derive-expanded normalized metadata descriptor (compile-time merge, no annotation interface)
There is NO runtime annotation object and NO `MergedAnnotations` traversal at all. A leaf attribute/derive macro (e.g. `#[component(name="x")]`, `#[transactional(propagation=Required)]`) expands at the DECLARATION SITE into a flat `const AnnotationMetadata` descriptor: a `&'static [AttributeEntry]` (kebab-canonical key -> typed `AttrValue` union of bool/i64/str/typeid/list) plus a `&'static [MetaPresence]` table listing every meta-present marker with its distance. Composed/user stereotypes are themselves macros: `#[domain_service]` is authored as a thin macro that expands to the same descriptor shape as `#[service]` plus its own marker, so meta-presence is resolved by the macro emitting the FULLY-FLATTENED, already-distance-resolved attribute set — the merge happens in `leaf-codegen` (a normal, testable library the thin macro calls), never at runtime. `@AliasFor` is expressed as a `forward = leaf::Component::name` path argument inside the composed-annotation macro definition; codegen validates reciprocity, identical-default, and same-type AT MACRO-EXPANSION TIME and emits a `compile_error!` with a full span-anchored diagnostic on misconfiguration (strictly better than Spring's runtime merge-time failure). The runtime only ever sees an already-merged `&'static AnnotationMetadata`; helpers like `meta.attr("name")`, `meta.is_meta_present(MarkerId::COMPONENT)`, `meta.nearest(MarkerId::SCOPE)` are O(1)/O(n) reads over const slices. 'Reading without loading' is automatic: the descriptor is a separate `const` carrying no reference to the target's constructor or `T: Default`, so conditions/scanning inspect it without ever instantiating the bean.

**Pros:** Zero runtime merge cost — distance-ordering and alias forwarding are fully resolved in codegen, honoring low-overhead #2 and the const-eval-baseline + const-TypeId capabilities (stable).; 'Read without loading' falls out for free: the metadata `const` is independent of the target's construction, so conditions/scanning never touch a constructor — directly satisfies the Spring intent for condition-driven optional-dependency back-off.; Alias misconfiguration becomes a COMPILE error with rich spans (invariant #9: failure as early as the richest diagnostic allows) — beats Spring's runtime AnnotationConfigurationException.; Thin macros (invariant #10): the attribute macro emits only a hand-writable `const` descriptor; all merge logic lives in `leaf-codegen` as a normal tested crate; `cargo expand` shows a legible flat const.; No `Box`/`Arc`/heap for metadata — pure `&'static` const data, no global locks (invariant #5).

**Cons:** The annotation 'vocabulary' is closed at the macro layer, not the type layer: a downstream crate composing a new stereotype must invoke leaf's codegen helper (it cannot just `derive` over a plain marker trait), so user-extensibility costs one macro definition per composed annotation.; Distance-ordering is frozen at expansion: a feature that wants to RE-MERGE with runtime-discovered overlays (e.g. a profile-supplied attribute override) cannot, because the merge already happened — pushes any late override into a separate runtime layer.; Codegen must canonicalize keys (kebab) and reconcile the same target attribute reached via multiple intermediate macros — the implicit-alias-set logic lives in code leaf must get right once, with good tests.; Two composed annotations from two crates that both override the same base attribute can disagree, and codegen at each site can't see the other site (single-invocation scope), so true cross-crate alias CONFLICT detection must be deferred to a runtime/startup self-check over collected descriptors.

**Mechanisms considered:** proc-macro attribute/derive emitting a flat `const AnnotationMetadata` descriptor; `leaf-codegen` normal library crate holding the merge/alias/distance algorithm called by the thin macro; const `&'static [AttributeEntry]` + `&'static [MetaPresence]` tables keyed by const `TypeId`/`MarkerId`; `proc_macro::Span` provenance embedded per attribute for diagnostics; `compile_error!` for alias-misconfiguration at expansion time

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The descriptor `const` is emitted into the declaring crate, so it travels with whatever links that crate — no classpath needed for the metadata ITSELF. But meta-presence resolution is the hazard: a thin composed-annotation macro in crate B that forwards into a base marker defined in `leaf-core` references only `::leaf_core::…` absolute paths (proc-macro hygiene), which works. What a single macro invocation CANNOT see is sibling/downstream composed annotations, so cross-crate ALIAS-CONFLICT and duplicate-target detection can only happen where the full set is visible (the binary), via a runtime self-check over the link-collected descriptor table (shares the discovery substrate with component-scanning; descriptors ride the same `linkme`/`inventory`/codegen channel). Generics: a metadata descriptor for `Foo<T>` has no single symbol — codegen must hard-error on generic annotated items or require a per-instantiation `register_annotation!(Foo<Concrete>)`, mirroring the catalog's generics limitation.

**T2 — execution context:** Entirely compile-time + cold-path. Merge/alias/distance computation runs in the proc-macro/codegen process during compilation; nothing async, nothing blocks at runtime. At runtime the metadata is `&'static` const data read synchronously on the startup/cold path (scanning, condition evaluation, registration) — never on a hot async path, so no executor, no `.await`, no cancellation surface. Errors are compile-time `compile_error!` (alias misconfig) or a startup-validation diagnostic (cross-crate conflict) — both before any bean future is spawned, so they never propagate through async cancellation.

#### Runtime MergedAnnotations engine over const annotation records (Spring-faithful, late-merge capable)
Replicate Spring's model more literally: the macro emits a minimal, NON-merged `const RawAnnotation { marker: MarkerId, attrs: &[AttributeEntry], meta: &[(MarkerId, distance)] }` per directly-applied annotation, and a runtime `MergedAnnotations` engine in `leaf-core` performs flattening, distance-ordering, and alias forwarding ON DEMAND. `@AliasFor` is data: each `RawAnnotation`/marker definition carries an `&'static [AliasEdge { from_key, to_marker, to_key }]` table (also const, emitted by the composed-annotation macro). The engine exposes `MergedAnnotations::from(records).get(MarkerId::COMPONENT) -> Option<MergedAnnotationView>` where the view lazily applies alias edges and returns the nearest value; a `SearchStrategy` parameter (`Direct` vs `TypeHierarchy`) mirrors the present-vs-find duality by optionally folding in records contributed for supertype markers. Because merging is deferred, late layers can inject additional records before a `.get()` (e.g. a profile or environment overlay contributing an attribute override), which the engine merges by the same distance rules. A `synthesize::<A>()` analogue returns a typed, alias-enforced struct view of one annotation's attributes.

**Pros:** Most faithful to Spring's intent — one merge algorithm, present-vs-find strategies, distance ordering, synthesized alias-enforced views — so neighbours (conditions, candidate-resolver, import-composition) consume an API shaped exactly like Spring's, easing intent-parity (#2).; Late-merge capability: runtime overlays (profile/environment-supplied attribute overrides, dynamic WASM-module-contributed markers) can be folded into the same merge — keeps the model open and origin-agnostic, which a fully-precomputed approach forecloses.; Cross-crate alias CONFLICT and duplicate-target detection happen naturally at the point the records are assembled (binary/startup), where the full set is visible — turns the single-invocation blind spot into a runtime check with full context.; Macros stay maximally thin (#10): they emit raw const records + alias-edge tables only; ALL logic lives in the normal `leaf-core` engine, fully unit-testable without compilation.

**Cons:** Runtime merge cost on every metadata query unless cached — violates low-overhead #2 if conditions/scanning re-merge repeatedly; needs a one-shot merged-cache keyed by element to amortize (adds a map, brushing against the no-global-locks discipline).; Alias misconfiguration regresses to RUNTIME detection (exactly Spring's trap, invariant #9 says prefer the earliest rich diagnostic) — only a separate compile-time lint or build-time validation pass recovers the early-failure property.; More moving parts at runtime: a `MergedAnnotations` engine, view objects, strategy enum — heavier than a flat const read, and view objects risk transient allocation if not carefully `&'static`-borrowing.; The 'synthesized annotation' as a typed struct still needs codegen to produce the typed accessor per annotation type, so it is not purely runtime — a hybrid that must be kept coherent.

**Mechanisms considered:** macro-emitted const `RawAnnotation` records (non-merged) + const `AliasEdge` tables; runtime `MergedAnnotations` flattening/merge engine in `leaf-core`; `SearchStrategy` enum (Direct vs TypeHierarchy) mirroring present-vs-find; lazy merged-view objects borrowing `&'static` data; optional merged-result cache to amortize repeated queries; late runtime overlay records for profile/dynamic-source contributions

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Records are const and travel with their declaring crate; alias-edge tables likewise. The full assembly (which records exist for a given element across all linked crates) is only knowable where the crate graph is visible — the binary — so the merge engine is fed by the same discovery substrate as component-scanning (link-time slice / inventory / build-time codegen), keeping it ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC: a record can come from a statically-linked crate OR from a dynamically-loaded WASM-module layer that contributes records into the engine at runtime, since the engine merges by data, not by where the data came from. Cross-crate alias conflicts are detected at engine-assembly time. Generics still cannot self-register a record; per-instantiation registration required.

**T2 — execution context:** Merge runs at runtime but strictly on COLD paths — startup scanning, condition evaluation, registration planning — never on the steady-state hot path, honoring invariant #4 (polling/heavier work only on cold paths). It is synchronous CPU work, no `.await`, so it occupies whatever (cold-path) context calls it; it must not block an async executor thread for long, which is fine since merge is bounded by the const record count. Late overlays must be installed BEFORE the merge that consumes them (a happens-before discipline the engine documents). Errors (alias misconfig, cross-crate conflict, missing reciprocal) are produced synchronously as rich diagnostics during the startup validation pass, before bean futures spawn — no async cancellation interaction. The optional cache, if shared, must use fine-grained or write-once-then-read semantics to avoid a global lock on the cold path.

#### Type-as-annotation: marker traits + typed attribute structs (zero-erasure, language-native vocabulary)
Drop the string-keyed annotation record entirely and model each annotation as a TYPE. A base marker is a trait (`trait Component {}`, `trait Scope { const KIND: ScopeKind; }`); a composed/user stereotype is a trait with supertrait bounds (`trait DomainService: Component + Transactional {}`), so META-PRESENCE is literally Rust supertrait reachability and DISTANCE is supertrait depth. Attributes are associated consts / associated types on the trait, and a stereotype carries its concrete attribute values as a `const` struct it returns (`fn meta(&self) -> &'static ComponentMeta`). The leaf derive macro implements the relevant marker traits for the annotated `T` and fills the attribute structs; `@AliasFor` becomes a const-fn projection — a composed annotation's attribute const is defined as `Component::name` forwarded via a const accessor, checked by the type system + a `const { assert!(...) }` for identical-default constraints. Queries are trait-bound: `T: Component` answers meta-presence at compile time; trait upcasting (stable 1.86) lets the registry store `Arc<dyn Component>` and coerce to any declared supertrait view; `dyn Any` + const `TypeId` key the attribute structs. There is no merge algorithm — the trait solver IS the distance resolver, and 'nearest wins' is expressed by the most-derived impl.

**Pros:** Maximally rust-native and zero-erasure where used generically: meta-presence and many attribute reads are COMPILE-TIME trait facts, the lowest possible overhead (#2), and leverages exactly the stable wins (trait upcasting 1.86, const TypeId 1.91, AFIT/RPITIT) the envelope blesses.; User-extensibility is language-native: defining a new stereotype is declaring a trait with supertrait bounds and a derive — no codegen-helper indirection, the most idiomatic 'extensible vocabulary'.; Strongest type safety: a bean that claims `@Transactional` semantics it doesn't satisfy fails to compile; alias same-type constraints are enforced by the type of the associated const.; Trait upcasting gives Spring's 'injectable as any supertype' for free at the registry layer, tightly aligning with candidate-resolver/autowiring-resolution.

**Cons:** Distance-ordered MERGING across UNRELATED annotations on the same element is awkward: traits compose vertically (supertraits) but an element with two independent directly-applied annotations whose attributes collide has no trait-level 'aggregate index' — needs an out-of-band ordering convention, partially reintroducing a merge step.; 'Read without loading' is the weakest here: trait bounds and attribute consts can often be read without constructing T, but anything phrased as `fn meta(&self)` needs an instance; conditions that must decide BEFORE construction force every attribute onto associated consts/types, which is more rigid and can't carry runtime-overlaid values at all.; Dynamic/origin-agnostic sources don't fit: a WASM-module-contributed annotation has no Rust type to bound on, so this model cannot represent dynamically-discovered markers — clashes with the origin-agnostic guardrail unless paired with a record-based fallback.; Generic stereotypes and blanket-impl ambiguity: expressing 'nearest wins' via most-derived impl edges toward specialization (an AVOID per the envelope); without it, conflicting attribute consts across supertraits are a coherence problem, not a clean override.; String-keyed introspection (tooling, diagnostics listing 'all attributes of element X') is hard — there is no flat attribute map to enumerate, hurting the rich-diagnostics intent (#9) and config-metadata-style tooling.

**Mechanisms considered:** marker traits with supertrait bounds for meta-presence/distance; associated consts / associated types as attributes; const-fn alias projection + `const { assert! }` for default-equality constraints; trait upcasting (stable 1.86) for supertype views in the registry; `dyn Any` + const `TypeId` for attribute-struct storage; explicitly NOT specialization (envelope: avoid) for nearest-wins

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Trait impls are cross-crate-natural: a downstream crate implements leaf's marker traits for its own types with no classpath needed, and the orphan rule is satisfied because the type is local. Meta-presence is resolved by the compiler, not a link-time registry, so the metadata layer itself is immune to the DCE/Layer-0 hazards — a major reliability win. The CATCH for discovery (not for the annotation model per se): to be SCANNED, an annotated type still has to be registered into the discovery substrate (linkme/inventory/codegen) like every other component, so the trait-based metadata rides on top of whatever the cross-crate composition meta-concept (Phase 2) picks. Dynamic origins cannot be expressed as types, so this realization must delegate any WASM-module-contributed markers to a record-based shim (realization 1 or 2), making it non-total on its own.

**T2 — execution context:** Almost entirely compile-time: meta-presence/distance via the trait solver during compilation, attribute consts evaluated at const-eval time. The only runtime touchpoints are upcast coercions and `dyn Any` downcasts in the registry, which are O(1) synchronous vtable/TypeId operations on cold (resolution) paths — no `.await`, no blocking, no cancellation surface. `fn meta(&self)` accessors, where used, run synchronously after construction and so are NOT usable for pre-construction conditions, a hard execution-context constraint that pushes condition-relevant attributes to associated consts. Errors are compile-time trait/coherence/const-assert failures — the earliest and richest possible, fully satisfying invariant #9 for the cases it covers.

### Rust clashes
- No annotation inheritance / no meta-annotations in the language: Java lets you annotate an annotation; Rust attributes are inert macro inputs with no transitive presence. Meta-presence must be MANUFACTURED — by codegen flattening (R1), by emitted meta-tables read by a runtime engine (R2), or by supertrait reachability (R3). There is no language-level transitive-annotation concept to lean on.
- No runtime reflection over attributes: Spring's whole `MergedAnnotations` API is runtime introspection of `java.lang.reflect` annotations; Rust has zero runtime attribute reflection. Every attribute value that the runtime needs must be deliberately materialized into `const`/trait data at compile time — nothing can be 'read off the type' later.
- Single-invocation proc-macro scope defeats `@AliasFor` cross-checking at the source: a Spring `@AliasFor` is validated against the target annotation's declaration at merge time; a leaf attribute macro sees ONLY its own invocation tokens and cannot inspect the base annotation's real definition, so same-type/identical-default reciprocity must be checked either against a hard-coded/imported const schema (absolute paths) or deferred to a build/runtime pass — it cannot be a free local check.
- No classpath for the 'aggregate' set: Spring assembles `MergedAnnotations` from a runtime-enumerable element; in Rust the set of annotations/records contributing to one logical element across crates is only knowable where the crate graph is visible (the binary), so cross-crate alias CONFLICT detection is structurally a binary/startup concern, not a per-site one.
- Synthesized-annotation proxy has no analogue: Spring returns a `java.lang.reflect.Proxy` implementing the annotation interface; Rust has no dynamic interface-proxy. The 'synthesized, alias-enforcing view' must be a generated typed struct (R1/R3) or a borrowing view object over const data (R2) — there is no zero-cost dynamic-proxy path.
- Generics cannot be link-registered: any annotation metadata attached to a generic `Foo<T>` has no single monomorphized symbol, so descriptor-based realizations must hard-error on generic annotated items or demand explicit per-instantiation registration — Spring's type-erased annotations have no such limit.
- Spring's 'read bytecode without loading the class' maps to 'read a const without constructing the value', which is natural for record/descriptor realizations but FORCES condition-relevant attributes onto associated consts in the trait realization (since instance methods need a value) — the no-load intent reshapes which attributes can exist where.
- Spring tolerates merge-time RUNTIME failure for misconfigured aliases; leaf's invariant #9 wants the earliest rich diagnostic, so the natural Rust move (compile-time `compile_error!`) is actually a divergence-as-improvement, but only the codegen/trait realizations can achieve it — the faithful runtime-engine realization inherits Spring's late-failure trap unless given an extra build-time lint.

### Interactions
- **component-stereotypes** — This IS the engine under stereotypes: `@Component` and user stereotypes (`@DomainService`) are meta-presence + name-forwarding aliases. Stereotypes consume meta-presence ('is this meta-annotated Component?') and the bean-name `@AliasFor(Component.value)` forwarding. The chosen realization must let a downstream crate mint a new stereotype that 'just works' for discovery/naming.
- **conditions / condition-family** — Conditions receive the merged metadata of the triggering annotation and must read it WITHOUT constructing the bean (the `@ConditionalOnClass`-against-optional-dependency intent). This feature supplies the 'read without loading' metadata view and the distance-ordered merged attribute values conditions evaluate (AND-semantics over a pure predicate).
- **import-composition / auto-configuration / profiles** — `@Enable*` and `@Profile` are composed annotations whose attributes forward into imported config / conditional metadata; the merged-annotation engine is what flattens `@SpringBootApplication`-style facades into their constituent concerns and routes `@AliasFor` attribute overrides (e.g. scanBasePackages -> ComponentScan) down to the right consumer.
- **candidate-resolver / autowiring-resolution** — Qualifiers and custom qualifier annotations are meta-present markers with forwarded attributes; the resolver reads merged annotation metadata to match candidates. Trait-upcasting in R3 directly serves the 'injectable as any supertype' resolution model.
- **scopes / bean-naming / lifecycle-callbacks** — `@Scope` (nearest-wins, not inherited from supertype per Spring), default bean name, and init/destroy method markers are all attributes read via the merged-annotation view; the distance-ordering rule ('directly-present @Scope beats composed') is exactly the merge semantics this feature defines.
- **component-scanning** — Scanning's default include filter is 'meta-present @Component'; scanning reads this feature's metadata from discovered candidates without loading them. The cross-crate assembly of metadata records shares the discovery substrate (link-time slice / inventory / build-time codegen) that component-scanning depends on.
- **config-metadata / startup-instrumentation / condition-report** — Tooling wants to ENUMERATE an element's merged attributes and provenance (declaration-site span) for self-documenting config and accountable condition reporting; the flat-record realizations (R1/R2) expose an enumerable attribute map and `proc_macro::Span` provenance, while the trait realization (R3) makes enumeration harder.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Where does the merge happen — compile-time (codegen flattens, frozen) vs runtime (engine merges on demand, late-overlay-capable)? This is a direct instance of the deferred metadata/codegen-boundary and compile-vs-runtime decisions; it trades zero-overhead + early diagnostics against openness to dynamic/profile-supplied overlays.
- Is leaf's annotation vocabulary string-keyed records, or types (marker traits)? This couples to the type-erasure & registry substrate decision: a type-as-annotation model wants trait-upcasting + TypeId keys, while a record model wants a flat const attribute map — and the choice determines whether dynamic (WASM-module) origins can contribute annotations at all.
- How is the cross-crate 'aggregate set' of annotation records for one logical element assembled, and where are cross-crate alias CONFLICTS detected? This rides entirely on the deferred cross-crate composition & tooling model (link-time slice vs inventory vs build-time codegen) and the predictability/scope concern from the global-registration author.
- Should `@AliasFor` misconfiguration fail at compile time (compile_error!), at a build-time validation pass, or at startup? This is an instance of the deferred unified error/diagnostics model and the invariant-#9 'earliest richest diagnostic' principle — and it differs per realization.
- Do conditions need any annotation attribute that is only knowable at runtime (profile/environment-overlaid), or can ALL condition-relevant attributes be const? The answer decides whether the model can be fully precomputed or must keep a runtime merge layer — and connects to the deferred conditional-strategy decision (cfg vs codegen vs runtime gates).
- How is the present-vs-find (supertype-walking) duality realized when Rust has no class-hierarchy reflection — via explicit declared supertype edges in metadata, via trait supertraits, or is 'find' semantics even needed given Rust composition favors traits over inheritance? Determines how much of Spring's SearchStrategy surface leaf must carry.
- Is there a single shared 'metadata' primitive that this feature, bean-definition, and config-metadata all consume (one AnnotationMetadata/AnnotatedTypeMetadata analogue), per the unification invariant #11 — or do beans/config carry their own? This wants resolution alongside the registry substrate so the whole framework reads one metadata shape.

### Notes
Strong recommendation to Phase 2/3: a hybrid is likely — R1 (compile-time-merged const descriptor) as the DEFAULT for the static common case (best overhead + earliest diagnostics), with R2's runtime engine kept as the late-merge/origin-agnostic layer for dynamic (WASM-module, profile-overlay) contributions, and R3's trait-upcasting reserved specifically for the registry's 'injectable as supertype' coercion rather than as the whole annotation model. The three are composable along a 'how frozen is the merge' axis, not mutually exclusive. Note that 'read without loading' has TWO sub-intents worth separating in Phase 2: (a) read metadata without CONSTRUCTING the bean (all three realizations can do this for const attributes) vs (b) Spring's literal 'without loading the class into the ClassLoader' (no Rust analogue — a linked type is already 'loaded'; the real cost Rust avoids is running the constructor, which is the (a) intent). Keep the `MarkerId`/`AttributeEntry` ABI minimal and ultra-stable since emitted code hard-codes paths to it workspace-wide (per the cross-crate doc's `leaf-core` guidance). Whatever the choice, distance-ordering MUST be data resolved at runtime/codegen, never link/section order (which is unspecified). The component-stereotypes Phase 1 design (a sibling, also foundational-adjacent) will be the first consumer and should be co-reviewed for whether the stereotype-minting DX it assumes matches the vocabulary-extensibility cost of the chosen realization.

## Persistence Exception Translation (@Repository)  `[exception-translation]`

Spring's intent (lines 462-484, 2217-2225): the persistence boundary is where leaky, vendor-specific exceptions (HibernateException, JPA PersistenceException, raw SQLException, IllegalArgument/StateException) cross into business code. The goal is a consistent, technology-agnostic DataAccessException hierarchy so the service layer can react to e.g. a duplicate-key or optimistic-locking failure WITHOUT importing the persistence vendor's types, and DAOs stay framework-unaware POJOs using the native API. The mechanics to replicate (INTENT, not mechanism): (1) @Repository is the NATURAL POINTCUT — a stereotype that marks precisely the boundary beans, so translation advises ONLY them, not every bean; (2) translation is woven via an AOP advisor on the bean's proxy — deliberately non-invasive (no base class to extend, no hand-written catch blocks), and it COMPOSES with other advice (the same proxy can carry both transaction and translation advice — same machinery family as @Transactional); (3) the advisor is THIN — it catches the native error and delegates to a chain of PersistenceExceptionTranslator beans AUTODETECTED from the context (from any scope, ordered by @Order); each translator either maps the native exception to a DataAccessException subtype or passes (returns null → next translator); the original is ALWAYS wrapped, never lost; (4) the hierarchy is unchecked (DataAccessException extends NestedRuntimeException) with structured subtypes (DataIntegrityViolation, OptimisticLockingFailure, DuplicateKey, ...) and transient/non-transient/recoverable top split; (5) in Boot it is OPT-IN-by-default: registered by a conditional auto-configuration (PersistenceExceptionTranslationAutoConfiguration), gated @ConditionalOnMissingBean + a property (spring.persistence.exceptiontranslation.enabled, default true) — overridable and absent in a bare engine; (6) the repository-marker annotation is PLUGGABLE (repositoryAnnotationType — you can point it at your own @Dao). The honest gotchas to reproduce: marking only an interface doesn't work (the CLASS must carry the marker); a self-invoked or final method bypasses the proxy and the native exception LEAKS (the classic proxy boundary, identical to leaf's self-invocation boundary from aop-proxying). THIS feature owns: the leaf DataAccessException hierarchy, the translator-provider contract + ordered chain + its cross-crate discovery, the repository-boundary pointcut marker, and the single around/after advisor that maps a native error result into the hierarchy. It does NOT own the proxy mechanism (aop-proxying), the advisor-union/ordering (auto-proxy-creator), or which crate's translators get force-linked (the Phase-2 cross-crate substrate).

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — A standard advisor riding the AOP chain: translation is one error-mapping interceptor + a discovered, ordered translator-provider chain
Realize exception-translation as a CONCRETE, dogfooded advisor that plugs into the upstream aop-proxying call-routing primitive (Interceptor/JoinPoint/proceed or tower Layer — mechanism held open) — exactly Spring's 'translation is just another advisor on the proxy' model, so it composes with transaction/cache advice on ONE chain. The advisor body is a thin error-mapping interceptor: `async fn intercept(jp) -> Result<Outcome, AdviceError> { match jp.proceed().await { Ok(v) => Ok(v), Err(e) => Err(self.translate(e)) } }`. It only acts on the ERROR result of proceed() (an after-throwing advisor); success passes through untouched. The pointcut is metadata: the advisor matches a bean iff the bean's descriptor carries a repository-boundary marker (the leaf analogue of @Repository — a stereotype meta-annotated like component-stereotypes, or the pluggable repositoryAnnotationType equivalent: a configurable marker type so a user `#[dao]` works too). The substrate THIS feature owns is the translator contract + chain + hierarchy: `trait DataAccessExceptionTranslator: Send + Sync { fn translate(&self, e: &(dyn Error + 'static)) -> Option<DataAccessError>; }` returning None = 'pass to next' (Spring's null-is-pass chain-of-responsibility). The advisor holds an ORDERED slice of `Arc<dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator>` (ordering-as-data via @Order on each provider — never link/registration order), walks them, takes the first Some, and ALWAYS chains the original native error as the source (`DataAccessError { kind: DataIntegrityViolation, source: Box<dyn Error> }`) so the cause is never lost (§2.9 causal chain). The hierarchy is a rust-native enum-or-trait family: a `DataAccessError` with a structured `kind` (DuplicateKey, OptimisticLockingFailure, DataIntegrityViolation, TransientDataAccess, ...) mirroring NonTransient/Transient/Recoverable, implementing std::error::Error with full source(). Translators are discovered as beans of type `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` (collection-injection of all matching beans, ordered) — so a vendor integration crate (leaf-sqlx, leaf-diesel) registers its translator as an ordinary bean and it auto-joins the chain, mirroring 'the JPA EntityManagerFactory IS a translator'. The advisor itself is installed by a conditional auto-configuration (the leaf analogue of PersistenceExceptionTranslationAutoConfiguration: @ConditionalOnMissingBean + a property default-on), so it's opt-in-by-default and absent in the bare engine (container-core parity).

**Pros:** Purest intent parity and maximal unification (§2.11): translation is NOT bespoke machinery — it is one Interceptor/Layer on the SAME chain transaction/cache/retry/validation ride, so 'the same proxy carries both tx and translation advice' falls out for free, ordering relative to tx advice is auto-proxy-creator's concern (not re-solved here), and the feature adds only a thin mapper + a discovered chain.; The translator-provider chain is exactly Spring's autodetected, @Order-sorted, null-is-pass PersistenceExceptionTranslator model re-expressed rust-natively (Option<DataAccessError> = pass/translate), and it is origin-agnostic: a translator is just a `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` bean, so a native crate, a test double, or a future WASM-module-sourced translator all join the chain identically with no new path.; DAOs stay framework-unaware POJOs (§2 DX #1): the only thing on the DAO is the repository-boundary stereotype marker; no base trait to implement, no hand-written catch — Spring's whole non-invasive promise, kept honestly.; Lowest added surface area: leaf-core defines DataAccessError + the translator trait + the marker pointcut + the thin mapping advisor; everything else (proxy mechanism, advisor union/order, force-linking translators) is delegated to aop-proxying / auto-proxy-creator / the Phase-2 cross-crate substrate, so this feature does not pre-judge any keystone.; Stable-buildable and reactive on the hot path: the mapper is pure-sync work AFTER an awaited proceed() (no extra await, no polling); the only allocation is wrapping the original error as a source (Box<dyn Error>) on the ERROR path only — success calls pay nothing beyond the chain hop the user already opted into.; Reproduces the self-invocation/leak gotcha HONESTLY and for the identical structural reason as aop-proxying: a self-called or non-proxied repository method bypasses the advisor and the native error leaks — leaf documents this as the same boundary, not a bug, and steers to the same workarounds.

**Cons:** Inherits aop-proxying's unresolved keystone in full: whether translation can be woven at all depends on the proxy mechanism (generated type vs runtime wrapper vs resolution-seam middleware) AND on whether a repository bean MUST expose a service trait (no-CGLIB consequence) — a trait-less concrete DAO may be un-advisable in Rust, so the 'any @Repository gets wrapped' guarantee may not hold uniformly.; Error identity at the chain is ERASED: the advisor catches the proxied method's error as `&dyn Error` (the chain is heterogeneous), but a translator that needs the TYPED native error (a sqlx::Error variant, a vendor SQLSTATE) must downcast — a runtime failure surface and the erased-vs-typed clash that aop-proxying flags; the translator authoring story is 'downcast then match', less ergonomic than a typed mapper.; The advisor only sees the error that proceed() actually RETURNS — but leaf's async errors may be wrapped by intervening advisors (a tx advisor that rolls back and re-wraps); ordering of translation relative to other error-transforming advice becomes correctness-relevant (cache-outside-tx analogue), pushing a real ordering concern onto auto-proxy-creator that this feature can only document.; Requires every advisable repository method to have a uniform error type the chain can carry (`Result<_, E>` where E erases to dyn Error + Send); a DAO method returning a non-Error failure (a custom enum not impl Error) can't be translated without an adapter — a discipline leaf must document.; Discovery of translators leans on collection-injection of `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` across crates, which inherits the silent-DCE hazard: a translator crate the binary never references contributes nothing and the chain is silently short — needs the anti-DCE self-check (expected-count / doctor) the cross-crate doc mandates.

**Mechanisms considered:** thin after-throwing/around Interceptor (or tower Layer) on the upstream aop-proxying chain — translation as one advisor, composing with tx/cache; `trait DataAccessExceptionTranslator { fn translate(&self, &dyn Error) -> Option<DataAccessError> }` — Option=null-is-pass chain-of-responsibility, @Order-sorted, original always chained as source; repository-boundary stereotype marker as the pointcut (component-stereotypes meta-annotation) + a pluggable/configurable marker type (repositoryAnnotationType analogue); translators discovered as `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` beans via collection-injection (any scope, ordered) — origin-agnostic provider; DataAccessError as a structured-kind error enum/trait family (Transient/NonTransient/Recoverable + DuplicateKey/OptimisticLocking/DataIntegrity...) impl std::error::Error with full source(); conditional auto-configuration installs the advisor (@ConditionalOnMissingBean + property default-on) — opt-in-by-default, absent in bare engine; downcast of &dyn Error to the typed native error inside a translator (erased-vs-typed bridge, a runtime failure surface)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Two cross-crate streams, both descriptor-shaped and origin-agnostic, both inheriting the DCE hazard. (a) Translator providers: each vendor integration crate (leaf-sqlx, leaf-diesel) contributes a translator as an ordinary `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` bean descriptor (`&'static TranslatorDescriptor { order, make_fn }`) — fits any Phase-2 substrate (linkme thin-const A/B, build-time codegen C, or explicit D) since it is just a bean of a known trait; ordering is @Order DATA resolved at runtime (never registration order). (b) The advisor + its pointcut marker: the advisor is installed by a conditional auto-configuration that itself is a descriptor in the auto-config stream. The two-source duality slots cleanly: the static link-time 'classpath' contributes native translator descriptors; an optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source contributes translator descriptors that bridge into the module — the chain dispatches both identically because a translator is just `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` (CANDIDATE, not a decision). The repository-boundary marker is metadata read at wiring (component-stereotypes / annotation-model), so the pointcut decision 'does this bean carry the marker?' is a runtime question over collected descriptors (consistent with auto-proxy-creator). leaf-core owns DataAccessError + the translator trait + the marker pointcut + the thin advisor; vendor crates depend UP on leaf-core and contribute translators. CRITICAL anti-DCE self-check: a vendor translator crate the binary never path-references silently contributes nothing → the chain is silently empty/short and native errors leak with NO error — leaf MUST expose a 'discovered-translators' introspection surface and a startup/doctor expected-count check (the cross-crate doc's mandate). Generics: a translator generic over a connection type cannot register (generics-can't-register) → hard-error with a per-instantiation shim.

**T2 — execution context:** Weaving (install the advisor onto a repository bean) is COLD-PATH: it runs in the instance-decoration phase (aop-proxying's after_init seam, driven by bean-instantiation inside the per-bean creation guard — NOT a global lock), exactly Spring's 'DI-time concern even though translation fires at runtime'. The HOT path is per-repository-call at steady state: the advisor awaits `jp.proceed()` (the real DAO method, which is where the actual persistence I/O and any blocking lives — a blocking driver MUST be on spawn_blocking, the caller's-execution-context discipline that the DAO/driver owns, not this advisor) and then, only on the Err branch, runs the translator chain. The chain walk is PURE SYNC CPU work (each translate() is a cheap match/downcast, NOT async — translators must not do I/O; this is a documented contract) so it adds no await and no polling — §2.4 reactive-on-hot-paths is satisfied because the only async point is the proceed() the user already had. Cancellation: if the caller's future is dropped mid-proceed(), standard async cancellation applies to the DAO call; the advisor holds NO resource across proceed() (it only inspects the returned Result), so there is no async-Drop / cancel-safety burden here (unlike tx advice) — a deliberately simple advisor. Errors: the mapper wraps the original native error as the `source` of the new DataAccessError, preserving the full causal chain (§2.9); a translator that itself panics or a downcast that fails must degrade to 'pass' (return the original error untranslated with a rich diagnostic noting which translator failed), never swallow. The translator chain construction (collecting + ordering the providers) happens once at wiring (cold), not per call.

#### R2 — Typed translation at the source, no advisor: a `#[repository]`-emitted Result-mapping wrapper that converts native errors via a typed From/translator at each method boundary
Question whether translation needs the AOP proxy chain at all. Spring needs the proxy because the JVM has no other interception point and the DAO is a compiled class; but leaf controls codegen for repository beans and Rust has a first-class, zero-cost error-mapping idiom (`Result::map_err` / `From` / the `?`-operator's `From` conversion). Realize translation as a TYPED mapping woven at each repository method boundary by the `#[repository]` macro (thin, §2.10 — emits only what a user could hand-write: a `.map_err(translate)` on each method's Result). The DAO stays a framework-unaware POJO; the macro generates a delegating wrapper whose methods call the inner method and map the error: `async fn find(&self, id) -> Result<User, DataAccessError> { self.inner.find(id).await.map_err(|e| ctx.translate_data_access(e)) }`. KEY divergence from R1: the translator chain is invoked through a TYPED entry point. Two sub-shapes, both candidates: (a) RUNTIME-CHAIN typed: the wrapper calls a context-held ordered `DataAccessExceptionTranslator` chain (same provider contract as R1) but passes the TYPED native error (the method's concrete Err type) so translators that impl `TranslatorFor<E>` get the typed value with no downcast; a blanket erased fallback covers unknown types. (b) TRAIT-DRIVEN typed: the native error type itself carries the mapping via a `trait IntoDataAccess { fn into_data_access(self) -> DataAccessError; }` that vendor crates impl for their error type (the rust-native 'the error knows how to translate itself', analogous to JPA's EntityManagerFactory being its own translator) — translation becomes a plain `From`/`Into` the `?` operator can even invoke, and the macro emits `.map_err(IntoDataAccess::into_data_access)`. The pointcut is still the repository-boundary marker (which beans get the `#[repository]` Result-mapping treatment). The hierarchy (DataAccessError) is identical to R1. The whole thing is opt-in-by-default via the same conditional-autoconfig gate, but the weaving is at CODEGEN, not at the proxy seam — so it works even for a trait-less concrete DAO (no proxy needed) and even where aop-proxying can't reach.

**Pros:** Sidesteps aop-proxying's hardest unresolved clashes entirely: NO proxy object, so no 'must the DAO expose a service trait' requirement, no self-invocation leak for the wrapped surface (the mapping is in the generated method body, not on an external reference — though see cons), and no dependence on the still-open proxy-mechanism keystone. Translation works on a trait-less concrete DAO, which the no-CGLIB clash blocks in R1.; Typed error mapping is the idiomatic, zero-cost rust path (§2 low-overhead #2): sub-shape (b) is a plain `From`/`Into` the `?` operator drives — no chain walk, no downcast, no boxed dispatch on the hot path; the compiler monomorphizes the mapping. This is strictly cheaper than R1's erased chain and avoids the erased-vs-typed clash that R1 inherits.; Best diagnostics (§2.9): the translator sees the CONCRETE native error type, so it can match precisely on vendor variants/SQLSTATE without a runtime downcast that could fail — the mapping is exhaustively checked by the type system, and the original is moved (not boxed-by-default) into the source chain.; DAO stays framework-unaware: it returns its native error type; the macro-generated wrapper does the mapping, so the source DAO has zero leaf imports (Spring's non-invasive promise) — and `cargo expand` shows an ordinary `.map_err(...)`, maximally thin-macro and hand-writable (the escape hatch is 'write the .map_err yourself').; Composes with collection-injection discovery for sub-shape (a) (same provider chain as R1) OR with the orphan-rule-respecting `IntoDataAccess` impl for (b) — and (b) is fully static/cross-crate-correct without ANY link-time registration (the impl is found by normal trait resolution), dodging the DCE hazard for the translator path entirely.; No async-Drop / cancel-safety concern (the mapping is post-await pure-sync, like R1) and no proxy/early-reference/cycle coupling at all — the most self-contained realization.

**Cons:** Biggest divergence from Spring's mechanism and from the unification goal (§2.11): translation is NO LONGER one advisor on the shared chain, so it does NOT automatically compose/order with transaction/cache advice on one proxy — leaf would have TWO weaving stories (codegen map_err for translation, proxy chain for tx), and the 'same proxy carries both' property is lost; ordering translation relative to a tx rollback becomes a codegen-vs-proxy interleaving question with no shared answer.; Changes the repository method's RETURN TYPE to `Result<_, DataAccessError>` at the generated boundary — which is honest and typed but means the DAO's own signature (native error) and its exposed signature differ; if a caller injects the DAO by its concrete type and calls the inner method, they get the NATIVE error (the self-invocation leak reappears as a 'which surface did you call' issue), and it ripples into autowiring-resolution (the injected type's method signatures changed).; Sub-shape (b) `IntoDataAccess` is orphan-rule-bound: a vendor error type from a third crate that leaf doesn't own and the vendor-integration crate doesn't own can't get the impl without a newtype — a real friction the JVM's reflective translator-bean approach (R1) doesn't have.; The macro must know each method's Err type to emit the right mapping — generic repository methods (`fn get<T>() -> Result<T, E>`) and methods whose Err is itself generic complicate codegen (generics-can't-register echo); the macro must hard-error or fall back to the erased chain.; Loses Spring's runtime pluggability of the translator set for sub-shape (b): you can't 'add a translator bean at runtime/another scope' to change mapping — the mapping is compiled in. Sub-shape (a) restores runtime pluggability but reintroduces R1's chain (and its DCE/ordering concerns), eroding the distinction.; Pre-judges the metadata/codegen boundary (§3 keystone) toward codegen-at-the-method-boundary, biasing a Phase-2 call — must stay a CANDIDATE.

**Mechanisms considered:** `#[repository]` macro emits a `.map_err(translate)` Result-mapping wrapper per method — translation woven at codegen, not at the proxy seam (thin-macro: emits hand-writable map_err); sub-shape (a): typed runtime chain — wrapper passes the CONCRETE native error to an ordered `TranslatorFor<E>` chain (no downcast for known types, erased fallback for unknown), same provider contract as R1; sub-shape (b): `trait IntoDataAccess { fn into_data_access(self) -> DataAccessError }` impl'd by vendor crates for their error type — translation as plain From/Into the `?` operator drives (the error self-translates, JPA-EMF-is-a-translator analogue); repository-boundary marker as the pointcut (which beans get the map_err treatment); same DataAccessError hierarchy as R1; conditional auto-configuration gate (opt-in-by-default) decides whether the macro-emitted mapping is active; newtype wrapper to satisfy the orphan rule when a vendor error type is foreign to both leaf and the integration crate

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Sub-shape (b) is the cross-crate STANDOUT: `impl IntoDataAccess for VendorError` is found by ordinary trait resolution — NO link-time registration, NO distributed slice, NO DCE hazard, NO force-link discipline, works identically on every target including wasm/embedded/staticlib (the cross-crate doc's most-reliable profile, matching Architecture C/D portability without a build step). The only cross-crate constraint is the orphan rule: the impl must live in leaf-core (owns IntoDataAccess) or the vendor crate (owns VendorError); a foreign-foreign pair needs a newtype in the integration crate. The mapping codegen is per-crate-local (the `#[repository]` macro runs where the DAO is defined and sees its method signatures) — no whole-graph view needed, so it sidesteps the 'binary is the only place the graph is visible' constraint that the proxy/translator-chain paths hit. Sub-shape (a) reintroduces the R1 translator-provider stream (descriptor-shaped, DCE-hazarded, needs the self-check) for runtime pluggability. The repository-boundary marker is read by the macro locally. leaf-core owns IntoDataAccess + DataAccessError; vendor crates impl the trait for their errors (depend UP on leaf-core). Generics: a generic repository method or generic Err type strains the per-method codegen — macro hard-errors with a guidance message or routes to the erased chain (generics-can't-register discipline). The two-source duality is less natural here: a dynamic WASM-module-sourced error type has no compile-time `IntoDataAccess` impl, so it must fall back to sub-shape (a)'s erased runtime chain — the duality splits (static=trait-driven, dynamic=runtime-chain), a CANDIDATE tension for Phase 2.

**T2 — execution context:** Weaving is COMPILE-TIME (codegen), not a refresh-phase step — so unlike R1 there is no after_init/instance-decoration cost at all; the wrapper exists as generated code. The HOT path is per-repository-call: the generated wrapper awaits the inner DAO method (where persistence I/O / blocking lives — driver owns spawn_blocking discipline) then, on Err, runs the mapping. Sub-shape (b) mapping is a monomorphized `From`/`Into` call — pure sync CPU, zero alloc beyond moving the original into the source field, no dispatch, the cheapest possible (§2 low-overhead). Sub-shape (a) mapping walks a typed chain (sync CPU, no I/O — translators-must-not-block contract). Either way the only async point is the inner method's await the user already had — §2.4 satisfied, no polling, reactive. Cancellation: dropping the caller future cancels the inner DAO call; the wrapper holds no resource across the await (mapping is post-await), so no async-Drop / cancel-safety burden. Errors: the original native error is MOVED into DataAccessError.source (not boxed unless erased), preserving the full causal chain (§2.9); an unknown error type in sub-shape (a) yields a 'no translator matched' DataAccessError that still carries the original — never swallowed. No global lock, no cross-thread hop introduced by translation itself.

#### R3 — Boundary-scoped translation context: an ambient translator registry captured around a repository call, with the marker driving where the boundary is established
Reframe translation as a BOUNDARY concern owned by the execution context rather than per-bean weaving or per-method codegen. The insight: 'the persistence boundary' is precisely where leaky errors cross — so model it as an explicit, ambient SCOPE that wraps the repository call, and let any error escaping that scope be translated by the ambient translator registry. This builds on context-propagation / locale-context (the ambient per-execution holder SPI, Phase-2): the container establishes a 'data-access boundary' around calls into repository-marked beans, capturing an ordered translator chain into the ambient context; errors propagating OUT of the boundary are translated as they cross. Two candidate shapes: (a) BOUNDARY-GUARD via a lightweight wrapper the resolution seam hands over (an `Advised`/`Repository<dyn T>` cousin from aop-proxying's R3 family) whose call enters a `with_data_access_scope(translators, || inner.call())` that maps the escaping error — interception as a property of the EDGE (the call into the repository), reusing the tower-Layer interception-tower primitive aop-proxying R3 proposes (a `TranslationLayer` that catches and maps the inner Service's error). (b) MACRO-DELIMITED boundary: `#[repository]` emits a `data_access_boundary!` scope guard around each method body whose result-path consults the ambient translator registry — the boundary is lexical, established by the generated wrapper, and the registry is looked up from context (so the translator set IS runtime-pluggable, unlike R2-b, because it's read from the ambient context at call time). The translator contract + ordered chain + DataAccessError hierarchy are the SAME as R1; what differs is WHERE the chain lives (ambient context, captured per-boundary) and the framing (a boundary you cross, not an advisor you attach). The marker (@Repository analogue) defines WHERE the container establishes the boundary. Opt-in-by-default via the same conditional-autoconfig gate, which here installs the boundary-establishment behavior + populates the ambient translator registry.

**Pros:** Models Spring's CONCEPTUAL intent most literally — 'the persistence boundary is where leaky exceptions cross into business code' becomes an explicit, named boundary you cross, not an implicit proxy detail; arguably the clearest mental model and best diagnostics (§2.9: the error report can say 'crossing the data-access boundary of UserRepo, translated SQLSTATE 23505 → DuplicateKey').; Strong unification (§2.11) with context-propagation / locale-context: the ambient translator registry rides the SAME capture/restore SPI that transaction sync, locale, and tracing use — so translation participates in the framework's one context-propagation story rather than inventing its own, and an async thread-hop (async-execution advice moving the call) carries the translator set across via the same mechanism that moves the tx/locale context.; The boundary naturally composes with transaction-management (the canonical boundary concern): a tx and a translation boundary can share one scope-establishment point, and ordering (translate AFTER tx rollback decided) is a boundary-nesting question with a single ambient answer — cleaner than R1's two-advisors-on-a-chain ordering puzzle for THIS specific compose.; Translator set stays runtime-pluggable (read from the ambient registry at call time) yet the boundary itself can be cheap; sub-shape (b) keeps the trait-less-DAO-works property of R2 (no proxy needed — the boundary is in the generated method body) while NOT compiling the translator set in.; Origin-agnostic and dynamic-source-friendly: the ambient registry is populated from translator descriptors (any origin — native or WASM-module), and the boundary mechanism doesn't care where a translator came from; the two-source duality blends as smoothly as R1.; Sub-shape (a)'s TranslationLayer reuses aop-proxying R3's tower interception-tower primitive directly, so if Phase-2 picks the tower model for AOP, translation is just another Layer with zero bespoke machinery.

**Cons:** DEEPLY entangled with two undecided Phase-2 keystones: context-propagation's ambient-holder mechanism (the rust-native ThreadLocal-across-await answer) AND, for sub-shape (a), aop-proxying's resolution-seam/tower model — presenting this as a realization biases BOTH signals; it is the most speculative of the three and must stay a strong CANDIDATE, not a lean.; Ambient/implicit boundary risks the predictability problem the cross-crate doc warns about (donsz.nl): an error translated by an ambient registry whose contents depend on what's in context is harder to reason about locally than R2's explicit typed map_err or R1's visible advisor — cuts against 'rich, legible' diagnostics if the ambient set is surprising.; The boundary must catch errors crossing it WITHOUT async Drop: if the inner call is cancelled or the future dropped mid-boundary, there is no async-Drop to run boundary-exit translation — so the translation must happen on the RESULT path (like R1/R2), not on scope-Drop, which collapses sub-shape (b)'s 'Drop-driven boundary' back toward an explicit result-mapping (the boundary guard can't rely on Drop for the mapping itself, only for context teardown).; Establishing + tearing down an ambient scope per repository call is more per-call machinery than R1's chain-walk or R2's map_err — a context capture/restore on every call (even successful ones) unless carefully made zero-cost-on-success; risks violating §2 low-overhead #2 if the ambient scope isn't free when no error occurs.; If the boundary is at the EDGE (sub-shape a), it inherits aop-proxying R3's 'is THIS edge translated?' consistency problem — some injection points might cross the boundary and others not, unless every handed-out repository reference establishes it; Spring's guarantee is 'the repository is wrapped once, globally', which an edge model must reproduce.; Most machinery to build and the least direct parity with Spring's actual advisor mechanism — risks over-engineering a concern R1 solves with one thin interceptor.

**Mechanisms considered:** ambient 'data-access boundary' scope established around repository-marked calls, carrying an ordered translator registry via the context-propagation/locale-context holder SPI; sub-shape (a): boundary-guard / TranslationLayer at the resolution edge (reusing aop-proxying R3's tower interception-tower primitive) — interception as a property of the A→repository edge; sub-shape (b): `#[repository]`-emitted lexical `data_access_boundary!` scope guard per method, consulting the ambient translator registry at call time (runtime-pluggable, trait-less-DAO-friendly); same translator contract + ordered chain + DataAccessError hierarchy as R1; the marker defines WHERE the boundary is established; translation on the RESULT path (not scope-Drop) because no async Drop — the boundary guard maps the escaping error explicitly; conditional auto-configuration installs the boundary behavior + populates the ambient translator registry (opt-in-by-default)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The translator-provider stream is the same descriptor-shaped, origin-agnostic, DCE-hazarded stream as R1 (populating the ambient registry instead of a per-bean advisor's chain) — fits any Phase-2 substrate and needs the same anti-DCE self-check. The ambient translator REGISTRY is a single context-scoped collection populated once at wiring (cold) from those descriptors, then captured into the boundary scope at call time; this couples translation to whatever context-propagation mechanism Phase-2 picks for ambient holders across .await (the rust-native ThreadLocal-across-await answer — undecided). Sub-shape (a) additionally couples to aop-proxying's resolution-seam model (a cross-crate API-surface concern if the handed-over type changes, like R3 there). The two-source duality blends smoothly: native and WASM-module-sourced translators both populate the ambient registry as descriptors and the boundary consults them uniformly (CANDIDATE). The marker (where the boundary is established) is read at wiring as metadata (annotation-model/component-stereotypes). leaf-core owns DataAccessError + translator trait + the boundary primitive (built atop the context-propagation SPI it does NOT define); vendor crates contribute translators. Generics-can't-register applies to translator descriptors (per-instantiation shim). The boundary establishment must be expressible identically whether the proxy/edge story is codegen or runtime, to stay origin-agnostic.

**T2 — execution context:** This realization is the most execution-context-INTENSIVE, so its T2 is load-bearing. The boundary is established on the CALLER's execution context around the repository call (cold-ish per-call setup; must be near-zero-cost when no error occurs — the success path should not pay for capture/restore it doesn't need). The ambient translator registry is captured into the boundary scope following the context-propagation discipline: if an async-execution advisor hops the call to another thread, the registry must travel via the same capture/restore SPI (this is the exact cross-thread-context case §2.3 demands rigor for). The inner repository call awaits (persistence I/O / blocking lives there — driver owns spawn_blocking). Translation fires on the RESULT/error path crossing the boundary — pure sync CPU chain walk (translators must not block/await), NOT on scope-Drop (no async Drop on stable; Drop only tears down the ambient context binding synchronously, which it can since the registry is just Arc<[Arc<dyn Translator>]>). §2.4: hot path is reactive (the only await is the inner call); the boundary adds no polling. Cancellation: if the caller future is dropped mid-call, the inner call cancels; the boundary's context-binding Drop runs synchronously (release the ambient slot) — the translation simply doesn't fire (there is no error result to translate), which is correct. No global lock; the ambient registry is read-shared (Arc), established per-boundary without contention. Errors carry the boundary identity (which repository, which method) + the original native error as source into the §2.9 chain — the richest diagnostic of the three, at the cost of the most machinery.

### Rust clashes
- No reflective exception-type matching — Spring's translators match against java.lang.reflect / instanceof on a runtime-loaded exception class hierarchy (HibernateException, JPA PersistenceException, SQLException), and SQLErrorCodeSQLExceptionTranslator reads vendor SQL error codes reflectively. Rust has no runtime RTTI: a translator cannot enumerate or match an arbitrary error's type by name. The native error must be matched either by downcast against a known concrete type (R1's erased &dyn Error → downcast, a runtime failure surface) or by a typed/From conversion the type system checks (R2, no reflection needed). 'Translate any vendor exception by reflecting on its class' has no Rust equivalent — leaf either downcasts known types (and passes unknowns) or relies on the vendor error impl'ing a translation trait.
- The DataAccessException hierarchy is unchecked exceptions thrown through call stacks; Rust has no exceptions and no unchecked-throw — errors are values in Result<T, E>. Spring's 'the proxy catches the thrown native exception and rethrows a DataAccessException' becomes 'the wrapper maps the Err variant of the returned Result'. This is actually CLEANER in Rust (the error is a value, mapping is map_err/From, the cause-chain is std::error::Error::source) — but it forces a discipline Spring doesn't need: every advisable repository method must return a Result whose Err can be carried/mapped (erased to dyn Error + Send for R1's chain, or a known type for R2). A DAO method that panics instead of returning Err (a vendor that panics on a constraint violation) cannot be translated by a value-mapping advisor — leaf must document that panics are NOT translated (catch_unwind is not the model), unlike the JVM where a thrown exception IS the channel.
- 'It can create a proxy you didn't ask for' + no-CGLIB — Spring wraps a @Repository bean in a proxy even with no other advice (JDK dynamic proxy, or CGLIB if no interfaces). Rust cannot synthesize a proxy for a trait-less concrete DAO (the aop-proxying no-CGLIB clash). So R1's 'every @Repository gets wrapped' guarantee may NOT hold for a concrete DAO exposing no service trait — leaf must either require repository beans to expose a trait (turning 'program to traits' into a hard requirement for translation), or use a codegen-at-the-method-boundary approach (R2) that needs no proxy and works on a concrete DAO. There is no 'transparently proxy any concrete repository class' option.
- The erased-vs-typed error clash (inherited from aop-proxying) bites translation specifically — the proxy chain is heterogeneous so it carries the error as &dyn Error, but a translator that distinguishes a DuplicateKey from a generic DataIntegrityViolation must inspect the CONCRETE native error (a sqlx::Error::Database with a specific SQLSTATE, a constraint name). R1 downcasts (runtime failure surface: a translator expecting the wrong type silently passes); R2 keeps it typed (compile-checked) but loses the uniform erased chain. The JVM hides this with reflection + instanceof; Rust forces the choice between an erased downcast and typed codegen — neither free.
- Translator discovery via collection-injection inherits the silent-DCE hazard — Spring autodetects PersistenceExceptionTranslator beans from a live classpath/context; leaf discovers `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` beans across crates over whatever Phase-2 substrate. A vendor translator crate the binary never path-references contributes NOTHING (Layer 0), and a dropped registration yields an empty chain with no error — so native errors leak UNtranslated, silently, possibly only in release/under LLD. This is worse than a missing bean (which fails loud) because untranslated-but-still-an-error looks like it 'worked'. Leaf MUST ship the anti-DCE self-check (expected-translator-count / doctor) the cross-crate doc mandates. R2-sub-shape-b sidesteps this (trait-resolution, no registration) but is orphan-rule-bound.
- No async Drop for boundary/advice teardown — relevant if translation is modeled as a scope/boundary (R3) or composed with a tx advisor that must roll back. The translation mapping itself is post-await pure-sync (cheap, no resource held across proceed()), so this feature ADDS little async-Drop burden — but if R3's boundary or a co-located tx boundary holds a resource, cancellation mid-call cannot run async cleanup; translation must fire on the result path, never on Drop. This constrains R3's 'Drop-driven boundary' sub-shape out: the boundary's Drop can only tear down the ambient context binding synchronously, not run async translation.
- The self-invocation / non-proxied-call leak is the SAME structural fact as aop-proxying, and translation makes its consequence sharper — a self-invoked or final/non-advised repository method bypasses the translating advisor (R1) and the NATIVE error leaks where the caller expected a DataAccessError. This is Spring's exact gotcha (line 2223), reproduced honestly in Rust for the identical reason (the advisor lives on the external reference). R2's codegen-at-the-method-boundary narrows the leak (the mapping is in the generated method body, not an external proxy) but reintroduces it as a 'which surface did you call' issue (the inner native method vs the mapped wrapper). There is no hidden escape hatch; leaf documents the boundary and steers to refactoring the self-call out.
- The pluggable repositoryAnnotationType has no reflection-driven analogue — Spring lets you point the post-processor at your own @Dao annotation, decoupling translation from the stereotype, decided at runtime from annotation metadata. Rust has no runtime annotation scan; the 'which marker counts as a repository boundary' decision must be DATA on the bean descriptor (the marker the macro stamped) read at wiring, and 'use my own marker' means leaf's stereotype/annotation-model must let a user mint a composed marker (component-stereotypes meta-annotation) whose descriptor flag the translation advisor's pointcut matches — a metadata convention, not a reflective annotation-type configuration knob.

### Interactions
- **aop-proxying** — R1 and R3-sub-shape-a build DIRECTLY on aop-proxying's substrate: the translating advisor is an Interceptor/Layer on the call-routing chain, woven via the wrap-and-swap seam. This feature is one CONCRETE advisor the substrate was kept mechanism-neutral for; it inherits every aop-proxying keystone (proxy mechanism, must-a-DAO-expose-a-trait, erased-vs-typed args, self-invocation boundary, async-Drop cancel-safety). R2 deliberately does NOT use the proxy substrate (codegen map_err instead), which is precisely why R2 sidesteps those clashes — the divergence axis between the realizations is largely 'ride the proxy chain vs not'.
- **auto-proxy-creator** — For R1, auto-proxy-creator collects the translation advisor into the SAME ordered per-bean chain as transaction/cache/validation advice and decides eligibility (does the bean carry the repository-boundary marker?). The classic 'cache outside tx' ordering concern (line 1772) has a translation analogue: translation should typically run INNERMOST (closest to the real method) so it catches the raw native error before a tx advisor re-wraps it — but that ordering is auto-proxy-creator's to resolve via @Order on the advisor, not this feature's. This feature only contributes one advisor descriptor with an order hint; the union/ordering is auto-proxy-creator's job.
- **aspect-model** — exception-translation is a DOGFOODED, framework-shipped advisor (not a user-declared @Aspect) — but it is the SAME shape aspect-model produces: a pointcut (the repository-boundary marker) + after-throwing/around advice (the error mapper). It demonstrates that leaf's own cross-cutting features are built on the same advisor model users get. aspect-model defines the advisor authoring contract; this feature is one instance of it that leaf ships, advising only repository-marked beans.
- **component-stereotypes** — The repository-boundary marker (leaf's @Repository analogue) is a STEREOTYPE from component-stereotypes — a @Component-equivalent meta-annotated marker that carries the extra semantic 'this is a persistence-boundary bean'. This is the natural pointcut: the stereotype marks precisely the beans translation should advise. The pluggable repositoryAnnotationType requirement means component-stereotypes must let a user mint their own composed marker (a `#[dao]`) whose descriptor flag the translation pointcut also matches — so 'decouple translation from the leaf stereotype' works as in Spring.
- **annotation-model** — The repository-boundary marker is resolved through annotation-model's metadata-as-data, distance-ordered, user-extensible vocabulary: 'is this bean a repository boundary?' is answered by reading a descriptor flag (meta-present marker), NOT a runtime annotation scan (which Rust lacks). The pluggable-marker requirement (use my own @Dao) is exactly annotation-model's composed/meta-annotation capability. The pointcut decision is metadata read at wiring time.
- **auto-configuration** — Translation is OPT-IN-BY-DEFAULT via a conditional auto-configuration — the leaf analogue of PersistenceExceptionTranslationAutoConfiguration. The auto-config registers the translating advisor (R1/R3) or activates the macro-emitted mapping (R2), gated by @ConditionalOnMissingBean (user can replace it) and a property (default-on). This is why translation works out of the box in leaf-boot but is absent in the bare leaf-framework engine (container-core parity). The auto-config is itself a descriptor in the auto-config ordering stream.
- **condition-family** — The auto-config gate uses OnMissingBean (back off if the user defined their own translation advisor) + OnProperty (leaf.persistence.exception-translation.enabled, default-on, matchIfMissing) — and likely OnClass/OnBean of a persistence integration so translation only activates when a data-access vendor crate is present. These conditions are pure predicates over container state (this feature supplies the metadata; condition-family supplies the predicates), making translation 'opinionated but always overridable'.
- **collection-injection** — R1 and R3 discover the translator chain by injecting ALL beans of type `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` as an ordered collection (ordered via @Order/@Priority — ordering is a consumer concern, strictly separate from startup order). This is exactly Spring's 'translators autodetected from any scope, sorted by Ordered' — re-expressed as leaf's collection-injection with ordering-as-data. The chain IS an ordered collection-injection of providers.
- **transaction-management** — The closest sibling and the canonical compose case: a repository bean often carries BOTH transaction advice and translation advice on the same proxy (Spring's 'the same proxy can carry both'). Ordering is correctness-relevant — translation should see the raw native error before a tx rollback re-wraps it, so translation typically sits innermost. R3's boundary model could share one scope-establishment point with the tx boundary. transaction-management is the proof that this feature's advisor must compose cleanly on the shared chain (R1) rather than being bespoke (R2's separate codegen story makes this compose harder).
- **context-propagation** — R3 is BUILT on context-propagation's ambient-holder capture/restore SPI (the translator registry rides it, and survives async thread-hops). Even in R1/R2, IF translation must follow the call across an async-execution thread hop, the translator set / boundary identity is ambient context that context-propagation must move across the boundary. This feature is a consumer of whatever ambient-context mechanism Phase-2 defines; R3 makes that dependency central, R1/R2 make it incidental.
- **async-execution** — If a repository call is dispatched onto another executor by async-execution advice (an interceptor that moves proceed() to a TaskExecutor), the translation advisor must still catch the error on the result path — and any ambient translator context (R3) must travel via context-propagation. This is the cross-thread case §2.3 demands rigor for: the translation must fire wherever the error materializes, and the translator chain (sync, no I/O) must be reachable on that executor.
- **bean-instantiation** — For R1/R3, weaving the translating advisor onto a repository bean happens at the instance-decoration point bean-instantiation drives (after_init, inside the per-bean creation guard — not a global lock); the possibly-wrapped handle becomes the bean's published identity so every injection of the repository gets the translating proxy. For R2 there is no instantiation-time weaving (the wrapper is generated code). This is the 'DI-time concern even though translation fires at runtime' (line 2219).
- **container-core** — container-core's engine-vs-façade split realizes the opt-in parity: the bare engine installs no translation auto-config, so repository beans are NOT advised and native errors flow through untranslated (Spring's plain-BeanFactory behavior — translation is opt-in, you declare the post-processor yourself). The leaf-boot façade installs the conditional auto-config that activates translation. This feature provides the advisor + hierarchy; container-core fixes the boundary that makes translation an opt-in-by-using-boot capability.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Does translation RIDE the shared AOP advisor chain (R1: one Interceptor composing with tx/cache, full unification but inheriting every aop-proxying keystone) or is it woven at the repository method boundary by codegen (R2: typed map_err/From, sidesteps the proxy keystones and works on trait-less DAOs, but loses the 'same proxy carries both tx and translation' compose and forks the weaving story)?
