This is THE keystone for this feature and it cross-cuts the metadata/codegen boundary (§3) and the aop-proxying proxy-mechanism decision. Held unresolved.
- Is the native error matched by RUNTIME DOWNCAST against known concrete types over an erased &dyn Error chain (R1, a runtime failure surface — wrong-type translator silently passes), or by TYPED conversion the compiler checks (R2's TranslatorFor<E> / IntoDataAccess, exhaustive but orphan-rule-bound and not runtime-pluggable for unknown types)? This is the rust-native re-expression of Spring's reflection+instanceof exception matching and it interacts directly with aop-proxying's erased-vs-typed args clash and the §2.9 diagnostics for the no-translator-matched / downcast-failure case.
- Is the translator set RUNTIME-PLUGGABLE (discovered as `dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator` beans across crates, ordered, any scope — Spring parity, but inheriting the silent-DCE hazard and needing an anti-DCE self-check) or COMPILE-RESOLVED (R2-b: the vendor error type impls a translation trait, found by normal trait resolution — zero DCE hazard, works on every target including wasm, but not runtime-extensible and orphan-rule-bound)? The two-source duality splits here: static link-time translators blend with a runtime chain (R1) smoothly, but a dynamic WASM-module-sourced error type has no compile-time trait impl and must fall back to the runtime chain — a candidate tension, not a decision.
- How is the silent-DCE failure of translator discovery surfaced? An empty/short translator chain yields UNtranslated native errors that still look like errors (worse than a loud missing-bean failure — it looks like it 'worked'). What is the expected-count / doctor self-check that turns 'a vendor translator crate was never linked' into a loud diagnostic, and does it belong to this feature or to the shared cross-crate substrate's self-check?
- Must a repository (data-access-boundary) bean expose a SERVICE TRAIT for translation to weave (R1's consequence of the no-CGLIB clash — a trait-less concrete DAO can't be proxied), making 'program to traits' a hard requirement for translation; or does leaf use a codegen-at-the-method-boundary path (R2) that needs no proxy and works on a concrete DAO? This cross-cuts aop-proxying's identical open question and injection-styles / component-stereotypes.
- Where does translation sit in the shared advisor ORDER relative to transaction-management on a co-advised repository bean (translation innermost so it catches the raw native error before a tx rollback re-wraps it)? This is the translation analogue of 'cache outside vs inside the tx' and is auto-proxy-creator's ordering to resolve, but this feature must declare the right order hint and the correctness contract — held as a cross-cutting ordering question.
- Should translation be modeled as an ambient BOUNDARY (R3) riding context-propagation's holder SPI — clearest mental model and unifying with tx/locale context across async thread-hops, but entangling translation with the undecided ambient-context mechanism AND risking the predictability problem of an implicit ambient registry — or as an explicit per-bean advisor (R1) / per-method mapping (R2)? Depends on the Phase-2 context-propagation and aop-proxying resolution-seam decisions; the most speculative coupling.
- What exactly is the DataAccessError hierarchy's rust-native shape — a single enum with a structured `kind` (cheap, exhaustive matching, but closed to user-added subtypes across crates) vs a trait family `dyn DataAccessError` with downcastable subtypes (open/extensible like Spring's class hierarchy, but heavier and needing downcast to discriminate)? This determines whether a vendor crate can introduce a new DataAccessError subtype (Spring allows it via subclassing) and how user code matches on it — and it interacts with the unified error/diagnostics model (§2.9 keystone).

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the dedicated @Repository section in full (lines 462-484: the only-stereotype-with-real-mechanics framing; the PersistenceExceptionTranslationPostProcessor-as-BPP-adds-advisor mechanic; translators autodetected from any scope and @Order-sorted; the DataAccessException hierarchy goal — catch DuplicateKeyException without importing Hibernate; @Repository as the natural pointcut; non-invasive via advisor/proxy not inheritance; composes with @Transactional; rejected alternatives = base class or hand-translation) and the DI-time-exception-translation section (2217-2225: DI-time weaving / runtime firing; it's the proxy that translates; the self-invoked/final leak gotcha; DataAccessException extends NestedRuntimeException unchecked; originals wrapped never lost; the v4 property rename + module move) plus the BeansException unchecked-by-design rationale (2170-2178) and the hidden concepts (479-484: marker must be on the CLASS not interface; can create a proxy you didn't ask for; repositoryAnnotationType pluggable; translators from any scope and ordered). I separated THIS feature (the DataAccessException hierarchy + translator-provider contract/chain/discovery + repository-boundary pointcut marker + the single thin mapping advisor) from aop-proxying (the proxy substrate it rides), auto-proxy-creator (advisor union/ordering), aspect-model (advisor authoring contract), and auto-configuration/condition-family (the opt-in-by-default conditional registration). I grounded the advisor on aop-proxying's call-routing primitive, the pointcut marker on component-stereotypes/annotation-model, the translator chain on collection-injection with ordering-as-data, and the opt-in gate on a conditional auto-config (OnMissingBean + OnProperty default-on). T1 grounded against the cross-crate doc's no-runtime-reflection reality, the DCE layers + silent-empty-iterable hazard + anti-DCE self-check mandate (135-152, 345-346), generics-can't-register (153, 349), the four architectures A/B/C/D (277-337), and the two-source duality (wasm path 171, 356; architecture C/D as the portable/wasm path). T2 grounded against charter §2.3/2.4/2.5/2.9 and the nightly doc: AFIT/RPITIT-not-dyn-safe forcing boxed futures at any dyn advisor chain (162-182, 260), no async Drop (273-281), trait-upcasting stable 1.86 for injecting a wrapped repository as dyn Trait (118-126), std::error::Error::source as the rust-native cause-chain.

The three realizations are genuinely divergent on the axis that DEFINES this feature — WHERE and HOW translation is realized: R1 = translation as a standard thin advisor on the shared AOP chain with a discovered, @Order-ed, null-is-pass translator-provider chain (purest Spring parity, maximal unification, composes with tx/cache on one proxy, but inherits ALL of aop-proxying's keystones and the erased-vs-typed downcast clash); R2 = translation woven at the repository method boundary by codegen as a typed map_err/From (sidesteps the proxy keystones entirely — works on trait-less concrete DAOs, zero-cost typed mapping, and sub-shape-b's IntoDataAccess trait dodges the DCE hazard via normal trait resolution — but forks the weaving story, loses the shared-proxy compose with tx, and is orphan-rule-bound); R3 = translation as an ambient persistence-BOUNDARY riding context-propagation's holder SPI (clearest conceptual model of 'where leaky errors cross', unifies with tx/locale context across async thread-hops, but most entangled with two undecided keystones — context-propagation AND aop-proxying's resolution-seam — and risks the implicit-ambient predictability problem). R1 vs R2 differ on ride-the-proxy-chain vs codegen-at-the-boundary; R1/R2 vs R3 differ on per-bean/per-method weaving vs an ambient boundary you cross.

Things I deliberately did NOT decide (held as Phase-2 questions): whether translation rides the AOP chain or is codegen at the boundary (the keystone); erased-downcast vs typed-conversion error matching; runtime-pluggable vs compile-resolved translator set; how silent-DCE of translators is surfaced; whether a repository bean must expose a service trait (the no-CGLIB consequence); translation's order relative to transaction advice; whether the ambient-boundary (R3) model is worth its context-propagation entanglement; and the DataAccessError hierarchy's enum-vs-trait-family shape. Every realization keeps the translator-provider contract ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (a dyn DataAccessExceptionTranslator / IntoDataAccess impl from a native crate, a test double, or a future WASM-module source joins identically), reproduces the self-invocation/non-proxied leak HONESTLY (same external-wrapper structural fact as aop-proxying), keeps the original native error always chained as the source (Spring's 'wrapped, never lost' → std::error::Error::source), and keeps the repository-boundary marker as DATA read at wiring (the rust-native replacement for Spring's reflective annotation scan + pluggable repositoryAnnotationType). All three inherit the silent-DCE self-check mandate for the translator-provider stream (except R2-b which avoids it via trait resolution). Naming ('DataAccessError', 'DataAccessExceptionTranslator', 'TranslatorFor<E>', 'IntoDataAccess', 'repository-boundary marker', '#[repository]'/'#[dao]', 'data_access_boundary', 'TranslationLayer') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (462-484 the @Repository real-mechanics section; 2170-2178 unchecked-by-design rationale; 2217-2225 DI-time exception translation + it's-the-proxy-that-translates + self-invocation leak + v4 property rename; 2413 the property-rename note); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2, deferred keystones §3, topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (135-171 DCE layers + silent-empty + linkme-default + thin-descriptor + ordering-as-data + anti-DCE self-check; 277-362 architectures A/B/C/D + generics-can't-register + two-source/wasm scoping + orphan-respecting trait-resolution as the most-portable path); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (118-126 trait-upcasting 1.86; 160-182 RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe forcing boxed-future at the dyn advisor chain; 260 dyn+Send boxing; 273-281 no async Drop).

## TaskExecutor Abstraction  `[task-execution]`

Spring's `TaskExecutor` (extends `java.util.concurrent.Executor`) is the unifying SPI for "run this Runnable somehow." Its whole reason to exist (Spring doc lines 2016-2023): direct `new Thread()`/`ExecutorService` use hides resource limits, cannot be centrally tuned/monitored, and cannot participate in container lifecycle/shutdown. By INJECTING a TaskExecutor, the same call site can be backed by a bounded pool (`ThreadPoolTaskExecutor`), a thread-per-task / virtual-thread executor (`SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor` with optional concurrency limit + graceful shutdown), or a context-propagating decorated executor — "chosen by configuration, not code." This is IoC applied to concurrency: code declares "run this," the container decides HOW. Consequences I must preserve as INTENT (not mechanism): (1) one-switch substrate swap — virtual-thread adoption is a deployment decision, not a code rewrite (lines 2049-2058), and the swap must NOT change the thread-safety / context-visibility contract; (2) the executor is a managed bean that participates in lifecycle — graceful drain on shutdown, lifecycle ordering (ties to runtime-lifecycle / context-close); (3) named executors carry role semantics — Spring's `applicationTaskExecutor` (the one default execution facility backing async events, background bootstrap, JPA bootstrap, blocking integrations) vs a `taskExecutor`-named bean for @EnableAsync, with primary/qualifier selection (lines 2062-2071); (4) `spring.task.execution.mode=force` lets the container centrally override all routing — central tuning is a first-class lever; (5) decorators compose into a chain (`CompositeTaskDecorator`, line 2153) so context propagation, MDC, observation wrap submitted work uniformly; (6) a concurrency ceiling (`@ConcurrencyLimit` / SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor limit, lines 2077-2081) matters precisely because unbounded substrates (virtual threads) have no implicit pool ceiling. The rust-native reframing: Rust has no raw threads to IoC over — it has an async runtime. So "TaskExecutor" becomes an injectable, container-owned, lifecycle-participating abstraction over WHERE work runs: spawning futures onto the runtime, offloading blocking work (spawn_blocking-equivalent), and bounding concurrency — selected by configuration, swappable (multi-thread pool / current-thread / bounded-permit / decorated) without touching call sites. This feature is the SHARED execution substrate that async-execution (#[async] proxy), scheduling (@Scheduled), event-multicaster (async dispatch), and background-bootstrap all draw on; it does NOT itself implement those — it is the "run somehow" seam they target. I deliberately do NOT decide the async-execution/context-propagation meta-model (charter §3) — only realize the injectable, tunable, lifecycle-bound execution-facility abstraction and its selection/decoration/shutdown semantics.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — `TaskExecutor` as an object-safe dyn trait bean: the literal injectable execution facility
Mirror Spring most directly: a small object-safe trait `TaskExecutor` is THE injectable abstraction, registered as a managed bean and injected as `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>` wherever code declares 'run this somehow.' The trait is split exactly as Spring splits Executor/AsyncTaskExecutor: a base `spawn(&self, task: BoxFuture<'static, ()>)` (fire-and-forget, the Executor analogue) plus `submit<...>`-style methods that return a join handle for value+error recovery (the AsyncTaskExecutor analogue), and an explicit `spawn_blocking(&self, f: Box<dyn FnOnce() + Send>)` seam so blocking work is a NAMED, separate call (execution-context discipline, charter §2.3) rather than silently smuggled onto an async worker. Because the trait must be a trait object (so the concrete substrate is swappable and a future WASM/test-double provider can stand in), its async surface is type-erased to boxed futures at the dyn boundary — the unavoidable RPITIT/AFIT-dyn wall (nightly doc: AFIT traits are not object-safe). Concrete impls are ordinary beans: `MultiThreadExecutor` (work-stealing runtime pool — the ThreadPoolTaskExecutor analogue, with core/queue tuning), `CurrentThreadExecutor` (LocalSet-style single-threaded), `BoundedExecutor` (wraps another + a `Semaphore` permit ceiling — the SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor-concurrency-limit / @ConcurrencyLimit primitive), and `DecoratedExecutor` (wraps another + an ordered `Vec<Arc<dyn TaskDecorator>>` applied at SUBMIT time on the caller's context — the ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator/CompositeTaskDecorator analogue). Selection is pure DI: the container's default-config registers ONE primary `applicationTaskExecutor` (role-marked, the shared facility); @async/scheduling/event-dispatch resolve it by primary, override by qualifier (`#[qualifier("...")]`), and a global `execution.mode=force` flag on the container makes the facade rebind those consumers to the primary regardless of local qualifiers. The 'virtual-thread one-switch' becomes 'a config property picks which concrete impl is primary' — no call site changes because every call site holds `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>`. Runtime substrate stays pluggable (charter §2.6): `leaf-tokio` provides the spawn/spawn_blocking impls; the trait itself lives in core and names no runtime.

**Pros:** Tightest intent parity: 'inject the abstraction, container picks the backing' is literally a dyn-trait bean with primary/qualifier selection — the Spring mental model ports 1:1 and is teachable.; Swap-by-config falls out for free: call sites bind `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>`, so changing which impl is primary (pool vs current-thread vs bounded vs decorated) needs zero code edits — the one-switch-virtual-threads intent realized.; Decorator chain is a first-class composition (DecoratedExecutor + ordered TaskDecorator list) matching CompositeTaskDecorator; context-propagation slots in as just another decorator without touching the executor or call sites.; Origin-agnostic by construction: consumers see only `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>`; a WASM-module-backed or test-fake executor is indistinguishable, satisfying the provider-of-dyn-Trait-must-not-assume-native constraint.; Blocking work is a named method (`spawn_blocking`), making 'what may block' explicit at the type level rather than emergent — directly serves charter §2.3 execution-context discipline.

**Cons:** The dyn boundary forces boxed futures + boxed closures per submission (alloc per task) — fine for cold/coarse work (bootstrap, scheduled jobs) but a real cost on hot per-request fan-out; the zero-cost monomorphized spawn path is lost on the dyn surface (charter §2.5 tension).; An object-safe trait can't carry generic `submit<F, T>` ergonomically; value-returning submit must erase the output (`BoxFuture<'static, Result<Box<dyn Any + Send>, _>>` or a typed JoinHandle newtype), pushing typed convenience into a separate non-dyn extension trait or free fn — two surfaces to learn.; Cancellation semantics must be designed into the trait (does the returned handle carry a cancel token? does drop abort?) — Rust has no thread-interrupt; getting this wrong silently leaks tasks (see T2).; 'Run a Runnable' is JVM-shaped; forcing a closure/future API risks importing the Executor shape where an async-native `spawn(future)` is the better primitive — the base method choice (future vs closure) pre-commits ergonomics.

**Mechanisms considered:** object-safe `dyn TaskExecutor` trait stored as `Arc<dyn TaskExecutor>` (the injectable bean); boxed futures + boxed FnOnce at the dyn boundary (async-trait/trait_variant shim — AFIT not dyn-safe per nightly doc); runtime registry: executor impls are ordinary beans selected by primary/qualifier via autowiring-resolution + a force-mode container flag; decorator chain as an ordered Vec<Arc<dyn TaskDecorator>> applied at submit time (CompositeTaskDecorator analogue); Semaphore-based BoundedExecutor wrapper for the concurrency-ceiling primitive (@ConcurrencyLimit / SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor limit); runtime substrate (spawn/spawn_blocking) provided by an integration crate (leaf-tokio), trait named in core — runtime-agnostic seam

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The trait + TaskDecorator SPI + the default impls' role descriptors live in leaf-core; the actual spawn/spawn_blocking implementations live in a runtime integration crate (leaf-tokio) that registers an `applicationTaskExecutor` bean. This is exactly the cross-crate force-link hazard the composition doc warns about (Layer-0): if `leaf-tokio` is declared but never path-referenced, its registration vanishes and the binary silently has NO execution facility — catastrophic, since async/scheduling/events all need it. Mitigation candidates (decisions deferred): the runtime crate registers its executor via the SAME descriptor substrate as every other bean (linkme distributed-slice default OR inventory OR explicit `with_executor(...)`), and the facade runs a NON-NEGOTIABLE self-check that fails loudly at refresh if no `applicationTaskExecutor` was found (expected-vs-found, the silent-DCE defense the doc mandates). Because a runtime is mandatory for an async-first framework, the bootstrap layer should additionally force-link the default runtime crate via an explicit reference (Architecture-D-leaning ergonomic helper) so the common case can't silently lose its executor. Decorators contributed by integration crates (a tracing/observation decorator in leaf-observability) register into the ordered decorator list via the same SPI; ordering is resolved at runtime by a priority field, never by link order (composition doc §'do all ordering at runtime'). The whole feature is substrate-neutral: it works identically whether executor/decorator descriptors arrive by slice, constructor, or explicit builder call.

**T2 — execution context:** This feature IS the execution-context primitive, so its T2 is load-bearing. Construction of executor beans is cold-path, async (a pool may pre-spawn workers; current-thread builds a LocalSet) and MUST be tied to the chosen runtime — the executor bean can only be built after the runtime exists, so it is among the earliest-instantiated infrastructure beans. `spawn(future)` runs the future on the backing runtime's workers (multi-thread = work-stealing, requires the future be `Send + 'static`; current-thread = `?Send` allowed but pinned). `spawn_blocking(closure)` MUST route to a dedicated blocking pool so it never stalls async workers — this is the explicit 'what may block' seam; the trait contract documents that the async `spawn` path must NEVER receive blocking work. Cancellation: the returned join handle carries a cancel signal; on handle-drop the policy (abort vs detach) must be explicit and documented per impl — there is no thread interrupt, so cancellation is cooperative (the task observes a token / its future is dropped at the next await). Errors: fire-and-forget `spawn` has nowhere to send a panic/error, so the executor must route uncaught task failures to a container-level handler (the AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler analogue) rather than swallowing them; `submit` surfaces errors through the join handle. Context propagation: decorators capture ambient context on the CALLER's task at submit time and restore it inside the spawned task before run, release after — across an `.await`/task hop, mirroring ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator; OFF by default (Spring's opt-in, to avoid stale-context data-bleed). Lifecycle: the executor is a Lifecycle bean — on context-close it stops accepting new work, then gracefully drains in-flight tasks up to a timeout, then aborts the remainder; no async Drop, so this is an explicit container-driven `shutdown().await` (ties to runtime-lifecycle, context-close). No global lock; the only shared state is a Semaphore (bounded impl) and the runtime's own scheduler.

#### R2 — Capability-typed execution facilities: distinct `Spawner` / `BlockingOffload` / `ConcurrencyGate` traits behind one named bean role
Question whether Rust wants ONE Executor trait at all. Spring's TaskExecutor conflates three distinct capabilities that the JVM lumps because everything is a thread: (a) run async work, (b) offload blocking work, (c) cap concurrency. In async-first Rust these are genuinely different execution contexts with different safety contracts, so split them into small composable traits — `Spawner` (spawn/submit futures), `BlockingOffload` (run blocking closures off the async workers), and `ConcurrencyGate` (acquire/release a permit) — and let an 'execution facility' be a named bundle of whichever capabilities a backing provides. The injectable thing is still a named, role-marked bean ('applicationExecution'), but instead of one fat dyn trait, consumers depend on exactly the capability they need: scheduling/events depend on `Arc<dyn Spawner>`; a DAO that wraps a blocking driver depends on `Arc<dyn BlockingOffload>`; @ConcurrencyLimit-style protection depends on `Arc<dyn ConcurrencyGate>`. A concrete `ExecutionFacility` struct implements all three over one runtime and is registered under a name; selection still works by primary/qualifier on whichever capability trait is the injection point's type. Decorators become capability-scoped (a `SpawnDecorator` wraps the Spawner; the concurrency gate is itself just a decorator that wraps a Spawner with a permit). The 'one-switch swap' is preserved because each capability is dyn-injected; the difference from R1 is that a call site advertises its EXACT execution need in its type, so 'this code may block' vs 'this code spawns async' is visible in the dependency signature, not hidden behind a do-everything Executor. This leans hardest into charter §2.3 (explicit execution context) and §2.11 (unify by composing small primitives, not bespoke fat interfaces).

**Pros:** Maximally honest about execution context (charter §2.3): a bean that needs to offload blocking work depends on `Arc<dyn BlockingOffload>` — the hazard is in the type signature, not buried in a comment; the dyn-blocking-on-an-async-worker footgun becomes hard to commit.; Each capability trait is tiny and independently swappable/mockable: a test can inject a synchronous-inline Spawner without faking submit/blocking/limit all at once.; ConcurrencyGate as a first-class injectable primitive directly serves the @ConcurrencyLimit / virtual-thread-needs-a-ceiling intent and is reusable by retry-resilience (concurrency limiting) — unification across neighbours rather than duplicating a limiter.; Composes cleanly: a decorated, bounded, multi-thread facility is `ConcurrencyGate(Decorator(Spawner))` — composition of small traits rather than a combinatorial set of fat-executor configs.; Avoids over-erasing: a call site that only ever spawns Send futures never pays for the blocking/limit surface.

**Cons:** Diverges from Spring's single-TaskExecutor landmark — practitioners porting mental models must learn that 'the executor' is now three capabilities; the by-name 'applicationTaskExecutor' singular identity is partly lost (teaching cost; intent-parity says acceptable but it's friction).; More API surface: three traits + a bundling struct + capability-scoped decorators is more concepts than one trait; risk of over-fragmentation if most call sites just want 'spawn this.'; Selection/qualifier semantics get subtler: 'force-mode central override' must rebind potentially three injection-point types, and 'which facility is primary' is now per-capability, so a misconfiguration could make Spawner resolve to one bean and BlockingOffload to another unexpectedly.; Still hits the same dyn-boxed-future wall on the Spawner surface (no escape from AFIT-non-dyn); the split doesn't reduce per-task alloc, only clarifies intent.; Three traits multiply the cross-crate registration/self-check burden (must verify each capability is provided), and the WASM/alternate-origin provider must implement the full bundle or carefully declare partial capability.

**Mechanisms considered:** capability split into `Spawner` / `BlockingOffload` / `ConcurrencyGate` object-safe traits, injected independently; one `ExecutionFacility` bean implementing all three, registered under a role/name, selected per-capability by primary/qualifier; ConcurrencyGate as a Semaphore-backed decorator reused by retry-resilience (shared concurrency-limit primitive); capability-scoped decorators (SpawnDecorator) composed by priority at runtime; boxed futures/closures at each dyn capability boundary (AFIT-non-dyn); runtime substrate provided by integration crate; capability traits + bundling struct named in core

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same substrate-neutral registration story as R1 — capability impls are beans registered via whatever descriptor mechanism Phase 2 picks. The added cross-crate nuance: because capabilities are separate types, an integration crate could contribute ONLY a BlockingOffload (e.g. a dedicated blocking pool for a JDBC-style driver) without providing a Spawner, and the container must compose the partial contributions — this is a pro for pluggability (matches charter §2.6 'pluggable everything') but raises the self-check bar: the facade must assert that EACH required capability is satisfied at refresh, not merely that 'an executor exists,' or a binary could silently lack blocking-offload and deadlock the runtime under load (a worse-than-R1 silent-DCE variant). Decorators registered per-capability still order at runtime by priority. The capability traits make the WASM/alternate-origin path explicit: a module-backed provider declares exactly which capabilities it offers. Force-link/self-check obligations are otherwise identical to R1, multiplied by capability count.

**T2 — execution context:** Sharpest T2 of the three by design — execution context is encoded in the dependency TYPE. `Spawner::spawn` runs Send 'static futures on async workers; `BlockingOffload::run` is the ONLY sanctioned blocking seam and routes to a dedicated blocking pool — a bean that holds neither cannot block or spawn, making 'pure async, never blocks' a type-checkable property. `ConcurrencyGate::acquire().await` yields a permit guard whose Drop releases (RAII), giving cooperative back-pressure without a global lock; setting the limit to 1 is the declarative-instance-lock intent (Spring's @ConcurrencyLimit(1)). Cancellation: spawn handles carry cancel tokens; gate permits release on guard-drop even if the gated task is cancelled (RAII guarantees no permit leak on cancellation — a Rust-native win over JVM throttle counters). Errors: fire-and-forget failures route to the container uncaught-failure handler per capability; submit surfaces via handle. Context propagation is a SpawnDecorator capturing/restoring ambient context across the task hop, opt-in. Lifecycle/shutdown: the bundling ExecutionFacility is the Lifecycle bean and drives graceful drain (stop accepting, drain in-flight to timeout, abort remainder) via explicit container-driven `shutdown().await` (no async Drop); each capability participates in the same drain. Construction is cold-path async and runtime-dependent, same ordering constraint as R1 (must follow runtime availability).

#### R3 — Execution policies as data: a thin runtime-owned dispatcher + named `ExecutionProfile` descriptors (config-as-the-IoC-lever)
Push the 'chosen by configuration, not code' intent to its logical end and the unification principle (charter §2.11) hardest: there is essentially ONE concrete runtime-owned dispatcher, and 'which executor backs this call site' is expressed as DATA — a named `ExecutionProfile` descriptor (kind: Pool{core,queue} | CurrentThread | ThreadPerTask{limit} | Inherit, plus an ordered decorator list, plus a drain-timeout) resolved at runtime. Call sites don't inject a concrete executor impl; they inject a lightweight handle `ExecutorHandle` (a cheap clonable token naming a profile) and call `handle.spawn(fut)`/`handle.spawn_blocking(f)`. The handle routes through the single dispatcher, which consults the named profile (default 'application') to decide HOW to run — pool, current-thread, bounded, decorated. This makes Spring's force-mode and one-property-swap NATIVE: changing the profile's `kind` in config (pool -> thread-per-task / virtual-thread-analog) rebinds behavior with zero code AND without even re-resolving DI graph edges, because the handle is stable and only the profile data changed. It dogfoods the config/environment + binder neighbours: an ExecutionProfile is a config-properties-bound POJO rooted at e.g. `leaf.execution.<name>`, validated, documented (config-metadata) — so executors are tuned exactly like every other piece of leaf config, with relaxed-binding and origin-tracking for free. The 'TaskExecutor abstraction' survives as the `ExecutorHandle` seam (still swappable, still injectable, still mockable by pointing a profile at a synchronous test dispatcher); the concrete substrate collapses into one tunable dispatcher + a table of profiles. Decorators and concurrency limits are profile fields, not separate wrapper beans. A custom dispatcher (WASM-backed, or a wholly alternate runtime) can still be registered as the dispatcher impl behind the handle — origin-agnosticism preserved at the dispatcher seam rather than the executor-bean seam.

**Pros:** Most literal realization of 'tuned by configuration, not code' and Spring's force-mode central lever: executor behavior is data bound from the Environment, so central tuning/monitoring is uniform with all other leaf config (config-properties/binder/config-metadata reuse — strong unification).; One-property substrate swap is the PUREST here — flip a profile's kind in a properties file; no DI re-wiring, no new bean, no code; the virtual-thread-as-deployment-decision intent is exactly modeled.; Minimal type surface for call sites: inject one cheap `ExecutorHandle` token (no fat dyn trait per concern), low overhead on the inject path (charter §2.5) — the handle is a small Copy-ish token, dispatch indirection is one lookup.; Profiles are inspectable/diagnosable data: 'list every execution profile, its kind, decorators, limits, and which call sites name it' is a trivial report (charter §2.9 diagnostics; ties startup-instrumentation), far easier than introspecting a graph of wrapper beans.; Self-documenting config surface (config-metadata) makes accepted executor tuning a discoverable API — Spring's `spring.task.execution.*` ergonomics, rust-native.

**Cons:** Heaviest indirection at the dispatch seam and most machinery to get right up front — risks Phase-1 over-engineering / smelling like a meta-architecture decision (must stay a CANDIDATE; the 'profiles-as-data' substrate pre-judges config coupling Phase 2 should weigh).; 'Behavior chosen by a string-named profile resolved at runtime' trades compile-time legibility for config flexibility — a typo'd / missing profile name is a runtime-or-startup error, not a compile error (charter §2.9 prefers earliest legible diagnostic; mitigated only by a startup validation pass over the profile table).; A single dispatcher consulting profile data per spawn adds a lookup + branch on every submission vs R1/R2's direct impl call; on hot fan-out paths this indirection (plus the profile decorator chain) may cost more than a direct typed spawn.; Less obviously 'an injectable abstraction' to a Spring user — the executor is no longer a bean you can `@Primary`/qualify in the usual way; selection moved from DI resolution into a config table, which is a real divergence from the IoC-bean framing the feature is named for.; Custom/alternate execution backends are now 'a custom dispatcher impl' (a deeper seam) rather than 'just another executor bean' — more invasive for the WASM/alternate-origin provider than R1/R2.

**Mechanisms considered:** named `ExecutionProfile` descriptors bound from the Environment via config-properties/binder (data, not impls); one runtime-owned dispatcher + cheap clonable `ExecutorHandle` tokens injected at call sites; profile fields carry decorator list + concurrency limit + drain-timeout (no separate wrapper beans); runtime topo/priority resolution of decorators within a profile (composition-doc ordering-at-runtime); custom dispatcher impl as the origin-agnostic seam (WASM/alternate runtime swaps the dispatcher, not the executor bean); startup validation pass over the profile table for fail-fast on missing/misnamed profiles (charter §2.9)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The dispatcher and the ExecutionProfile schema live in core; profile VALUES come from the Environment (config files / env vars) resolved at runtime, so adding/removing/retuning a profile needs NO recompile and NO cross-crate registration at all — this side-steps the link-time DCE hazard for tuning entirely (a genuine advantage: config is a real runtime classpath for execution policy). What still crosses crates: the concrete dispatcher impl (runtime spawn/spawn_blocking) ships in leaf-tokio and must be force-linked + self-checked exactly as R1/R2 (a missing dispatcher is fatal). Decorators that are CODE (context-propagation, tracing) still register as beans/descriptors via the chosen substrate and are referenced BY NAME from profile data — so a profile naming a decorator that wasn't linked is a startup-validation failure (loud, not silent). Custom dispatchers from integration crates register at the dispatcher seam. Net: less link-time fragility for the tuning surface, same fragility (with the same self-check obligation) for the one mandatory dispatcher and for code-decorators.

**T2 — execution context:** The dispatcher owns ALL execution-context decisions, centralizing 'what runs where' in one auditable place (charter §2.3). `handle.spawn(fut)` consults the named profile and routes to the runtime accordingly (pool workers for Pool/ThreadPerTask kinds, the LocalSet for CurrentThread); `handle.spawn_blocking(f)` always routes to the dedicated blocking pool regardless of profile (blocking offload is never optional). The profile's concurrency limit is enforced by a per-profile Semaphore inside the dispatcher (RAII permit release on task completion/cancellation — no leak). Cancellation tokens flow through the handle's returned join handle; profile-level drain-timeout governs shutdown. Errors from fire-and-forget go to the container uncaught-failure handler; the dispatcher is the natural single chokepoint to also emit metrics/observation per profile (the central-monitoring intent). Context propagation: a profile lists context decorators applied at submit time on the caller's task, restored in the spawned task, opt-in per profile (so 'application' can be undecorated while a 'request-bound' profile propagates request context — finer than Spring's single global toggle). Lifecycle/shutdown: the dispatcher is the single Lifecycle bean; on context-close it drains every profile's in-flight work to each profile's drain-timeout then aborts, via explicit container-driven `shutdown().await` (no async Drop). Construction is cold-path async and runtime-dependent; profile binding happens during the Environment-ready phase before consumers spawn. No global lock; per-profile Semaphores are the only added shared state.

### Rust clashes
- No raw threads to abstract over — Rust has an async runtime. Spring's TaskExecutor IoC's over `Thread`/`ExecutorService`; the rust-native equivalent must IoC over an async runtime's spawn + a spawn_blocking offload + a concurrency ceiling. The whole 'run a Runnable somehow' shape becomes 'run a Future / offload a blocking closure somehow,' which is a reframing, not a port. This also means an executor bean can only be constructed AFTER a runtime exists, forcing it to be among the earliest infrastructure beans (a bootstrap-ordering constraint with no Spring analogue).
- Runtime-agnosticism vs a named substrate: charter §2.6 wants tokio/smol pluggable and the core to name no runtime, but a TaskExecutor is intrinsically runtime-coupled (spawn semantics differ across runtimes). The trait must live in core naming no runtime while the impl lives in an integration crate — and that integration crate is MANDATORY (an async-first framework with no runtime cannot run), which collides with the Layer-0 DCE hazard: a declared-but-unreferenced runtime crate silently yields a binary with NO execution facility. A non-negotiable self-check + likely an explicit force-link of the default runtime is required.
- async fn in traits is not dyn-compatible (nightly doc, load-bearing): the executor MUST be a trait object (so the substrate is swappable and a WASM/test provider can stand in) AND its core operation is async — these can't both be zero-cost. Every realization eats a boxed future (and boxed closure) per submission at the dyn boundary. Hot per-request fan-out pays an alloc per task; the zero-cost monomorphized spawn is only available off the dyn surface.
- No thread interrupt / cancellation is cooperative: Spring can interrupt a pooled thread; Rust cancels by dropping a future or signaling a token, and the task only stops at its next await point. The executor abstraction must make cancellation semantics on handle-drop EXPLICIT (abort vs detach) — a silent-task-leak footgun with no JVM analogue. Graceful drain on shutdown must rely on cooperative cancellation + timeouts.
- No async Drop on stable (nightly doc: avoid; tokio-shutdown crash): Spring's executor participates in lifecycle and is gracefully shut down by the container. Rust cannot ride Drop for async drain, so the executor's 'participate in lifecycle / graceful shutdown' intent becomes a hard, explicit container-driven `shutdown().await` (ties to runtime-lifecycle, context-close) — the container owning teardown is a non-syntactic discipline, not automatic.
- Send/'static bounds fracture the 'run anything' contract: Spring runnables carry no such bound. A multi-thread (work-stealing) executor requires `Send + 'static` futures; a current-thread executor allows `?Send`. So the SAME injected abstraction imposes DIFFERENT bounds depending on which impl is primary — the 'swap substrate with zero code change' intent can break at compile time if code relied on a non-Send future and the primary flips to multi-thread (RTN / trait_variant Send-variant tensions from the nightly doc surface exactly here). This is a genuine clash with Spring's 'virtual threads don't change the contract.'
- Context propagation across a task hop has no ThreadLocal: Spring's ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator captures ThreadLocals and restores on the borrowed thread. Rust async tasks have no inherent thread-locals that survive an .await/task move (a task can migrate threads mid-execution under work-stealing). The decorator analogue must capture from a rust-native ambient-context mechanism (the context-propagation / locale-context neighbours' SPI, itself Phase-2-deferred) and restore into the spawned task's scope — and ThreadLocal-based holders are actively WRONG here, so this feature can only DECLARE the decorator seam, not implement propagation (that depends on the unresolved ambient-context model).
- Unbounded concurrency is the default hazard, inverted from Spring: a virtual-thread / thread-per-task substrate has no implicit ceiling, and async `spawn` will happily queue unbounded tasks. Where Spring's pools gave an accidental ceiling, leaf's async-native default gives none — making the ConcurrencyGate / bounded-permit primitive not a nice-to-have but a needed default-safety lever (charter abuse-resistance, §2.3), reused by retry-resilience.

### Interactions
- **async-execution** — Primary consumer. The #[async]-method AOP interceptor hands the wrapped invocation to THIS feature's injected executor (Spring's AsyncExecutionInterceptor -> AsyncTaskExecutor). Executor-selection-by-qualifier (#[async("name")]), the void/Future return-type discipline, and the AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler routing all bind to the abstraction defined here; async-execution owns the proxy/interception, this feature owns the run-somewhere seam it targets.
- **scheduling** — Sibling consumer. @Scheduled (cron/fixedRate/fixedDelay) runs its periodic work via a TaskScheduler that is the time-aware cousin of this TaskExecutor — same swap-by-config / virtual-thread / lifecycle-drain intent. Scheduling's 'default is single-threaded, a long task serializes the others' caveat (Spring line 2045) is a direct consequence of which executor profile backs it; this feature provides the spawn substrate, scheduling adds the timing/trigger layer on top.
- **event-multicaster** — Consumer for async dispatch. The multicaster's opt-in async event delivery routes listener invocations through an injected executor here (Spring's SimpleApplicationEventMulticaster.setTaskExecutor, line 1850/1868). The async caveats (no return-value chaining, exceptions to a handler not the caller, context-propagation needs a decorator) are inherited from this feature's submission semantics + decorator chain.
- **background-bootstrap** — Consumer at startup. @Bean(bootstrap = BACKGROUND) slow-independent-singleton init runs on a bootstrapExecutor that Spring delegates to applicationTaskExecutor (lines 2010-2012, 2477). This feature provides that executor; background-bootstrap collects the in-flight construction futures and joins them before refresh completes — so this feature must be constructable BEFORE the eager-singleton phase that background-bootstrap parallelizes.
- **retry-resilience** — Shares the concurrency-ceiling primitive. @ConcurrencyLimit (Spring lines 2077-2081) caps concurrent invocations via a SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor limit / throttle — the same ConcurrencyGate / bounded-permit primitive R2 makes first-class and R1/R3 carry as a wrapper/profile field. Limit-of-1 as a declarative instance lock is shared intent. Retry's async retry/backoff also needs this executor to schedule attempts (cold-path polling, charter §2.4).
- **context-propagation** — Provides the decorator the executor composes. The TaskDecorator chain here is where context-propagation wires its capture/restore across the task hop (Spring's ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator on the executor). This feature defines the decorator SPI + chain composition (CompositeTaskDecorator analogue); context-propagation defines WHAT is captured/restored. Opt-in by default (data-bleed avoidance) is shared intent. Hard dependency: the rust-native ambient-context model context-propagation defines is Phase-2-deferred, so this feature only exposes the seam.
- **locale-context** — Indirect, via context-propagation. 'The current locale/request/tenant' ambient values are exactly what the executor's decorator must carry across a spawn — and ThreadLocal-style holders are wrong across async task hops, so this feature's decorator seam is the boundary where locale-context values must be explicitly captured/restored.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — The executor IS a Lifecycle bean. Its graceful start/stop, phase ordering, and async-stop-with-drain-timeout are exactly the running-state-machine runtime-lifecycle defines (Spring's smart/graceful shutdown). The executor must stop accepting work on stop() and drain in-flight tasks; immediate-destroy-without-stop tolerance (Spring lines 2083-2085) applies.
- **context-close** — Symmetric teardown. Because no async Drop, context-close must drive this feature's explicit shutdown().await — stop the executor (quiesce runtime activity) before destroying singletons, so in-flight tasks don't reference half-destroyed beans. The executor is typically among the LAST infrastructure torn down (after the beans whose work it runs).
- **container-core** — The executor is registered/selected through the engine + façade defined by container-core: role-marked as Infrastructure (auto-detected), resolved by primary/qualifier via autowiring-resolution, and its mandatory presence enforced by the façade's expected-vs-found self-check (the silent-DCE defense container-core's openNotes mandates). The runtime-availability ordering constraint is a refresh-template concern.
- **concurrency-contract** — Complements the safe-publication contract. concurrency-contract guarantees fully-built singletons are safely published; this feature governs what runs concurrently ON those singletons. Spring's line: container assembles, you choose what runs concurrently via the TaskExecutor abstraction (line 69). The Send/'static bounds this feature imposes are the rust-native teeth of that division of labor.
- **config-properties** — Tuning surface (strongest in R3). Executor pool size / queue / concurrency-limit / drain-timeout / virtual-thread-switch are bound from the Environment via config-properties + binder, documented via config-metadata, with relaxed-binding — the rust-native realization of Spring's spring.task.execution.* and spring.threads.virtual.enabled one-property switches.
- **environment** — Source of the swap-by-config lever. The active profile + property stack decides which executor impl/profile is primary (the virtual-thread-as-deployment-decision intent). Conditions over the Environment (an OnProperty/OnThreading analogue) gate which executor wiring activates — mirroring Spring's @ConditionalOnThreading(VIRTUAL).
- **conditions** — Gates executor wiring. The default executor backs off if a user executor bean exists (@ConditionalOnMissingBean analogue), and the virtual-thread vs pooled choice is a condition over a property+capability (Spring's @ConditionalOnThreading checks both the flag and runtime capability). Auto-config of the default executor is opinionated-but-overridable via conditions.
- **startup-instrumentation** — Diagnosability hook. Executor construction (pool warm-up, worker spawn) and the background-bootstrap join are natural named startup steps; R3's profile table is a trivially inspectable diagnostic surface (which profiles exist, their kind/decorators/limits, which call sites name them).

### Open questions for Phase 2
- The async execution & context-propagation model (charter §3, explicitly deferred): what is the rust-native answer to ThreadLocal holders across an .await / task hop? This feature can only DECLARE the TaskDecorator capture/restore seam; the actual ambient-context mechanism (ScopedValue-analog? structured-concurrency scope? an explicit Context value threaded through spawn?) is the keystone that decides whether context-propagation, locale-context, and this feature's decorator chain are even implementable as designed. Unresolved.
- Is the executor ONE fat dyn trait (R1), a set of capability traits (R2), or a config-driven dispatcher + handle (R3)? This is a shape call that touches every consumer (async-execution, scheduling, events, background-bootstrap, retry) — many neighbours assume 'inject the executor bean,' which R3 partially breaks by moving selection from DI into a config table. Needs the whole Phase-1 catalog to judge.
- Send/'static bounds vs swap-by-config: how does leaf preserve Spring's 'swapping the substrate changes no code' guarantee when a multi-thread executor requires Send+'static futures and a current-thread one doesn't? Does leaf mandate all spawned work be Send+'static (losing current-thread flexibility), expose two executor surfaces, or accept that some substrate swaps are compile-breaking? Ties to the RTN / trait_variant Send-variant question from the nightly doc. Cross-cuts async-execution and the async-execution-model decision.
- Cancellation semantics on handle-drop and at shutdown: abort vs detach as the default? Cooperative-only cancellation means a non-cooperative task can ignore shutdown drain. What is the framework-wide contract for cancel tokens, drain timeouts, and what happens to tasks that outlive the drain window? Cross-cuts runtime-lifecycle, context-close, retry-resilience, scheduling — needs one unified answer.
- Where does uncaught task failure go? Fire-and-forget spawn has nowhere to return a panic/error. Is there a single container-level async-uncaught-failure handler (the AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler analogue), and how does it compose with the unified error/diagnostics model (charter §3, deferred §2.9)? Cross-cuts async-execution, events, the error model.
- Mandatory runtime crate + Layer-0 DCE: an async-first framework cannot run without a runtime-backed executor, yet the runtime impl lives in an optional integration crate vulnerable to silent non-linking. What is the cross-crate substrate (linkme / inventory / explicit force-link / build-time codegen) AND the force-link + self-check policy that GUARANTEES a binary never silently lacks its execution facility? This binds to the deferred cross-crate composition model (§3) and is more safety-critical here than for ordinary beans.
- Is the TaskScheduler (scheduling) a separate abstraction or a time-aware capability of this same execution facility? Spring keeps TaskExecutor and TaskScheduler distinct but parallel; unification (charter §2.11) might fold scheduling's trigger layer onto this feature's spawn substrate. Cross-cuts scheduling and retry-resilience (backoff timers).
- Does the concurrency-ceiling primitive (ConcurrencyGate / bounded-permit) belong to THIS feature, to retry-resilience (@ConcurrencyLimit), or to a shared lower primitive both consume? It is needed in both places and as a default-safety lever against unbounded async fan-out — a unification candidate that shouldn't be solved twice.
- How much executor configuration is compile-time-legible vs runtime config (charter §2.9 richest-diagnostic-timing)? R3's profiles-as-data maximize tuning flexibility but make a missing/misnamed profile a runtime-or-startup error; a compile/codegen path could catch it earlier. The compile-vs-runtime split (charter §3) decides where executor selection errors surface.
- Origin-agnostic provider depth: should the swap seam be at the executor-bean level (R1/R2 — a WASM/alternate-runtime executor is 'just another bean') or at a deeper dispatcher level (R3)? This interacts with the registry/scope substrate (charter §3) and with whether an alternate-origin provider can satisfy the full execution contract (spawn + spawn_blocking + gate) or only part of it.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent (the Spring TaskExecutor section is short but explicit at lines 2016-2023, and I read the full surrounding cluster — virtual threads 2049-2058, auto-config naming/force-mode 2062-2071, @ConcurrencyLimit 2077-2081, and the entire context-propagation/decorator section 2089-2164 — because this feature's whole point is realized through those neighbours). The single most important INTENT to preserve is 'the same call site, backed by a different substrate chosen by configuration not code' — in Rust that is realized by injecting an abstraction (dyn trait / capability set / handle) so call sites never name a concrete runtime or pool; I treated that as the fixed constraint across all three realizations.

The three realizations are genuinely divergent on the axis that matters HERE — WHAT the injectable execution abstraction IS: (R1) one fat object-safe TaskExecutor trait, the literal Spring port; (R2) a capability split (Spawner/BlockingOffload/ConcurrencyGate) that makes execution context type-visible, leaning hardest into charter §2.3; (R3) execution-policy-as-data (named ExecutionProfiles bound from config + a thin dispatcher + a cheap handle), leaning hardest into 'tuned by configuration' and reusing config-properties/binder. They span the spectrum from most-Spring-faithful (R1) to most-rust-native-and-unifying (R2/R3). All three are substrate-neutral on the cross-crate registration mechanism and on the scope/ownership model, which I refused to settle.

Three things to carry forward: (1) This feature is NOT self-contained — it is the SHARED execution seam that async-execution, scheduling, event-multicaster, background-bootstrap, and retry-resilience all target; its design must be judged against those consumers, not in isolation. (2) The deepest Rust clash is that there are no threads to IoC over, only an async runtime — so 'TaskExecutor' becomes 'spawn a future / offload blocking / cap concurrency,' and the Send/'static-bounds-vs-swap-by-config tension (a current-thread substrate allows ?Send, a multi-thread one does not) genuinely threatens Spring's 'swapping substrate changes no code' guarantee. (3) Two hard constraints I held fixed across all three: the mandatory runtime crate is a worse-than-ordinary Layer-0 DCE hazard (a binary silently missing its executor is catastrophic, so a loud self-check + force-link is non-negotiable), and lifecycle participation must be an explicit container-driven shutdown().await (no async Drop on stable). The context-propagation decorator seam is DECLARED but cannot be implemented until Phase 2 settles the ambient-context-across-.await model — I surfaced that as a phase2Question rather than inventing a ThreadLocal-shaped answer (which would be actively wrong across task hops).

I did not write any files. Grounding paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (TaskExecutor 2016-2023; virtual threads 2049-2058; auto-config/force-mode 2062-2071; @ConcurrencyLimit 2077-2081; context-propagation + ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator + CompositeTaskDecorator 2089-2164; assembler-not-runtime-concurrency-manager 69, 1920); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2.3/§2.4/§2.5/§2.6/§2.11, deferred §3); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (Layer-0 DCE, mandatory-crate force-link/self-check, runtime ordering, Architectures A-D); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (AFIT/RPITIT not dyn-safe -> boxed futures at dyn boundary, trait_variant Send-variant, RTN watch, no async Drop -> explicit shutdown, trait upcasting 1.86); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/design/explorations/wasm-modules-for-leaf.md (origin-agnostic provider seam, WASI 0.3 async, directory-scanned dynamic 'classpath' duality). Neighbour Phase-1 designs (async-execution, scheduling, context-propagation, runtime-lifecycle, retry-resilience, background-bootstrap) do not yet exist in docs/design — referenced by catalog intent only.

## Scheduling (@EnableScheduling / @Scheduled)  `[scheduling]`

Spring's scheduling (doc lines 2039-2058, 1054, 2045): `@EnableScheduling` activates a `ScheduledAnnotationBeanPostProcessor` that scans managed beans for `@Scheduled` methods (cron / fixedRate / fixedDelay, with optional initialDelay) and registers each as a recurring task against an injected `TaskScheduler` abstraction. The INTENT I must preserve as intent (not mechanism): (1) DECLARATIVE periodic work — annotate a method, the container turns it into a recurring trigger; no hand-rolled timer loop in business code. (2) Three trigger KINDS with distinct semantics: cron (wall-clock calendar expression, next-fire computed from a schedule), fixedRate (fire every N regardless of how long the body takes — fires can overlap/queue), fixedDelay (wait N AFTER the previous completion — self-pacing, never overlaps). (3) The scheduler is a TaskScheduler ABSTRACTION (the time-aware cousin of task-execution's TaskExecutor): code declares 'run this periodically,' the container decides the substrate — so the same call site is backed by a pooled, single-threaded, or virtual-thread scheduler chosen by configuration. (4) THE load-bearing production gotcha (line 2045): without an explicit scheduler the default is SINGLE-THREADED (pool size 1), so one long-running scheduled task serializes and delays ALL the others — leaf must make this either a non-trap default or a loud diagnostic. (5) Virtual-thread one-switch (lines 2049-2058): `spring.threads.virtual.enabled` flips the scheduler to fire a fresh virtual thread per execution (one scheduler thread for triggers, work runs concurrently) — adoption is a deployment decision, not a code rewrite, and the thread-safety/context contract is UNCHANGED. (6) `@EnableScheduling` is sugar over @Import + ImportAware metadata (line 1054) — there is no special 'enable' machinery; it reads attributes off the importer and configures itself; customization via a `SchedulingConfigurer` callback (register tasks programmatically, supply the scheduler). (7) Scheduling can be enabled WITHOUT async — it is its own facility. The rust-native reframing: leaf has no threads and an async runtime; '@Scheduled' becomes a registered recurring async task whose TRIGGER (when to fire next) is computed by a cron/interval calculator and whose firing SPAWNS the body onto an injected execution facility (task-execution). Per charter §2.4 the trigger MUST be timer/waker-driven (sleep-until-next-fire), NEVER an interval busy-poll — scheduling is the canonical 'explicit scheduled task' cold-path that §2.4 sanctions for await-on-a-timer, but the timer itself must be reactive (a runtime timer wheel firing a waker), not a spin loop. I deliberately do NOT decide: whether TaskScheduler is a distinct abstraction or a time-aware capability of task-execution's facility (cross-cuts that sibling), the cron-engine implementation, the registration substrate, or the async-context-propagation model — only the declarative-scheduling intent, its trigger semantics, lifecycle, and how it targets the execution seam.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Literal TaskScheduler dyn-trait bean + a ScheduledTask post-processor that registers trigger descriptors (the direct Spring port)
Mirror Spring's three pieces 1:1. (a) An object-safe `TaskScheduler` trait is THE injectable time-aware abstraction (the time cousin of task-execution's TaskExecutor): `schedule(&self, task: ScheduledTask) -> ScheduleHandle` where `ScheduledTask { kind: Trigger, body: Arc<dyn ScheduledBody> }`, `enum Trigger { Cron(CronExpr), FixedRate(Duration), FixedDelay(Duration), OneShot{at} }`, and an optional `initial_delay`. The trait is dyn so the substrate swaps; concrete beans are `PooledScheduler` (a worker set draining a delay-queue — the ThreadPoolTaskScheduler analogue), `VirtualThreadScheduler` (one trigger driver, spawns a fresh task per fire — the SimpleAsyncTaskScheduler analogue), and `DecoratedScheduler` (wraps another + the same TaskDecorator chain task-execution defines, for context propagation). The scheduler does NOT busy-poll: it holds a min-heap/timer-wheel of next-fire instants and `tokio::time::sleep_until`s on the earliest, waking exactly when due (charter §2.4 reactive). (b) `@Scheduled` is realized as a thin attribute macro on an async method that emits a `ScheduledMethodDescriptor { bean_type, method, trigger_spec, qualifier }` — the macro emits ONLY hand-writable data (charter §2.10). (c) A `ScheduledTask` instance-tier post-processor (riding post-processor-spi's after_init hook) inspects each managed bean's descriptors, binds each `@Scheduled` method to a closure capturing the bean's Arc, and calls `scheduler.schedule(...)` — exactly the ScheduledAnnotationBeanPostProcessor analogue. (d) `@EnableScheduling` is import-composition sugar that registers the post-processor + a default scheduler bean (backed off via conditions if the user supplies one); a `SchedulingConfigurer`-analogue bean (`fn configure(&self, registrar: &mut ScheduledTaskRegistrar)`) lets users register tasks/triggers/scheduler programmatically — the escape hatch. Selection is pure DI (primary/qualifier); the virtual-thread switch is a config property choosing which scheduler impl is primary, zero call-site change. Firing routes the body to task-execution's injected executor (the scheduler owns WHEN, the executor owns WHERE), so the single-thread gotcha is structurally avoided: a long body runs on the executor's workers, never blocking the trigger driver.

**Pros:** Tightest intent parity: TaskScheduler-as-bean + a scanning post-processor + @Enable-as-import maps the three Spring pieces 1:1 — maximally teachable, and the SchedulingConfigurer escape hatch ports directly.; Reuses two upstream designs cleanly: the after_init instance-tier hook (post-processor-spi) for discovery and the run-somewhere executor seam (task-execution) for firing — scheduling adds only the time/trigger layer, satisfying the unification invariant (§2.11) by NOT reinventing execution.; Structurally defeats the single-thread gotcha (line 2045): the trigger driver only computes next-fire + spawns; the body runs on the executor, so a slow task can never serialize the others by default — turning Spring's frequent production surprise into a non-trap (with an opt-in serialize-per-task lever for the cases that want it).; Swap-by-config falls out: call sites never name a scheduler; flipping which impl is primary (pooled vs virtual-thread) needs zero code (the virtual-thread one-switch intent), and the trigger semantics are identical across substrates.; Reactive trigger (sleep_until on a min-heap) honors §2.4 — the canonical 'explicit scheduled task' cold path, await-on-a-timer not interval-poll; the runtime's timer wheel does the waking.

**Cons:** Two abstractions to keep coherent — TaskScheduler here and TaskExecutor in task-execution — risks duplicating selection/lifecycle/decorator machinery; the phase2 tension 'is the scheduler a separate abstraction or a time-aware executor capability' is live and this realization commits to separate.; The dyn TaskScheduler boundary eats a boxed future + boxed body per fire (AFIT-not-dyn-safe), and the ScheduledBody is an Arc<dyn ...> closure — acceptable on a cold periodic path but a cost if fire frequency is high.; Cancellation/overlap policy must be designed into the handle and the Trigger: fixedRate can overlap (does leaf queue, skip, or run-concurrently?); fixedDelay must observe completion to schedule the next fire — getting the completion-feedback loop wrong silently drifts the schedule (see T2).; Cron-expression semantics (timezone, DST, the 6-vs-7-field dialect, 'fire missed runs?') are a large surface the Trigger enum hides behind CronExpr; the calendar engine is real work and a divergence source from Spring's exact dialect.; Registration timing: the post-processor can only schedule a task once the bean exists and is fully initialized — so tasks must not start firing until the whole context is up (a smart-initializing-style barrier), or an early fire hits a half-built graph.

**Mechanisms considered:** object-safe dyn TaskScheduler bean selected by primary/qualifier via autowiring-resolution; virtual-thread variant chosen by an OnProperty/OnThreading condition; instance-tier post-processor (post-processor-spi after_init) scans @Scheduled descriptors and registers ScheduledTasks — the ScheduledAnnotationBeanPostProcessor analogue; thin attribute macro emits a ScheduledMethodDescriptor (data only); the post-processor binds it to the live bean Arc and a trigger; reactive trigger driver: a min-heap / timer-wheel of next-fire instants + tokio::time::sleep_until (no interval busy-poll), firing a waker when due; firing SPAWNS the body onto task-execution's injected executor (scheduler owns WHEN, executor owns WHERE) — defeats the single-thread serialization gotcha; @EnableScheduling as import-composition sugar registering the post-processor + a default scheduler (condition-backed-off); a SchedulingConfigurer-analogue registrar as the programmatic escape hatch; cron calendar engine computing next-fire from a CronExpr (timezone/DST aware) as the cron Trigger kind

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The TaskScheduler trait, Trigger enum, ScheduledMethodDescriptor schema, and the registrar SPI live in leaf-framework (kernel); the concrete scheduler impls' spawn/timer primitives live in the runtime integration crate (leaf-tokio, providing sleep_until + the timer wheel), exactly like task-execution's executor — so the same Layer-0 force-link + self-check discipline applies: a declared-but-unreferenced runtime crate yields NO scheduler and @Scheduled silently does nothing (the worst silent-DCE symptom). Each @Scheduled descriptor is collected via the SAME catalog substrate beans use (linkme distributed-slice default / inventory / build-time codegen / explicit — Phase-2 decides), with the trigger spec as DATA on the const descriptor (cron string, durations, qualifier) so generics hard-error and ordering/conditions resolve at runtime, never link order. The façade self-check should assert that if any @Scheduled descriptor was discovered, a scheduler bean is present (expected-vs-found) — failing loud rather than silently never firing. Crucially this inherits a SCHEDULING-SPECIFIC silent-DCE hazard worse than ordinary beans: a dropped descriptor isn't a missing bean you'll notice at injection, it's a task that simply never fires — invisible until production — so the self-check must enumerate discovered-vs-expected scheduled tasks. The scheduler is origin-agnostic at the bean seam: a WASM-module-provided scheduler (or a @Scheduled method ON a WASM-module bean whose body is a dyn ScheduledBody) registers identically. Decorators (context-propagation) are shared with task-execution's chain via the same SPI, ordered at runtime by priority.

**T2 — execution context:** This feature's T2 is its essence — it is a TIME-aware execution primitive. Construction is cold-path async and runtime-dependent (the timer wheel needs the runtime), so the scheduler is an early infrastructure bean. The TRIGGER DRIVER runs as a single long-lived task per scheduler: it maintains a min-heap of (next_fire_instant, task_id), `sleep_until`s the earliest, and on wake SPAWNS the due body onto task-execution's executor (NEVER runs the body inline — that is the structural fix for the single-thread gotcha) then recomputes/re-inserts the next fire. So 'what runs where': trigger computation + sleep on the driver (cheap, never blocks); the @Scheduled body on the executor's workers (may .await; MUST NOT block — blocking work uses spawn_blocking, charter §2.3). Trigger semantics map to context: fixedRate re-inserts next_fire = fire_time + period independent of body completion (overlap possible → an explicit policy: queue / skip-if-still-running / allow-concurrent, default skip-if-running to bound concurrency since async has no implicit ceiling — ties task-execution's ConcurrencyGate); fixedDelay re-inserts next_fire = completion_time + delay, so it AWAITS the body's JoinHandle before scheduling the next — a completion-feedback loop that needs the executor to report done; cron computes next_fire from the calendar engine at the current instant. Cancellation: a ScheduleHandle carries a cancel token; on context-close the scheduler stops accepting/firing new triggers, lets in-flight bodies drain to a timeout, then aborts — cooperative (no thread interrupt), via an explicit container-driven shutdown().await (no async Drop; ties runtime-lifecycle/context-close). Errors: a panicking/failing scheduled body has no caller — it routes to a container-level scheduled-task error handler (the analogue of async-execution's uncaught handler) and the schedule CONTINUES (one failed fire must not silently kill the recurring task — a documented contract); a malformed cron expression fails at a startup validation pass (parsed when the descriptor is registered, earliest legible — charter §2.9), not at first fire. Tasks must not start firing until the context is fully up (a smart-initializing barrier), so an early fire never hits a half-built graph. No global lock — the only shared state is the per-scheduler timer heap (a fine-grained mutex or a single-owner driver task; charter §2.5).

#### R2 — Scheduling as a thin trigger layer over task-execution: a Trigger SPI + a single SchedulerCore that drives any execution facility (no separate scheduler abstraction)
Push unification (§2.11) hard and answer the phase2 tension by NOT introducing a second runtime abstraction. Observe that Spring keeps TaskExecutor and TaskScheduler parallel-but-distinct only because the JVM splits ScheduledExecutorService from Executor; in async Rust the ONLY thing scheduling adds over execution is 'compute when to fire and sleep until then' — the actual running is exactly task-execution's spawn. So model scheduling as: (a) a `Trigger` SPI — `trait Trigger { fn next_fire(&self, ctx: &TriggerContext) -> Option<Instant>; }` where TriggerContext carries last_scheduled/last_completion/last_actual_fire (Spring's Trigger.nextExecution shape) — with built-in `CronTrigger`, `FixedRateTrigger`, `FixedDelayTrigger` impls, and user-implementable for custom cadences. (b) ONE container-owned `SchedulerCore` (not injected as a user-facing abstraction; an internal infrastructure bean) that holds a timer-wheel of (Trigger, body) registrations, sleeps to the earliest next_fire, and on wake calls task-execution's injected ExecutorHandle/Spawner to run the body — the scheduler is literally 'a timer wheel that calls the executor.' (c) @Scheduled still emits a descriptor + an instance-tier post-processor registers it into SchedulerCore. The user-facing 'how is scheduled work run' lever is therefore task-execution's EXISTING selection (which executor profile/facility backs scheduled work, by qualifier/config) — scheduling adds NO new pool/virtual-thread/decorator machinery, it reuses task-execution's entirely. Virtual-thread support, concurrency limiting, context-propagation decorators, graceful drain — all inherited from task-execution because the body runs through that facility. The 'scheduler' a user can customize is the Trigger (cadence) and the executor selection (substrate), not a TaskScheduler bean. SchedulingConfigurer-analogue still exists to register Triggers programmatically.

**Pros:** Maximal unification (§2.11): scheduling stops being a second execution abstraction and becomes a trigger SPI + a thin timer that delegates to task-execution — one execution facility to tune/monitor/shutdown, not two; the decorator chain, concurrency gate, virtual-thread switch, and lifecycle drain are reused, not duplicated.; Directly resolves the phase2 'separate scheduler vs time-aware executor capability' tension in favor of capability — fewer concepts, smaller kernel surface, and a single answer to 'where does scheduled work run' (task-execution's profile/qualifier).; The Trigger SPI is more expressive and more rust-native than a fixed Trigger enum: cron/rate/delay are just impls, and a user custom trigger (e.g. 'fire at market open, skip holidays') is first-class — matches Spring's actual Trigger interface, which is richer than the three annotation shortcuts.; The single-thread gotcha cannot even arise: there is no scheduler pool to misconfigure — bodies always run on the chosen executor, whose ceiling/concurrency is task-execution's concern; 'scheduling serializes' is structurally impossible unless you deliberately pick a current-thread executor.; Smallest added surface for the body-run path: SchedulerCore is internal infra, so users learn 'annotate @Scheduled, pick an executor' — no new injectable abstraction to understand, lower DX overhead (#1/#2).

**Cons:** Diverges from Spring's landmark: there is no injectable TaskScheduler bean to @Primary/qualify or hand to a SchedulingConfigurer.setTaskScheduler — practitioners porting code that injects a TaskScheduler directly lose that seam (mitigable by exposing SchedulerCore as a thin facade, but that re-introduces a second abstraction).; Couples scheduling tightly to task-execution's final shape: if task-execution chooses the config-profile dispatcher (its R3) vs a dyn-trait bean (its R1), scheduling's 'call the executor' wiring differs — scheduling can't be designed fully until that sibling settles (a real ordering dependency, surfaced as a phase2 question).; A single SchedulerCore timer driver is a centralization point: all triggers across the app share one wheel — fine for reactive efficiency (one sleep, one waker) but it must be carefully concurrency-safe and its failure is global (every scheduled task stops); needs robust supervision.; fixedDelay still needs completion feedback from the executor (await the body's handle before computing next_fire) — so SchedulerCore must thread the executor's JoinHandle back into the Trigger's TriggerContext, a coupling the Trigger SPI must expose (last_completion), making the SPI slightly heavier than a pure 'next from clock' function.; If task-execution ends up NOT existing as a separate feature design in time, this realization has nothing to delegate to — it presupposes the sibling, so it is the least self-contained.

**Mechanisms considered:** Trigger SPI (next_fire(&TriggerContext) -> Option<Instant>) with CronTrigger/FixedRateTrigger/FixedDelayTrigger impls + user-custom — Spring's Trigger interface, not a closed enum; one internal container-owned SchedulerCore timer-wheel that delegates ALL running to task-execution's injected executor (no separate scheduler pool); reuse task-execution's executor selection (profile/qualifier), decorator chain, concurrency gate, virtual-thread switch, and graceful-drain lifecycle wholesale; instance-tier post-processor registers @Scheduled descriptors as (Trigger, body) into SchedulerCore; TriggerContext threads last_scheduled/last_completion/last_actual_fire so fixedDelay's completion-feedback and cron's calendar both compute from one shape; SchedulingConfigurer-analogue registers Triggers programmatically; executor choice is task-execution's lever, not a scheduler bean

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Even thinner kernel surface than R1: leaf-framework exports the Trigger SPI + TriggerContext + the @Scheduled descriptor schema + SchedulerCore; the timer primitive (sleep_until/timer-wheel) and ALL execution come from the runtime crate via task-execution's already-required executor — so scheduling adds ONE more reason the runtime crate must be force-linked, but no NEW mandatory crate beyond what task-execution already mandates. @Scheduled descriptors ride the same catalog substrate (linkme/codegen/explicit) with trigger spec as const data; the self-check is shared with task-execution's (if scheduled descriptors exist, the executor facility must exist) plus a scheduling-specific 'discovered-vs-expected scheduled tasks' enumeration — the same worse-than-ordinary silent-DCE hazard (a dropped @Scheduled descriptor is a task that never fires). Because there's no separate scheduler bean, there's one fewer cross-crate registration to drop. Origin-agnostic: a custom Trigger or a @Scheduled body from a WASM module registers into SchedulerCore identically; the body runs through the same origin-agnostic executor seam. The hard cross-crate dependency on task-execution's chosen shape (dyn bean vs profile dispatcher) is the notable T1 risk — scheduling's delegation code is parameterized over whatever 'spawn this body' seam task-execution exposes.

**T2 — execution context:** One SchedulerCore driver task per container owns ALL trigger timing: it asks each due Trigger for next_fire, sleeps_until the global earliest (reactive, §2.4 — one waker for the whole app, maximally efficient), and on wake delegates the body to task-execution's executor (so the body NEVER runs on the driver — the single-thread serialization gotcha is structurally absent). 'What runs where': next_fire computation + the single sleep on the driver (must never block — Trigger.next_fire is pure/cheap, a contract); the body on task-execution's workers under that facility's concurrency/blocking discipline (spawn_blocking for blocking bodies). Trigger semantics live in next_fire over TriggerContext: FixedRateTrigger returns last_scheduled+period (driver re-arms immediately, overlap bounded by the executor's concurrency gate — reuses task-execution's ceiling, the right place for it); FixedDelayTrigger returns last_completion+delay, so the driver must AWAIT the body's completion (via the executor's JoinHandle) before asking for next_fire — SchedulerCore threads completion back into TriggerContext; CronTrigger computes calendar-next from the wake instant. Cancellation: SchedulerCore participates in the container's explicit shutdown().await — stops arming new fires, lets in-flight bodies drain via task-execution's own graceful-drain (inherited), aborts on timeout; cooperative, no thread interrupt, no async Drop. Errors: a failed body routes to task-execution's uncaught-failure handler (shared, not a second handler) and the recurring schedule CONTINUES (one bad fire ≠ dead task — a documented contract); a bad cron expression fails at startup-validation when the Trigger is built (earliest legible, §2.9). No global lock beyond the single driver's timer-wheel state (single-owner task → no shared-mutable lock at all, charter §2.5 ideal). Tasks don't fire until the context is fully up (a smart-initializing barrier gates SchedulerCore's start).

#### R3 — Scheduling as data: @Scheduled triggers as Environment-bound descriptors + a compile/startup-validated schedule table feeding a reactive driver
Push 'chosen by configuration, not code' and richest-early-diagnostics (§2.9) hardest, paralleling task-execution's R3. @Scheduled is metadata; the cadence is config-overridable; the whole set of scheduled tasks is a TABLE of descriptors validated as early as possible.
