(a) `@Scheduled(cron = "...")` / `(fixed_rate = ...)` emits a `ScheduleDescriptor { task_id, trigger_spec, executor_ref, condition }` where trigger_spec can be a literal OR a `${leaf.schedule.<id>.cron}` placeholder resolved from the Environment (config-properties/binder/relaxed-binding reuse) — so an ops team retunes a cron or DISABLES a task (`enabled=false`) without recompiling, exactly Spring's `@Scheduled(cron = "${my.cron}")` ergonomics, and the accepted schedule surface is self-documenting via config-metadata. (b) A startup VALIDATION PASS builds the full schedule table: parses every cron expression, resolves every placeholder, checks no duplicate task ids, and validates executor refs exist — failing LOUD and EARLY with the full set of bad entries (charter §2.9), rather than discovering a typo'd cron at first fire weeks later. (c) The validated table feeds a reactive driver (the R1/R2 timer-wheel) that fires onto an executor. The 'scheduler' is barely an object — it's a validated data table + a driver; selection of substrate is a per-task `executor_ref` naming a task-execution profile/qualifier (finest-grained: task A on the virtual-thread executor, task B on a bounded pool). Disabling/enabling/retuning a task is pure config. A programmatic registrar still exists for dynamic tasks, writing into the same table.

**Pros:** Richest-early diagnostics (§2.9): every cron parsed and every placeholder resolved at a dedicated startup-validation pass — a malformed expression or a disabled-by-typo task fails LOUD before the app serves traffic, not silently at some 3am fire weeks later (the single worst scheduling failure mode).; Purest 'tuned by configuration not code': retune a cron, change a rate, disable a task, or repoint a task to a different executor profile — all in a properties file, zero recompile (Spring's `@Scheduled(cron="${...}")` + config-driven enable, rust-native and uniform with all leaf config).; The schedule table is a first-class inspectable/diagnosable artifact (ties startup-instrumentation): 'list every scheduled task, its resolved cron/rate, next fire, which executor, enabled?' is a trivial report — far better operability than a graph of scheduler beans.; Finest-grained substrate selection: per-task executor_ref means a heavy nightly batch task and a light health-ping can run on different executor profiles — more expressive than Spring's single shared scheduler, and it composes with task-execution's per-profile tuning.; Config-disable of a task is a clean kill switch (Spring needs @ConditionalOnProperty gymnastics); enabling/disabling a scheduled task in production is a config flip, a real operational win.

**Cons:** Heaviest machinery and most config coupling — risks Phase-1 over-engineering and pre-judging the metadata/codegen boundary and the config model (both Phase-2 keystones); must stay a CANDIDATE, not a stance.; A trigger named by a `${...}` placeholder is a runtime/startup error if the key is missing/typo'd — better than first-fire-failure (the validation pass catches it) but still later than a compile error a literal cron could (partially) get; the compile-vs-runtime split (charter §3) decides where this lands.; 'Scheduling is a config table' is the least Spring-shaped framing — a practitioner expecting an injectable TaskScheduler bean finds selection moved into config + an executor_ref string; a typo'd executor_ref is a startup error, not a compile error.; Still depends on task-execution for the actual run (per-task executor_ref points into that sibling) AND on the config/Environment/binder neighbours for resolution — the most cross-feature-coupled realization, hard to design in isolation.; Per-task executor selection multiplies the validation burden (every executor_ref must resolve) and the silent-DCE surface (a referenced executor profile/crate must be linked) — more to self-check than R1/R2.

**Mechanisms considered:** @Scheduled emits a ScheduleDescriptor whose trigger_spec + enabled flag can be a literal OR an Environment placeholder (config-properties/binder/relaxed-binding/config-metadata reuse); a dedicated startup-validation pass: parse all crons, resolve all placeholders, dedupe ids, validate executor_refs — fail loud with the full bad-entry set (§2.9); the validated schedule table feeds a reactive timer-wheel driver (shared with R1/R2) firing onto a per-task-named executor profile (task-execution); per-task executor_ref for finest-grained substrate selection; config-driven enable/disable as a kill switch; programmatic registrar writes dynamic tasks into the same validated table; the table is an inspectable diagnostic artifact (startup-instrumentation): tasks, resolved triggers, next-fire, executor, enabled

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The ScheduleDescriptor schema + the validation pass + the driver live in leaf-framework; trigger VALUES (cron strings, rates, enabled flags) can come from the Environment at runtime, so RETUNING/disabling a task needs no recompile and no cross-crate registration — sidestepping the link-time DCE hazard for the TUNING surface entirely (a genuine advantage: config is a real runtime 'classpath' for schedule policy, mirroring task-execution's R3). What still crosses crates: the @Scheduled descriptors themselves (ride the catalog substrate, same silent-DCE 'a dropped descriptor never fires' hazard, same enumeration self-check) and the per-task executor_ref pointing into task-execution's facility (which must be force-linked + present). The startup-validation pass is the natural home for BOTH the anti-DCE self-check (discovered-vs-expected scheduled tasks) AND the schedule validation (crons/placeholders/refs) — one loud early gate. A WASM-module-provided scheduled task contributes a descriptor whose cron is config-resolvable and whose executor_ref names a profile identically — origin-agnostic. Generics hard-error (no symbol). The dynamic WASM-module 'classpath' duality applies: static link-time descriptors plus an optional directory-scanned source both feed ONE schedule table (candidates, not a decision), and config-resolved triggers are the cleanest dynamic surface of the three.

**T2 — execution context:** Two phases. VALIDATION phase: cold-path, single-threaded, around startup — parse every cron (a pure, possibly-CPU-bound calendar parse; if remote-config reads are needed they .await on the cold path), resolve placeholders against the Environment, dedupe and check executor_refs, then FREEZE the schedule table; failures abort here all-at-once with the full bad-entry set (earliest legible, §2.9). DRIVE phase: identical to R1/R2 — one reactive driver task per table sleeps_until the earliest next_fire over the validated entries and spawns due bodies onto the per-task executor profile (task-execution), never running bodies inline (single-thread gotcha structurally absent); enabled=false entries are simply absent from the table so they never arm. Trigger semantics (fixedRate overlap policy, fixedDelay completion-feedback, cron calendar-next) are as R1/R2, computed from the validated spec; the per-task executor_ref means each body's concurrency/blocking/decorator discipline is that profile's (task-execution). Cancellation/shutdown: the driver participates in the container's explicit shutdown().await (no async Drop), draining in-flight bodies via task-execution's graceful-drain, cooperative. Errors at RUNTIME (a body fails) route to task-execution's shared uncaught handler and the schedule continues; errors at VALIDATION (bad cron/missing placeholder/dangling executor_ref) are the early loud failure. Config-disable means a task is inert with zero runtime cost. The driver holds only the frozen table (read-mostly) + the timer heap — no global lock (§2.5); the table being frozen post-validation means the hot drive path does no re-resolution.

### Rust clashes
- No threads, no ScheduledExecutorService to IoC over — Rust has an async runtime. Spring's TaskScheduler abstracts a ScheduledExecutorService (a thread pool that knows about delays). The rust-native equivalent is 'compute next-fire + sleep on a runtime timer + spawn the body' — a reframing, not a port. The scheduler can only be constructed after a runtime exists (it needs the timer wheel), forcing it among the earliest infrastructure beans (a bootstrap-ordering constraint with no Spring analogue), and it is intrinsically runtime-coupled even though the trait must name no runtime (charter §2.6 tension, inherited from task-execution).
- The single-thread default gotcha INVERTS in async Rust — and the danger inverts too. Spring's pool-size-1 default serializes scheduled tasks (line 2045). In leaf, if the trigger driver SPAWNS bodies onto an async executor, the natural default is the OPPOSITE: unbounded concurrent fires (a fast cron + a slow body piles up tasks with no implicit ceiling — virtual threads / async spawn have no pool to throttle them). So leaf must make a deliberate per-trigger overlap policy (skip-if-running / queue / bounded-concurrent) the DEFAULT, reusing task-execution's concurrency gate — the absence of an accidental ceiling is the rust-native hazard where Spring's accidental ceiling was the hazard.
- fixedDelay needs completion feedback across a task boundary — Spring's ScheduledExecutorService observes the Runnable returning on its own thread to schedule the next fixed-delay fire. In leaf the body runs as a spawned future on a DIFFERENT execution context than the trigger driver, so the driver must AWAIT the body's JoinHandle (or receive a completion signal) before computing next_fire — a cross-task completion-feedback loop. Getting this wrong (firing the next fixedDelay on a timer instead of on completion) silently turns fixedDelay into fixedRate and drifts the schedule — a subtle correctness clash with no Spring analogue because Spring's scheduler and worker share the JVM thread model.
- Cancellation is cooperative, with no thread interrupt — Spring can interrupt a pooled scheduler thread to stop a task. Rust cancels by dropping the body future or signaling a token; a non-cooperative body (a tight CPU loop, a blocking call not on spawn_blocking) ignores shutdown drain. So 'stop the scheduler gracefully' must be cooperative + timeout-bounded, and a scheduled body that outlives the drain window is aborted (future dropped at next await) or detached — an explicit policy with a silent-task-leak footgun absent on the JVM.
- No async Drop on stable — Spring's scheduler is a Lifecycle bean gracefully shut down by the container; the immediate-destroy-without-stop rule (line 2083-2085) requires runtime-mutable state be volatile and robust to out-of-order shutdown. leaf cannot ride Drop for async drain, so the scheduler's lifecycle participation becomes an explicit container-driven shutdown().await (ties runtime-lifecycle/context-close), and the 'destroy without a preceding stop' tolerance must be a designed-in state-machine property, not an emergent one.
- Cron-expression semantics are a real engine, not a library primitive — Spring delegates to a cron parser with a specific 6/7-field dialect, timezone, and DST handling. Rust has crates but no std answer; leaf must pick/own a calendar engine and decide DST/timezone/missed-fire semantics. This is genuine work and a divergence source: 'fire missed runs after a pause/clock-jump?' has no obvious right answer and Spring's exact behavior may differ from any chosen Rust crate — a parity-vs-pragmatism clash, and the calendar parse is the rare CPU-bound bit on an otherwise IO/timer cold path.
- async fn in traits is not dyn-compatible, but the scheduler/body/trigger surfaces want dyn — a TaskScheduler bean (R1) and a ScheduledBody closure are dyn-erased (heterogeneous bodies across crates, origin-agnostic incl. WASM), and AFIT isn't object-safe → boxed futures + boxed closures per fire (Pin<Box<dyn Future + Send>>). Acceptable on a cold periodic path (fires are infrequent vs request hot paths) but a real per-fire alloc; the Trigger.next_fire computation is kept SYNC to avoid boxing the hot timing path (R2/R3).
- Context propagation across the fire boundary has no ThreadLocal — Spring's scheduled task can carry MDC/request/security context via a TaskDecorator on the scheduler. In leaf the body runs on a spawned task that may migrate threads; ThreadLocal-based holders are WRONG across the .await/spawn hop. Scheduling can only DECLARE that it reuses task-execution's TaskDecorator capture/restore seam — the actual ambient-context mechanism is the Phase-2-deferred context-propagation model, so propagation into a scheduled fire is unresolved exactly as it is for async-execution.

### Interactions
- **task-execution** — THE primary dependency. Scheduling owns WHEN (the trigger/timer), task-execution owns WHERE (the run-somewhere executor seam). The scheduled body is SPAWNED onto task-execution's injected executor, so virtual-thread support, concurrency limiting (the overlap ceiling), the TaskDecorator/context-propagation chain, and graceful-drain lifecycle are all INHERITED, not duplicated. R2/R3 lean on this hardest (no separate scheduler pool; per-task executor_ref). The single-thread serialization gotcha is structurally avoided precisely because firing delegates to this facility. Scheduling cannot be fully designed until task-execution's shape (dyn bean vs profile dispatcher) settles.
- **post-processor-spi** — The @Scheduled scanner is an INSTANCE-TIER post-processor riding this SPI's after_init hook (the ScheduledAnnotationBeanPostProcessor analogue): it reads each fully-initialized bean's @Scheduled descriptors, binds each method to the live bean Arc, and registers a recurring task. The 'after init, bean fully built' timing is exactly what post-processor-spi guarantees — scheduling must register AFTER the bean is initialized, never against a half-built instance.
- **import-composition** — @EnableScheduling is sugar over @Import + ImportAware metadata (doc line 1054): there is no special enable machinery — the enabling annotation registers the scheduler post-processor + a default scheduler/SchedulerCore and reads attributes (e.g. proxy/mode/scheduler-name) off the importer. Scheduling is one of the marquee @Enable* features this neighbour exists to support.
- **conditions** — The default scheduler/executor backs off if the user supplies one (@ConditionalOnMissingBean analogue), and the virtual-thread vs pooled choice is a condition over a property+capability (the @ConditionalOnThreading(VIRTUAL) analogue — checks both the flag and runtime capability). Whether a given @Scheduled task is active can itself be condition/profile-gated; R3's config-driven enable/disable is the rust-native kill switch.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — The scheduler/SchedulerCore IS a Lifecycle bean — it starts (begins arming triggers) only after the context is up and stops (quiesces firing) on shutdown, with phase ordering and graceful drain. Spring's immediate-destroy-without-stop tolerance (line 2083-2085) applies: the driver's runtime-mutable state must be robust to destroy-without-stop. Scheduling must not begin firing until the running-state machine says go.
- **context-close** — Symmetric teardown: because there is no async Drop, context-close drives the scheduler's explicit shutdown().await — stop arming new fires, drain in-flight bodies to a timeout (via task-execution's drain), then abort the remainder, BEFORE destroying the beans whose methods the tasks invoke (so a late fire never touches a half-destroyed bean). The scheduler is torn down early in the stop phase (quiesce activity) but its bodies' beans outlive it until drain completes.
- **smart-initializing** — Scheduled tasks must not start firing until EVERY eager singleton exists — firing against a half-built graph is a hazard. The smart-initializing 'all eager singletons exist' barrier (lock-free, after preInstantiateSingletons) is the natural trigger to START the scheduler driver: discovery happens via the post-processor per-bean, but ARMING the timer wheel waits for this single post-init callback.
- **retry-resilience** — Shares the concurrency-ceiling primitive (task-execution's ConcurrencyGate / @ConcurrencyLimit) used to bound scheduled-fire overlap, and the cold-path polling sanction (charter §2.4): retry/backoff timers and scheduled triggers are the SAME class of 'await on a reactive timer, cold path' work and may share the timer-wheel primitive. A @Scheduled method that is also @Retryable composes the two interceptor/trigger layers.
- **context-propagation** — Scheduling reuses task-execution's TaskDecorator chain to capture/restore ambient context (request/locale/tracing/MDC) across the fire boundary — the body runs on a spawned task, so ThreadLocal-style holders are wrong; scheduling only DECLARES it consumes this seam. The actual capture/restore mechanism is this neighbour's Phase-2-deferred ambient-context model, so propagation into a scheduled fire is unresolved by intent.
- **async-execution** — Sibling under the same @Enable* / TaskExecutor lineage: @EnableAsync and @EnableScheduling are independent (scheduling works without async, doc line 2041) but both target task-execution's executor seam and both route uncaught body failures to a container-level handler (scheduling reuses the AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler analogue rather than inventing a second). A @Scheduled @Async method is a known composition (the schedule fires, the async proxy re-dispatches).
- **container-core** — The scheduler is registered/selected through the engine + façade: role-marked as Infrastructure (auto-detected), resolved by primary/qualifier via autowiring-resolution, with its presence enforced by the façade's expected-vs-found self-check — extended here to the scheduling-specific 'if @Scheduled descriptors were discovered, a scheduler/executor must exist' check, since a missing scheduler means tasks silently never fire.
- **config-properties** — Strongest in R3: cron expressions, rates, initial delays, pool/virtual-thread choice, and per-task enable flags are bound from the Environment via config-properties + binder + relaxed-binding, documented via config-metadata — the rust-native realization of Spring's @Scheduled(cron="${my.cron}") and config-driven scheduling tuning.
- **environment** — Source of the swap-by-config and per-task tuning levers: the active profile + property stack decides which scheduler/executor impl is primary (virtual-thread as a deployment decision) and resolves ${...} placeholders in trigger specs; conditions over the Environment gate which scheduling wiring activates.
- **value-injection** — @Scheduled trigger attributes that use ${...} placeholders are resolved through the same placeholder/value-resolution path value-injection provides — a cron or rate expressed as a property is resolved exactly like any @Value, with type conversion (string→Duration) at the binding point.
- **type-conversion** — Trigger durations (fixedRate/fixedDelay/initialDelay as '5s'/'PT1M'/millis) are coerced via the central ConversionService/Duration parser — the same duration/size conversion type-conversion provides for @Value and config binding, so a scheduled rate written as a human duration string parses uniformly.
- **startup-instrumentation** — Scheduler construction (timer-wheel init) and the R3 schedule-table validation pass are natural named startup steps; R3's validated schedule table is a trivially inspectable diagnostic surface (every task, resolved trigger, next-fire, executor, enabled) — the rust-native 'list my scheduled tasks' operability Spring lacks out of the box.
- **expression-language** — Spring allows SpEL in @Scheduled (e.g. cron via #{...}); leaf's expression layer is the analogue evaluation engine if trigger attributes support expressions beyond plain ${...} placeholders — a richer-cadence escape hatch parallel to the Trigger SPI's custom impls.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is the scheduler a SEPARATE injectable abstraction (R1's TaskScheduler bean, faithful to Spring's deliberate TaskExecutor/TaskScheduler split) or a TIME-AWARE CAPABILITY layered on task-execution's single execution facility (R2/R3: a Trigger SPI + a thin timer that delegates all running to the executor)? This is a unification stance (§2.11) that cross-cuts task-execution directly and decides whether leaf has one execution abstraction or two — and scheduling cannot be finalized until task-execution's own shape (dyn-trait bean vs capability split vs config-profile dispatcher) is chosen, because scheduling's 'spawn the body' wiring is parameterized over it.
- What is the framework-wide OVERLAP / concurrency policy for recurring fires, and what is the DEFAULT? Async spawn has no implicit ceiling (the inverted single-thread gotcha), so a fast trigger + slow body piles up unboundedly. Is the default skip-if-still-running, queue, or bounded-concurrent, and does the ceiling reuse task-execution's ConcurrencyGate / @ConcurrencyLimit primitive? This must be one unified answer shared with retry-resilience and task-execution, not solved per-feature.
- How much of the schedule is COMPILE-TIME-legible vs RUNTIME config (charter §2.9 richest-diagnostic-timing, §3 compile-vs-runtime split)? R3 binds crons/rates/enable from the Environment and validates at a startup pass (retune without recompile, but a missing placeholder is a startup error); a literal-only path could catch malformed crons earlier. Where do scheduling errors (bad cron, dangling executor_ref, disabled-by-typo) surface, and does the cron parse happen in a macro, a build.rs, or a startup pass? This pre-judges the metadata/codegen boundary if decided here.
- What is the cron CALENDAR ENGINE and its semantics — which dialect (6 vs 7 field), timezone/DST handling, and crucially the MISSED-FIRE policy after a pause/clock-jump/long-GC-equivalent (fire all missed runs, fire once, or skip to next)? Rust has no std answer and candidate crates differ from Spring's exact behavior; this is a parity-vs-pragmatism call with no obvious right answer, cross-cutting type-conversion (duration parsing) and the diagnostics model.
- What is the CANCELLATION + graceful-drain contract for in-flight scheduled bodies at shutdown, and for fixedDelay's completion-feedback? Cooperative cancellation means a non-cooperative body ignores drain; what happens to a body that outlives the drain window (abort vs detach), and how does the driver reliably observe body completion to schedule the next fixedDelay fire without silently drifting into fixedRate? Needs one unified answer with runtime-lifecycle, context-close, task-execution, and retry-resilience.
- Where do uncaught scheduled-body failures go, and must the recurring schedule CONTINUE after a failed fire? Fire-and-forget bodies have no caller; is there a single container-level scheduled-task error handler (shared with async-execution's AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler analogue), and how does it compose with the unified error/diagnostics model (§2.9, deferred)? The 'one bad fire must not kill the recurring task' contract needs framework-wide agreement.
- Does scheduling share a SINGLE global timer-wheel/driver (R2/R3: one sleep, one waker for the whole app — maximally reactive per §2.4 but a single point whose failure stops every task) or per-scheduler drivers? And is that timer primitive shared with retry-resilience's backoff timers and any periodic-refresh/health cold-path work (a unification candidate for 'await on a reactive timer')?
- The anti-DCE self-check for scheduling specifically: a dropped @Scheduled descriptor is WORSE than a missing bean — it is a task that silently never fires, invisible until production. What is the canonical 'discovered-vs-expected scheduled tasks' manifest, where does it live (façade? generated app-root? the R3 validation pass?), and how does it produce a rich diagnostic — and does it differ across catalog substrates (linkme vs codegen vs explicit)? This is the scheduling instance of the deferred cross-crate composition model.
- How does context propagation reach a scheduled fire? The body runs on a spawned task that may migrate threads, so ThreadLocal holders are wrong; scheduling can only declare it reuses task-execution's TaskDecorator seam — the actual rust-native ambient-context-across-.await mechanism is the deferred context-propagation keystone. Until that settles, MDC/request/security/tracing context into a scheduled task is unresolved by intent.
- Origin-agnostic depth: should a @Scheduled body or a custom Trigger from a non-native source (WASM module, the optional directory-scanned dynamic 'classpath') register through the same descriptor seam as native ones (R1/R2) — and can a WASM-module-provided scheduler/Trigger satisfy the full trigger+spawn contract, or only contribute a body? This interacts with task-execution's origin-agnostic executor seam and the registry/scope substrate.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. The Spring scheduling section is short but explicit (lines 2039-2058) and I read it with its full neighbour cluster, because scheduling is realized almost entirely THROUGH other features: the TaskExecutor abstraction (2016-2023) and virtual-thread one-switch (2049-2058) that scheduling's body-run delegates to, the @Enable-as-@Import+ImportAware mechanism (1054), the @ConcurrencyLimit ceiling (2077-2081) that bounds fire overlap, and the immediate-destroy-without-stop Lifecycle rule (2083-2085). The single most load-bearing INTENT to preserve is the production gotcha at line 2045 (single-threaded default serializes all tasks) — every realization structurally defeats it by separating WHEN (trigger driver) from WHERE (executor), which inverts it into the async hazard (unbounded fire overlap) that the overlap-policy/concurrency-gate answers. The second is the virtual-thread one-switch (adoption is deployment, not code) — realized by never letting call sites name a scheduler, only an injected abstraction whose primary impl is config-chosen.

The two upstream designs I was handed are decisive: post-processor-spi gives the after_init instance-tier hook the @Scheduled scanner rides (the ScheduledAnnotationBeanPostProcessor analogue), and task-execution gives the run-somewhere seam scheduling's trigger layer drives. I deliberately built ON both rather than reinventing — scheduling adds ONLY the time/trigger layer (cron/rate/delay computation + a reactive timer). This is why the central phase2 tension is whether the scheduler is a second abstraction (R1, Spring-faithful) or a time-aware capability of task-execution's facility (R2/R3, maximal unification) — and scheduling genuinely cannot be finalized until task-execution's own shape settles (a real ordering dependency I surfaced, not resolved).

The three realizations diverge on the axis that matters HERE — WHERE scheduling's intelligence lives: (R1) a literal TaskScheduler dyn-bean + scanning post-processor, the direct Spring port, two abstractions; (R2) a Trigger SPI + one thin SchedulerCore that delegates ALL running to task-execution, maximal unification, no second abstraction; (R3) scheduling-as-data — Environment-bound trigger descriptors + a startup-validation pass + a config-tunable schedule table, leaning hardest into richest-early-diagnostics and config-driven tuning (parallel to task-execution's R3). They span Spring-faithful → rust-native-unifying → config-as-the-lever.

Charter §2.4 (reactive on hot paths, polling only on cold paths) is the load-bearing invariant for THIS feature and I held it fixed across all three: the trigger MUST be a runtime timer-wheel + sleep_until/waker (reactive), NEVER an interval busy-poll — scheduling is exactly the 'explicit scheduled task' cold path §2.4 sanctions for await-on-a-timer, but the await must be on a reactive timer, not a spin. Other fixed constraints across all three: firing SPAWNS the body (never runs it on the trigger driver — the structural fix for the single-thread gotcha); cancellation is cooperative + timeout-bounded (no thread interrupt); lifecycle/teardown is explicit container-driven shutdown().await (no async Drop on stable); a failed fire must not kill the recurring task (continue, route to a shared error handler); tasks don't arm until smart-initializing's all-singletons barrier; bad crons fail at a startup-validation pass (earliest legible, §2.9), not at first fire. Every realization keeps the body/scheduler/trigger seams origin-agnostic (a WASM-module body or custom Trigger registers identically) and trigger spec as DATA on the descriptor (no generics, ordering/conditions resolved at runtime, not link order). The scheduling-specific silent-DCE hazard is worse than ordinary beans (a dropped @Scheduled descriptor is a task that never fires — invisible until production), so the discovered-vs-expected self-check is non-optional and I flagged it.

Things I deliberately did NOT decide (phase2Questions): separate-scheduler-vs-time-aware-capability; the overlap/concurrency default and whether it reuses task-execution's gate; compile-vs-runtime split for the schedule (and thus the cron parse location); the cron calendar engine + missed-fire semantics; cancellation/drain + fixedDelay completion-feedback contract; the shared error handler; single-vs-per-scheduler timer driver and whether the timer primitive is shared with retry-resilience; the scheduling self-check manifest; context-propagation into a fire (depends on the deferred ambient-context model); origin-agnostic provider depth. Naming ('TaskScheduler','Trigger','SchedulerCore','ScheduledMethodDescriptor','ScheduleDescriptor','ScheduleHandle','TriggerContext') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files.

Grounding paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (@EnableScheduling/@Scheduled 2039-2058, esp. single-thread gotcha 2045 and virtual-thread one-switch 2049-2058; TaskExecutor 2016-2023; @Enable-as-@Import+ImportAware 1054; @ConcurrencyLimit 2077-2081; immediate-destroy-without-stop 2083-2085; annotation-driven-POJO model consistent-with-@Scheduled 1816); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2.3 execution-context, §2.4 reactive-hot/poll-cold [load-bearing here], §2.5 no-global-lock, §2.6 runtime-pluggable, §2.9 early-rich-diagnostics, §2.10 escape-hatch, §2.11 unification; deferred §3; topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (Layer-0 DCE + force-link/self-check 149-152,168-169,345-346; ordering-as-data/runtime-topo-sort 154-155,170,355; architectures A-D 277-337; mandatory-runtime/generics-cannot-register 153,349); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (AFIT/RPITIT not dyn-safe → boxed futures at dyn boundary 160-182; no async Drop → explicit shutdown 273-281; trait upcasting 1.86 118-126; async closures for factories 262-264). The two upstream Phase-1 designs (post-processor-spi, task-execution) were handed to me in the prompt and built on concretely; neighbour designs scheduling depends on but that don't yet exist as docs (async-execution, context-propagation, runtime-lifecycle, retry-resilience) are referenced by catalog intent only.

## Transaction Management (@Transactional)  `[transaction-management]`

This feature is the CANONICAL declarative cross-cutting concern — the proof that leaf's whole AOP-via-DI stack composes. Its INTENT (Spring §1693-1716, §2114-2118): `@Transactional` is, by itself, INERT METADATA on a method/type (line 1712). The `@EnableTransactionManagement` selector does the real work — it registers (a) the auto-proxy-creator BPP, (b) the feature's Advisor (`BeanFactoryTransactionAttributeSourceAdvisor`, @Role(ROLE_INFRASTRUCTURE), in `ProxyTransactionManagementConfiguration`), and (c) the `TransactionInterceptor` that IS the behavior — it opens a transaction, proceeds, then commits-on-success / rolls-back-on-matching-exception, all AROUND the real method call (lines 1705/1712). The annotation is the marker; the @Enable* selector is the wiring; the interceptor is the behavior. leaf must replicate this LAYERED intent, NOT the JVM mechanism.\n\nFive load-bearing intent layers I must preserve: (1) DECLARATIVE DEMARCATION — a `#[transactional]` marker on a method/impl carries attributes (propagation, isolation, read-only, timeout, rollback-rules, a named transaction-manager qualifier) as pure DATA; the marker fires NO behavior on its own. (2) THE INTERCEPTOR woven into the ONE shared proxy chain by the upstream auto-proxy-creator (my direct upstream): tx is a Role::Infrastructure advisor, role-isolated from application aspects, ordered deterministically relative to cache/validation/retry/async within the single chain (lines 1770-1772 — the cache-outside-vs-inside-tx ordering is the canonical correctness concern). I do NOT own the proxy mechanism or the collection/match/order policy — auto-proxy-creator + aop-proxying own those; I CONTRIBUTE one advisor descriptor into their pipeline. (3) THE VENDOR-NEUTRAL ABSTRACTION — Spring's `PlatformTransactionManager` (getTransaction/commit/rollback over a `TransactionDefinition`, yielding a `TransactionStatus`) + the reactive `ReactiveTransactionManager` (6.1+, carries context in Reactor Context not ThreadLocal). leaf needs the rust-native equal: a `TransactionManager` trait the interceptor drives, origin-agnostic (a native JDBC-style manager, a test double, or a WASM-backed manager all implement it identically), so the DAO/data-access tech stays framework-unaware. (4) RESOURCE BINDING TO THE EXECUTION CONTEXT — Spring's `TransactionSynchronizationManager` binds the transactional resource (the same `Connection`/`EntityManager`) to the thread for the transaction's duration, NEVER inheritable (lines 2114-2118: 'silently sharing a transactional Connection with a child thread would shatter isolation and connection-pool accounting; a transaction must live and die on one thread'). This is the deepest tie to context-propagation/locale-context: the bound resource is ambient per-execution state that nested data-access LOOKS UP rather than receives as a parameter — and in async Rust 'the thread' is the wrong scope (a task migrates workers across .await), so the resource belongs to the LOGICAL TASK. (5) PROPAGATION SEMANTICS — REQUIRED (join-or-start), REQUIRES_NEW (suspend outer, start fresh, resume), NESTED (savepoint), SUPPORTS/NOT_SUPPORTED/MANDATORY/NEVER — the suspend/resume of bound resources around the call. Plus transaction synchronizations (the before/after-commit/rollback hooks transactional-events rides) and rollback rules (which error types trigger rollback vs commit).\n\nI realize INTENT not mechanism: no ThreadLocal (actively wrong across .await), no runtime classpath scan for the advisor, no reflective method-attribute read. The marker emits attribute DATA at macro time; the interceptor is a normal async interceptor in the shared chain; the resource binding rides leaf's (deferred) async context-propagation substrate; the manager is a dyn trait. I do NOT decide the proxy mechanism (aop-proxying keystone), the collection/match/order substrate (auto-proxy-creator + Phase-2 T1), the ambient-context-across-.await primitive (context-propagation keystone, charter §3), nor the scope/ownership model the bound resource lives in — those are upstream/Phase-2. I own: the `#[transactional]` attribute DATA model, the TransactionManager trait + TransactionDefinition/TransactionStatus, the interceptor's open/commit/rollback/propagation logic, the rollback-rule policy, and HOW the resource binds to the execution context (delegating the primitive to context-propagation).

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Faithful interceptor port: a TransactionInterceptor advisor over a dyn TransactionManager, resource bound via the context-propagation substrate (the direct TransactionInterceptor analogue)
The most literal intent-port. `#[transactional]` is a THIN attribute macro that emits, per marked method, a `const TxAttribute { propagation: Propagation, isolation: Isolation, read_only: bool, timeout: Option<Duration>, rollback_on: &'static [ErrorMatch], no_rollback_on: &'static [ErrorMatch], manager: Option<&'static str> }` plus a method-metadata entry the auto-proxy-creator can match (the rust-native replacement for reflective @Transactional discovery — presence-as-data). The feature contributes ONE `AdvisorDescriptor { role: Role::Infrastructure, order, pointcut: matches-methods-carrying-a-TxAttribute, make_interceptor: |c| Arc::new(TransactionInterceptor::new(c.resolve::<dyn TransactionManager>())) }` into the auto-proxy-creator's collection stream (origin-agnostic, exactly like exception-translation/caching/validation/async contribute theirs). The `TransactionInterceptor` is a normal async interceptor in the ONE shared chain: on a call it reads the join-point's TxAttribute, asks the resolved `TransactionManager` to begin/join a transaction per propagation, `proceed().await`s the rest of the chain (the real method), then commits on success or rolls back if the returned Err matches a rollback rule. `trait TransactionManager: Send + Sync { async fn begin(&self, def: &TxDefinition, cx: &ExecutionContext) -> Result<TxState, TxError>; async fn commit(&self, st: TxState) -> Result<(), TxError>; async fn rollback(&self, st: TxState) -> Result<(), TxError>; }` is the PlatformTransactionManager analogue — async-first, origin-agnostic. THE RESOURCE BINDING is the crux: begin() binds the transactional resource (a Connection/Session handle) into the EXECUTION CONTEXT via a `TransactionResources` context value that context-propagation governs — so nested data-access on the same logical task LOOKS UP the bound resource (`ctx.tx_resource::<Conn>()`) and reuses it (the TransactionSynchronizationManager bindResource/getResource intent). Because async tasks migrate workers, the binding rides context-propagation's task-scoped store (NOT a thread_local), and the resource is marked NEVER-PROPAGATE-TO-CHILD-TASK by default (the never-inheritable rule, lines 2114-2118 — realized as context-propagation's exclude-by-default per-key policy). Propagation: REQUIRED checks the context for an active tx and joins or begins; REQUIRES_NEW SUSPENDS the current binding (saves+unbinds), begins fresh, runs, then RESUMES (rebinds) the suspended one in a Drop/finally-shaped guard; NESTED takes a savepoint via the manager; MANDATORY/NEVER/SUPPORTS/NOT_SUPPORTED are guard checks over the context state. Commit/rollback fire the transaction-synchronization hooks (before/after-commit/rollback) that transactional-events consumes.

**Pros:** Tightest intent parity: it IS TransactionInterceptor — open/proceed/commit-or-rollback around the call, driving a vendor-neutral manager, binding the resource to the execution context. The Spring mental model and docs transfer 1:1, and it slots cleanly as ONE advisor into the auto-proxy-creator pipeline exactly as the catalog's upstream design expects (tx is its named example interaction).; Maximal reuse of decided upstreams: the proxy/wrap/route is aop-proxying's; the collect/match/order/role-isolation is auto-proxy-creator's; the ambient-resource-across-.await is context-propagation's. This feature adds only the tx-specific behavior (manager protocol + propagation + rollback rules), maximizing unification (charter §2.11) — it is a thin consumer, not a parallel subsystem.; Origin-agnostic TransactionManager + resource handle: a native pool-backed manager, a test double (an in-memory manager that records begin/commit for unit tests without a DB), or a WASM-module-backed manager implement the SAME trait; the interceptor never assumes native construction (charter provider-of-dyn-Trait neutrality).; The never-inheritable correctness rule (lines 2114-2118) is realized HONESTLY as a per-key context-propagation exclusion (the tx resource is excluded-by-default from child-task propagation), so a connection cannot silently ride to a spawned task and shatter isolation — the rust-native form of 'inheritance made impossible, not merely discouraged', and abuse-resistant by construction (charter §2.3).; Async-first and cancellation-shaped: begin/commit/rollback are .await points the interceptor owns on a known execution context; the suspend/resume of REQUIRES_NEW is a scoped guard, and a cancelled (dropped) future mid-transaction triggers rollback through the guard's teardown — explicit execution-context discipline (charter §2.3).; Rollback rules as DATA (rollback_on/no_rollback_on ErrorMatch lists on the const attribute) keep the macro thin (charter §2.10) and make the policy inspectable/diagnosable (charter §2.9: 'this method rolls back on these error types').

**Cons:** LEANS HARD on two still-deferred keystones: the ambient-context-across-.await primitive (context-propagation, charter §3) AND the scope/ownership model the bound resource lives in. If context-propagation lands as explicit-Context-threading (its R3) rather than ambient, the 'nested data-access looks up the bound resource' intent becomes 'thread a &Context param', changing the DAO ergonomics — so this realization's correctness is gated on a decision it cannot make.; Async Drop does not exist on stable (nightly doc 273-281: AsyncDrop crashes tokio shutdown) — so the REQUIRES_NEW suspend/resume guard and the commit/rollback-on-scope-exit CANNOT ride an async Drop. The interceptor must perform commit/rollback as EXPLICIT .await calls in its own body (try/finally-shaped via a sync-Drop guard that only flags 'must rollback', with the actual async rollback driven explicitly) — a real structural constraint that makes the suspend/resume and the cancellation-rollback paths intricate.; Self-invocation limitation inherited from proxy-based AOP (lines 1714-1729): a `this.inner_txn_method()` call inside the target bypasses the proxy, so its `#[transactional]` silently does not run — a known Spring footgun that leaf inherits unless the proxy mechanism (aop-proxying, deferred) is per-call rather than per-bean-reference. Must be documented loudly and diagnosed (charter §2.9), but cannot be fixed at this layer.; The TxAttribute method-metadata that the auto-proxy-creator matches on must be GENERATED as data by the (deferred) proxy/metadata mechanism — so this realization implicitly assumes method-attribute-presence is captured as data at macro time, pre-judging part of aop-proxying's metadata keystone.; Resolving the TransactionManager (and possibly multiple, by named qualifier) at make_interceptor time couples to autowiring-resolution + candidate-resolver (which manager for this @Transactional('txMgr2')?) — fine, but the multi-manager-by-qualifier case adds resolution complexity the interceptor must carry per join-point.; The synchronization hooks (before/after-commit) that transactional-events needs are a second protocol on the manager/binding the interceptor must drive and order correctly — extra surface beyond the core open/commit/rollback.

**Mechanisms considered:** #[transactional] thin attribute macro emitting a const TxAttribute (propagation/isolation/read-only/timeout/rollback-rules/manager-qualifier) + a method-metadata entry the auto-proxy-creator matches (presence-as-data, no reflection); ONE AdvisorDescriptor { Role::Infrastructure, order, pointcut=carries-TxAttribute, make_interceptor } contributed into the auto-proxy-creator collection stream (origin-agnostic, same as cache/validation/exception-translation); TransactionInterceptor as a normal async interceptor in the single shared chain: begin/join -> proceed().await -> commit | rollback (rollback-rule match on Err); trait TransactionManager (async begin/commit/rollback over TxDefinition -> TxState) — the PlatformTransactionManager analogue, dyn, origin-agnostic; resolved (by optional named qualifier) via autowiring; resource binding via a TransactionResources context value governed by context-propagation's task-scoped store (NOT thread_local), excluded-by-default from child-task propagation (the never-inheritable rule); propagation as interceptor logic over the context state: REQUIRED join-or-begin, REQUIRES_NEW suspend(save+unbind)/resume(rebind) in a sync-Drop-flag + explicit-async-rollback guard, NESTED savepoint, MANDATORY/NEVER/SUPPORTS/NOT_SUPPORTED guard checks; transaction-synchronization hooks (before/after commit/rollback) the interceptor drives, consumed by transactional-events

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The `#[transactional]` macro, TxAttribute, TransactionManager trait, TxDefinition/TxState, the interceptor, and the AdvisorDescriptor live in leaf-framework (the tx feature crate / kernel). The advisor descriptor is contributed into the auto-proxy-creator's collection stream via WHATEVER Phase-2 substrate that pipeline picks (linkme distributed-slice default / inventory / build-time codegen / explicit) — tx does NOT pick its own; it emits the same thin AdvisorDescriptor shape every advice crate emits. CRITICAL DCE hazard (cross-crate doc 149/152/345): if the tx feature crate is declared-but-never-path-referenced by the binary, its advisor descriptor silently never registers and @Transactional becomes a no-op with NO error — a correctness bug. This rides the SAME anti-DCE self-check the auto-proxy-creator mandates for its advisor stream (force-link the tx crate via the generated app-root / scan! list; a startup self-check asserting 'tx advisor present' / `leaf doctor` lists it). The TransactionManager is a separate contribution: a concrete native manager (in an integration crate like leaf-jdbc) registers as a bean; the interceptor resolves `dyn TransactionManager` by type (or by the @Transactional('name') qualifier via candidate-resolver). Generics-can't-register (153/349): a manager generic over a connection type needs a concrete per-instantiation registration shim. Ordering of the tx advisor RELATIVE to cache/validation/retry/async within the chain is encoded AS DATA (order:i32 + role-tier) on the descriptor, resolved by the auto-proxy-creator's sort — NEVER link order (154/355); the cache-vs-tx ordering correctness (lines 1770-1772) depends on this being data-driven and inspectable. The two-source duality (362): a static link-time tx manager is the default; a dynamic (WASM-module) TransactionManager registers via the dynamic source and the interceptor drives it identically (origin-agnostic) — though a WASM-backed manager's resource binding crossing the WIT boundary is a real marshalling question (the connection handle lives guest-side). The binary/app-root (260) is where the enabled tx capability + chosen manager become visible.

**T2 — execution context:** All tx work runs on the HOT per-call path inside the shared proxy chain (the interceptor is one hop). begin/commit/rollback are .await points — they MUST run on the same logical task as proceed() so the resource binding (task-scoped via context-propagation) is visible to nested data-access; the interceptor must NOT spawn proceed() onto a different task or the binding is lost (explicit execution-context discipline, charter §2.3). A blocking transaction manager (a sync JDBC driver) MUST be wrapped so begin/commit/rollback use spawn_blocking — the interceptor documents that the manager's async methods own that discipline; the interceptor itself never blocks the executor. CANCELLATION is the sharp hazard: if the wrapped future is dropped mid-transaction (cancelled at an .await inside proceed()), the transaction MUST roll back — but async Drop is unavailable (nightly doc 273-281), so the interceptor cannot rely on a future-cancelled async-rollback running. The rust-native answer: structure the interceptor so the transaction lifecycle is driven by EXPLICIT .await within a `select!`/scope it owns, and a sync-Drop guard flags an un-finalized transaction so that EITHER an explicit cleanup runs OR the dropped-mid-flight resource is reclaimed by the manager's connection-pool timeout/leak detection (a degraded-but-safe fallback) — this is a genuine Phase-2 cancellation-contract question shared with context-close. REQUIRES_NEW suspend/resume runs synchronously around the inner begin (save+unbind the outer binding, run inner, rebind) — the rebind must happen even on inner failure (finally-shaped). Errors propagate as the method's own Result: a rollback-matching Err triggers rollback then re-propagates with FULL causal context (which tx, which propagation, which rollback rule matched, the manager error) into the §2.9 chain; a commit failure surfaces as a distinct TxError. The transaction-synchronization hooks fire on the same task at commit/rollback boundaries (synchronous-by-default, preserving the caller's context — the transactional-events tie). No global lock; the binding is a per-task context value (Arc/stack, minimal heap); the manager is a shared `Arc<dyn TransactionManager>` read-only after refresh.

#### R2 — Transaction as a context-scope value with an explicit `transactional` combinator + injectable Tx handle (context-first; minimize the ambient resource lookup)
Question whether the transactional RESOURCE must be ambient at all. Spring binds the Connection to a ThreadLocal because threading it through every DAO method is viral (lines 2112); but leaf controls the data-access seam and is async-first, where state already rides futures. Model an active transaction as a first-class, context-scoped VALUE — a `Transaction` handle (an Arc-backed thing carrying the bound resource + the propagation state) — and make `#[transactional]` LOWER to an explicit combinator the macro emits: the method body is wrapped so it runs inside `tx_scope(def, manager, |tx| async { ...original body... }).await`, where `tx_scope` begins/joins per propagation, runs the body, and commits/rolls-back. The `Transaction` handle is published into the execution-context scope (the scopes/context-propagation substrate) for the body's duration so nested data-access can resolve it — BUT the primary, abuse-resistant path is that data-access primitives RECEIVE the Transaction (or a connection derived from it) as an injected/passed value, with ambient lookup as a bridge only where a param can't be threaded. The interceptor/advisor still EXISTS (so external callers get demarcation transparently via the auto-proxy-creator, preserving the declarative DX), but the macro ALSO offers the hand-writable combinator form — the macro is thin sugar over a public `tx_scope`/`Transaction` API (charter §2.10: nothing hidden in the macro; the declarative form and the explicit form are the same machinery). Propagation lives in `tx_scope`: REQUIRED inspects the context for a live Transaction and reuses it; REQUIRES_NEW opens a nested scope with a fresh Transaction, the outer one structurally suspended (it is simply not the innermost scope value); NESTED takes a savepoint on the resolved Transaction. The never-inheritable rule is automatic: the Transaction is a scope value bound to THIS task's scope; a spawned child task does NOT inherit it unless explicitly handed it.

**Pros:** Most abuse-resistant and most honest (charter §2.3): 'this code runs in a transaction' is visible as a scope/combinator, and the resource is a value the data-access layer can RECEIVE rather than fish out of an ambient holder — the never-inheritable rule becomes structural (a child task simply doesn't have the Transaction scope value) rather than a per-key exclusion policy that can be misconfigured.; Thin-macro / hand-writable parity (charter §2.10): because #[transactional] lowers to a public tx_scope combinator over a public Transaction handle, `cargo expand` shows ordinary code the user could have written, and the programmatic escape hatch (call tx_scope yourself, e.g. for dynamic propagation decisions) exists by construction — the strongest 'nothing hidden in the macro' story.; Reduced dependence on the ambient-context-across-.await keystone for CORRECTNESS: the Transaction rides the future as a scope value (like context-propagation's R2/R3), so it survives work-stealing migration without a per-poll thread-local reinstall — the propagation is value-passing, not ambient capture, on the primary path.; Cancellation is cleaner: tx_scope owns the begin/body/commit-or-rollback as explicit async within one lexical scope, so a body that errors or a future dropped at the body's await leads to a rollback driven by tx_scope's explicit control flow (still no async Drop, but the lifecycle is lexically bounded, not spread across an interceptor's separate proceed()).; Unifies with scopes/scoped-proxies: the Transaction-as-scope-value reuses the request/custom-scope machinery (a transaction is a short scope), and 'the current transaction' is just the innermost Transaction scope value — one scope mechanism, not a bespoke tx holder (charter §2.11).; REQUIRES_NEW / NESTED map naturally onto nested scopes / savepoints, and the inner-vs-outer suspension is just scope nesting (the outer Transaction is not the innermost value) — no explicit save+unbind+rebind dance.

**Cons:** Larger DIVERGENCE from the pure declarative intent: Spring's whole point is that @Transactional fires via the proxy with ZERO body change (lines 1697/1712); lowering to a body-wrapping combinator means the marked method's body is rewritten by the macro — which (a) reintroduces a 'self-invocation actually works for the combinator form but not the proxy form' inconsistency, and (b) if BOTH the advisor AND the combinator are active, risks DOUBLE demarcation (the proxy opens a tx AND tx_scope opens one) unless carefully reconciled.; Two coexisting demarcation paths (advisor-for-external-callers + combinator-in-body) is a coherence COST: the propagation logic must live in tx_scope and the advisor must DELEGATE to it (not re-implement), or they desync; and a developer must understand which path applies where — a new failure mode R1 doesn't have.; The data-access-receives-Transaction-as-value ideal collides with reality: most data-access libraries (and the AOP cluster — a @Cacheable or @Retryable method inside a tx) cannot thread a &Transaction param through user code, so the ambient bridge is STILL load-bearing for exactly the cross-cutting cases — R2 doesn't escape the ambient question, it just makes ambient the secondary path (and now must keep the value-path and bridge-path coherent, R3-of-context-propagation's exact tension).; Body-wrapping macro is LESS thin than R1's metadata-only macro: it must capture the body, generate the closure, and handle early returns / the `?` operator / error mapping inside the wrapper — more macro logic, more `cargo expand` surface, more edge cases (charter §2.10 prefers minimal macro logic).; Pre-judges the scope/ownership model (charter §3 keystone): 'Transaction as a scope value resolved via the scope SPI' assumes a particular scope/lifetime shape; presenting it as a realization biases that deferred decision and must stay a CANDIDATE.; Suspend/resume of an OUTER transaction for REQUIRES_NEW is clean as scope-nesting ONLY if the outer resource truly suspends (the DB connection isn't used by the inner) — but with a single physical connection, REQUIRES_NEW needs a SECOND connection from the pool, so the 'just nest the scope' picture hides a real resource-acquisition step the manager must perform.

**Mechanisms considered:** #[transactional] lowers to an explicit `tx_scope(def, manager, |tx| async {body}).await` combinator (thin sugar over a public API; the declarative and programmatic forms are the SAME machinery); Transaction as a first-class context-scope VALUE (Arc-backed, carries the bound resource + propagation state); 'the current transaction' = the innermost Transaction scope value; data-access RECEIVES the Transaction/connection as an injected/passed value on the primary path; ambient lookup as a bridge only where a param can't be threaded (AOP seams); the advisor still exists for transparent external-caller demarcation, but DELEGATES to tx_scope (single propagation implementation, no desync); propagation via scope nesting: REQUIRED reuse innermost, REQUIRES_NEW nested scope + fresh Transaction (outer structurally suspended), NESTED savepoint on the resolved Transaction; never-inheritable as structural: the Transaction is a scope value bound to this task's scope; a child task lacks it unless explicitly handed it; reuse scopes/scoped-proxies machinery — a transaction is a short scope, no bespoke holder

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same advisor-contribution story as R1 for the transparent-external-caller path (one Role::Infrastructure AdvisorDescriptor into the auto-proxy-creator stream, same DCE/force-link/self-check obligations). BUT R2 adds a public `tx_scope`/`Transaction` API in leaf-framework that the macro lowers to — so a chunk of the feature works with NO cross-crate registration at all (the combinator is a plain function call the macro emits inline), which is a RELIABILITY win: the in-body transactional behavior can't silently vanish to DCE because there's no link-time collection for it (only the transparent-proxy path rides the advisor stream). The TransactionManager contribution is identical to R1 (a bean, resolved by type/qualifier, origin-agnostic incl. WASM). The scope binding couples to the scope/registry substrate (charter §3) — 'how is the Transaction scope value bound and resolved' is a scopes-neighbour question, more entangled than R1's context-value approach. Generics/ordering/two-source-duality as R1. The key T1 nuance: because the macro rewrites the body, the macro must emit ABSOLUTE paths to tx_scope/Transaction (proc-macro isolation, 353) and the user must be able to write the combinator by hand — the macro is thin sugar, so the hand-written form is the cross-crate-stable surface.

**T2 — execution context:** tx_scope owns the full lifecycle on ONE logical task within ONE lexical async scope: begin/join -> run body -> commit | rollback, all explicit .await, all on the caller's task (so the scope-bound Transaction is visible to nested data-access on the same task). The Transaction rides the future as a scope value, so work-stealing migration across the body's awaits is automatically correct (no per-poll reinstall — value, not ambient). CANCELLATION: if the body's future is dropped (cancelled at an await), tx_scope's control flow is interrupted — and because async Drop is unavailable (273-281), the same hazard as R1 exists, BUT lexically bounded: tx_scope can wrap the body in a structure that, on the cancelled/error path, drives an explicit async rollback IF it gets a chance to run, with a sync-Drop guard + pool-timeout fallback for the truly-dropped case (same Phase-2 cancellation-contract question). The advisor path delegates to tx_scope, so it shares the identical execution context. REQUIRES_NEW acquires a SECOND resource from the manager (a real .await — pool acquisition) before nesting; the outer Transaction's resource is held (not used) during the inner scope and resumed after — the manager must support holding multiple resources per task. Errors propagate as the method's Result with full §2.9 context; synchronization hooks fire at commit/rollback on the same task (transactional-events tie). Send/Sync: the Transaction handle must be Send (it rides a spawnable future) — a !Send connection cannot be the bound resource on a multi-worker runtime, a compile-time-visible constraint (charter §2.3). No global lock; Transaction is per-scope Arc, minimal heap.

#### R3 — Compile-checked transactional graph: macro-resolved manager + a build-time/refresh-time demarcation plan, with a typed Tx effect on the data-access boundary
Push correctness as early as the richest diagnostic allows (charter §2.9). Two coordinated moves: (A) the manager-selection and rollback-rule resolution that R1 does at runtime is RESOLVED at build-time/refresh-start into a `TxPlan` — a checked artifact mapping each `#[transactional]` method to its resolved manager (by qualifier), effective TxDefinition, and rollback ErrorMatch set — so 'no TransactionManager bean satisfies @Transactional("txMgr2") on method X' is a BUILD/refresh error (the earliest legible point), not a runtime surprise. The interceptor then does an O(1) plan lookup, not per-call attribute parsing + manager resolution. (B) Make the data-access BOUNDARY type-aware: the transactional resource carries a typed marker (a `Tx` capability / effect parameter) so that data-access methods requiring an active transaction take `&Tx<'_>` (or resolve it from a typed context), turning 'called a @Transactional-requiring repository method outside any transaction' into a TYPE error rather than a runtime MANDATORY-propagation failure where statically provable. Where the type can't prove it (dynamic propagation, the AOP cluster, external entry), it FALLS BACK to R1's runtime ambient binding + interceptor — so R3 is R1 with a compile/refresh-time front-end and an optional typed effect, not a replacement. The `#[transactional]` macro emits the same const TxAttribute data (so the auto-proxy-creator matches identically) PLUS feeds the TxPlan generator; the interceptor consumes the plan. Propagation/never-inheritable/synchronization semantics are inherited from R1.

**Pros:** Strongest diagnostics (charter §2.9): manager-mismatch, unknown-qualifier, and (where statically provable) tx-required-but-no-tx become BUILD-time or refresh-time errors with full context (which method, which qualifier, which managers were considered) — the earliest legible failure, far better than a runtime 'no transaction manager' or silent no-op.; Lowest steady-state overhead (charter §2 low-overhead #2): the per-call interceptor work is an O(1) TxPlan lookup (resolved manager + effective definition + rollback set already computed), not per-call attribute reparse + manager resolution by qualifier — the cheapest hot path of the three.; The typed-Tx-effect (B) makes a class of transaction-misuse bugs UNREPRESENTABLE on the statically-provable paths — e.g. a repository method that mutates state requiring `&Tx` cannot be called without one, catching 'forgot @Transactional' at compile time, which Spring cannot do at all. A genuine rust-native improvement over the parity target.; Reproducibility/CI win (cross-crate doc 228/235, sqlx model): a checked-in `--check`-able TxPlan turns 'silently lost the tx advisor / wrong manager wired' into a CI failure — directly attacking the silent-DCE failure mode for the most safety-critical cross-cutting concern.; Plan-as-artifact is inspectable: 'which manager, which propagation, which rollback rules for method X' is answerable by reading the plan (charter §2.9), and the tx advisor's position in the shared chain (vs cache) can be surfaced alongside.; Best fit for the AOT/portable path (Architecture C): the TxPlan is generated code, platform-independent incl. wasm, avoiding the manager-resolution DCE hazard (the plan references the managers explicitly, force-linking them).

**Cons:** Build-time manager resolution pre-judges the metadata/codegen boundary AND the compile-vs-runtime split (charter §3, two keystones) HARD — it presumes managers are knowable at build/refresh-start, which conflicts with RUNTIME conditions/profiles (a @ConditionalOnProperty manager, a profile-selected manager): the plan must either be conditional (plan-per-profile, explosive) or fall back to runtime resolution, eroding the benefit. Must stay strictly a CANDIDATE.; The typed-Tx-effect (B) fights the declarative, non-invasive intent (lines 1697/1729): Spring's @Transactional explicitly does NOT change the method signature (that's the point of the proxy). Threading `&Tx` is viral and intrusive — exactly the API pollution the holder pattern was invented to avoid (lines 2112) — so (B) is attractive for safety but cuts against DX #1 for deep call graphs, and only works where propagation is statically known (REQUIRED-joining-an-ambient-tx is fundamentally dynamic).; Most complex of the three: a build/refresh-time plan generator + a runtime fallback + an optional type-level effect = three sub-mechanisms to keep coherent, the highest design surface (charter §2.11 prefers shared machinery; this adds a bespoke plan layer on top of R1).; Cross-crate plan generation must see, across crates, both the #[transactional] methods AND the available TransactionManager beans AND the qualifiers — leaf-codegen in the binary can do this via cargo-metadata/manifest convention (228/317) but it deepens coupling to the cross-crate composition model, and managers contributed by sibling crates must surface to the planner.; Dynamic-source (WASM-module) managers discovered after the plan freezes can't be plan-resolved — a late manager either isn't selectable by plan or forces a re-plan, so R3 favors the static manager set and degrades on the dynamic half of the two-source duality (worse than R1 here).; Inherits ALL of R1's runtime hazards (no async Drop for cancellation-rollback, self-invocation, ambient-resource-across-.await dependence) on the fallback path — so the hard problems are not solved, only front-loaded with diagnostics for the static subset.

**Mechanisms considered:** build-time/refresh-time TxPlan: resolve per-method manager (by qualifier) + effective TxDefinition + rollback ErrorMatch set into a checked, --check-able artifact (sqlx model) or in-memory refresh-start plan; interceptor reduced to O(1) plan lookup (manager + definition + rollback set already resolved); same advisor contribution into the auto-proxy-creator stream; typed Tx capability/effect on the data-access boundary (&Tx<'_> or typed-context resolution) — tx-required-but-absent becomes a TYPE error where statically provable; runtime fallback to R1 (ambient binding + per-call resolution) for dynamic propagation / AOP-cluster / external-entry / conditional managers; #[transactional] macro emits the same const TxAttribute (auto-proxy-creator matches identically) AND feeds the TxPlan generator; propagation/never-inheritable/synchronization-hooks/resource-binding inherited from R1 (R3 is R1 + compile/refresh front-end + optional effect)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Strongest fit for Architecture C (build-time codegen, 305-317): the TxPlan is a generated artifact, platform-independent, and a missing tx advisor / mis-wired manager manifests as a MISSING or INVALID PLAN ENTRY that a checked-in --check-able plan turns into a loud CI failure rather than a silent runtime no-op — the best answer to the #1 silent-DCE failure mode for the most safety-critical feature. The advisor descriptor still rides the auto-proxy-creator's collection substrate (same DCE/force-link obligations), but the plan REFERENCES the resolved managers explicitly, force-linking them (sidestepping the manager-resolution DCE). CRITICAL T1 tension: the planner must see across crates the #[transactional] methods, the TransactionManager beans, AND the qualifiers — leaf-codegen in the binary crate walks DECLARED inputs via cargo-metadata/manifest (228/317), but sibling-crate managers must surface their descriptors to the planner, coupling plan generation to the cross-crate composition model and the binary/app-root visibility (260). Conditional/profile-selected managers are the hard boundary (209: link/build-time answers 'what could', runtime answers 'what does') — the plan straddles it and must reconcile with a runtime fallback. Generics-can't-register applies to managers AND to the typed Tx effect's monomorphizations (153/349). Two-source duality: static managers fully plannable; a dynamic WASM-module manager is unplannable and needs the R1 runtime path (R3 favors static, degrades on dynamic). The typed-Tx-effect (B) is purely type-level/per-crate — no registration, no DCE — a reliability win for that sub-part.

**T2 — execution context:** Plan generation is COLD-PATH: at BUILD time (Architecture C — fails before the program runs, the richest-earliest diagnostic) or ONCE at refresh-start (after manager beans + tx attributes are known, before eager singletons), single-conceptual-thread, no .await (it consumes const TxAttributes + manager descriptors, produces a plan structure). The per-call interceptor is an O(1) plan lookup then the SAME begin/proceed/commit-or-rollback as R1 — so the HOT-path execution context, cancellation hazard (no async Drop → explicit rollback + sync-Drop-flag + pool-timeout fallback), resource-binding-on-the-same-task discipline, and synchronization-hook firing are IDENTICAL to R1 on the runtime path. The typed-Tx-effect (B) is compile-time only — it changes NO runtime execution context, just makes some misuse a type error, so it adds zero runtime cost and zero cancellation surface. The runtime fallback (dynamic propagation / conditional managers) runs on the refresh/creation or per-call context exactly as R1, and a 'plan said manager M, runtime condition selected M2' divergence must carry rich §2.9 context. Errors at plan-build carry full context (method, qualifier, candidate managers, why-no-match) at the earliest-legible point. Cancellation of refresh aborts plan build cleanly (all-or-nothing per refresh-lifecycle). No global lock; plan is read-only after build/refresh, interceptor reads it via shared Arc.

### Rust clashes
- NO ThreadLocal for resource binding — it is ACTIVELY WRONG across .await, not merely 'breaks at hops'. Spring's TransactionSynchronizationManager binds the Connection to a NamedThreadLocal (lines 2114-2118) because classic JDBC work is pinned to one thread. In async Rust a task migrates workers at EVERY .await under work-stealing, so a thread_local read after an await inside a transactional method may return a DIFFERENT worker's binding (or none). The transactional resource belongs to the LOGICAL TASK (the future), not the OS thread — so the entire ThreadLocal storage strategy behind TransactionSynchronizationManager must NOT be ported; the binding must ride leaf's (deferred) task-scoped context-propagation substrate. This is the charter §3 keystone clash, inherited directly.
- No async Drop on stable — so commit/rollback-on-scope-exit and the REQUIRES_NEW suspend/resume cannot ride a destructor. Spring's JVM finalizes transactions via try/finally in the interceptor, and a GC'd Connection is eventually reclaimed. In Rust the natural shape — an RAII tx guard whose Drop commits-or-rolls-back — is IMPOSSIBLE for the async commit/rollback (nightly doc 273-281: AsyncDrop crashes tokio shutdown, no stable path). The interceptor must drive commit/rollback as EXPLICIT .await in its body, and the cancellation case (future dropped mid-transaction) cannot run an async rollback in Drop — leaving only a sync-Drop flag + connection-pool-timeout/leak-detection fallback for the truly-cancelled path. A genuine correctness constraint with no JVM analogue.
- No runtime reflection to discover @Transactional or read its attributes. Spring's TransactionInterceptor reflectively reads @Transactional off the invoked java.lang.reflect.Method at call time, and the advisor's pointcut matches by reflective annotation presence (line 1705/1712). Rust has neither — so @Transactional presence and its attributes (propagation/isolation/rollback rules) must be captured AS DATA at macro-expansion time (a const TxAttribute) and the auto-proxy-creator must match over DECLARED method metadata, not a reflective walk. The matchable surface is fixed at macro time, not reflectively open, and method identity must be stable-as-data or method-keyed matching mis-fires across refactors.
- No runtime classpath scan for the advisor — and a silent-DCE hazard if the tx crate is unreferenced. Spring's @EnableTransactionManagement selector registers the infra advisor into a running container that scans the classpath; ProxyTransactionManagementConfiguration is found because spring-tx is on the classpath. Rust has no classpath: the tx advisor descriptor must be collected via the auto-proxy-creator's link-time/codegen/explicit substrate, and a tx feature crate the binary declares-but-never-references contributes NOTHING — @Transactional silently becomes a no-op with NO error (cross-crate doc 149/152). This is the worst failure mode for a SAFETY-critical feature (a transaction silently not happening = data corruption) and demands the anti-DCE self-check + force-link discipline be non-optional for the tx advisor and the manager.
- Self-invocation bypasses the proxy — inherited from proxy-based AOP (lines 1714-1729). A `this.inner()` call to another @Transactional method inside the same bean goes to the raw target, so inner()'s demarcation silently does not run. Spring documents this as the honest boundary of the wrapper model; leaf inherits it UNLESS the (deferred) aop-proxying proxy mechanism is per-call rather than per-bean-reference. There is no clean Rust escape at this layer — it must be loudly documented and diagnosed, and the sanctioned workarounds (refactor to a collaborator, self-inject the proxy) carried over. Rust's ownership may even make self-injection of one's own proxy harder than in the GC'd JVM.
- Send/'static bounds on the bound resource fracture 'bind any Connection'. Spring binds any Object as a transactional resource freely. In async Rust the transactional resource (Connection/Session) rides a spawnable, work-stealing future, so it must be Send + 'static (and the Transaction handle Send) — a !Send connection (some embedded/single-thread driver) CANNOT be the bound resource on a multi-worker runtime, a compile-time wall Spring has none of. This makes 'which connections are even usable transactionally' a type-level question, and ties to context-propagation's identical Send-bounds-vs-swap-by-config tension.
- Reactive vs imperative transaction managers — the duality is sharper in Rust. Spring has TWO managers (PlatformTransactionManager over ThreadLocal, ReactiveTransactionManager over Reactor Context, 6.1+) because its imperative and reactive worlds are separate. leaf is async-FIRST with no sync/async bimodality (charter §2.3), so there is ONE async TransactionManager trait — but the binding still must be context-not-thread scoped (the ReactiveTransactionManager's reason for existing: 'Reactor uses Context, not ThreadLocal', line 1893). leaf collapses Spring's two managers into one async-first manager whose resource binding rides the task-scoped context — a unification Spring couldn't make, but it forces the context-propagation primitive to exist first.
- Rollback-rule matching on error TYPES, not exception class hierarchy. Spring's rollback rules match thrown exception classes against a hierarchy (rollbackFor/noRollbackFor). Rust errors are values returned in Result, often enums, with no class hierarchy and no unwinding-based 'this threw X'. So rollback rules must match over the returned Err VALUE — by type (downcast/TypeId) or by a user-provided predicate — a different mechanism (data-driven ErrorMatch over the Result, not a catch-by-type), and the 'unchecked exceptions roll back, checked commit' default has no Rust analogue (all errors are values), so leaf must define a sensible default (e.g. any Err rolls back unless no_rollback_on matches) rather than port the checked/unchecked distinction.

### Interactions
- **auto-proxy-creator** — DIRECT upstream — the decision/collection layer this feature contributes ONE advisor into. transaction-management emits a single AdvisorDescriptor { role: Role::Infrastructure, order, pointcut=method-carries-TxAttribute, make_interceptor=builds TransactionInterceptor } into the auto-proxy-creator's collection stream; the auto-proxy-creator MATCHES it against @Transactional methods, computes the UNION with other advisors, ORDERS it by role-tier+order, and installs the one chain. This feature does NOT collect/match/order — it only contributes and supplies the tx-specific interceptor behavior. The infra-vs-aspect role isolation that keeps tx advice from being matched by application aspects is the auto-proxy-creator's role-filter consuming this descriptor's Role::Infrastructure.
- **aop-proxying** — DIRECT substrate (two levels up): aop-proxying owns the proxy wrap-and-swap and the AdviceChain call-routing/proceed primitive the TransactionInterceptor rides. The interceptor calls proceed().await to run the rest of the chain (and ultimately the real method) between begin and commit. The self-invocation limitation (a this.inner() call bypassing the proxy, so inner's @Transactional silently does not run) is aop-proxying's wrapper-boundary property that transaction-management inherits and must document — it cannot fix it at this layer.
- **context-propagation** — DEEPEST tie and shared keystone. The transactional resource binding (the same Connection reused by nested data-access) is ambient per-execution state that must ride context-propagation's task-scoped store, NOT a thread_local (wrong across .await). The never-inheritable rule (lines 2114-2118: a transaction must live and die on one execution; sharing its connection with a child task shatters isolation) is realized as context-propagation's EXCLUDE-BY-DEFAULT per-key policy for the tx-resource key — its named example of inverted-default propagation. This feature's correctness is GATED on context-propagation's deferred ambient-context-across-.await primitive.
- **locale-context** — Sibling ambient-holder family. Spring's TransactionSynchronizationManager IS one of the named holders locale-context abstracts (current locale, request attributes, transaction sync). The tx-resource binding and the active-transaction state ('is a transaction active', isolation, read-only, name) are the per-execution 'current X' lookups locale-context defines the holder shape for; transaction-management is a concrete, correctness-critical CONSUMER of that holder mechanism, with the strictest rule (never inheritable).
- **caching** — The canonical co-located infrastructure advisor and the textbook ORDERING-correctness case (lines 1770-1772). A method that is both @Transactional and @Cacheable gets ONE proxy whose chain contains both interceptors; whether cache sits OUTSIDE or INSIDE the transaction is a subtle, invisible-in-object-identity correctness bug. transaction-management's order:i32 + role-tier on its advisor descriptor, resolved by the auto-proxy-creator's sort, decides this — and the assembled order must be inspectable (charter §2.9). The two share the role-isolation and single-chain machinery.
- **transactional-events** — DIRECT downstream built ON this feature's synchronization hooks. @TransactionalEventListener defers @EventListener invocation to a transaction phase (before/after commit/rollback/completion) by binding to the current transaction's synchronizations (line 1893); if no transaction is active the listener is skipped (fallbackExecution). transaction-management must expose the transaction-synchronization registration + the phase callbacks (fired by the interceptor/manager at commit/rollback boundaries on the same task) that transactional-events consumes — the explicit illustration that the event model is built upon tx.
- **validation** — Sibling Role::Infrastructure advisor in the shared chain. A method that is @Transactional and @Validated gets both interceptors; validation typically must run OUTSIDE the transaction (reject bad args before opening a tx), so the deterministic ordering (validation before tx) is a correctness concern the auto-proxy-creator's sort decides using both advisors' order data. Same role-isolation, same single-chain co-residence as caching.
- **retry-resilience** — Sibling Role::Infrastructure advisor with a sharp ordering interaction.
