Whether retry sits OUTSIDE the transaction (each attempt is a fresh tx — usually correct) or INSIDE (retry within one tx — usually wrong, the failed tx is already poisoned) is a correctness decision driven by the relative order of the retry and tx advisors in the shared chain. transaction-management co-resides with retry-resilience in the chain; their ordering data must position retry outside tx by default.
- **async-execution** — Sibling Role::Infrastructure advisor AND a context-propagation hazard. @Async dispatches proceed() onto a TaskExecutor — a thread/task hop. If a @Transactional @Async method (or an @Async call inside a transaction) hops to another task, the tx-resource binding is on the ORIGINATING task and (correctly, per never-inheritable) does NOT follow — so the async work runs WITHOUT the transaction unless a NEW one is begun there. This is the never-inheritable rule meeting the async hop: transaction-management relies on context-propagation excluding the tx resource from the async hop, and async-execution must not silently carry it.
- **exception-translation** — Sibling advisor on the same data-access beans, co-resident in the shared chain. exception-translation converts vendor exceptions to a neutral hierarchy on @Repository beans; its ordering relative to tx matters (translation typically wraps the tx so a tx commit/rollback failure is also translated). Both are leaf's own dogfooded infrastructure advisors using the auto-proxy-creator's collect/match/order pipeline — exception-translation and tx are the canonical pair Spring cites as composing on one proxy.
- **autowiring-resolution** — The interceptor RESOLVES the TransactionManager bean (by type, or by the @Transactional('name') qualifier) via autowiring-resolution + candidate-resolver. The multi-manager case (different methods using different named managers) drives the qualifier-disambiguation path; the fail-fast 'no/ambiguous TransactionManager' diagnostic is autowiring-resolution's, surfaced at make_interceptor time (R1) or plan-build time (R3).
- **scopes** — Provides the lifetime anchor for the transaction-as-scope-value (R2) and frames the connection-per-transaction concurrency model. A transaction is a short, per-execution scope; in R2 the Transaction IS a scope value resolved via the scope SPI. concurrency-contract's 'scope choice is the primary concurrency lever' applies — a stateful transactional resource is the opposite of a stateless singleton, lives only for the transaction's duration, and must not be shared across tasks.
- **context-close** — Shutdown-ordering and cancellation-contract tie. Open transactions at context close must be resolved (rolled back) before the connection pool / TransactionManager beans are destroyed; the manager is a Lifecycle/closeable resource quiesced before singleton destruction. The no-async-Drop constraint means tx finalization on shutdown/cancellation needs explicit container-driven teardown (charter), shared with context-close's reverse-order destruction and the cancellation-rollback question.
- **bean-instantiation** — Installs the proxy at after_init (the interceptor woven by the auto-proxy-creator) so every injected reference to a @Transactional bean is the proxy, and the raw target is never handed out raw (the allowRawInjectionDespiteWrapping concern, line 2537: a stale un-proxied reference would bypass @Transactional advice). bean-instantiation owns publishing the proxy as the bean's identity inside the per-bean creation guard; transaction-management only contributes the advice.
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Drives WHEN the tx advisor is collected and (R3) WHEN the TxPlan is built — at a fixed refresh point after the advisor descriptors and TransactionManager beans are available, before eager singletons. The all-or-nothing refresh guarantee means a tx-wiring failure (no manager, mis-qualified) aborts refresh cleanly with full §2.9 context rather than a runtime surprise.
- **environment** — Source of the tx capability enablement (the @EnableTransactionManagement analogue) and per-environment manager/propagation defaults. Whether transaction management is enabled, the default manager, and any tx-related properties are bound from the Environment; conditions over the Environment gate whether the tx advisor and a given manager wire up (a @ConditionalOnProperty manager).

### Open questions for Phase 2
- What is the rust-native primitive for binding the transactional resource to the execution context across .await? This IS the context-propagation keystone (charter §3) viewed through transactions: (a) an ambient task-scoped store the nested data-access looks up (R1, faithful to TransactionSynchronizationManager), (b) the Transaction as a context-scope VALUE that rides the future and is received by data-access (R2, abuse-resistant), or (c) explicit Tx threading (R3-B, compile-checked). This MUST be co-decided with context-propagation, locale-context, and the async-execution model — transaction-management cannot pick it, and its correctness is gated on it.
- How is transaction finalization handled under cancellation, given NO async Drop on stable? If a future is dropped mid-transaction, the transaction MUST roll back, but an RAII async-rollback-in-Drop is impossible (nightly doc 273-281). Candidates: explicit interceptor-driven rollback on the error/early-return path + a sync-Drop flag + connection-pool-timeout/leak-detection for the truly-cancelled-at-await case; or a structured-concurrency scope that guarantees finalization. This is a cross-cutting cancellation-contract question shared with context-close, task-execution, and retry-resilience — needs ONE unified answer for all resource-holding interceptors, not a per-feature hack.
- Where does the @Transactional demarcation decision live and is it per-bean-global or per-edge? Spring decides once at after_init (every external caller of the proxy gets demarcation; self-invocation bypasses it). If aop-proxying's proxy mechanism is per-injection-edge, self-invocation could be fixed but per-edge consistency becomes the issue. transaction-management inherits whatever aop-proxying decides — but the self-invocation footgun's severity (silent missing transactions) makes this choice more consequential here than for cache/validation.
- How are rollback rules expressed and matched over Rust Result values (no exception hierarchy)? Spring matches thrown exception CLASSES (rollbackFor/noRollbackFor over a hierarchy); Rust returns Err VALUES with no unwinding-based type-catch and no checked/unchecked distinction. Candidates: match by error TypeId/downcast, a user predicate, or a trait the error implements; and what is the sensible DEFAULT (any Err rolls back unless no_rollback_on matches?). Cross-cuts the unified error/diagnostics model (charter §3) — rollback matching should reuse whatever error-matching vocabulary that model defines.
- Does leaf collapse Spring's PlatformTransactionManager + ReactiveTransactionManager into ONE async-first TransactionManager trait, and what is the manager <-> resource-binding protocol? Async-first + no bimodality (charter §2.3) suggests one trait, but the manager must coordinate with the context-binding primitive (who binds the resource — the manager in begin(), or the interceptor?), suspend/resume for REQUIRES_NEW, savepoints for NESTED, and the synchronization-hook firing. This protocol shape is entangled with the context-propagation decision and the scope/ownership model.
- Is manager-selection + rollback-rule resolution done at runtime (R1, condition/profile-friendly) or precomputed into a build/refresh-time TxPlan (R3, earliest-diagnostic but conflicts with runtime-conditional managers)? This cross-cuts the metadata/codegen boundary AND the compile-vs-runtime split (charter §3). A build-time plan gives the richest-earliest diagnostic for mis-wired managers but cannot resolve @ConditionalOnProperty/profile-selected managers without explosion or a runtime fallback — the same plan-vs-condition reconciliation the auto-proxy-creator faces.
- Should leaf offer a typed Tx effect/capability on the data-access boundary (R3-B) to make 'tx-required-but-absent' a compile error where statically provable, despite it cutting against the non-invasive declarative intent (signatures shouldn't change, lines 1697/2112)? It catches a class of bugs Spring cannot, but is viral and only works where propagation is static (REQUIRED-joining-ambient is inherently dynamic). The explicit-vs-ambient line here mirrors context-propagation's R3 question and must be decided consistently across both.
- How does a WASM-module-backed TransactionManager (the dynamic two-source-duality half) marshal its resource binding across the WIT boundary? The connection/session handle lives guest-side; the host interceptor drives begin/commit/rollback over the boundary, but the bound resource that nested data-access reuses is guest-owned — how is 'reuse the same connection' expressed when the connection is a WASM resource handle, and can nested host-side data-access even share it? Binds to the origin-agnostic provider seam and the context-binding primitive.
- What is the Send/Sync bound policy for transactional resources, and does it gate runtime choice? A multi-worker runtime forces the bound Connection + Transaction handle to be Send (+ likely Sync); a !Send single-thread driver cannot be used transactionally there. Is this a hard mandate (all tx resources Send+'static) or is there a current-thread/single-worker escape (matching context-propagation's and task-execution's identical Send-bounds-vs-swap-by-config question)? Must be one coherent answer across the async substrate.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. The single most important intent: @Transactional is INERT metadata; the @Enable* selector wires an infra advisor + interceptor + auto-proxy-creator; the interceptor opens/commits/rolls-back AROUND the call, driving a vendor-neutral manager and binding the resource to the execution context which nested data-access reuses — and that binding is NEVER inheritable (correctness over convenience).\n\nI carefully kept this feature in its lane per the catalog and the rich upstream designs handed to me: auto-proxy-creator owns COLLECT/MATCH/ORDER/role-isolation (tx is its explicitly-named Role::Infrastructure example interaction — I CONTRIBUTE one advisor, I do not run the pipeline); aop-proxying owns the proxy/wrap/route substrate (deferred mechanism — I drive proceed()); context-propagation owns the ambient-resource-across-.await primitive (deferred keystone — I bind the tx resource through it, with the never-inheritable exclude-by-default policy as my named case). I did NOT re-decide any of those. R1 is the parity baseline, R2 the rust-native abuse-resistant inversion, R3 the diagnostics/safety-maximizing front-end; all three SHARE the TransactionManager trait, the propagation/rollback/synchronization semantics, the single-advisor contribution, and the no-async-Drop cancellation problem — so they are composable layers, not exclusive, which I left for Phase 2/3 to merge.\n\nThings I deliberately did NOT decide (held as phase2Questions): the resource-binding-across-.await primitive (the context-propagation keystone); the cancellation-rollback contract under no-async-Drop; per-bean-global vs per-edge demarcation (and thus the self-invocation footgun's fate); rollback-rule matching over Result values + the sensible default; one-vs-two managers + the manager/binding protocol; runtime-vs-build-time manager resolution; the typed-Tx-effect question; WASM-manager resource marshalling; and the Send/Sync policy for tx resources. Every realization keeps the TransactionManager origin-agnostic (native/test-double/WASM implement the same dyn trait), keeps @Transactional attributes as pure DATA (no reflection), contributes ONE Role::Infrastructure advisor with ordering AS DATA (never link order), realizes never-inheritable as an exclude-by-default context policy (honest to lines 2114-2118), and inherits the anti-DCE self-check mandate (a silently-missing tx advisor = silently-missing transactions = data corruption, the worst failure mode). ## Caching Abstraction (@Cacheable)  `[caching]`

Caching is, alongside transaction-management, the canonical DECLARATIVE CROSS-CUTTING CONCERN: a Role::Infrastructure advisor whose interceptor (Spring's `CacheInterceptor`) is woven by the auto-proxy-creator into the shared per-bean interceptor chain (Spring lines 1635/1705/1712/1772). Spring's load-bearing intent, grounded:

(1) INERT-MARKER + WIRING + BEHAVIOUR split (line 1712): `@Cacheable`/`@CacheEvict`/`@CachePut` (and `@Caching`/`@CacheConfig`) are by themselves *inert metadata*. `@EnableCaching` is the wiring — it registers the auto-proxy-creator (the InfrastructureAdvisorAutoProxyCreator family) PLUS caching's `Advisor` and its `CacheInterceptor`. The interceptor is the behaviour: it consults the cache before `proceed()` and populates it after. So caching contributes ONE advisor descriptor + ONE interceptor into the stream the auto-proxy-creator collects (the upstream design's primary consumer relationship); caching does NOT own proxy creation, matching, union, or ordering — that is auto-proxy-creator's job.

(2) THE CACHE-INTERCEPTOR SEMANTICS (the part caching genuinely OWNS, around `proceed()`): `@Cacheable` = look up a key in a Cache; on HIT short-circuit `proceed()` and return the cached value; on MISS call `proceed()`, store the result, return it. `@CachePut` = always `proceed()`, then store (refresh). `@CacheEvict` = `proceed()`, then evict one key (or `allEntries`); `beforeInvocation` flips evict to before `proceed()`. Each operation carries: cacheNames, a KEY (default = a KeyGenerator over method args; or a `key` expression), an optional CONDITION (skip caching unless true) and `unless` (skip storing based on result), a CacheManager/CacheResolver selecting the backing Cache(s), and a `sync` flag (single-flight: only one concurrent miss computes, others wait).

(3) ABSTRACTION SHAPE: `CacheManager` -> named `Cache` (get/put/evict/clear), a `KeyGenerator`, a `CacheResolver`, and a `CacheErrorHandler`. The cache is a pluggable backing store (in-memory/Caffeine/Redis); the abstraction is the contract, backends attach behind it (charter "pluggable everything-else"). Spring composes caching with transactions in ONE chain where ordering is correctness-critical and invisible in object identity (line 1772): cache-outside-vs-inside-tx is the canonical subtle bug — auto-proxy-creator owns the ORDER, caching owns being a well-behaved orderable participant.

For leaf the intent splits into: (a) contribute caching as ONE origin-agnostic Role::Infrastructure advisor + interceptor into auto-proxy-creator's collection (concretely building on the upstream auto-proxy-creator design — I do NOT re-decide proxy/chain mechanism); (b) realize the around-`proceed()` cache protocol ASYNC-FIRST (an async miss-compute is the norm, not Spring's sync `Object proceed()`), with single-flight (`sync`) as a reactive (waker-driven, not polling) hot-path concern; (c) realize key generation / condition / unless as a rust-native expression over typed method args (delegating to the expression-language neighbour's EvalContext shape, NOT a string interpreter by default); (d) make `CacheManager`/`Cache` an origin-agnostic provider so an in-memory default, a Caffeine-class crate, or a dynamic/WASM-sourced cache slot in identically. The clash to surface: Spring's cache key is an erased `Object` over reflectively-read args and the cached value is an erased `Object`; Rust has typed args and a typed return per method, so the key is a typed-hashable and the value store is heterogeneous-by-method — the erasure Spring needs largely dissolves, but a SHARED multi-method `Cache` store must reconcile typed values behind one named cache.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Caching as one Role::Infrastructure advisor + async CacheInterceptor over a CacheManager service (the direct CacheInterceptor analogue)
Realize caching as the literal `CacheInterceptor` analogue: ONE `AdvisorDescriptor { id, order:i32, role: Role::Infrastructure, pointcut, make_interceptor }` contributed into the auto-proxy-creator's collection stream (building directly on its R1/R2/R3 collection contract — caching is advice-agnostic to it). The pointcut MATCHES methods carrying caching metadata; the `make_interceptor(&Container)` builds a `CacheInterceptor` resolving a `CacheManager` bean from the container. Per advised call the interceptor implements the around-`proceed()` protocol ASYNC-FIRST: `async fn around(&self, jp: &mut JoinPoint, op: &CacheOpMeta) -> Result<Value, CacheError>`. For `@Cacheable`: build the key (via a `KeyGenerator`/expr closure over the typed JoinPoint args), evaluate `condition`; `cache.get(key).await` — on HIT short-circuit (do NOT call `jp.proceed()`); on MISS `jp.proceed().await`, evaluate `unless` on the result, `cache.put(key, v).await`, return. `@CachePut` always proceeds then puts; `@CacheEvict` evicts before/after per `beforeInvocation`. `sync=true` is single-flight via a per-key in-flight map of shared futures (waker-driven: concurrent callers `.await` the SAME computation, never poll). Caching's OWN machinery is a set of leaf-core beans: `trait CacheManager { fn cache(&self, name: &str) -> Option<Arc<dyn Cache>>; }`, `trait Cache { async fn get(&self,k:&CacheKey)->Result<Option<StoredValue>,CacheError>; async fn put(...); async fn evict(...); async fn clear(...); }`, a `KeyGenerator`, a `CacheResolver`, a `CacheErrorHandler`. The metadata (`CacheOpMeta`: op kind, cache names, key-expr-closure, condition-closure, unless-closure, sync flag) is emitted as DATA by the thin `#[cacheable]`/`#[cache_evict]`/`#[cache_put]` attribute macros (invariant 10 — macros only emit the descriptor + the typed key/condition closures; heavy lowering in leaf-codegen). `@EnableCaching` analogue = a `leaf::enable_caching!()`/feature that (i) ensures caching's advisor descriptor is collected and (ii) ensures the auto-proxy-creator's CreatorPolicy considers infrastructure advisors — the inert-marker-to-behaviour wiring. The StoredValue erasure problem (one named Cache holding values of different method-return types) is solved by keying the store on (method-id, key) and storing a typed boxed value (`Box<dyn Any + Send + Sync>`) downcast at read by the call site that knows its return type — or, per-method sub-caches behind one cache name.

**Pros:** Sharpest intent parity (line 1712): caching IS one infrastructure advisor + one interceptor contributed into the shared chain, exactly Spring's CacheInterceptor; the inert-marker/@Enable-wiring/interceptor-behaviour triad maps 1:1, and the cache-outside-vs-inside-tx ordering is decided by auto-proxy-creator's role-tier+order sort with caching simply carrying an order:i32 — the canonical correctness concern (line 1772) is honored without caching re-implementing chain assembly.; Clean separation of concerns: auto-proxy-creator owns collect/match/union/order; aop-proxying owns the JoinPoint/proceed primitive and the short-circuit (cache HIT = not calling proceed) is a substrate capability caching merely uses; caching owns ONLY the around-proceed cache protocol + the CacheManager/Cache/KeyGenerator/CacheResolver/CacheErrorHandler abstraction. Each is independently testable — the protocol is a pure async fn over (JoinPoint, CacheOpMeta, CacheManager).; Async-first (invariant 3) by construction: miss-compute is `jp.proceed().await` and `cache.get/put` are async (a Redis-class backend awaits I/O on its own execution context); `sync` single-flight is reactive/waker-driven (concurrent callers await one shared future), NEVER an interval poll (invariant 4). Cache HIT is the zero-extra-work hot path: one async get + short-circuit, no proceed.; Origin-agnostic CacheManager/Cache providers (guardrail): `Arc<dyn CacheManager>`/`Arc<dyn Cache>` are resolved as beans, so an in-memory default, a Caffeine-class crate, or a dynamic/WASM-sourced cache all slot in behind the SAME trait — no assumption of native construction. Backends attach as integration crates (charter pluggable-everything-else).; Thin macros (invariant 10): `#[cacheable]` emits the AdvisorDescriptor contribution + the CacheOpMeta-as-data + a typed key/condition/unless closure (lowered from the key/condition expression by leaf-codegen) — all hand-writable; `cargo expand` shows a plain interceptor registration and plain closures, no hidden interpreter.; Rich diagnostics (invariant 9): a missing named cache, an unresolvable CacheManager, or a key-closure type error surface as a startup/refresh error (advisor build) or a typed CacheError with full context (which method, which cache, which op) — better than Spring's runtime ClassCastException on an erased key/value.

**Cons:** The shared-Cache value-erasure problem is real: one named `Cache` is consulted by methods with DIFFERENT return types, so the store must hold `Box<dyn Any + Send + Sync>` (or per-method sub-caches), reintroducing a downcast at read — an erasure Rust would otherwise avoid, touching the deferred type-erasure/registry-substrate keystone. A wrong downcast (two methods sharing a cache name + key but different types) is a runtime hazard the design must structurally prevent (key includes a type-id).; make_interceptor(&Container) resolving a CacheManager couples advisor construction to the container; if the CacheManager bean itself depends on async config (a Redis pool), interceptor construction may need to .await on the refresh/creation context — execution-context discipline must document this and a blocking backend init must spawn_blocking. Pre-judges that the interceptor holds an Arc<dyn CacheManager> resolved at refresh (vs per-call resolution).; Single-flight (`sync`) needs a per-cache in-flight map keyed by CacheKey holding shared futures — a fine-grained per-cache concurrent structure (NOT a global lock, invariant 5), but it IS shared mutable state on the hot miss path; its memory/ownership (who owns the in-flight future, cancellation of a waiter vs the computer) is a real async-correctness concern with no Spring analogue (Spring's sync uses a JVM lock + Callable).; Inherits the auto-proxy-creator's whole collection/DCE story: caching's advisor descriptor must survive dead-code-elimination (a 'caching crate the binary never path-references' silently disables ALL @Cacheable — the #1 silent-failure mode), so caching rides the anti-DCE self-check mandate; and whether the descriptor is collected at all depends on the (undecided) Phase-2 collection substrate.; The key/condition/unless expressions depend on the expression-language neighbour's chosen realization: if leaf ships typed closures (its R1) caching is clean; if it ships an interpreter, caching's key path inherits the erased Value->key hop. So caching's key story is entangled with an undecided neighbour decision and cannot be fully settled here.; Self-invocation caveat carries over from the substrate (line 1716): an internal `self.cached_method()` call bypasses the proxy so caching silently does not run — a Spring limitation leaf inherits via aop-proxying unless a different interception mechanism is chosen; caching must DOCUMENT this, not solve it.

**Mechanisms considered:** One Role::Infrastructure AdvisorDescriptor + CacheInterceptor contributed into auto-proxy-creator's collection (the literal CacheInterceptor analogue; advice-agnostic to the creator); async fn around(JoinPoint, CacheOpMeta) implementing get/short-circuit/proceed/put/evict, with cache HIT NOT calling proceed (substrate short-circuit); CacheManager/Cache/KeyGenerator/CacheResolver/CacheErrorHandler as leaf-core trait + beans (Arc<dyn …> providers, origin-agnostic); CacheOpMeta emitted as DATA by thin #[cacheable]/#[cache_evict]/#[cache_put] macros; key/condition/unless lowered to typed closures by leaf-codegen; sync single-flight via a per-cache in-flight map of shared awaitable futures (waker-driven, not polling); StoredValue erasure resolved by (type-id, method-id, key) composite + Box<dyn Any+Send+Sync> downcast at the typed call site, or per-method sub-caches behind one cache name; make_interceptor(&Container) resolving the CacheManager bean at refresh; order:i32 carried as data so auto-proxy-creator positions cache relative to tx

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Caching contributes ONE thin AdvisorDescriptor (id, order:i32, role=Infrastructure, pointcut, make_interceptor fn-ptr) into auto-proxy-creator's collection stream — riding WHATEVER Phase-2 substrate that feature picks (linkme/codegen/explicit), origin-agnostic. The DCE hazard is acute and direct: a 'leaf-cache' advisor crate the binary never path-references contributes NO descriptor and silently disables every @Cacheable with no error (the #1 silent-failure mode, line 345) — so caching MUST be covered by the anti-DCE self-check (expected-advisor-count / manifest cross-check) and `leaf doctor` should report 'caching advisor present, N cacheable methods matched'. CacheManager/Cache backends are integration crates contributing `Arc<dyn CacheManager>`/`Arc<dyn Cache>` beans via the general bean-discovery substrate (a Redis/Caffeine crate = a normal integration crate, dep:-hidden umbrella feature + force-link per the additive-feature rule, line 351/207) — and a dynamic/WASM-module cache source feeds the SAME CacheManager trait (the two-source duality: static link-time backends as default 'classpath' PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned cache source — CANDIDATES, not decided). Ordering relative to tx is encoded AS DATA (order:i32 on the descriptor) resolved by auto-proxy-creator's runtime sort — NEVER link order (line 355). Generics-can't-register (line 349): a generic cached method (`fn get<T>()`) cannot register a concrete descriptor — the macro hard-errors with a per-instantiation shim requirement. The CacheOpMeta key/condition closures are lowered in the crate that writes the attribute, so they compile/link where that code does (zero link-time exposure for the metadata itself, full wasm support — the metadata side is portable even if the advisor-collection side is not).

**T2 — execution context:** Advisor descriptor collection + make_interceptor are COLD-PATH at refresh (refresh-lifecycle): descriptor is const data; make_interceptor resolves the CacheManager bean and may .await if the backend's construction is async (a Redis pool) — runs on the refresh/creation context inside the per-bean guard (no global lock); a blocking backend init MUST spawn_blocking (execution-context discipline). The HOT path is per advised call inside the assembled AdviceChain: `around()` is async — `cache.get(key).await` (a Redis backend awaits network I/O; an in-memory backend resolves immediately), on miss `jp.proceed().await` (the real method, async), then `cache.put(key, v).await`. Cache HIT short-circuits with one get + no proceed (lowest steady-state work). `sync` single-flight is WAKER-DRIVEN: concurrent missers await one shared in-flight future (invariant 4 — reactive, no busy-wait); cancellation of a waiter must NOT cancel the in-flight computation for the others (a structured-concurrency concern — the computer owns the future, waiters hold a shared handle), and if the computer is cancelled the next waiter must be promoted or the wait fails with a CacheError carrying full context. Errors propagate as `CacheError` through the CacheErrorHandler (Spring's policy: a cache backend failure may be swallowed-and-log or fail-fast — a policy knob); a get/put failure must NOT corrupt the method result path (on a cache error the protocol can fall through to proceed()). All cache I/O respects the async context; nothing blocks the executor thread. Refresh cancellation aborts advisor build cleanly (all-or-nothing, no half-wired interceptor).

#### R2 — Caching as a thin macro-emitted typed wrapper around the method body (compile-time woven cache protocol), sharing the Cache abstraction but not a runtime advisor
Question whether caching needs a RUNTIME advisor/interceptor at all for the common case. Because leaf knows the cached method's typed signature and return type at the attribute site, the `#[cacheable]` macro can LOWER the around-proceed protocol directly into a typed wrapper the way a hand-written cache would be — emitting (in `cargo expand`-legible form) a wrapper that: builds a typed `CacheKey` from the (typed) args, evaluates the typed `condition` closure, calls `cache_manager.cache(NAME).get_typed::<Ret>(key).await` (a TYPED get — no erasure, the return type is known here), on miss awaits the real body, evaluates `unless`, puts the typed value, returns. This makes the cache store PER-METHOD-TYPED (a `Cache<K, Ret>` view obtained from the manager by name+type) so the `Box<dyn Any>` downcast of R1 DISSOLVES — each call site is monomorphized over its own return type. The shared primitives are the SAME as R1 — `CacheManager`/`Cache`/`KeyGenerator`/`CacheError` — but the cache PROTOCOL is woven at compile time into the method rather than dispatched through a runtime interceptor chain. Two sub-shapes (candidates): (a) the macro wraps the method body in-place (the caller calls the same fn; the wrapper is the fn body) — closest to a hand-written cache, but then it is NOT a chain participant and ordering-with-tx must be re-expressed as nesting order of the woven wrappers (cache wrapper outside/inside the tx wrapper — a compile-time decision); (b) the macro emits a typed `CacheAround<F>` adapter that STILL plugs into the auto-proxy-creator's chain as a typed (non-erased) interceptor for THIS method, getting chain ordering for free while keeping the typed store. `@EnableCaching` becomes 'the cache abstraction beans are present' (a CacheManager bean exists); the marker macros do the weaving. Erasure is eliminated on the value path; the only runtime indirection is `dyn CacheManager`/`dyn Cache` (origin-agnostic backends).

**Pros:** Eliminates the value-erasure problem entirely (R1's main con): the cache store is consulted via a TYPED `get_typed::<Ret>`/`put_typed` because the return type is statically known at the woven call site — no `Box<dyn Any>`, no downcast, no (type-id,key) collision hazard. This is the most rust-native realization of caching's value path and the strongest 'prove the JVM erasure unnecessary' (invariant 2) of the three.; Lowest steady-state overhead (invariant 2) for the in-place sub-shape: a cache HIT is a typed get + return with NO chain dispatch, NO boxed-future-per-interceptor-hop — the woven wrapper is monomorphized; ideal for hot read-through caches where the cache lookup dominates.; Key/condition/unless are monomorphized typed closures over the real typed args (no JoinPoint arg-erasure), so they type-check at COMPILE time (invariant 9, earliest legible diagnostic): a key referencing a non-existent arg or a condition on a mistyped field is a compile error at the attribute span, not a runtime surprise.; Thin-macro-faithful in spirit (invariant 10): the woven wrapper is exactly what a careful developer would hand-write (get → on-miss compute → put), fully visible in `cargo expand`, and the manual escape hatch IS just writing that wrapper yourself against the public CacheManager API — caching becomes 'sugar over a hand-writable cache-aside' rather than hidden interception.; Portable: the woven protocol is plain generated code in the method's own crate — zero link-time/DCE exposure for the caching LOGIC (only the backend beans ride discovery), full wasm/embedded support (Architecture C affinity). A missing @EnableCaching can be a compile/startup error (the CacheManager bean must resolve) rather than silent.; Shares the SAME CacheManager/Cache/KeyGenerator abstraction as R1, so backends are identical and origin-agnostic — only the PROTOCOL placement (compile-time weave vs runtime interceptor) differs, keeping Phase-3 merge cheap.

**Cons:** The in-place sub-shape (a) BREAKS the single-shared-chain model (line 1772): if cache is woven into the method body and tx is a runtime interceptor, they are no longer co-ordered in ONE chain — the cache-outside-vs-inside-tx ordering becomes a compile-time nesting decision that the auto-proxy-creator CANNOT see or arbitrate, re-introducing exactly the invisible-misordering bug Spring's single-chain eliminated. Sub-shape (b) avoids this but gives up some of (a)'s zero-dispatch benefit.; Pre-judges the metadata/codegen boundary AND compile-vs-runtime split (§3 keystones) HARD toward compile-time weaving — presenting a woven protocol as THE shape biases those decisions; must stay strictly a CANDIDATE. The runtime-advisor model (R1) is the more neutral default.; Self-invocation: weaving into the method body could actually FIX the self-invocation limitation (an internal call hits the woven body) — but only for sub-shape (a), and ONLY for caching, creating an INCONSISTENCY where @Cacheable-via-weave fires on self-calls but @Transactional-via-interceptor does not; that divergence is confusing (a behaviour Spring users do not expect) and cuts against unification.; Conditional caching presence (conditions/profiles — is caching even active in this profile?) is awkward when woven at compile time: a compile-time weave is always present; turning it off per-profile needs a runtime gate inside the woven wrapper (check 'is caching enabled?'), eroding the zero-overhead benefit and straddling the compile-vs-runtime line (cross-crate doc line 209).; Typed per-method cache views complicate a SHARED named cache used across methods/services for cross-cutting eviction: `@CacheEvict` from method A must evict an entry put by method B under the same name+key — if each method has its own typed view, the manager must reconcile typed views over one physical store (a (name) -> physical store with per-(method,type) namespacing), partially re-introducing the erasure at the STORE boundary even if the call sites are typed.; More macro-emitted code per cached method (the whole protocol inline) than R1's single shared interceptor — larger codegen footprint, and any protocol bugfix requires re-expansion across all sites rather than fixing one interceptor.

**Mechanisms considered:** #[cacheable] macro lowering the around-proceed protocol into a typed wrapper over the method (compile-time weave), reusing CacheManager/Cache abstraction; TYPED cache views: cache_manager.cache(NAME).get_typed::<Ret>(key)/put_typed — eliminating Box<dyn Any> by monomorphizing over the known return type; sub-shape (a) in-place body weave (closest to hand-written cache-aside; not a chain participant; ordering = nesting); sub-shape (b) typed CacheAround<F> adapter that still plugs into the auto-proxy-creator chain for ordering, keeping the typed store; monomorphized typed key/condition/unless closures over real args (compile-time type-checked); physical-store namespacing by (cache-name, method-id, type-id) to reconcile typed views with cross-method shared eviction; runtime enable-gate inside the woven wrapper for conditional/profile-based activation

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Strong Architecture C affinity (build-time/compile-time, line 305): the cache protocol is woven as plain generated code in the cached method's own crate — fully platform-independent (incl. wasm/embedded), avoiding the advisor-collection DCE hazard for the caching LOGIC entirely (the protocol is referenced because the method is called). The ONLY cross-crate discovery surface is the CacheManager/Cache BACKEND beans (and KeyGenerator/CacheResolver), which ride the general bean-discovery substrate exactly as in R1 — a Redis/Caffeine crate is a normal integration crate (dep:-hidden feature + force-link, line 207), and the two-source duality (static link-time backends + optional dynamic/WASM cache source feeding the same CacheManager trait) applies to backends only (CANDIDATES). Because the protocol is woven, a missing CacheManager bean can be a STARTUP error (the woven wrapper resolves the manager at first use / at refresh) rather than a silent no-op — better failure mode than R1's silent-disabled-advisor. CRITICAL T1 tension if sub-shape (b): the typed CacheAround adapter must still surface an order:i32 + role to the auto-proxy-creator (ordering-as-data, line 355) so it co-orders with tx — coupling the woven adapter to the creator's collection contract. Generics-can't-register applies to a generic cached method as in R1 (per-instantiation). The typed-view-over-shared-physical-store reconciliation is a cross-crate concern if method A (crate X) and method B (crate Y) share a cache name — the physical store namespacing must be stable across crates (name-hash + type-id as data).

**T2 — execution context:** The woven protocol runs INLINE on the caller's execution context (no chain dispatch for sub-shape a): build typed key (sync, pure), evaluate condition (sync typed closure), `cache.get_typed::<Ret>(key).await`, on miss await the real (async) body, evaluate unless (sync), `cache.put_typed(key, v).await`. Cache HIT = one typed async get + return, monomorphized, no boxed-future hop (lower per-call cost than R1's chain). `sync` single-flight is the SAME waker-driven in-flight-future concern as R1 (reactive, not polling) but the in-flight map is typed per method (`InFlight<K, Ret>`) — cancellation semantics (waiter cancel must not cancel the shared computation) identical. Backend I/O (Redis get/put) awaits on its own context; nothing blocks the executor. For sub-shape (b) the typed adapter runs inside the AdviceChain like R1 but as a typed (non-erased) hop. The conditional-activation runtime gate (if caching disabled-in-this-profile) is a cheap sync branch on the cold-ish first-call path. Errors: typed `CacheError` through the CacheErrorHandler policy (swallow-and-proceed vs fail-fast); a cache failure falls through to the real body so correctness is preserved. Compile-time weaving means MANY of R1's refresh-time concerns vanish (no make_interceptor .await for the protocol itself; only backend bean construction is async at refresh). Cancellation of the awaited body propagates normally; a cancelled miss-compute must not leave a poisoned in-flight entry (clear-on-cancel).

#### R3 — Cache-aside as an explicit first-class typed handle (CacheStore<K,V>) injected as a bean; @Cacheable is thin sugar that resolves and calls it
Question whether caching should be PRIMARILY a declarative interceptor at all, or whether the rust-native primitive is an explicit, typed, injectable cache HANDLE — with the annotation as a thin convenience layer over it (mirroring deferral-primitives' philosophy of 'an honest, debuggable call site' over hidden bytecode, Spring line 2567). Shape: leaf exposes `trait CacheStore<K: CacheKey, V: Clone + Send + Sync>: Send + Sync { async fn get_or_compute<F>(&self, k: K, f: F) -> Result<V, CacheError> where F: AsyncFnOnce() -> Result<V, E>; async fn get(&self,k:&K)->...; async fn put(...); async fn evict(...); }` — a strongly typed cache obtained from the `CacheManager` by name+type and INJECTED as an ordinary bean (`cache: Arc<dyn CacheStore<UserId, User>>`). The user can call `self.cache.get_or_compute(id, || self.load(id)).await` directly — fully explicit, fully typed, fully testable, no proxy, no self-invocation caveat. THEN `#[cacheable]` is thin sugar: the macro resolves the appropriate `CacheStore` for the method's (key-type, return-type) and emits a call to `get_or_compute` wrapping the body — so the declarative form and the programmatic form share ONE typed primitive (maximal unification, invariant 11). Conditional caching / unless / cross-method eviction are expressed against the typed handle (`@CacheEvict` = the macro emits `self.cache_for(name).evict(key).await`). The CacheManager is the registry of named typed stores; `CacheStore` is the origin-agnostic provider (an in-memory, Caffeine, Redis, or WASM-sourced store all implement it). For ordering-with-transactions: because the cache is a call against an injected handle (not a chain interceptor), tx/cache ordering is the ORDER of statements/wrappers in the method — explicit and visible (the opposite of Spring's invisible-misordering), OR, for users who want the chain model, an optional adapter re-exposes a CacheStore-backed interceptor into the auto-proxy-creator chain (so R3's handle is the substrate, R1's interceptor is an optional facade over it).

**Pros:** Maximal unification (invariant 11): ONE typed `CacheStore<K,V>` primitive serves BOTH the programmatic cache-aside call AND the declarative @Cacheable (sugar over it). No separate interceptor machinery vs handle machinery — the annotation literally emits the hand-writable handle call (invariant 10 taken to its limit; the escape hatch is the primitive itself).; Most rust-native, most honest call site (Spring line 2567 philosophy): caching becomes an explicit typed operation at the moment of use, fully testable WITHOUT the container or any proxy (inject a fake CacheStore), with NO self-invocation caveat (an internal call to `self.cache.get_or_compute` always works) — eliminating the single most surprising Spring AOP limitation (line 1716) for the caching case.; Fully typed value path (no erasure, invariant 2): `CacheStore<K,V>` is monomorphized per (key,value) type, so get/put are typed, no `Box<dyn Any>` downcast, compile-time key/value type-checking (invariant 9 earliest diagnostic). The JVM erasure that motivates Spring's Object cache is PROVEN unnecessary here.; Async-first and reactive (invariants 3/4): `get_or_compute` is the natural single-flight point — the trait can guarantee one concurrent miss computes while others await the shared future (waker-driven, not polling), and this guarantee lives in ONE well-tested primitive rather than scattered in an interceptor. Backend I/O awaits on its own context.; Origin-agnostic and pluggable to the maximum: `Arc<dyn CacheStore<K,V>>` providers come from any source (in-memory default, Caffeine crate, Redis crate, dynamic/WASM source) — and because it is an injected bean, conditions/profiles decide which store is active FOR FREE (an advisor-activation problem R1 has, dissolved here exactly as the auto-proxy-creator's R3 dissolves advisor activation).; Decouples caching from the AOP substrate's undecided proxy mechanism: R3 needs NO proxy for the common case, so it is robust to whatever aop-proxying/auto-proxy-creator decide; the optional chain adapter is available for users who specifically want cache-in-the-tx-chain ordering.

**Cons:** LEAST faithful to Spring's declarative mental model: a Spring user expects `@Cacheable` to be transparent interception, not 'inject a typed cache handle'. The DX is excellent but DIVERGENT — the @Cacheable sugar narrows the gap, but power use (cross-cutting eviction across many beans, dynamic cache names) is more explicit/verbose than Spring's annotation-only style.; Breaks the single-shared-chain ordering with transactions (line 1772) for the common case even harder than R2: cache is a statement in the method, tx is a chain interceptor, so cache-vs-tx ordering is now method-statement-order — explicit but NOT arbitrated by the auto-proxy-creator; the optional chain adapter recovers it but then you are back to R1's model for that case. Reconciling 'handle by default, chain when needed' is a real two-models tension.; Typed `CacheStore<K,V>` injection requires the (key,value) types at the injection point — the macro must KNOW them for the @Cacheable site (fine, the signature is present) but a SHARED cache used by heterogeneous methods needs many typed store views over one physical backend, the same typed-view-over-shared-store reconciliation as R2 (name-hash + type-id namespacing in the physical store).; Pre-judges the scope/ownership AND type-erasure/registry keystones (§3): 'inject a typed CacheStore<K,V> bean' commits to how typed generic beans are stored/resolved (generics-can't-register, line 349 — a `CacheStore<UserId,User>` is a generic instantiation needing per-type registration), biasing the registry-substrate signal; must stay a CANDIDATE.; Cross-method/declarative @CacheEvict and @Caching (multiple ops) are clean as a handle call but @Caching's COMPOSITION (several puts+evicts in one annotation) and Spring's CacheResolver dynamism (cache chosen per-invocation) are more naturally expressed in an interceptor that sees the whole CacheOpMeta than as a sequence of handle calls — R3 must emit a sequence, which is fine but less unified than one interceptor pass.; Generic-bean injection for `Arc<dyn CacheStore<K,V>>` interacts with whatever leaf decides about generic beans and collection-injection — if leaf cannot inject arbitrary generic instantiations cleanly, the typed-handle ergonomics degrade; entangled with autowiring-resolution's generic handling.

**Mechanisms considered:** trait CacheStore<K,V> with async get_or_compute (single-flight built into the primitive) as the first-class injectable typed cache handle; #[cacheable]/#[cache_evict] as THIN sugar resolving the matching CacheStore<K,V> and emitting get_or_compute/evict calls (annotation = sugar over the handle, invariant 11); CacheManager as a registry of named typed stores; CacheStore<K,V> obtained by name+type; optional chain-adapter facade: a CacheStore-backed interceptor that plugs into the auto-proxy-creator chain for users who want cache-in-tx-chain ordering; conditions/profiles selecting the active CacheStore provider (advisor-activation dissolved into bean activation); physical-store namespacing (cache-name + key + value-type-id) to share one backend across typed views; origin-agnostic Arc<dyn CacheStore<K,V>> providers (in-memory/Caffeine/Redis/WASM)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** `CacheStore<K,V>` is a GENERIC trait, so each concrete instantiation (`CacheStore<UserId,User>`) is a distinct monomorphized type — and generics-can't-register (line 349) bites directly: a typed store cannot be registered as a cross-crate descriptor without a per-instantiation shim; leaf's macros must hard-error or emit per-(K,V) registration. This is the sharpest T1 cost of R3 and entangles it with the type-erasure/registry substrate keystone. The CacheManager (non-generic) is a normal bean; CacheStore PROVIDERS (a Redis-backed `CacheStore<K,V>` impl) come from integration crates and must register per concrete (K,V) used — heavier cross-crate than R1's single erased advisor. The two-source duality (static link-time stores + optional dynamic/WASM store source) applies to providers (CANDIDATES). Because the @Cacheable sugar emits a typed handle CALL in the method's own crate, the caching LOGIC has zero link-time/DCE exposure (full wasm support) — only store-provider discovery rides the substrate. A missing store for a (name,K,V) is a resolution error at startup/first-use (rich diagnostic, invariant 9) rather than a silent no-op — strong failure mode. Cross-method shared eviction across crates needs stable physical-store namespacing as data (name-hash + type-id). The optional chain adapter, if used, surfaces order:i32 to the auto-proxy-creator (ordering-as-data) as in R1/R2(b).

**T2 — execution context:** The cache operation runs on the CALLER's execution context as an ordinary async call: `cache.get_or_compute(k, || async { … }).await`. Single-flight lives INSIDE the primitive (one well-tested place): concurrent missers await one shared in-flight future (waker-driven, invariant 4 — no polling); the primitive owns the cancellation contract (a cancelled waiter does not cancel the shared computation; if the computer is cancelled, a waiter is promoted or the wait errors with full context) — this is the cleanest home for the single-flight async-correctness concern of the three because it is encapsulated, not scattered. Backend I/O (Redis) awaits on its own context; in-memory resolves immediately. No proxy/chain dispatch on the common path (no boxed-future-per-hop) → lowest dispatch overhead, but each get_or_compute is a real async call. The optional chain adapter runs inside the AdviceChain (R1-like) only when used. Refresh-time: CacheStore provider beans (incl. async backend init like a Redis pool) build on the refresh/creation context; a blocking init MUST spawn_blocking (execution-context discipline). Errors: typed `CacheError` via CacheErrorHandler policy (swallow-and-compute vs fail-fast); a backend failure falls through to the compute closure preserving correctness. Cancellation of the awaited compute propagates normally; the primitive must clear an in-flight entry on compute-cancel to avoid poisoning subsequent callers. No self-invocation hazard (a direct handle call always executes).

### Rust clashes
- Spring's cache KEY is an erased Object built reflectively from method args, and the cached VALUE is an erased Object retrieved by ClassCastException-risking cast. Rust has typed args and a typed return per method, so the key is a typed-hashable (`impl CacheKey`/`Hash+Eq`) and the value is typed at the call site — the erasure Spring needs largely DISSOLVES (invariant 2). The residual clash: a SHARED named cache consulted by methods with DIFFERENT return types must still reconcile typed values behind one physical store (either `Box<dyn Any>` + downcast keyed by type-id, or per-(method,type) namespacing over one backend) — so leaf must decide whether a 'cache name' is one heterogeneous store or a family of typed views; entangled with the deferred type-erasure/registry substrate.
- Spring's `CacheInterceptor` calls a synchronous `Object proceed()` and `cache.get/put` are synchronous; leaf is async-first (invariant 3), so the around-proceed protocol is async (`jp.proceed().await`, `cache.get(...).await`) and the cache backend (Redis-class) awaits real I/O. Spring's `sync=true` single-flight uses a JVM lock + Callable; leaf must realize single-flight as a WAKER-DRIVEN shared-future wait (invariant 4 — reactive, not the busy-wait/poll a naive port might do), and must define async cancellation semantics with NO Spring analogue: does cancelling one waiter cancel the shared computation, who owns the in-flight future, what happens if the computer is cancelled mid-flight.
- Spring's cache key/condition/unless are SpEL strings evaluated reflectively at runtime over `#root.args`/`#result`; Rust has no runtime reflective interpreter and the args/result are typed. leaf's key/condition/unless should be TYPED closures over the real args (compile-time type-checked, invariant 9) rather than a string interpreter — but this directly entangles caching with the expression-language neighbour's UNDECIDED realization (typed-closure vs interpreter): if leaf ships closures, caching's key path is clean and erasure-free; if an interpreter, caching inherits an erased Value->key hop. Caching cannot settle this alone.
- Spring's `@Cacheable` fires via a proxy, so an internal `this.cachedMethod()` self-call SILENTLY does not cache (line 1716) — the defining proxy-AOP limitation. If leaf realizes caching as a runtime interceptor over the aop-proxying substrate (R1), it INHERITS this exact silent caveat and must document it. If leaf realizes caching as a woven body wrapper (R2) or an injected typed handle (R3), self-calls DO cache — fixing the limitation for caching but creating an INCONSISTENCY with transaction-management (still proxy-based, still self-invocation-bypassed), a divergence Spring users will not expect.
- The cache-outside-vs-inside-transaction ORDERING is correctness-critical yet INVISIBLE in object identity (line 1772) — Spring orders cache and tx within ONE proxy chain by advisor order. In Rust, if caching is a chain interceptor (R1), ordering is data (order:i32) resolved by the auto-proxy-creator's sort — fine, but the misordering remains invisible unless leaf surfaces the assembled chain order as an inspectable diagnostic (invariant 9). If caching is woven/handle-based (R2/R3), ordering-with-tx is no longer in one chain at all — it becomes statement/nesting order that the auto-proxy-creator cannot arbitrate, trading invisibility for a different (explicit but un-unified) ordering model.
- `@EnableCaching` registers the auto-proxy-creator + caching's advisor as INERT-marker-to-behaviour wiring (line 1712); in Rust there is no runtime classpath to register into and no 'most-capable-creator-wins' bean race (link order is unspecified, line 355). leaf must re-express 'enabling caching' as (a) ensuring caching's advisor descriptor is collected via the Phase-2 substrate AND (b) ensuring the auto-proxy-creator's CreatorPolicy considers infrastructure advisors AND (c) the anti-DCE self-check so a never-path-referenced caching crate does not SILENTLY disable all @Cacheable (the #1 silent-failure mode, line 345) — a hazard with no JVM equivalent (the JVM would just have the class on the classpath).
- Spring's `CacheManager`/`Cache` store and retrieve freely-aliased GC'd Object references; Rust must decide OWNERSHIP of cached values — a cached value handed to multiple callers is `Arc<V>` or `V: Clone`, the cache OWNS its entries with an eviction policy (TTL/LRU) that is the backend's concern, and a value crossing into a Redis backend must be serializable (a constraint Spring's in-JVM cache does not impose but a distributed cache does). This is the scope/lifetime/ownership keystone viewed through the cache: who owns a cached entry, when is it dropped, is it shared by Arc or cloned.
- Spring's `CacheResolver` can choose the target Cache(s) dynamically per invocation; realizing this in Rust over a typed store model (R2/R3) is awkward (the store is resolved by name+type at the call site), whereas the erased-interceptor model (R1) accommodates dynamic resolution more naturally — so the choice of realization constrains how much of Spring's dynamic-cache-resolution flexibility survives, a parity-vs-typed-ergonomics tension.

### Interactions
- **auto-proxy-creator** — PRIMARY upstream dependency (building directly on its delivered design). Caching contributes ONE Role::Infrastructure AdvisorDescriptor + CacheInterceptor into auto-proxy-creator's COLLECTION stream (R1), or a typed chain adapter (R2b), and the creator owns MATCH/eligibility (which methods carry caching metadata), UNION with other advisors, and the role-tier+order SORT that positions cache relative to transactions in the single shared chain (line 1772). Caching is advice-AGNOSTIC to the creator — it only supplies a pointcut + interceptor + order:i32 + Role::Infrastructure. The creator's CreatorPolicy considering infrastructure advisors is what activates caching's advice (the @EnableCaching analogue). In R2(a)/R3 caching may BYPASS the creator entirely (woven/handle), interacting only via the optional chain adapter.
- **transaction-management** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor and the CANONICAL ordering-correctness pair (line 1772): a @Transactional + @Cacheable bean gets both advice in ONE chain where cache-outside-vs-inside-tx is a real, invisible bug. Caching must be a well-behaved ORDERABLE participant (carry an order:i32) so the auto-proxy-creator positions it deterministically relative to the TransactionInterceptor. Caching and tx share the SAME advisor-collection + role-isolation pipeline; their RELATIVE order is the thing leaf must make inspectable (invariant 9). In R2/R3 the ordering-with-tx becomes statement/nesting order rather than chain order — a divergence to reconcile.
- **aop-proxying** — SUBSTRATE dependency (transitively, via auto-proxy-creator). aop-proxying owns the JoinPoint/proceed primitive and — critically for caching — the ability to SHORT-CIRCUIT proceed() (a cache HIT returns without calling the real method), which is a substrate capability caching uses, not implements. Caching's interceptor is an Interceptor in aop-proxying's AdviceChain (R1). The self-invocation limitation (line 1716) is aop-proxying's boundary that caching inherits in R1. Caching does NOT redefine the proxy mechanism.
- **expression-language** — DIRECT consumer for cache KEY, condition, and unless (Spring's `@Cacheable(key="#root.args[0]", condition=..., unless="#result==null")`). Caching delegates key/condition/unless evaluation to expression-language's EvalContext shape (root/args/result/BeanResolver) — but caching strongly prefers expression-language's TYPED-CLOSURE realization (its R1) so the key path is erasure-free and compile-time-checked; if expression-language ships an interpreter, caching inherits the erased Value->key hop. The `#result` exposure for `unless`/key-on-result is a specific requirement caching places on the EvalContext. This is an unresolved cross-feature entanglement.
- **type-conversion** — Sibling on the key/value path. A cache KEY built from args may need coercion to a canonical hashable form, and a value crossing into a serializing backend (Redis) needs conversion/serialization — type-conversion's ConversionService is the natural home, and caching must agree on the boundary (typed key -> CacheKey, typed value -> serialized bytes for distributed backends). For in-memory typed stores (R2/R3) no conversion is needed; for distributed backends it is required.
- **conditions** — Decides whether caching is ACTIVE (is @EnableCaching's effect present in this profile/config?) and whether a specific CacheManager/CacheStore bean participates. In R1 this is advisor-activation (the caching advisor active iff conditions hold) — the same compile-vs-runtime reconciliation the auto-proxy-creator faces. In R3 it dissolves into ordinary bean activation (the active CacheStore provider is a conditional bean). Spring's per-operation `condition`/`unless` are RUNTIME predicates distinct from registration-time conditions — caching must keep these two condition layers separate.
- **container-core** — The engine-vs-facade split decides whether caching's advisor is installed at all: a bare engine with no auto-proxy-creator leaves @Cacheable inert (line 49/110 parity), exactly like a Spring BeanFactory without @EnableCaching. The context facade is where caching's advisor + CacheManager beans are wired, and the only place the full enabled-capability set (is caching enabled?) is visible (binary/app-root, cross-crate line 260). The CacheManager/CacheStore are container-resolved beans.
- **events** — Cache EVICTION is a classic event-driven cross-cutting reaction (Spring line 1792 names cache eviction as the canonical decoupled-event use case). Caching may EXPOSE eviction as event listeners (an @EventListener that evicts on a domain fact) and may EMIT cache events (hit/miss/evict) for observability — interacting with events/event-multicaster as both a producer and consumer, keeping the emitter oblivious to cache wiring.
- **bean-instantiation** — Calls auto-proxy-creator's match+wrap at after_init so a @Cacheable bean is published as its proxy (R1), inside the per-bean creation guard. For R3 the cached bean is unwrapped; the CacheStore is injected as an ordinary dependency. The CacheManager backend bean (possibly async to construct — a Redis pool) is instantiated like any other bean, on the creation context.
- **scopes / concurrency-contract** — The CacheManager/Cache/CacheStore are typically singletons (stateless contract over a shared backing store), so their concurrency safety is the safe-publication guarantee (concurrency-contract) plus the cache's own internal concurrency (the in-flight single-flight map, the eviction structure) which must be fine-grained, not a global lock (invariant 5). A request/session-scoped cache would ride scopes/scoped-proxies — a less common but valid composition.
- **retry-resilience / async-execution / validation** — FELLOW Role::Infrastructure advisors sharing the auto-proxy-creator's collect+order pipeline. A method may be @Cacheable AND @Retryable AND @Validated AND @Async — all four contribute infrastructure advisors into ONE chain whose order is correctness-relevant (e.g. validate before cache-lookup; retry around the cache miss-compute). Caching must co-order deterministically with these siblings via order:i32, exactly as with transactions. They share the same role-isolation and ordering machinery.
- **deferral-primitives** — PHILOSOPHICAL kin to R3: deferral-primitives realizes optional/lazy/multiple access as an explicit, honest, debuggable call site (a live handle) rather than hidden bytecode (line 2567). R3's CacheStore<K,V> handle is the same philosophy applied to caching — an injected typed handle you call explicitly, testable without the container, no proxy magic. If leaf leans into deferral-primitives' handle philosophy, R3's cache-handle model is the natural fit.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is caching realized as a RUNTIME interceptor over the shared auto-proxy-creator chain (R1, faithful to Spring's CacheInterceptor and to single-chain ordering-with-tx), as a COMPILE-TIME woven typed wrapper (R2, erasure-free + low overhead but breaks single-chain ordering), or as an INJECTED typed CacheStore handle with @Cacheable as thin sugar (R3, maximal unification + no self-invocation caveat but most divergent from Spring's declarative model)? This cross-cuts the metadata/codegen boundary AND compile-vs-runtime split (§3) and determines whether cache/tx ordering is chain-arbitrated or statement/nesting order.
- How is a SHARED named cache reconciled with Rust's typed values when consulted by methods of different return types — is a 'cache name' ONE heterogeneous physical store (Box<dyn Any> + type-id-keyed downcast, R1) or a family of TYPED views over one backend (per-(method,type) namespacing, R2/R3)? This is the type-erasure/registry-substrate keystone viewed through caching, and it determines whether cross-method @CacheEvict can evict a value put by another method under the same name+key.
- Where do cache KEY / condition / unless expressions live, given no runtime reflection — TYPED closures over real method args (compile-time-checked, erasure-free) or the expression-language interpreter (erased Value->key hop)? This is fully entangled with the expression-language feature's own undecided closure-vs-interpreter realization, plus the specific requirement to expose `#result` for unless/key-on-result.
- How is single-flight (`sync=true`) realized as a waker-driven (not polling, invariant 4) shared-future wait, and what are its async-cancellation semantics — does cancelling one waiter cancel the shared miss-computation, who OWNS the in-flight future, and what happens if the computer itself is cancelled mid-flight? Spring's JVM-lock+Callable has no async analogue; this is a structured-concurrency design point shared with retry-resilience.
- Should leaf REPLICATE Spring's self-invocation caveat for caching (R1, inherits it; consistent with tx) or ELIMINATE it (R2/R3, self-calls cache) — and if eliminated, how is the resulting INCONSISTENCY with transaction-management (still proxy-based, still self-bypassed) reconciled, given users expect cache and tx to behave the same? Cross-cuts aop-proxying's interception-mechanism keystone.
- How does the CacheManager/Cache/CacheStore PROVIDER abstraction stay origin-agnostic across the two-source duality — static link-time backends (in-memory default, Caffeine/Redis integration crates as dep:-hidden umbrella features + force-link) as the default 'classpath' PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned/WASM-module cache source feeding the same trait? And how is the anti-DCE self-check applied so a never-path-referenced caching/backend crate does not SILENTLY disable @Cacheable (the #1 silent-failure mode)? A candidate-comparison, not a decision.
- What is the ownership/lifetime model of a cached VALUE (the scope/ownership keystone through caching) — Arc-shared vs Clone-per-read, who owns and drops entries, the eviction-policy (TTL/LRU) home, and the serialization constraint a distributed backend imposes that an in-memory store does not? And does Spring's dynamic per-invocation CacheResolver survive a typed-store model (R2/R3) or only the erased-interceptor model (R1)?
- How is the cache/transaction RELATIVE ORDER (correctness-critical, invisible in object identity, line 1772) surfaced as an inspectable diagnostic (invariant 9) — a `leaf doctor`/introspection surface reporting 'method M's chain is [validate, tx, cache, retry] because…' — and is this the same ordering model as collection-injection's @Order/@Priority or a distinct correctness-grade ordering shared with the other infrastructure advisors?

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. Grounded directly in the Spring caching material: line 1635 (cross-cutting advice incl. @Cacheable applied by proxy wrapping + interceptor chain), 1705 (InfrastructureAdvisorAutoProxyCreator considers only @Role(ROLE_INFRASTRUCTURE) advisors — how @EnableCaching keeps cache advice isolated), 1708 (deliberate infra-vs-aspect role isolation), 1712 (the inert-marker/@Enable-wiring/CacheInterceptor-behaviour triad — the load-bearing 'annotation is marker, selector is wiring, interceptor is behaviour'), 1716 (self-invocation caveat naming @Cacheable explicitly), 1772 (ONE proxy many concerns, @Transactional+@Cacheable in one chain, cache-outside-vs-inside-tx the canonical invisible-misordering bug, ordering by @Order/Ordered/@Priority within one chain), 1659/1090 (proxyTargetClass/global proxy-type default applying to @Cacheable), 1792 (cache eviction as canonical decoupled-event use). I built CONCRETELY on the delivered auto-proxy-creator design (caching is its 'primary infrastructure-advisor consumer' interaction) and treated aop-proxying's JoinPoint/proceed/short-circuit + the advisor-collection contract as GIVENS I drive, not mechanisms I re-decide.

I deliberately scoped caching to what it OWNS — the around-proceed cache protocol (get/short-circuit/proceed/put/evict, condition/unless, sync single-flight) + the CacheManager/Cache/KeyGenerator/CacheResolver/CacheErrorHandler abstraction — and deferred chain assembly/match/order/role-isolation to auto-proxy-creator, and the proxy mechanism to aop-proxying. The three realizations diverge on the axis that DEFINES caching's rust-native shape: R1 = runtime infrastructure-advisor + async CacheInterceptor (most Spring-faithful, single-chain ordering with tx, inherits self-invocation caveat, has the shared-store value-erasure problem); R2 = compile-time woven typed wrapper (erasure-free typed value path, lowest overhead, but breaks single-chain ordering and pre-judges compile-time weaving); R3 = injected first-class typed CacheStore<K,V> handle with @Cacheable as thin sugar (maximal unification, no self-invocation caveat, most rust-native/honest call site per the deferral-primitives philosophy, but most divergent from Spring's declarative model and bitten hardest by generics-can't-register on the typed generic store). R1↔R2 differ on runtime-interceptor vs compile-time-weave; R1/R2↔R3 differ on transparent-interception vs explicit-typed-handle.

Things I deliberately did NOT decide (held as phase2Questions): the interceptor-vs-weave-vs-handle realization; how a shared named cache reconciles typed values (the type-erasure keystone through caching); where key/condition/unless expressions live (entangled with expression-language's own undecided realization); single-flight async-cancellation semantics; whether to replicate or eliminate the self-invocation caveat (and the resulting tx inconsistency); the origin-agnostic backend two-source duality + anti-DCE self-check; cached-value ownership/lifetime + dynamic CacheResolver survival; and how cache/tx relative order is made inspectable. Every realization keeps the CacheManager/Cache/CacheStore providers ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (Arc<dyn …> beans — in-memory/Caffeine/Redis/WASM slot in identically), keeps caching async-first with reactive (waker-driven, non-polling) single-flight, encodes cache-vs-tx ordering as DATA (order:i32) resolved at runtime by the creator's sort (never link order), inherits the anti-DCE self-check mandate for caching's advisor stream, and reproduces the bare-engine inert-advice parity. Naming ('CacheInterceptor', 'CacheManager', 'Cache', 'CacheStore', 'CacheOpMeta', 'CacheKey', 'KeyGenerator', 'CacheResolver', 'CacheErrorHandler', 'StoredValue', 'JoinPoint', 'AdvisorDescriptor', 'Role') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files.

Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (1635-1664 proxy substrate + proxyTargetClass; 1693-1772 the auto-proxy/advisor section incl. 1705 infra-role isolation, 1712 inert-marker-to-interceptor wiring naming CacheInterceptor, 1716 self-invocation naming @Cacheable, 1772 one-proxy-many-concerns cache-vs-tx ordering; 1792 cache-eviction-as-event); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (§2 invariants, §3 deferred keystones, §5 candidate meta-concepts, §6 topology); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (149-155 DCE layers + silent-failure + generics-can't-register + ordering-unspecified; 166-171 linkme-default + ordering-as-data + scoped-not-global; 190-207 additive-features + dep:-hidden + two-things-a-feature-must-do; 277-337 Architectures A-D + two-source/two-question framing; 345-362 hard constraints incl. anti-DCE self-check + scope-discovery-not-global); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (11-13 trait upcasting/RPITIT/AFIT adopt; 130-136/160-181 RPITIT/AFIT not dyn-safe forcing boxed-future/trait_variant at the dyn chain; 264 async closures for factories; 273-281 no async Drop → explicit container teardown).

## Validation Abstraction (@Validated)  `[validation]`

This feature is the rust-native realization of Spring's declarative validation — the `@Validated` / JSR-303 family — and it has THREE separable concerns I must keep distinct because they map onto different parts of leaf's stack:

(1) THE CONSTRAINT ENGINE (the JSR-303/Hibernate-Validator analogue, the actual heart of THIS feature). Spring delegates to a Bean Validation provider that, given a typed value and its declared constraints (`@NotNull`, `@Min`, `@Email`, `@Size`, cross-field, custom), walks the object graph and produces a SET of violations (not fail-on-first — it COLLECTS all violations with paths, messages, and the rejected value, then the caller decides). `@Valid` CASCADES into nested objects/collections (descending the object graph, accumulating property paths like `address.zip`). This engine is the shared primitive both faces below consume. Grounded intent: rich, descriptive errors (a violation carries path + constraint + actual value + a parameterized, i18n-resolvable message), and fail-fast.

(2) DECLARATIVE METHOD VALIDATION (the `@Validated`-on-a-bean AOP face, lines 1635/1705/1712). `@Validated` is INERT METADATA; an `@Enable*` selector registers a Role::Infrastructure ADVISOR (`MethodValidationInterceptor`) that the auto-proxy-creator (my designed upstream) collects, matches against validation-marked methods, and orders into the single shared interceptor chain. The interceptor runs AROUND the call: it validates method ARGUMENTS before `proceed()` and the RETURN VALUE after, throwing a descriptive `ConstraintViolationException` on violation. This face is almost entirely a thin Interceptor that delegates to engine (1) — I OWN the interceptor's contract and the typed-argument-access need, NOT the proxy/collection mechanism (that is aop-proxying + auto-proxy-creator, already designed).

(3) BEAN VALIDATION REUSED BY CONFIG-PROPERTIES BINDING (lines 1614/1616). The SAME engine (1) is invoked by the binder as a `ValidationBindHandler` analogue: after binding a property tree onto a typed config object, validate it, and on violation FAIL FAST AT STARTUP with a `BindValidationException`-equivalent that names the offending PROPERTY KEY (with origin/provenance from origin-tracking), not just the Rust field — "config is data, fail at boot, not with a deferred NPE." `@Valid` cascades into nested config objects. This is the canonical reuse the INTENT calls out: config constraints look like every other validated bean.

Load-bearing intent to replicate (intent, not mechanism): violations are COLLECTED (a set with paths), not fail-on-first; messages are parameterized and i18n-resolvable (delegating to messages-i18n); validation FAILS FAST and EARLY (startup for config, method-entry for method validation) with the richest possible diagnostic (§2.9); the constraint vocabulary is USER-EXTENSIBLE (custom constraints, like JSR-303's `@Constraint`); and the SAME engine serves both the AOP method-validation face and the config-binding face (unification, §2.11). I must NOT import the JVM mechanisms: no runtime reflection to discover annotations on fields/parameters, no runtime classpath to find a provider, no reflective object-graph walk. Constraints must be captured AS DATA at macro-expansion time, the validator must walk a value via generated/declared accessors not reflection, and the provider must be discoverable cross-crate without a classpath. I do NOT decide the metadata/codegen boundary, the proxy mechanism, the error-model realization, or the cross-crate substrate — those are Phase 2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Per-type derived Validate impl + a thin method-validation Interceptor: constraints compile into a hand-writable `fn validate(&self, ctx) -> Result<(), Violations>` on each type, the engine is just the recursive driver
Realize the constraint engine as a TRAIT every constrainable type implements: `trait Validate { fn validate(&self, cx: &mut ValidationContext); }` where the body checks each field's declared constraints and pushes `Violation { path, constraint_id, message_key, params, rejected_value_repr }` into the context's violation SET (never fail-on-first). A `#[derive(Validate)]` proc-macro (THIN — emits only what a user could hand-write) reads field-level constraint attributes (`#[validate(not_null, min = 1, email)]`, `#[validate(nested)]` for the `@Valid` cascade) and emits the field-by-field checks plus, for `#[validate(nested)]` fields, a recursive `child.validate(cx.enter("field_name"))` call that pushes a path segment. Each built-in constraint (`NotNull`, `Min`, `Email`, `Size`, `Pattern`) is an ordinary value-level checker `fn(&T, &Params) -> Option<Violation>` living in leaf-core; custom constraints are user fns/types referenced the same way (the JSR-303 `@Constraint` extensibility, but as plain code the macro calls — no reflection). Cross-field/class-level constraints are a type-level `#[validate(with = my_check_fn)]` that receives `&Self`. The 'engine' is then almost nothing: a tiny recursive driver + the ValidationContext (path stack + violation accumulator + a handle to messages-i18n for message resolution). FACE (2) METHOD VALIDATION: a `MethodValidationInterceptor` (Role::Infrastructure advisor contributed into the auto-proxy-creator's stream) wraps the call; its pointcut matches methods on `@Validated` beans; it calls `arg.validate(cx)` on each `#[valid]`-marked argument before proceed() and on the return after, raising a `ConstraintViolationError` carrying the whole violation set if non-empty. Because the interceptor sees TYPED arguments (the typed-vs-erased need this feature surfaces to aop-proxying), it can call the concrete `Validate::validate` directly — or, at the erased dyn boundary, go through a `dyn Validate` object-safe shim (the trait is object-safe: no generics, no async in the hot validate path). FACE (3) CONFIG BINDING: the binder, after producing a typed config value, calls the same `Validate::validate` and converts the violation set into a startup `BindValidationError` that maps each `Violation.path` back to the originating PROPERTY KEY + origin (collaborating with relaxed-binding's path model and origin-tracking).

**Pros:** Maximally honors thin-macros (§2.10) and hand-writability: `#[derive(Validate)]` emits a plain `fn validate` a user could write by hand; `cargo expand` shows exactly the field checks; a user can hand-implement `Validate` for a foreign type with zero macro magic. The engine is genuinely 'almost nothing' — a recursive driver — so there is no hidden subsystem.; Zero-reflection by construction and the cleanest fit for Rust: constraints are compiled into direct field accesses (`self.email`), so there is no runtime object-graph reflection, no `dyn Any` field walking, no per-field metadata lookup — the validation of a type is monomorphized, branch-predictable code. Lowest steady-state overhead (§2 low-overhead #2) for the method-validation hot-ish path.; The `@Valid` cascade is trivially and statically expressed as a recursive `child.validate(cx.enter(...))` — the object-graph descent JSR-303 does reflectively becomes ordinary generated recursion with a path-pushing context, and cycles (a validated graph with a back-edge) are caught structurally (a visited-set in the context) rather than infinite-looping.; Unification across both faces is structural and obvious: face (2) interceptor and face (3) binder both call the identical `Validate::validate` — ONE engine, ONE violation model, ONE message-resolution path (§2.11). The 'reused by config-properties binding' intent is satisfied by literally calling the same trait method, no adapter.; Object-safe `dyn Validate` slots cleanly into the erased registry substrate (the deferred type-erasure keystone) and is ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC: a WASM-module-sourced value that implements (or has a registered) `dyn Validate` validates identically — no assumption of native construction, no central registry needed for the engine itself.; Rich diagnostics fall out: the derive emits the constraint_id, the field path, and a stable message_key per check, so a violation carries path + constraint + rejected-value-repr + a parameterized i18n message — the full §2.9 causal payload — assembled at the exact field, not reconstructed from reflection.

**Cons:** Constraint vocabulary is fixed at the derive call-site: a custom constraint authored in a DOWNSTREAM crate can be USED (it's just a fn the macro calls), but the derive macro must be able to RESOLVE the constraint's path at expansion — there is no runtime 'discover all registered constraints' query, so a constraint that needs runtime-pluggable selection (e.g. chosen by config) doesn't fit the static-derive model and must fall back to a type-level `with = fn`. Less dynamic than JSR-303's runtime constraint registry.; No central place to ask 'what constraints does type T have?' as DATA — the constraints are baked into generated code, not introspectable.
