This collides with config-metadata (the self-documenting config-property surface): emitting 'this property must be a valid email, min 1' into the metadata needs the constraints AS DATA at build time, which the derive would have to ALSO emit as a separate descriptor — duplicating the constraint declaration into both code and metadata (a pre-judgement of the metadata/codegen boundary, §3).; The typed-argument-access need for method validation (the interceptor must see `&ConcreteArg` to call its `Validate`) presses HARD on aop-proxying's erased-vs-typed keystone: if the proxy chain passes arguments type-erased (`&dyn Any` / a boxed arg tuple), the interceptor needs either a `dyn Validate` upcast or a per-method generated validation shim — so this realization implicitly leans on the proxy mechanism preserving enough type information, pre-judging part of aop-proxying's deferred decision.; Cross-field / class-level constraints (`@ScriptAssert`, password-match) are second-class here: they're a type-level `with = fn` escape hatch rather than first-class declarative constraints, so the DX for cross-field validation is lower than JSR-303's (which treats class-level constraints uniformly). A real ergonomic gap for a common case.; Group/sequence validation (JSR-303 validation groups — validate subset A at one phase, subset B at another) has no natural home: `validate(&self)` validates EVERYTHING. Supporting groups would require threading a group selector through the context and tagging each generated check, complicating the 'almost nothing' engine and the thin derive.; Message i18n resolution is per-violation at validate time, which means the validate path touches messages-i18n (a bean) — so the ValidationContext carries a resolver handle, lightly coupling the pure validate fn to the container; alternatively messages resolve LATER (store message_key+params, resolve at error-formatting), which is cleaner but defers the question of where resolution happens.

**Mechanisms considered:** `trait Validate { fn validate(&self, cx: &mut ValidationContext); }` — object-safe, sync, no generics, pushes Violations into an accumulating context (collect-all, not fail-first); `#[derive(Validate)]` THIN proc-macro emitting per-field constraint checks + `#[validate(nested)]` → recursive `child.validate(cx.enter(seg))` for the @Valid cascade; built-in constraints as plain value-level checker fns in leaf-core (NotNull/Min/Email/Size/Pattern); custom constraints as user fns the macro references by path (the @Constraint extensibility, no reflection); cross-field/class-level constraints as a type-level `#[validate(with = fn)]` receiving &Self; ValidationContext = path stack + violation Vec/Set + visited-set (cycle guard) + optional messages-i18n resolver handle; MethodValidationInterceptor (Role::Infrastructure advisor) calling `arg.validate(cx)` pre-proceed + return post-proceed, via concrete dispatch or a `dyn Validate` upcall at the erased boundary; config-binding face: binder calls the same `Validate::validate`, maps Violation.path → property key + origin into a startup BindValidationError

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Strong cross-crate story BECAUSE there is no central registry to populate: a type's `Validate` impl travels WITH the type in its defining crate (the derive expands locally, like `#[derive(Serialize)]`), so there is NO link-time collection, NO DCE hazard, and NO 'unreferenced crate contributes nothing' problem for the ENGINE — if you can name the type, you can validate it. This sidesteps the entire cross-crate-composition fragility for validation's core. The METHOD-VALIDATION advisor is the ONLY part that needs cross-crate collection, and it rides the auto-proxy-creator's already-designed advisor-descriptor stream (so the anti-DCE self-check obligation is inherited from THAT feature, not re-invented here): one MethodValidationInterceptor descriptor, contributed when method-validation is enabled, carries the DCE risk that a never-referenced validation-enabling crate silently disables `@Validated` — covered by the auto-proxy-creator's 'advisors discovered' introspection + `leaf doctor`. Custom constraints contributed by downstream crates are just fns/types referenced by path at the derive site — no registration, no DCE, but the derive macro (single-invocation scope, line 89) can only reference constraints whose PATH is given in the attribute, never 'discover all constraints in the workspace.' Generics: a generic `Foo<T>` deriving Validate is FINE here (unlike linkme registration, line 153) because the impl is monomorphized at each use — a genuine advantage of the per-type-impl model over a registry model. The metadata/codegen tension (config-metadata wants constraints AS DATA): the derive would need to ALSO emit a const constraint-descriptor for tooling, which either duplicates or forces a build-time codegen path — a Phase-2 boundary question. The two-source duality: a static native type and a dynamic WASM-sourced value both validate via `dyn Validate`, so the engine is origin-agnostic, but a WASM module's constraints would have to be expressed as a `dyn Validate` impl at the boundary (or a runtime constraint interpreter for that source — R2's model), so this realization handles the static source natively and needs an adapter for a truly dynamic constraint source.

**T2 — execution context:** Validation runs SYNC and is CPU-bound — `Validate::validate` is a pure, non-blocking, non-async tree walk (no `.await`), which is the right call: validation is fast field checks, and making it async would pessimize the common case (charter §2.3 execution-context discipline — validation declares itself a sync, non-blocking computation). FACE (2) method validation: the interceptor's pre-proceed argument validation and post-proceed return validation run ON the calling task's context, INSIDE the interceptor chain hop (which is itself a boxed-future hop per the dyn-chain reality, nightly-doc lines 160/442) — but the validate CALL within that hop is sync, so it adds no extra await point; a violation SHORT-CIRCUITS (the interceptor returns the error WITHOUT calling proceed(), so the real method never runs — fail-fast at method entry), and the error propagates back up the chain as a normal `Result::Err` carrying the violation set (no panic, no cancellation needed). FACE (3) config binding: validation runs on the COLD startup/refresh path (refresh-lifecycle), single-threaded-conceptually, before eager singletons that depend on the config — a violation aborts refresh cleanly (all-or-nothing, no partially-built graph), surfacing as a startup BindValidationError. If a CUSTOM constraint needs to do async/blocking work (e.g. validate a value against a remote service — rare and arguably an anti-pattern), it CANNOT in this sync model — it must be done as a separate post-validation check or via a different (async) validation entry point, a deliberate restriction that keeps the common path fast and is documented. Message i18n resolution, if deferred to error-formatting time, runs wherever the error is rendered (cold path), avoiding touching messages-i18n on the validate hot path. No global locks: the ValidationContext is a stack-local, per-call structure (charter §2.5) — no shared mutable validation state.

#### R2 — Central constraint-descriptor registry + a reflective-over-declared-metadata Validator engine: types contribute a `ConstraintSet` descriptor, the engine interprets it generically (the closest JSR-303-provider analogue)
Question whether validation logic should be COMPILED PER TYPE (R1) or DATA INTERPRETED BY ONE ENGINE (the JSR-303 mental model: a provider walks metadata). Here a `#[derive(Validate)]` (or `#[constraints(...)]` attribute) emits, for each type, a CONST `ConstraintSet` DESCRIPTOR — pure data: `&[FieldConstraints { field_name, accessor: fn(&ErasedValue) -> ConstraintValue, constraints: &[ConstraintRef], cascade: bool }]` plus type-level cross-field constraints. A SINGLE `Validator` engine (the provider analogue, a context bean) takes a value + its ConstraintSet and INTERPRETS it: for each field it pulls the value via the generated accessor fn-ptr (the rust-native replacement for reflective field access — accessor is generated code, not reflection), runs each referenced constraint checker, and for `cascade` fields looks up the NESTED type's ConstraintSet and recurses, threading the path. Constraint CHECKERS themselves are registered in a `ConstraintRegistry` (built at refresh): each `ConstraintDescriptor { id, role, check: fn(&ConstraintValue, &Params, &mut Cx) }` — built-ins shipped by leaf-core, custom constraints contributed cross-crate via the SAME descriptor-collection substrate the rest of leaf uses (linkme/codegen/explicit — candidate). This makes the constraint VOCABULARY a first-class, introspectable, runtime-extensible registry (true JSR-303 `@Constraint` parity: a new constraint type is discovered, not hard-referenced). Validation GROUPS are natural: each FieldConstraints/ConstraintRef carries a group tag, and `validator.validate(value, set, groups)` filters — so face (2)/(3) can request different groups (the JSR-303 group-sequence feature). FACE (2): the MethodValidationInterceptor holds the Validator + looks up the argument type's ConstraintSet (keyed by TypeId) and validates erased — no need for the interceptor to know the concrete type, only its TypeId + ConstraintSet, which dovetails with aop-proxying passing arguments erased. FACE (3): the binder asks the same Validator for the config type's ConstraintSet. The 'constraints as data' descriptor ALSO directly feeds config-metadata (the self-documenting surface) — ONE constraint declaration, consumed by both the validator AND the metadata emitter.

**Pros:** Constraints-as-data is the strongest fit for config-metadata reuse: the SAME `ConstraintSet` descriptor that the validator interprets is what config-metadata emits into the self-documenting property surface ('this property must be a valid email, ≥1') — ONE declaration, two consumers, no duplication, directly serving the 'config is a discoverable API' intent (lines 1618/1622). R1 must duplicate; R2 unifies.; True JSR-303 `@Constraint` extensibility parity: the constraint VOCABULARY is a runtime-extensible REGISTRY, so a downstream crate can contribute a wholly new constraint type that is DISCOVERED (not hard-referenced at a derive site), exactly mirroring how JSR-303 lets libraries ship constraint annotations + validators — the most faithful to Spring's 'custom logic looks like every other constraint' intent.; Validation GROUPS and group-sequences (a real JSR-303 feature R1 fumbles) are first-class: a group tag on each constraint ref + a group filter in the validate call. This matters for the method-validation face where Spring's `@Validated(GroupA.class)` selects groups per method — R2 supports it cleanly, R1 cannot without contorting the derive.; The erased validate path suits the proxy's erased-argument reality: the interceptor validates by (TypeId → ConstraintSet) lookup over an erased value via generated accessors, so it needs NO concrete-type knowledge — a cleaner fit for aop-proxying passing arguments type-erased than R1's need-the-concrete-type model, reducing pressure on the erased-vs-typed keystone.; One central `Validator` engine + `ConstraintRegistry` is the literal 'provider' analogue, so the Spring mental model ('a Bean Validation provider validates against declared constraints') transfers exactly, and the provider is REPLACEABLE (a user can supply a different Validator bean — the escape hatch, §2.10) and INTROSPECTABLE (you can ask the registry 'what constraints exist', 'what does type T declare').; Origin-agnostic and dynamic-source-friendly: a ConstraintSet is plain data with fn-ptr accessors/checkers, so a WASM-module-sourced type could contribute a ConstraintSet at runtime (the dynamic 'classpath') and be validated by the SAME engine — the smoothest of the three for the two-source duality, since the engine already interprets data rather than calling compiled-in impls.

**Cons:** The accessor fn-ptr per field + the descriptor indirection is HEAVIER than R1's monomorphized direct field access: every field validation is an indirect call through a fn-ptr that erases then re-reads the value (`fn(&ErasedValue) -> ConstraintValue`), plus a registry lookup per constraint — measurably more overhead than R1 on the method-validation path (cuts against §2 low-overhead #2, though validation is rarely the bottleneck).; The `ConstraintRegistry` (custom constraints collected cross-crate) re-introduces the FULL DCE / link-time-collection hazard for the constraint VOCABULARY: a custom-constraint crate the binary never path-references silently contributes no constraint, so a `#[validate(my_custom)]` reference would fail to resolve its checker at runtime — needing the anti-DCE self-check (line 152) AND a way to detect 'constraint id referenced but not registered.' R1 avoids this entirely (constraints are hard-referenced fns).; Generics-can't-register (line 153/349) bites: a generic `Foo<T>`'s ConstraintSet can't be a single registered const descriptor (no monomorphized instantiation for the registry to point at) — so generic constrainable types need per-instantiation descriptor emission (a macro hard-error otherwise), a real limitation R1 doesn't have (R1's per-type impl monomorphizes naturally).; The ErasedValue / accessor model entangles validation with the type-erasure & registry substrate (the deferred keystone, §3): how a value is erased, how the accessor reads it back, and how TypeId-keying works all pre-judge that decision — presenting this as a realization biases the erasure signal, so it must stay strictly a CANDIDATE.; More machinery for a conceptually simple job: a central engine + registry + descriptor format + accessor codegen is a SUBSYSTEM, cutting against the 'almost nothing engine' simplicity of R1 and risking over-engineering validation relative to its weight. The thin-macro invariant is harder to honor — the derive emits opaque descriptor data + accessor fns, which is less hand-writable/legible in `cargo expand` than R1's plain field checks.; Cross-crate constraint id collisions / versioning: constraint ids in a shared registry are a global namespace, so two crates shipping a constraint named 'positive' collide — needs namespacing discipline the registry must enforce, a coordination cost R1's path-based references don't incur.

**Mechanisms considered:** per-type CONST `ConstraintSet` descriptor (field accessors as fn-ptrs + constraint refs + cascade flags + group tags) emitted by a derive — constraints as DATA, not compiled checks; a single `Validator` engine bean that INTERPRETS a ConstraintSet against an (erased) value, recursing on cascade fields via TypeId→ConstraintSet lookup (the JSR-303 provider analogue); a `ConstraintRegistry` of `ConstraintDescriptor { id, check: fn-ptr }` built at refresh, collecting built-ins + cross-crate custom constraints via the shared link-time/codegen/explicit substrate (candidate); validation GROUPS as a tag on each constraint ref + a group filter in `validate(value, set, groups)` — JSR-303 group-sequence parity; TypeId-keyed ConstraintSet lookup so the method interceptor validates ERASED arguments without concrete-type knowledge; the SAME ConstraintSet descriptor feeding config-metadata's self-documenting property surface (one declaration, two consumers); origin-agnostic dynamic source: a WASM-module type contributes a ConstraintSet at runtime, validated by the same engine

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** This is the T1-heaviest of the three because it RE-INTRODUCES cross-crate collection for the constraint VOCABULARY. The per-type ConstraintSet descriptor itself can travel with the type (like R1, emitted locally — no DCE for the per-type metadata IF it's referenced via the type), but the CUSTOM-CONSTRAINT-CHECKER registry is a genuine cross-crate registry with the full hazard set: a custom constraint contributed by a sibling crate must register its `ConstraintDescriptor` via whatever Phase-2 substrate is chosen (linkme distributed slice / inventory / build-time codegen / explicit) and is subject to Layer-0 (unreferenced crate = no registration, line 149), `--gc-sections` (line 143), and silent-empty-iterable failure (line 152) — so a `#[validate(my_custom)]` whose checker crate isn't force-linked would resolve to 'no such constraint' at runtime. MANDATORY anti-DCE self-check: assert that every constraint-id REFERENCED by some ConstraintSet is PRESENT in the registry (a startup cross-check turning silent DCE into a loud error), plus the 'discovered constraints by crate' introspection for `leaf doctor`. Ordering-as-data: constraint evaluation order within a field must be deterministic (an order field on the ref), never link order (line 154). Generics: generic constrainable types need per-instantiation ConstraintSet registration (line 153) — macro hard-error otherwise. The binary/app-root is where the full constraint vocabulary is visible (line 260), so the registry assembly anchors there. The metadata/codegen boundary: because constraints are ALREADY data here, config-metadata generation is a clean second consumer of the SAME descriptors (a genuine T1 win over R1) — but whether those descriptors are emitted by proc-macro (visible only to its own invocation, line 89) or by a build-time codegen pass that sees the resolved graph is the Phase-2 metadata/codegen decision. Two-source duality (line 362): the static link-time constraint vocabulary PLUS an optional dynamic (WASM-module) source contributing ConstraintSets/checkers at runtime both feed the SAME engine — the cleanest blend of the three, since the engine interprets data regardless of origin.

**T2 — execution context:** Engine `Validator::validate` is SYNC and CPU-bound like R1 (no `.await` on the validate path — fast field checks via accessor fn-ptrs), and the same execution-context discipline applies: validation declares itself sync/non-blocking; a custom constraint that wants async/blocking work is disallowed on this path (must be a separate async check). The CONSTRAINT-REGISTRY BUILD is cold-path at refresh (refresh-lifecycle): collect descriptors, build the TypeId→ConstraintSet map and the id→checker map, single-conceptual-thread, before eager singletons — descriptor collection is const data, no `.await`; the anti-DCE self-check runs here and aborts refresh with a rich error if a referenced constraint is missing. FACE (2) method validation runs on the caller's task inside the interceptor chain hop (a boxed-future hop, lines 160/442), but the validate call itself is sync (no added await point); a violation short-circuits proceed() and propagates as a `Result::Err` carrying the violation set — fail-fast at method entry, no panic, cancellation-transparent (the future simply resolves to Err). FACE (3) config binding runs on the cold startup/refresh path; a violation aborts refresh all-or-nothing. The accessor fn-ptrs and checker fn-ptrs are called SYNC on whatever context drives validate; because they read an erased value, they must be `Send + Sync` if the validated value might cross threads (e.g. a singleton config validated on the refresh context but conceptually shared) — a constraint the descriptor ABI must encode. No global locks: the registry is read-only after refresh-build (immutable shared, no lock on the validate path, charter §2.5); per-call state is a stack-local context. Message resolution deferred to error-format time (cold path) keeps the validate path free of messages-i18n.

#### R3 — Validation as a typed parse/Result boundary (parse-don't-validate) + a unifying `Valid<T>` newtype, with the method/binding faces reusing the type-conversion failure channel rather than a bespoke violation set
Question whether validation should be a SEPARATE post-construction pass at all, or whether it should be FUSED with construction/conversion as a fallible typed boundary — the rust-native 'parse, don't validate' idiom, reframing JSR-303's 'validate an already-built object' into 'you cannot build an invalid object.' Shape: constraints are expressed as TYPES via a `Validated<T, C>` / `Valid<T>` smart-newtype family where `C` is a constraint (e.g. `Valid<String, Email>`, `Valid<i32, Min<1>>`) whose ONLY constructor is `Valid::new(raw) -> Result<Self, Violation>` running the check — so a value of type `Valid<String, Email>` is a COMPILE-TIME PROOF that the email constraint held. A `#[derive(Validate)]` still exists for ergonomics on aggregate structs (it generates a `try_from_unvalidated`/`validate` that builds the typed-valid version field by field, ACCUMULATING violations into a Result<Self, Violations>), but the PRIMITIVE is the typed boundary. CRUCIALLY this FUSES with type-conversion: the binder's job becomes 'convert + validate as one fallible coercion' — binding a property into a `Valid<i32, Min<1>>` field runs conversion THEN the constraint in one step, and a failure flows through the SAME `ConversionError`/result channel type-conversion already defines, so the binder doesn't need a separate ValidationBindHandler — validation is just a constraint-carrying conversion target. FACE (3) config binding: a config struct's fields ARE `Valid<...>` types (or the derive produces a validated constructor), so binding either yields a fully-valid config or fails at startup with the accumulated errors — invalid config is UNREPRESENTABLE post-bind. FACE (2) method validation: a `@Validated`-style method declares validated parameter types directly (`fn charge(&self, amt: Valid<Money, Positive>)`) so the COMPILER enforces the caller validated — OR, for retro-fitting onto plain-typed params, the MethodValidationInterceptor (still a Role::Infrastructure advisor) runs the derive-generated validation on arguments at the seam, raising the unified error. The 'violation set' becomes the accumulating-Result the conversion/binding layer already threads.

**Pros:** Deepest unification with type-conversion (§2.11): validation and conversion become ONE fallible-coercion boundary instead of two passes — the binder converts-and-validates in one step through one error channel, eliminating the separate ValidationBindHandler/violation-vs-conversion-error split. The 'reused by config-properties binding' intent becomes 'binding IS validating' — there is no seam to reuse because there is one boundary.; Strongest static guarantee and most rust-native: `Valid<String, Email>` makes invalid state UNREPRESENTABLE — a validated value carries its proof in its TYPE, so downstream code needn't re-check and the COMPILER prevents whole classes of 'forgot to validate' bugs. This is the parse-don't-validate idiom Rust developers expect, raising DX (§2 #1) for type-conscious users above a JVM-style after-the-fact validate.; Method validation can become COMPILE-TIME for free where parameters are typed as `Valid<..>`: the caller must produce a validated value to call, so the constraint is enforced at the type boundary with NO interceptor, NO proxy, NO runtime cost — the richest/earliest diagnostic (§2.9) and zero overhead, for the subset of cases where the API author controls the parameter types. The interceptor becomes the FALLBACK for retrofitting, not the only mechanism.; No central registry and no reflective walk: like R1, constraints are compiled in (each `Valid<T,C>` monomorphizes its check), so zero DCE hazard for the engine, generics-friendly, and origin-agnostic — a value of a validated type is valid regardless of how it was constructed (native or WASM-sourced), because the type's constructor enforced it.; Composition is type-algebraic and elegant: constraints compose via type composition (`Valid<String, All<(NotBlank, Email, MaxLen<255>)>>`), cascade is just nesting validated types, and the constraint set of a value is READABLE from its type signature — self-documenting at the type level, feeding config-metadata from the field TYPES rather than a separate descriptor.; Errors flow through the unified conversion/result channel, so there's ONE error model for 'this string couldn't become this typed value' whether the reason is a parse failure (not a number) or a constraint failure (number too small) — coherent §2.9 diagnostics, and the accumulating-Result naturally collects multiple field failures.

**Cons:** The biggest intent DIVERGENCE from Spring: JSR-303 validates EXISTING objects (you build a POJO then validate it, and the SAME object can be valid for group A and invalid for group B); the `Valid<T,C>` model bakes validity into TYPE IDENTITY, which fights mutation (a validated config you later mutate is no longer provably valid), validation GROUPS (a single value can't simultaneously be `Valid<_, GroupA>` and not), and re-validation. This is arguably BETTER Rust but materially different semantics — a parity risk that must be surfaced, not hidden.; Pervasive `Valid<T,C>` wrapper types are INVASIVE and leak into every signature and the bean graph: a config field typed `Valid<String, Email>` forces every consumer to unwrap or thread the wrapper, and injecting a validated bean means its type is the wrapped type everywhere — high ceremony that cuts against low-ceremony DX (§2 #1) for the common case, and clashes with Spring's 'POJO stays a plain POJO' non-invasiveness (lines 1639/1697 mental model: advice/validation is added AROUND, not baked into the type).; Cross-field / class-level constraints (password-match, end-after-start) DON'T fit the per-field-type model at all — they're inherently about the WHOLE object, so they need the derive's accumulating `validate(&self) -> Result<Self, Violations>` aggregate path, meaning R3 cannot be purely type-level and must ALSO carry R1's struct-level validation — so it's really R1-plus-a-newtype-layer, not a standalone model. Added complexity, two mental models.; Retrofitting method validation onto EXISTING plain-typed APIs (the common `@Validated` case — annotate a bean whose methods take normal types) still needs the full interceptor + derive machinery; the elegant compile-time-via-`Valid<..>`-params path only helps NEW APIs whose authors opt into wrapper-typed parameters. So for the actual Spring parity case (decorate an existing service), R3 collapses back to R1/R2's runtime interceptor, and the headline benefit doesn't apply.; Fusing validation INTO type-conversion pre-judges BOTH the type-conversion design AND that validation should not be independently invokable — but Spring deliberately SEPARATES them (you can validate an already-converted/constructed object without re-converting). Coupling them entangles two features' designs and biases Phase 2; must stay a CANDIDATE, and the 'can I validate without converting' case must still be answerable.; Const-generic constraints (`Min<1>`, `MaxLen<255>`) are limited to const-expressible parameters; constraints with runtime/config-derived parameters (e.g. min length from a property) can't be a const-generic type parameter and must fall back to a runtime-parameterized check — so the type-level model is incomplete and needs a runtime tier anyway, fragmenting the constraint vocabulary across compile-time-typed and runtime-parameterized forms.

**Mechanisms considered:** `Valid<T, C>` / `Validated<T, C>` smart-newtype whose only constructor runs constraint C — validity as a TYPE-LEVEL proof (parse-don't-validate); constraints as types (`Email`, `Min<1>`, `MaxLen<255>`, `All<(..)>` composition); cascade as nested validated types; fusing validation with type-conversion: binder treats a validated field as a constraint-carrying CONVERSION target, failures flow through the unified ConversionError/Result channel (no separate ValidationBindHandler); `#[derive(Validate)]` producing an aggregate `try_validate(self) -> Result<ValidSelf, Violations>` for struct-level + cross-field constraints (the part the type-level model can't express); compile-time method validation where parameters are typed `Valid<..>` (no interceptor); runtime MethodValidationInterceptor as the retrofit FALLBACK for plain-typed params; accumulating-Result as the 'violation set' (collect-all via a Result that gathers multiple field errors); const-generic constraint parameters for static params; runtime-parameterized checks as a fallback tier

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Like R1, the core engine has a MINIMAL cross-crate footprint: `Valid<T,C>` and each constraint type `C` are ordinary types/impls that travel with their defining crate (no registry, no DCE for the engine, generics-friendly since each `Valid<T,C>` monomorphizes). A custom constraint is a user type implementing the constraint trait — referenced by PATH where used, not discovered, so no link-time collection and no silent-empty-registry failure for the vocabulary. The METHOD-VALIDATION interceptor (retrofit path) again rides the auto-proxy-creator's advisor stream, inheriting THAT feature's anti-DCE obligation. The deep T1 entanglement is with TYPE-CONVERSION: fusing the failure channels means validation's cross-crate error type IS type-conversion's, so the two features' error ABIs must co-evolve — a coupling Phase 2 must own. config-metadata can read constraints from field TYPES (the `Valid<_, C>` signatures) at codegen time, but extracting constraint metadata from generic type parameters at build time is harder than reading an explicit descriptor (R2) — the metadata path is type-introspection-driven, which a proc-macro can do for the local crate (line 89) but not across crates for foreign validated types. Generics: a STRENGTH here — `Valid<T,C>` is inherently generic and monomorphizes per use, so unlike R2's registry there's no generics-can't-register wall (line 153). Two-source duality: a WASM-sourced value is valid-by-type if constructed through a `Valid` constructor, but a dynamic source that wants to apply NEW constraints at runtime can't synthesize a new `Valid<T,C>` type at runtime (types are static) — so R3 is the WEAKEST of the three for a truly dynamic constraint source (it would need R2's runtime-interpreted descriptors as an adjunct).

**T2 — execution context:** Construction-time validation (`Valid::new`) is SYNC and CPU-bound — it runs wherever the value is constructed (binder/refresh cold path for config, or the caller's context for a method parameter), no `.await`, non-blocking, same discipline as R1/R2 (a constraint wanting async work doesn't fit and must be a separate check). The headline T2 property: where validation is COMPILE-TIME (typed `Valid<..>` parameters), there is NO runtime execution context at all — the check ran at the construction site, possibly far from the method, which is the earliest/cheapest point (§2.9) but means the violation surfaces at CONSTRUCTION, not at method entry (a different — arguably better — diagnostic LOCATION than Spring's at-the-proxy-seam, and one the developer must understand). FACE (3) config binding: convert-and-validate fused on the cold startup path; failures abort refresh all-or-nothing through the unified Result channel. FACE (2) retrofit interceptor: identical to R1/R2 — sync validate inside a boxed-future chain hop, violation short-circuits proceed() and propagates as Err, fail-fast at method entry. The accumulating-Result threads multiple field errors without shared mutable state (each field's check returns into the accumulator on the local stack — no locks, charter §2.5). Cancellation-transparent (validation is sync and fast; a cancelled task simply doesn't reach the validate point or its Result is dropped). Because validity is type-encoded, there's no re-validation cost on the hot path — a `Valid<T,C>` value needs no re-check when passed around, a steady-state overhead win, at the cost of the construction-site-validation timing shift.

### Rust clashes
- No runtime reflection to discover constraints or walk the object graph — JSR-303 providers (Hibernate Validator) reflectively read constraint annotations off fields/getters/parameters at runtime AND reflectively traverse the object graph for the `@Valid` cascade. Rust has neither: there is no runtime annotation metadata and no reflective field enumeration. So constraints must be captured AS DATA at macro-expansion time (a derive reads `#[validate(...)]` attributes) and the object-graph walk must be GENERATED RECURSION (a `Validate` impl that calls into nested fields' impls) or descriptor-driven accessor fn-ptrs — never a reflective walk. The matchable/validatable surface is fixed at compile time, not reflectively open: you can only validate what the derive/descriptor captured, which is cleaner and faster but means the constraint vocabulary visible to a value is whatever was declared, not 'anything reflection can find.'
- No runtime classpath / ServiceLoader to discover the validation PROVIDER or custom constraints — Spring/JSR-303 finds the Bean Validation provider and custom `@Constraint` validators via the classpath (`META-INF/services`, classpath scanning). Rust has no classpath: a central constraint-vocabulary registry (R2) must be synthesized from link-time registration (linkme/inventory, with the silent-DCE hazard: an unreferenced custom-constraint crate contributes nothing and a `#[validate(my_custom)]` silently resolves to no-such-constraint) or build-time codegen or explicit registration — carrying the anti-DCE self-check obligation. The per-type-impl realizations (R1/R3) sidestep this by referencing constraints by PATH (compiled in, no discovery), trading runtime extensibility for cross-crate reliability — a genuine tension with JSR-303's 'custom constraints are discovered like any other.'
- Violations as a COLLECTED SET with property paths, not exceptions thrown at first failure — JSR-303 returns a `Set<ConstraintViolation>` each carrying a `Path` (`address.zip`), the constraint descriptor, the invalid value, and a parameterized message; validation does NOT stop at the first violation. Rust's idiomatic `Result<T, E>` short-circuits on the first `?`, so leaf must DELIBERATELY use an ACCUMULATING pattern (a violation Vec/Set threaded through a context, or an accumulating Result/Validation applicative) to collect ALL violations before failing — the opposite of `?`-based early return. This is a real idiom clash: the rich-diagnostics invariant (§2.9, 'candidates considered', full causal chain) DEMANDS collect-all, but Rust's ergonomics pull toward fail-first — leaf must design the accumulator and resist the `?` reflex.
- Property PATHS must be synthesized as data, not derived from a reflective walk — Spring's violation paths (`order.items[2].price`) come from the reflective traversal knowing field names and collection indices. In Rust there's no reflective walk, so paths must be built by GENERATED code pushing path segments into a context (`cx.enter("items").index(2).enter("price")`) at each recursion/iteration step — the derive must emit the field NAME as a string literal and the cascade recursion must thread the index. Field names become DATA the macro captures (and must stay stable across refactors or paths silently change), and for the config-binding face these paths must map back to the canonical kebab-case PROPERTY KEY (collaborating with relaxed-binding's path model + origin-tracking), not just the Rust field name — a path-translation concern with no JVM analogue (Spring's binder already speaks property paths).
- The method-validation interceptor needs TYPED argument access through an erased proxy chain — Spring's `MethodValidationInterceptor` gets the `Object[] args` and reflectively validates each against its parameter constraints. leaf's interceptor runs in a dyn-dispatched chain hop (the AdviceChain), where arguments are likely type-erased (boxed/`dyn Any`/an erased tuple) — so to validate a concrete argument the interceptor must either upcall a `dyn Validate` (R1, needs the trait object-safe and the arg to carry it), look up a TypeId→ConstraintSet and read the erased value via accessor fn-ptrs (R2), or the parameter is already a `Valid<..>` type (R3, validated before the call). This presses on aop-proxying's deferred erased-vs-typed keystone: how much type information survives to the interceptor determines which validation mechanism is even possible — a cross-feature entanglement Spring doesn't have (reflection sees everything regardless).
- Validation is synchronous CPU work in an async-first framework — JVM validation is just synchronous method calls. leaf is async-first (§2.3), but validation is fast field-checking that should NOT be async (making it async would pessimize the common path and add await points). So leaf must DECLARE validation a sync, non-blocking computation that runs inside an async interceptor hop without itself awaiting — and explicitly handle the rare custom-constraint-wants-IO case (validate against a remote service) as OUT of the sync path (a separate async check), a deliberate execution-context boundary (§2.3) the JVM never had to draw because it has no async/sync split.
- i18n message resolution couples validation to messages-i18n and the locale-context — JSR-303 messages are templates (`{javax.validation.constraints.Email.message}`) resolved against a `MessageInterpolator` with the current `Locale`. leaf's equivalent must resolve a message_key + params against messages-i18n using the AMBIENT locale (locale-context) — but the ambient locale is a per-execution holder that, in async leaf, must survive `.await` (the context-propagation problem). So 'what locale is this violation's message in?' is entangled with context-propagation in a way Spring's ThreadLocal-backed LocaleContextHolder hides; leaf likely defers message resolution to error-FORMATTING time (storing key+params in the violation) to avoid touching the locale holder on the validate path — a structural divergence from inline interpolation.
- Validation GROUPS / group-sequences assume a value can be validated against DIFFERENT constraint subsets at different times — JSR-303 groups (`@Validated(Create.class)` vs `@Validated(Update.class)`) validate the SAME object against different constraint subsets. This needs constraints tagged by group and a group selector at validate time (natural in R2's descriptor model, awkward in R1's compile-everything derive, and fundamentally at odds with R3's validity-as-type-identity where a value can't simultaneously be valid-for-group-A and not). There's no clean Rust type-level encoding of 'this object satisfies group A's constraints but not group B's' without either runtime group filtering (giving up static guarantees) or a combinatorial explosion of marker-typed wrappers — a genuine expressiveness gap leaf must decide how far to honor.

### Interactions
- **auto-proxy-creator** — UPSTREAM dependency for the method-validation face. The `MethodValidationInterceptor` is contributed as a Role::Infrastructure ADVISOR into the auto-proxy-creator's already-designed advisor-collection stream (the `@Validated`/`MethodValidationInterceptor` analogue, lines 1705/1712). The auto-proxy-creator COLLECTS it, MATCHES its pointcut against `@Validated`-marked beans/methods, and ORDERS it (role-tier-first) into the single shared interceptor chain. This feature OWNS only the validation interceptor's behavior + pointcut (match validation-marked methods) + the typed-argument-access NEED it surfaces; it does NOT own collection, matching, ordering, or proxy installation. The Role::Infrastructure tag keeps validation advice isolated from application aspects, exactly per the auto-proxy-creator's role-isolation design.
- **aop-proxying** — INDIRECT downstream (via auto-proxy-creator). The validation interceptor runs as one hop in aop-proxying's AdviceChain. CRITICAL TENSION this feature surfaces to aop-proxying: method validation needs TYPED access to method arguments (to run their constraints), but the chain may pass arguments ERASED — so aop-proxying's deferred erased-vs-typed keystone directly determines which validation mechanism (R1 dyn-upcall / R2 TypeId-descriptor / R3 pre-typed-params) is viable. This feature does NOT decide the proxy mechanism; it states the typed-argument requirement as input to that Phase-2 decision.
- **config-properties** — PRIMARY non-AOP consumer and the INTENT's headline reuse ('reused by config-properties binding', lines 1614/1616). After config-properties binds a property tree onto a typed config object, it invokes THIS feature's engine (the `ValidationBindHandler` analogue) to validate the bound object, FAILING FAST AT STARTUP on violation with a `BindValidationError` that names the offending property. The SAME engine serves both faces — this is the unification the feature embodies (§2.11). `@Valid` cascades into nested config objects via the engine's cascade mechanism.
- **binder** — The binder is the programmatic engine config-properties uses, and it is the precise integration POINT for the config-validation face: the binder (or a validation BindHandler riding it) calls this feature's engine on the bound result, and in R3 specifically the binder FUSES validation with conversion (validate-as-a-constraint-carrying-conversion-target). The binder owns binding + the result monad; this feature owns the constraint check that the binder invokes and the violation→property-key mapping.
- **type-conversion** — DEEPLY related: conversion coerces strings→typed values; validation checks the typed value satisfies constraints. They are SEQUENTIAL in config binding (convert then validate) and in R3 are FUSED into one fallible-coercion boundary sharing ONE error channel. Even in R1/R2 they should share the error/diagnostics vocabulary (a conversion failure and a constraint failure are both 'this input couldn't become this valid typed value'). Tension: fusing them (R3) maximizes unification but pre-judges type-conversion's design and Spring's deliberate separation — a Phase-2 question.
- **origin-tracking** — For the config-validation face, a violation's diagnostic must name not just the Rust field/property path but the ORIGIN (file/line, owning source) of the offending value — so this feature's violation→property-key mapping collaborates with origin-tracking to produce 'app.timeout must be ≥1 (was 0, from application.yaml line 12)'. Origin-tracking supplies the provenance that makes the fail-fast startup error fully actionable (§2.9).
- **relaxed-binding** — Violation property PATHS (built as data during the engine's cascade walk, in Rust field-name terms) must map back to the canonical kebab-case property KEY that the user actually wrote — relaxed-binding owns the canonical-key↔source-form mapping, so this feature must translate its internal field-path into relaxed-binding's canonical key for the config-validation error message. Without this, the error names the Rust field, not the property the user can fix.
- **messages-i18n** — Violation messages are parameterized, i18n-resolvable templates (the `MessageInterpolator` analogue). This feature stores a message_key + params on each violation and resolves them against messages-i18n's MessageSource — likely DEFERRED to error-formatting time (not on the validate path) to avoid coupling the sync validate hot path to a bean lookup. The localized, parameterized message is what makes violations 'descriptive' per the intent.
- **locale-context** — Message resolution needs the AMBIENT locale (which language for this violation's message). locale-context supplies 'the current locale' as a per-execution holder. This couples to context-propagation in async leaf (the locale must survive .await) — a reason this feature likely defers message resolution to format-time and lets the formatter read the ambient locale then, rather than capturing it during validation.
- **config-metadata** — config-metadata emits the self-documenting config-property surface; constraints are part of that surface ('this property must be a valid email, ≥1'). This feature's constraint declarations should FEED config-metadata. R2 (constraints-as-data descriptors) unifies this cleanly (one declaration, two consumers); R1/R3 must additionally surface constraints as build-time data for the metadata emitter — a metadata/codegen-boundary tension (§3).
- **refresh-lifecycle** — Drives WHEN config validation happens: the config-properties validation runs at a fixed refresh point (after binding, before eager singletons that depend on the config), and a violation aborts refresh ALL-OR-NOTHING (no partially-built graph published) — the fail-fast-at-startup intent realized as a clean refresh abort with a rich error. The constraint-vocabulary registry build (R2) is also a cold-path refresh step.
- **transaction-management** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor in the SAME shared interceptor chain. Validation and transaction advice are both Role::Infrastructure advisors the auto-proxy-creator orders into one chain; their relative ORDER matters (validate arguments BEFORE opening a transaction — you don't want to start a tx for a call that will fail validation). This feature contributes its advisor with an order that positions validation appropriately relative to tx/cache/retry — a correctness-ordering concern the auto-proxy-creator's deterministic sort resolves.
- **caching** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor. Ordering relative to validation matters: typically validate arguments before consulting the cache (don't serve/populate a cache entry for an invalid call). Like tx, this is positioned via the advisor order the auto-proxy-creator sorts deterministically within the single shared chain.
- **retry-resilience** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor. A ConstraintViolationError from validation should generally NOT be retried (invalid input won't become valid on retry) — so the ordering (validate outside/before retry, or retry's policy excluding validation errors) is a correctness concern. This feature's error type should be classifiable so retry-resilience's policy can treat validation failures as non-retryable; ordering is resolved by the auto-proxy-creator.
- **expression-language** — POTENTIAL relationship for cross-field/class-level constraints: JSR-303's `@ScriptAssert`/`@AssertTrue` and Spring's expression-driven constraints evaluate an expression over the object. leaf's expression-language could back expression-style cross-field constraints (`#[validate(assert = "end > start")]`) — but this couples constraint authoring to the expression engine; the alternative (R1's plain `with = fn`) avoids it. A candidate, not a requirement.
- **component-stereotypes** — The `@Validated` marker (and per-method/parameter validation markers) is INERT metadata on a bean/method, analogous to how stereotypes mark beans — the annotation-model/stereotype machinery is how `@Validated` is recognized and how the method-validation pointcut identifies eligible beans. This feature defines the validation markers; the annotation/stereotype model carries them as composable metadata.
- **concurrency-contract** — Validated config objects are typically singletons (shared, immutable post-validation). The concurrency-contract guarantees safe publication of fully-built singletons — a config object validated at refresh and then shared must be safely published; this feature's validate-at-construction (esp. R3's validity-as-type) reinforces that a published config is provably valid, and the validator's descriptor fn-ptrs (R2) must be Send+Sync if the validated value crosses threads.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- WHERE do constraints live — COMPILED PER TYPE as generated check code (R1/R3, monomorphized, zero-DCE, generics-friendly, not introspectable) or as a CENTRAL DATA DESCRIPTOR + interpreting engine (R2, introspectable, feeds config-metadata, JSR-303-provider-faithful, but re-introduces the cross-crate-registry DCE hazard and generics-can't-register wall)? This cross-cuts the metadata/codegen boundary AND the type-erasure/registry substrate (§3, two keystones): the descriptor model pre-judges toward a registry, the per-type model toward local derives. Held unresolved.
- Is the constraint VOCABULARY a runtime-extensible REGISTRY (R2 — custom constraints discovered cross-crate like JSR-303 `@Constraint`, with the silent-DCE hazard) or a set of compiled-in, path-referenced checkers (R1/R3 — reliable cross-crate, generics-friendly, but a constraint must be NAMED at the derive site, not discovered)? This is the JSR-303-extensibility-vs-Rust-reliability tradeoff and it cross-cuts the cross-crate composition model + the anti-DCE self-check mandate.
- Should validation be FUSED with type-conversion into one fallible-coercion boundary (R3 — maximal unification, one error channel, binding-IS-validating) or remain a SEPARATE pass over an already-constructed/converted value (R1/R2, Spring's deliberate separation, independently invokable)? Fusing pre-judges type-conversion's design and biases Phase 2; separation keeps 'validate without converting' answerable. Cross-cuts type-conversion's design and the unified error model.
- How much TYPE INFORMATION survives to the method-validation interceptor through the (deferred) proxy mechanism, and which validation dispatch does that permit — `dyn Validate` upcall (R1), TypeId→ConstraintSet descriptor lookup over an erased value (R2), or pre-typed `Valid<..>` parameters (R3)? This is entirely downstream of aop-proxying's erased-vs-typed keystone; validation can't pick its method-face mechanism until that's decided.
- WHERE and WHEN are violation MESSAGES resolved against messages-i18n + the ambient locale — inline at validate time (couples the sync validate path to a bean lookup + the locale holder, which must survive .await per context-propagation) or DEFERRED to error-formatting time (store key+params, resolve when rendering)? Cross-cuts messages-i18n, locale-context, and the context-propagation model.
- How are validation GROUPS / group-sequences (validate the same value against different constraint subsets at different phases — `@Validated(Create.class)`) realized, if at all? Natural as a runtime group-filter in R2's descriptor model, awkward in R1's compile-everything derive, and fundamentally at odds with R3's validity-as-type-identity. Is full group parity worth the cost, or is it a deliberately-dropped Spring feature? Cross-cuts the metadata model and the static-vs-runtime split.
- Is the COLLECT-ALL-violations accumulator (a violation set/Vec threaded through a context, or an accumulating-Result applicative) the SAME mechanism the binder and type-conversion use for accumulating multiple field errors, or a bespoke validation accumulator? Unifying it (§2.11) is attractive — one 'collect all the things that went wrong across this typed boundary' primitive — but it cross-cuts the unified error/diagnostics model (§2.9) and the binder's result monad.
- How does the violation PROPERTY-PATH (built as Rust-field-name data during the cascade walk) map back to the canonical kebab-case property KEY + ORIGIN for the config-validation error, across the relaxed-binding key-translation and origin-tracking provenance layers? The path-translation has no JVM analogue (Spring's binder natively speaks property paths) and cross-cuts relaxed-binding, origin-tracking, and the binder.
- Does the two-source duality apply — a static link-time constraint vocabulary PLUS an optional dynamic (WASM-module) source contributing constraints/ConstraintSets at runtime? R2's interpreting-engine handles a dynamic source natively (it interprets data regardless of origin); R1/R3's compiled-in impls can't synthesize new constraint types at runtime and would need R2's descriptor model as an adjunct. A candidate-comparison shaping how origin-agnostic the engine must be, not a decision.
- Can/should any validation be COMPILE-TIME (R3's typed `Valid<..>` parameters enforce constraints with no runtime cost, the earliest §2.9 diagnostic) versus always-runtime (R1/R2)? Compile-time validation shifts the diagnostic LOCATION to the construction site (not the method seam) and only helps new APIs that opt into wrapper types — a DX/timing tradeoff that cross-cuts the compile-vs-runtime split (§3) and the diagnostics-timing invariant (§2.9).

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. I read the dedicated validation material: lines 1614/1616 (the config-binding face — `@Validated` + JSR-303 constraints, `@Valid` cascade, fail-fast-at-startup `BindValidationException`, `configurationPropertiesValidator`, the 'config constraints look like every other validated bean' reuse intent); 1635 (validation is one of the proxy-applied cross-cutting concerns); 1705 (`@Validated` keeps its advice isolated via Role::Infrastructure on the InfrastructureAdvisorAutoProxyCreator); 1712 (`@Validated` is inert metadata; the `@Enable*` selector registers the auto-proxy-creator + the `MethodValidationInterceptor` advisor; 'the interceptor validates arguments around the real method call'); plus the surrounding proxy/AOP section (1693-1716) and the config-properties/binder context (1553-1606) for the binding face.

I deliberately SEPARATED this feature's THREE concerns and assigned ownership precisely: (a) THE CONSTRAINT ENGINE (the JSR-303-provider analogue — the actual heart, the part THIS feature owns) — given a typed value + its declared constraints, collect-all violations with paths/messages, cascade via `@Valid`; (b) THE METHOD-VALIDATION FACE — a thin Role::Infrastructure Interceptor riding the ALREADY-DESIGNED auto-proxy-creator (I do NOT re-own collection/matching/ordering/proxy-mechanism — those belong to auto-proxy-creator + aop-proxying; I own only the interceptor's behavior + pointcut + the typed-argument NEED it surfaces); (c) THE CONFIG-BINDING FACE — the same engine invoked by the binder as a ValidationBindHandler analogue, the INTENT's headline reuse. I did NOT re-decide the proxy mechanism, the metadata/codegen boundary, the type-erasure substrate, the conditional strategy, or the error model.

The three realizations are genuinely divergent on the axis that DEFINES validation — where constraint logic lives and how the engine is structured: R1 = PER-TYPE derived `Validate` impl, the engine is 'almost nothing' (a recursive driver), constraints compiled into direct field checks, thinnest macros + lowest overhead + zero-DCE + generics-friendly, but constraint vocabulary fixed at the derive site and not introspectable (clashes with config-metadata). R2 = CENTRAL constraint-DESCRIPTOR registry + an interpreting `Validator` engine (the literal JSR-303-provider analogue), constraints-as-data feed config-metadata cleanly + true `@Constraint` runtime extensibility + groups + erased-arg-friendly, but heavier, re-introduces the full cross-crate-registry DCE hazard, and hits generics-can't-register. R3 = parse-don't-validate via `Valid<T,C>` typed boundaries FUSED with type-conversion, strongest static guarantee + possible compile-time/zero-cost method validation, most rust-native, but invasive wrapper types, fights JSR-303's validate-existing-object/groups/mutation semantics, can't express cross-field constraints without falling back to R1's struct-level path, and pre-judges type-conversion. R1↔R2 differ on compiled-per-type vs data-interpreted-by-one-engine; R1/R2↔R3 differ on validate-after-construction vs validity-as-type-identity-fused-with-conversion.

Things I deliberately did NOT decide (held as Phase-2 questions): per-type-compiled vs central-descriptor; runtime-extensible-registry vs compiled-in-checkers; fuse-with-conversion vs separate-pass; how much type info reaches the method interceptor (downstream of aop-proxying's erased-vs-typed keystone); inline vs deferred message resolution; validation-groups realization; whether the collect-all accumulator unifies with the binder's; the violation-path→property-key+origin mapping; the two-source duality for a dynamic constraint source; and compile-time vs always-runtime validation. Every realization keeps the engine ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC (a value validates the same whether native or WASM-sourced), makes validation a SYNC non-blocking computation inside async hops (with the custom-constraint-wants-IO case explicitly out-of-path), uses COLLECT-ALL violation accumulation (resisting the `?` fail-first reflex), builds PROPERTY PATHS as generated data (not reflection), inherits the method-validation advisor's anti-DCE obligation from the auto-proxy-creator, and reuses ONE engine across both the method-validation and config-binding faces (the §2.11 unification the intent demands). Naming ('Validate', 'ValidationContext', 'Violation', 'ConstraintSet', 'ConstraintDescriptor', 'ConstraintRegistry', 'Validator', 'MethodValidationInterceptor', 'BindValidationError', 'Valid<T,C>') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files.

Relevant grounded source paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (1614/1616 the config-binding validation face + fail-fast intent; 1635 validation as a proxy-applied concern; 1705 Role::Infrastructure isolation; 1712 inert-`@Validated`→`MethodValidationInterceptor` wiring; 1693-1716 proxy/AOP context; 1553-1606 config-properties + binder context; 1606 the ValidationBindHandler/BindHandler machinery); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 esp. DX#1/overhead#2/async-first§2.3/reactive§2.4/no-global-locks§2.5/rich-diagnostics§2.9/thin-macros§2.10/unification§2.11; deferred keystones §3; topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (89 macro single-invocation scope; 149 Layer-0 unreferenced-crate; 152 silent-DCE self-check mandate; 153 generics-can't-register; 154 ordering-unspecified; 209 runtime-conditions-answer-what-does-wire; 260 binary/app-root sees the whole graph; 277-337 architectures A/B/C/D; 362 two-source duality); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (160/442 RPITIT/AFIT-not-dyn-safe → boxed-future at the dyn chain hop, relevant to the validation interceptor running in a boxed hop; 126 trait-upcasting 1.86 for `dyn Validate`→`dyn Any`; 481 `HashMap<TypeId, ...>` + downcast for R2's TypeId-keyed ConstraintSet lookup; 86/280 no async Drop / explicit-shutdown discipline).

## Retry / Resilience (@Retryable / @ConcurrencyLimit)  `[retry-resilience]`

Spring Framework 7 folds the old Spring Retry into core (`org.springframework.core.retry`: `RetryTemplate` + `RetryPolicy` as an imperative primitive) and adds two declarative resilience annotations enabled together via `@EnableResilientMethods` (spring-boot-4-di-design.md lines 226, 2075-2085, 1712): (a) `@Retryable` → Spring Retry's `RetryOperationsInterceptor`, which wraps the real call in a retry loop governed by a policy (which exceptions are retryable, max attempts) and a backoff strategy (fixed/exponential/jitter), with recovery/fallback on exhaustion; (b) `@ConcurrencyLimit(n)` → a `ConcurrencyThrottleInterceptor` (sync) or a `SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor` concurrency limit (async) that caps concurrent invocations. The load-bearing INTENT to replicate (not the mechanism): (1) resilience is DECLARATIVE cross-cutting advice — `@Retryable`/`@ConcurrencyLimit` are inert markers; an `@Enable*` registers an interceptor that runs AROUND the method (line 1712), so business code stays resilience-unaware; (2) it is also available as an explicit imperative PRIMITIVE — `RetryTemplate.execute(...)` — usable WITHOUT the proxy/AOP path (the self-invocation escape hatch and the testable-without-container path); (3) `@ConcurrencyLimit` is positioned for the UNBOUNDED-CONCURRENCY world: virtual threads (Spring) / unbounded async spawn (leaf) have NO implicit pool ceiling, so an explicit concurrency gate is a needed default-safety lever, and limit=1 is a DECLARATIVE INSTANCE LOCK — a structural alternative to `synchronized`/`Mutex` for protecting a non-thread-safe target (lines 2079-2081); (4) ordering within the single shared proxy chain matters and is invisible in object identity — retry RELATIVE to transaction/cache/concurrency-limit is a correctness concern decided by advisor order (lines 1770-1772). The rust-native reframing: retry/backoff is the canonical COLD-PATH polling loop the charter explicitly sanctions (§2.4 — async retry/backoff/reconnect are permitted only on cold paths, never busy-wait on hot paths); the concurrency limit is a Semaphore-style permit gate with RAII release (no thread-interrupt, cancellation is cooperative). I realize the DECLARATIVE interceptor (contributed as one `Role::Infrastructure` advisor that the auto-proxy-creator collects/matches/orders) AND the IMPERATIVE `RetryTemplate`/`RetryPolicy`/`BackoffPolicy` primitive the interceptor is built on, and I lean on task-execution's `ConcurrencyGate`/bounded-permit primitive for the limit rather than reinventing it. I deliberately do NOT decide the proxy mechanism (aop-proxying), the advisor-collection substrate, the async-execution/cancellation model, or the unified error model — those are upstream/Phase-2.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Two infrastructure interceptors over shared primitives: a RetryInterceptor (built on a standalone RetryTemplate) + a ConcurrencyLimitInterceptor (built on task-execution's ConcurrencyGate), each contributed as one Role::Infrastructure advisor
The most direct intent port, mirroring Spring's 'RetryOperationsInterceptor + ConcurrencyThrottleInterceptor, two advisors, one chain.' Define an imperative PRIMITIVE first (usable with zero AOP): `RetryTemplate { policy: RetryPolicy, backoff: Arc<dyn BackoffPolicy> }` with `async fn execute<F, T, E>(&self, op: F) -> Result<T, E> where F: FnMut() -> BoxFuture<Result<T,E>>` — a pure async retry loop that on a retryable error consults `backoff.next_delay(attempt)` and `.await`s a timer (the sanctioned cold-path poll, §2.4), exhausting into the last error or an optional recovery fn. `RetryPolicy` is a small struct/trait (max_attempts, `is_retryable(&E) -> bool`, predicate over error classification); `BackoffPolicy` is a trait with `Fixed`/`Exponential{ base, mult, max, jitter }`/`None` impls. The DECLARATIVE layer is then a thin wrapper: a `RetryInterceptor` (implements aop-proxying's `Interceptor`) holds a `RetryTemplate` (resolved per-method from the `#[retryable(...)]` attribute captured AS DATA) and, in its `around(invocation)` hook, drives `RetryTemplate::execute(|| invocation.proceed())`. Separately, a `ConcurrencyLimitInterceptor` holds an `Arc<dyn ConcurrencyGate>` (task-execution's bounded-permit primitive) sized from `#[concurrency_limit(n)]`; its `around` does `let _permit = gate.acquire().await; invocation.proceed().await` — the permit's Drop releases on completion OR cancellation (RAII, no leak), and n=1 is the declarative instance lock. Each interceptor is packaged as ONE `AdvisorDescriptor { role: Role::Infrastructure, order, pointcut: matches methods carrying the retryable/concurrency-limit metadata, make_interceptor }` contributed into the auto-proxy-creator's collected advisor stream (origin-agnostic — native, test, or WASM-sourced contribute identically). The `#[leaf::enable_resilient_methods]` analogue (or auto-config) ensures the two descriptors are linked and the creator's policy considers Role::Infrastructure advisors. Ordering relative to tx/cache is the creator's deterministic role-tier+order sort.

**Pros:** Tightest intent parity (lines 1712, 2075-2081): two interceptors over a standalone RetryTemplate + a ConcurrencyGate, exactly Spring's 'RetryOperationsInterceptor + ConcurrencyThrottleInterceptor + RetryTemplate primitive' — the mental model ports 1:1 and is teachable.; The imperative RetryTemplate is a FIRST-CLASS primitive usable WITHOUT any AOP (charter §2.10 escape hatch, and Spring's own core.retry split): a user can `retry_template.execute(|| do_work()).await` directly, bypassing the self-invocation limitation and giving a container-free, unit-testable retry path.; ConcurrencyLimit reuses task-execution's ConcurrencyGate rather than inventing a second limiter (charter §2.11 unification; the upstream task-execution design explicitly flags the bounded-permit primitive as shared with retry-resilience) — one Semaphore-permit concept, one place to get cancellation-safe release right.; Retry/backoff is realized as the canonical sanctioned cold-path poll (§2.4): an .await on a timer between attempts, waker-driven, never a busy-wait — and because it lives inside the interceptor/template, the hot per-call path stays the substrate's plain chain walk.; Clean separation: retry and concurrency-limit are INDEPENDENT advisors, so a method can carry one, the other, or both, and their relative order (and order vs tx/cache) is the creator's data-driven sort — matching Spring's one-proxy-many-concerns model without coupling the two concerns.; RAII permit release gives a cancellation-safety property the JVM lacks: a cancelled/dropped gated future releases its permit at guard-drop, so cooperative cancellation cannot leak the concurrency budget (a rust-native win over JVM throttle counters).

**Cons:** Two interceptors mean two advisor descriptors and two DCE-self-check obligations; a resilience crate the binary never path-references silently disables BOTH @retryable and @concurrency_limit (the catastrophic Layer-0 silent-DCE failure mode) — needs a loud expected-vs-found startup self-check.; Retryable error CLASSIFICATION (`is_retryable(&E)`) is awkward when the proceed() result type is type-erased at the dyn Interceptor boundary: the interceptor sees an erased error (Box<dyn Error>/the unified error type), so 'retry only on these error variants' must be expressed over the erased error (downcast/classification predicate) rather than typed match arms — a real ergonomics/precision loss vs a typed RetryTemplate used directly.; Retrying an arbitrary `invocation.proceed()` assumes the operation is IDEMPOTENT and re-runnable; but proceed() may consume by-value arguments or carry side effects, and re-invoking a boxed future requires the invocation to be RE-CREATABLE (FnMut yielding a fresh future), not a single already-started future — the interceptor contract must guarantee proceed() is replayable, which constrains aop-proxying's invocation model (a coupling to an upstream-deferred decision).; The concurrency limit caps at the PROXY seam (external calls), so self-invocation bypasses it exactly like Spring (line 1716) — and unlike a real Mutex on the target, the 'instance lock' (limit=1) only protects externally-routed calls, a subtle correctness footgun the docs must call out.; make_interceptor must build the gate/template per-method-or-per-bean from captured attribute data; if the gate is per-method the limit semantics (per-method vs per-instance vs per-type concurrency) must be pinned, and getting the gate's SHARING granularity wrong silently changes the ceiling's meaning.

**Mechanisms considered:** standalone RetryTemplate primitive (async execute loop + RetryPolicy + BackoffPolicy trait) usable without AOP — mirrors core.retry; RetryInterceptor / ConcurrencyLimitInterceptor implementing aop-proxying's Interceptor::around, each contributed as one Role::Infrastructure AdvisorDescriptor; task-execution's ConcurrencyGate (Semaphore-backed bounded permit, RAII release) reused for the limit — shared primitive, not a new limiter; backoff as a cold-path .await on a runtime timer (charter §2.4 sanctioned polling), exponential/fixed/jitter as BackoffPolicy impls; retryable-error classification as an is_retryable predicate over the (possibly erased) error at the dyn boundary; attribute data (#[retryable(max=..,backoff=..)], #[concurrency_limit(n)]) captured AS DATA at macro expansion, consumed by make_interceptor; force-link + expected-vs-found self-check for the two advisor descriptors (anti-DCE)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Two thin AdvisorDescriptors (retry, concurrency-limit) live in leaf-framework's resilience module and are contributed into the auto-proxy-creator's collected advisor stream via whatever Phase-2 substrate is chosen (linkme distributed-slice default / build-time codegen / explicit) — origin-agnostic by construction (a flat descriptor with a make_interceptor fn-ptr + a &'static Pointcut). The Layer-0 DCE hazard applies sharply: if the resilience capability is in a crate the binary never path-references, the descriptors vanish and ALL resilience silently no-ops — so `#[leaf::enable_resilient_methods]` / the auto-config must emit a force-link reference AND the façade must run a non-negotiable self-check (expected resilience advisors discovered? by role/crate) reported by `leaf doctor`. Ordering is encoded AS DATA (order:i32 + role-tier) and resolved by the creator's runtime sort — never link/section order (cross-crate doc line 154/170). The standalone RetryTemplate primitive crosses crates trivially as an ordinary generic type (no registration needed) — only the DECLARATIVE advisor path touches the discovery substrate. Generics-can't-register (line 153): a `#[retryable]` method on a generic bean needs per-instantiation advisor shims; the contributing macro hard-errors otherwise. The two-source duality (WASM exploration §6, cross-crate line 80): a static link-time resilience advisor is the default; a dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module provider could contribute a resilience advisor descriptor into the SAME stream — but realistically resilience is leaf-owned infrastructure, so the dynamic-source relevance is mainly that a WASM-backed BEAN behind `Arc<dyn Trait>` can be the TARGET advised by retry/concurrency-limit, not the source of the advice (the proxy is native; the gate/template wrap the coarse WIT call). RetryPolicy backoff tuning (max attempts, delays, limit n) is naturally config-bound (config-properties), so a profile can retune resilience without recompiling — a config 'classpath' for the policy values that sidesteps DCE entirely.

**T2 — execution context:** The defining T2: the retry loop is COLD-PATH polling (§2.4) — RetryInterceptor.around drives a loop that calls proceed() (the wrapped business future, on the current task/executor), and on a retryable error .awaits `backoff.next_delay(attempt)` on a runtime timer (waker-driven, no busy-wait) before retrying; this runs on the CALLER's task — it does NOT spawn, so it inherits the caller's execution context and cancellation. ConcurrencyLimitInterceptor.around does `gate.acquire().await` (yields cooperatively when the permit budget is exhausted — back-pressure, not a global lock) then proceed(); the permit guard's Drop releases on normal completion AND on cancellation/drop (RAII — no permit leak, the cancellation-safety win). Cancellation propagates naturally: dropping the outer future drops the in-flight proceed() future and (for retry) abandons the loop; the framework-wide cancel-token/drain contract is a Phase-2 cross-cutting question. Errors: the interceptor surfaces the LAST attempt's error (or the recovery fn's result) up the chain with full causal context — which attempt, how many, why-retryable, total elapsed (charter §2.9 rich diagnostics); exhaustion is a first-class diagnostic, not a swallowed retry. Blocking discipline: the wrapped operation may itself block — but that is the target's concern; the interceptor never blocks the executor (it only .awaits timers and permits). spawn_blocking offload, if the target is blocking, is task-execution's seam, not this feature's. No async Drop (nightly doc line 86, 273-280): the gate's permit release rides ordinary sync Drop (a Semaphore permit guard is sync-droppable), so no async-teardown hazard. The RetryTemplate primitive used imperatively runs entirely on the caller's task with the same cold-path-timer semantics.

#### R2 — A unified ResiliencePolicy decorator pipeline: one composable async middleware (retry ∘ limit ∘ timeout ∘ bulkhead…) wrapping the call, surfaced both as a Tower-style Layer primitive and as one advisor
Question whether retry and concurrency-limit should be TWO separate interceptors at all. Both are instances of the SAME shape: an async middleware that wraps `proceed()` with a policy. Unify them (charter §2.11) into one composable resilience pipeline — a `trait ResilienceLayer { fn wrap(&self, next: Next) -> BoxFuture<Result<Out, Err>> }` (the Tower/axum Layer pattern the cross-crate doc cites as the rust-native composition idiom, line 220) — with small composable layers: `RetryLayer`, `ConcurrencyLimitLayer`, `TimeoutLayer`, `RateLimitLayer`, `FallbackLayer`. A method's resilience config (captured from its attributes) compiles to an ORDERED `ResiliencePipeline` (a stack of layers); the declarative path contributes ONE `ResilienceInterceptor` advisor whose make_interceptor builds the per-method pipeline from attribute data, and around() simply drives `pipeline.run(|| proceed())`. The IMPERATIVE primitive is the SAME pipeline: a user composes `Resilience::builder().retry(policy).concurrency_limit(8).timeout(5s).build()` and calls `pipeline.run(op).await` directly — RetryTemplate becomes just the single-layer special case. Ordering WITHIN resilience (retry-outside-limit vs limit-outside-retry — a genuine correctness choice: do you hold a permit across all retries, or re-acquire per attempt?) is explicit in the pipeline's layer order, declared by attribute order or an explicit `order=`. The whole resilience pipeline is ONE advisor in the auto-proxy-creator's chain (so its position relative to tx/cache is still the creator's concern), but internally it is a self-contained, independently-testable, runtime-or-imperative composition. This positions leaf to grow Resilience4j/Polly-style policies (circuit-breaker, bulkhead, rate-limit) as more layers without new advisors or new container plumbing.

**Pros:** Maximal unification (§2.11): retry, concurrency-limit, timeout, fallback, future circuit-breaker are ONE primitive (composable async layers) with ONE imperative API and ONE advisor — not a growing set of bespoke interceptors; the framework gains a whole resilience FAMILY for the cost of one mechanism.; The intra-resilience ordering decision (hold-permit-across-retries vs re-acquire-per-attempt; timeout-per-attempt vs timeout-total) becomes EXPLICIT and inspectable as layer order, rather than an emergent accident of two independent interceptors' relative order — directly attacking the 'misordering is invisible' hazard (lines 1770-1772) at the resilience granularity.; Tower/axum-idiomatic (cross-crate doc line 220 cites this as the proven rust-native composition camp): the Layer/Next shape is familiar, value-level, type-checkable, and zero linker magic for the COMPOSITION itself (only the single advisor descriptor touches discovery) — strong DX for power users who compose pipelines imperatively.; Single advisor = a single DCE self-check obligation and a single chain slot — simpler cross-crate force-link story than R1's two descriptors, and one place to surface 'this method's resilience pipeline is [retry(3,exp) ∘ limit(8) ∘ timeout(5s)]' as a §2.9 diagnostic.; The imperative pipeline (no container, no AOP) is the cleanest escape hatch (§2.10) and the most powerful testable primitive — Spring's RetryTemplate generalized to the full policy set.; Forward-positioned for the resilience landscape Spring's core.retry is the seed of (Resilience4j parity): bulkhead, rate-limit, circuit-breaker slot in as layers with no container changes.

**Cons:** Over-generalization risk for Phase 1: building a whole layer-pipeline framework when Spring ships exactly TWO concerns may pre-judge scope and smell like a meta-decision (must stay a CANDIDATE) — Spring's @ConcurrencyLimit is explicitly NOT a generic pipeline, it's a throttle; conflating them could obscure the distinct intents.; Type-erasure friction at the Layer boundary: a generic Tower-style Layer<Next> is monomorphized and zero-cost, but the DECLARATIVE path must store the pipeline behind the dyn Interceptor boundary, forcing boxed futures and an erased Out/Err per layer hop (alloc per layer per call) — heavier than R1's two direct interceptors on a hot method, and the typed Layer ergonomics are lost on the erased path (the AFIT-not-dyn wall, nightly line 162).; The 'one advisor, internal pipeline' hides the resilience layers from the auto-proxy-creator's GLOBAL ordering — so a user who wants retry OUTSIDE the transaction but concurrency-limit INSIDE it cannot express that via the single-advisor model (both resilience layers are on the same side of tx); R1's two independent advisors CAN interleave with tx/cache. This is a real expressiveness loss where resilience and tx/cache ordering must interleave.; More concepts to learn (Layer/Next/pipeline/builder) than 'two annotations' — a DX cost for the common case that just wants @retryable; the unification taxes the simple path to benefit the advanced one (tension with DX#1 for the median user).; Re-acquire-per-attempt concurrency semantics interact subtly with retry: holding a permit across a long backoff sleep wastes the concurrency budget, but re-acquiring per attempt can starve under contention — the pipeline makes this CHOICE explicit (good) but also makes it a decision every user must now understand (cost).

**Mechanisms considered:** a ResilienceLayer/Next trait (Tower/axum-style composable async middleware) with RetryLayer/ConcurrencyLimitLayer/TimeoutLayer/FallbackLayer impls; a ResiliencePipeline (ordered layer stack) compiled per-method from attribute data; intra-resilience order explicit; one ResilienceInterceptor advisor whose around() drives pipeline.run(|| proceed()) — single chain slot; imperative Resilience::builder()…build().run(op).await as the primitive; RetryTemplate = single-layer case; ConcurrencyLimitLayer wraps task-execution's ConcurrencyGate (still the shared permit primitive); monomorphized layers on the imperative path; boxed-future erasure only at the single dyn advisor boundary

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Only ONE advisor descriptor (the ResilienceInterceptor) crosses the discovery substrate, simplifying the force-link + anti-DCE self-check vs R1's two descriptors — a single expected-vs-found assertion covers all resilience. The pipeline COMPOSITION is plain value-level Rust (Tower-style), so it has NO link-time discovery surface at all — layers are composed by the make_interceptor fn from attribute data, fully within the binary, sidestepping DCE for everything except the one advisor. This is the most platform-portable shape: the pipeline works identically under linkme/codegen/explicit and on wasm/embedded (the layers are just code). Ordering-as-data (cross-crate line 170) applies both to the single advisor's position (vs tx/cache, role-tier+order) AND to the intra-pipeline layer order (declared by attribute order / explicit order=). Generics: a generic layer (a typed RetryLayer<E>) is fine imperatively (monomorphized) but the declarative per-instantiation shim rule (line 153) applies to a #[resilient] generic bean. Two-source duality: identical to R1 — resilience is leaf-owned infra advice; a WASM-module bean is the advised TARGET (native proxy wrapping the coarse WIT call), not a contributor of resilience layers. Config-binding the policy values (attempts, limit, timeout, backoff) is natural and recompile-free. A subtle T1 win: because the imperative pipeline is decoupled from the container, a downstream crate can build and SHIP its own resilience pipeline as plain code with zero leaf-discovery coupling — the escape hatch needs no registration.

**T2 — execution context:** Each layer owns its execution-context contract, composed in order.
