RetryLayer: cold-path retry loop (.await backoff timer between attempts, §2.4), on the caller's task, requires Next to be re-runnable (FnMut → fresh future) — same replayability constraint as R1. ConcurrencyLimitLayer: gate.acquire().await (cooperative back-pressure, RAII permit release on completion/cancellation). TimeoutLayer: races Next against a runtime timer, dropping the in-flight future on timeout (cooperative cancellation — the future stops at its next await). The CRITICAL T2 subtlety the pipeline makes explicit: layer order determines whether the permit is held ACROSS the retry backoff sleep (ConcurrencyLimitLayer outside RetryLayer) or re-acquired per attempt (inside) — holding-across wastes the budget during sleeps, re-acquiring risks starvation; the pipeline forces this to be a declared, auditable choice rather than an accident. All layers run on the caller's task (no implicit spawn) so cancellation propagates by dropping the outer future, which unwinds the layer stack and releases permits via Drop (no async Drop needed, nightly line 86). Errors propagate up the layer stack carrying per-layer context (which layer, which attempt, elapsed, permit-wait time) into the unified causal chain (§2.9). Blocking work inside Next is the target's concern, offloaded via task-execution's spawn_blocking if needed — the resilience layers never block the executor (only timers/permits). The imperative pipeline has identical semantics off-AOP.

#### R3 — Resilience as runtime-bound policy DATA (named ResilienceProfiles) over one dispatcher + a thin per-method handle: config-as-the-IoC-lever, attributes name a profile
Push 'declarative, tuned by configuration not code' (Spring's @Enable + property-tuned policies) to its end and lean hardest on config-properties/binder reuse. Instead of each #[retryable] capturing a literal policy in code, a method's attribute NAMES a resilience profile (`#[resilient("db-writes")]`), and the profile — max attempts, retryable-error classes, backoff curve, concurrency limit, timeout — is a config-properties-bound POJO rooted at `leaf.resilience.<name>`, validated, documented (config-metadata), with relaxed-binding and origin-tracking for free. There is essentially ONE runtime-owned `ResilienceDispatcher` and a table of named profiles; the declarative path contributes ONE advisor whose around() looks up the named profile and applies retry+limit accordingly. Call sites that want it imperatively inject a cheap `ResilienceHandle` (a token naming a profile) and call `handle.run(|| op()).await` — the dispatcher consults the named profile's policy data. Changing retry attempts, backoff, or the concurrency ceiling becomes a one-property edit (Spring's spring.task.* / resilience-tuning ergonomics, rust-native) with NO recompile and NO code change — central tuning as a first-class lever, exactly the @ConcurrencyLimit-under-virtual-threads operational story (you raise/lower the ceiling per environment). A default 'application' profile gives sane defaults; profiles are inspectable data ('list every resilience profile, its policy, and which methods name it' is a trivial §2.9 report). The concurrency gate per profile is a per-profile ConcurrencyGate (task-execution primitive) keyed by profile name, so two methods sharing a profile share a ceiling (a deliberate, declarable pooling semantic).

**Pros:** Purest realization of 'tuned by configuration, not code' and the operational reality of @ConcurrencyLimit under unbounded concurrency (lines 2077-2081): the ceiling and retry policy are environment-tunable data (config-properties/binder/config-metadata reuse — strong §2.11 unification with the config subsystem), so ops can flip a profile's limit per deployment without a rebuild.; Profiles are self-documenting, inspectable, --check-able config (config-metadata): 'which methods use profile db-writes, what is its policy, which profiles exist' is a trivial diagnostic surface (§2.9), far easier than reading scattered literal attributes — and a typo'd profile name fails at a startup validation pass with full context (the richest-diagnostic-at-the-right-time intent §2.9).; SHARED ceilings fall out naturally: methods naming the same profile share one ConcurrencyGate (a bulkhead/pool semantic Spring's per-method limit cannot express), and limit=1 on a profile shared by several methods is a declarative MULTI-method serialization lock — a strictly more expressive instance-lock than Spring's per-method @ConcurrencyLimit(1).; Minimal code-side surface: methods carry a short profile name, not a literal policy spec — low ceremony (DX#1), and the policy lives once in config rather than duplicated across attributes.; One dispatcher = one advisor, one DCE self-check, one chain slot (like R2) — simple cross-crate force-link story; and profile DATA needs no link-time discovery at all (it comes from the Environment, a real runtime 'classpath' for policy, sidestepping DCE for tuning entirely, cross-crate doc line 209).; Reuses the conditions/profiles machinery: a resilience profile can be profile-active (prod vs dev backoff curves) via the existing condition/Environment levers — no bespoke gating.

**Cons:** Heaviest indirection and most machinery up front; presenting profiles-as-data risks pre-judging the config-coupling and compile-vs-runtime split (charter §3 keystones) — must stay strictly a CANDIDATE, not a meta-decision.; 'Behavior chosen by a string-named profile resolved at runtime' trades compile-time legibility for flexibility: a missing/misnamed profile is a startup-or-runtime error, not a compile error (charter §2.9 prefers the earliest LEGIBLE diagnostic) — mitigated only by a startup validation pass cross-checking every named profile exists, which must be non-negotiable.; Retryable-error CLASSIFICATION is the hardest fit for config data: 'retry on these error variants' is a TYPE-level predicate that doesn't serialize to a properties file cleanly — so error classification must either stay in code (a per-profile classifier fn registered separately, breaking the pure-data story) or be reduced to coarse string/category matching (a precision loss), making the data model leak.; Less obviously 'declarative AOP advice' to a Spring user (@Retryable carries its policy inline); moving the policy to a config table is a real divergence from the annotation-carries-intent framing the feature is named for — a teaching cost.; A dispatcher consulting profile data per call adds a lookup + branch on every resilient invocation vs R1/R2's direct interceptor — negligible on cold/coarse work but a measurable indirection on a hot resilient method.; Shared-ceiling-by-profile is powerful but a footgun: two unrelated methods accidentally naming the same profile silently share a budget and can starve each other — the inverse of Spring's safely-isolated per-method limit; needs loud diagnostics about which methods share a profile.

**Mechanisms considered:** named ResilienceProfile descriptors bound from the Environment via config-properties/binder (policy as data: attempts, backoff, limit, timeout, retryable-categories); one runtime-owned ResilienceDispatcher + cheap ResilienceHandle tokens; #[resilient("name")] names a profile; per-profile ConcurrencyGate (task-execution primitive) keyed by profile name → shared ceilings / bulkhead semantics; one advisor whose around() resolves the named profile and applies retry+limit; single chain slot; startup validation pass cross-checking every named profile exists + which methods reference it (anti-typo, §2.9); error classification as a registered per-profile classifier fn (the leak where pure data fails) OR coarse category strings; conditions/profiles gating which profile values are active per environment (reuse)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The dispatcher + ResilienceProfile schema live in leaf-framework; profile VALUES come from the Environment (config files/env vars) resolved at runtime, so adding/retuning a profile needs NO recompile and NO cross-crate registration — sidestepping the link-time DCE hazard for tuning entirely (config is a real runtime 'classpath' for resilience policy, cross-crate line 209: link-time answers 'what could', runtime config answers 'what does'). What still crosses crates: the single advisor descriptor (force-link + self-check, like R2) and any per-profile error-CLASSIFIER fns that are code (if a profile names a classifier from a sibling crate, that classifier must be registered/linked — a profile naming an unlinked classifier is a startup-validation failure, loud not silent). Ordering-as-data (line 170): the single advisor's position vs tx/cache is role-tier+order; intra-resilience (retry vs limit) order is a profile field. Generics: #[resilient] on a generic bean still needs per-instantiation advisor shims (line 153) though the PROFILE is shared. Two-source duality: a WASM-module bean is the advised target (native proxy over the coarse WIT call), and — uniquely useful here — a directory-scanned WASM module could ship its own profile DEFAULTS as config that the operator merges, fitting the dynamic-config story. The strongest cross-crate property: resilience tuning is decoupled from the build graph entirely, so retuning across environments is purely operational.

**T2 — execution context:** The dispatcher centralizes all resilience execution-context decisions in one auditable place (§2.3). `handle.run(op)` / the advisor's around() resolves the named profile then runs: ConcurrencyGate.acquire().await (per-profile permit, cooperative back-pressure, RAII release on completion/cancellation — shared-profile methods contend on one budget) then a retry loop (cold-path .await on the profile's backoff timer, §2.4) optionally inside a timeout race. All on the caller's task (no implicit spawn), so cancellation propagates by dropping the outer future, releasing the permit via sync Drop (no async Drop, nightly line 86). The dispatcher is the natural single chokepoint to emit per-profile metrics/observation (attempts, retries, permit-wait, exhaustions) — the central-monitoring intent. Profile binding happens during the Environment-ready phase before any resilient call (cold-path); a missing profile is caught by the startup validation pass with full context (§2.9) rather than a cryptic runtime miss. Errors carry profile name + attempt history + permit-wait into the causal chain. Blocking work inside op is the target's concern (task-execution spawn_blocking seam), never blocking the executor. The per-profile ConcurrencyGate is the only added shared state (one Semaphore per profile — fine-grained, no global lock, §2.5).

### Rust clashes
- No thread interrupt — concurrency-limit and timeout cancellation are COOPERATIVE. Spring's ConcurrencyThrottleInterceptor and SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor limit gate threads that can be interrupted; Rust caps via a Semaphore permit and cancels by DROPPING the gated future, which only stops at its next .await. So the 'concurrency limit' is a permit-acquire-await (back-pressure) not a hard preemptive cap, and a non-cooperative (CPU-bound, await-free) gated task ignores cancellation/timeout. The rust-native win: RAII permit release on guard-drop means cancellation cannot LEAK the concurrency budget (a hazard JVM throttle counters have); the rust-native loss: no preemption, so the ceiling is honored at await points only.
- Retry requires a REPLAYABLE operation, but Rust futures are single-shot. Spring's RetryOperationsInterceptor re-invokes the Method (a fresh call) per attempt; in Rust, retrying `proceed()` means RE-CREATING the future each attempt — the interceptor cannot just .await the same future twice (a future is consumed when polled to completion, and cannot be re-polled). So the invocation contract must hand the retry loop a `FnMut() -> BoxFuture` (a factory), not a single future — and re-running it re-executes any captured side effects and re-borrows arguments, which constrains aop-proxying's invocation model (proceed() must be replayable) and silently misbehaves if the operation is non-idempotent. There is no JVM analogue to 'the future is already consumed.'
- Retryable-error CLASSIFICATION fights type-erasure at the dyn boundary. Spring's RetryPolicy matches on exception TYPES (a reflective instanceof over a thrown Throwable). At leaf's dyn Interceptor seam the error is erased (Box<dyn Error> / the unified error type), so 'retry only on these variants' must be a predicate over an erased error (downcast or a classification fn), not typed match arms — losing the precise, exhaustive typed matching the imperative RetryTemplate (used directly, with a concrete E) would give. The declarative path is inherently coarser than the imperative one on error classification.
- Backoff polling is a COLD-PATH loop the charter explicitly sanctions — but only there (§2.4). Spring's backoff is a Thread.sleep on a pooled thread; leaf's is an .await on a runtime timer (waker-driven, non-blocking). This is fine BECAUSE retry/reconnect/backoff is named in §2.4 as permitted cold-path polling — but it means resilience must NEVER be applied as a busy-wait on a hot path, and the timer must be the runtime's (task-execution / the chosen runtime), not a blocking sleep that stalls the executor. The clash is conceptual: 'sleep between attempts' is trivially blocking on the JVM and trivially WRONG (blocking) in async Rust unless it's an async timer.
- @ConcurrencyLimit(1) as a 'declarative instance lock' is NOT a Mutex and only guards the proxy seam. Spring sells limit=1 as a synchronized-replacement (lines 2079-2081). In Rust this is a Semaphore(1) permit, which serializes externally-routed calls but (a) does NOT provide the &mut exclusivity or memory-ordering a real Mutex gives over the target's state, and (b) is bypassed by self-invocation (line 1716) exactly like all proxy advice. So 'declarative instance lock' protects concurrency COUNT, not data-race-freedom — a non-thread-safe target's interior mutability still needs its own discipline; selling it as a Mutex-equivalent would be a correctness lie in Rust's ownership model.
- No async Drop on stable (nightly doc lines 86, 273-280) — but resilience mostly dodges it. The concurrency permit guard releases via ORDINARY sync Drop (a Semaphore permit is sync-droppable), so no async-teardown hazard arises for the limit. The clash is only that any resilience resource needing async cleanup (none in the core retry/limit case) could not ride Drop — confirming the design should keep the gate's release sync (which it naturally is) and never introduce an async-drop dependency.
- Dyn-erased interceptor boxes the future per call (AFIT/RPITIT not object-safe, nightly line 162). The declarative resilience path runs behind aop-proxying's dyn Interceptor, so each around() hop and each retry attempt's proceed() is a boxed future — an alloc per attempt per call. Fine on cold/coarse resilient operations (DB writes, remote calls — the typical @Retryable target), but the zero-cost monomorphized path is only available via the IMPERATIVE RetryTemplate/pipeline used directly (static dispatch). Intent parity (declarative resilience) and zero-overhead are in tension exactly at the dyn boundary; the imperative primitive is the escape valve.
- Ordering vs tx/cache is correctness-critical AND invisible (lines 1770-1772) — and in Rust there's an extra intra-resilience ordering axis with no Spring equivalent: whether a concurrency permit is HELD ACROSS retry backoff sleeps or RE-ACQUIRED per attempt. Holding-across wastes the budget during async sleeps (a real cost under unbounded-concurrency scale); re-acquiring risks starvation. Spring's two separate interceptors don't surface this as a single decision; leaf must make it an explicit, auditable, data-driven choice (advisor order or pipeline layer order) because misordering is invisible in object identity and the async-sleep-while-holding-a-permit cost is a uniquely async hazard.

### Interactions
- **auto-proxy-creator** — DIRECT upstream — retry-resilience CONTRIBUTES its advisor(s) into the auto-proxy-creator's collected advisor stream as Role::Infrastructure descriptors (the RetryOperationsInterceptor-analogue and ConcurrencyLimit-analogue, line 1712). The creator MATCHES them against methods carrying #[retryable]/#[concurrency_limit] metadata, computes the UNION with tx/cache/validation advisors, and ORDERS them deterministically (role-tier + order) into the one shared chain. retry-resilience never decides matching/ordering — it only contributes role-tagged, order-tagged descriptors and the make_interceptor factory; the creator owns collection/match/union/order/role-isolation.
- **aop-proxying** — DIRECT substrate — the resilience interceptor implements aop-proxying's Interceptor::around contract and drives `invocation.proceed()`. CRITICAL constraint this feature imposes on the (deferred) substrate: proceed() must be REPLAYABLE (a FnMut yielding a fresh future) because retry re-invokes per attempt and a Rust future is single-shot — the invocation model cannot hand retry a single already-started future. This feature stays proxy-mechanism-neutral; it only requires the proceed primitive be re-runnable and erase value/error at the boundary.
- **task-execution** — DIRECT shared-primitive dependency — the concurrency limit is built on task-execution's ConcurrencyGate / bounded-permit primitive (the upstream task-execution design explicitly names retry-resilience as the co-owner of this primitive: @ConcurrencyLimit and the bounded-permit are the same Semaphore-permit concept). retry-resilience does NOT reinvent a limiter; it acquires/holds/releases task-execution's permit (RAII). The retry backoff timer is the runtime's (task-execution / the chosen runtime), never a blocking sleep. Any blocking target offloads via task-execution's spawn_blocking seam, not this feature.
- **transaction-management** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor with correctness-critical RELATIVE ORDERING (lines 1770-1772). Whether retry wraps OUTSIDE the transaction (retry the whole tx — each attempt a fresh tx, the usual safe choice) or INSIDE it (retry within one tx — usually wrong) is decided by advisor order in the shared chain, resolved by the auto-proxy-creator. retry-resilience surfaces its order-as-data; the creator positions it. A common subtle bug (retry-inside-tx re-running on a doomed transaction) must be diagnosable via the inspectable chain order (§2.9).
- **caching** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor; relative ordering with retry matters (retry-outside-cache re-checks the cache each attempt; retry-inside-cache retries only the cache-miss compute). Like tx, ordered by the auto-proxy-creator's deterministic sort over role-tier+order. A cache short-circuit (hit) inside a retried operation interacts with attempt counting — retry should see the cache hit as success, not a retryable miss.
- **async-execution** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor sharing the same chain and the SAME execution substrate (task-execution). Both can apply to one method (an @async + @retryable method); their relative order decides whether retry happens on the calling task before dispatch or on the async worker after dispatch — a real semantic choice the creator's ordering settles. Both draw the concurrency-limit primitive from task-execution (async tasks under @ConcurrencyLimit map to the SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor-limit analogue, line 2077).
- **validation** — SIBLING infrastructure advisor in the shared chain; relative order matters (validate-before-retry so a bad argument fails fast without retrying, vs the rarer validate-per-attempt). Ordered by the auto-proxy-creator. Illustrates the same one-proxy-many-concerns model (lines 1770-1772) retry participates in.
- **config-properties** — TUNING SURFACE (strongest in R3, present in all). Retry max-attempts, backoff curve/jitter, concurrency-limit n, timeout are bound from the Environment via config-properties + binder, documented via config-metadata, with relaxed-binding — the rust-native realization of property-tuned resilience (raise/lower the ceiling per environment without recompiling). R3 elevates this to named ResilienceProfiles; R1/R2 bind individual policy values.
- **conditions** — GATES whether resilience wiring activates and which policy is active. @ConditionalOnMissingBean-style backoff lets a user-supplied RetryTemplate/policy override the default; profile/property conditions select environment-specific policies (aggressive backoff in prod, none in dev). The #[leaf::enable_resilient_methods] analogue is itself condition/import-driven. retry-resilience reuses the existing condition machinery rather than bespoke gating.
- **profiles** — Environment profiles select which resilience policy/profile is active (prod vs dev retry curves and ceilings) — R3's named ResilienceProfiles can be profile-active via the existing Environment/condition levers; R1/R2's policy values can be profile-bound. Build-once, tune-per-environment, structurally — mirroring Spring's @Profile-is-a-@Conditional.
- **context-propagation** — INDIRECT but important under retry/concurrency-limit: because the retry loop runs on the CALLER's task (no spawn) and the concurrency gate only awaits a permit on the same task, ambient context (request/tenant/tracing) is NATURALLY preserved across attempts and permit-waits — no thread hop, so no capture/restore needed. IF a future variant spawns gated work onto another task (e.g. async @ConcurrencyLimit dispatching to an executor), then task-execution's TaskDecorator capture/restore seam (which context-propagation fills) must carry context across that hop. The ambient-context-across-.await model is Phase-2-deferred; this feature only flags the seam.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — The per-profile/per-method ConcurrencyGate and any retry-scheduler state participate in lifecycle: on stop, the gate should stop admitting new permits and let in-flight gated/retrying work drain to a timeout (the graceful-drain intent). Immediate-destroy-without-stop tolerance (lines 2083-2085) applies — gate state must survive an out-of-order shutdown. Ties to context-close (explicit container-driven shutdown, no async Drop).
- **context-close** — On teardown, in-flight retry loops and gated calls must be cancelled cooperatively (drop the futures) and their permits released via sync Drop before dependent beans are destroyed, so a retrying call doesn't reference a half-destroyed collaborator. Because no async Drop (nightly line 86), this is container-driven explicit shutdown ordering, not automatic.
- **container-core** — The engine-vs-façade split realizes 'resilience silently does nothing in a bare engine' parity (line 49/110): the bare engine installs no auto-proxy-creator, so the resilience advisor never matches and @retryable/@concurrency_limit are inert; the context façade installs the creator and force-links + self-checks the resilience advisors. The façade is also where the mandatory force-link reference + expected-vs-found self-check for the resilience capability lives (anti-DCE), and the only place the full enabled-capability set is visible.
- **concurrency-contract** — COMPLEMENTS the safe-publication contract. concurrency-contract guarantees fully-built singletons are safely published; @ConcurrencyLimit governs how many concurrent invocations run ON a target. The clash to surface (see rustClashes): limit=1 is a permit-count serialization at the proxy seam, NOT a Mutex over the target's interior state — concurrency-contract's 'developer owns mutable-state safety' division holds, and limit=1 is a count lever, not a memory-safety guarantee.
- **scheduling** — NEIGHBOUR that also lives on cold-path timers. A @Scheduled method that is also @retryable composes (retry a failed scheduled run with backoff); both rely on the runtime's timer/executor (task-execution). Scheduling owns the trigger; retry owns the per-invocation backoff loop. Shared timer/cold-path-polling discipline (§2.4).

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Is resilience TWO bespoke interceptors (R1, intent-faithful, independently interleavable with tx/cache), ONE composable layer-pipeline primitive (R2, maximal §2.11 unification, grows circuit-breaker/bulkhead/rate-limit for free but over-generalizes Spring's two concerns and loses the ability to interleave individual resilience layers with tx/cache), or runtime-bound policy DATA over a dispatcher (R3, purest config-tuning + shared ceilings but moves intent out of the annotation and fights error classification)? The shape call cross-cuts unification (§2.11), DX (§2 #1), and the config-coupling/compile-vs-runtime split (§3). Held unresolved.
- WHERE does retry's error CLASSIFICATION live given type-erasure at the dyn boundary — a typed predicate on the imperative RetryTemplate (precise, monomorphized) vs an erased predicate / classification fn on the declarative advisor (coarse) vs config-string categories (R3, leaks the pure-data model)? This binds to the unified error/diagnostics model (§3): what IS the erased error type at the interceptor seam, and can it carry enough structure for variant-level retryable classification? Unresolved.
- Must aop-proxying's `proceed()` be REPLAYABLE (a FnMut → fresh future), and how does that constrain the (deferred) proxy/invocation mechanism and the ownership model — re-running proceed() re-borrows arguments and re-runs side effects, and a future is single-shot? This is a hard requirement retry imposes on aop-proxying that tx/cache/validation do not. Cross-cuts aop-proxying's mechanism keystone and the scope/ownership model (§3).
- Where does the concurrency-ceiling primitive live — task-execution's ConcurrencyGate, retry-resilience's @ConcurrencyLimit, or a shared lower primitive both (and async-execution) consume? It is needed in three places and as a default-safety lever against unbounded async fan-out; a unification candidate (§2.11) that must not be solved three times. (Surfaced identically by the task-execution design.) Unresolved.
- Is the concurrency permit HELD ACROSS retry backoff sleeps or RE-ACQUIRED per attempt — a uniquely-async correctness/throughput decision (holding wastes the budget during sleeps at scale; re-acquiring risks starvation) with no clean Spring analogue? And more broadly, is intra-resilience ordering (retry vs limit vs timeout) the SAME ordering model as the auto-proxy-creator's advisor order, or a distinct resilience-internal order? Where does the assembled order get surfaced as an inspectable §2.9 diagnostic (since misordering is invisible in object identity)? Unresolved.
- What is leaf's framework-wide CANCELLATION + drain contract that retry/limit/timeout must honor — cooperative-only cancellation means a non-cooperative (await-free, CPU-bound) gated/retrying task ignores timeout and shutdown drain? This cross-cuts task-execution, runtime-lifecycle, context-close, and scheduling and needs ONE unified answer (cancel tokens, drain timeouts, what happens to tasks outliving the drain window). The concrete async-execution/cancellation model is Phase-2-deferred (§3). Unresolved.
- How is the resilience advisor's link-time COLLECTION ('the capability could contribute') reconciled with runtime ACTIVATION ('it does'), and what is the mandatory force-link + expected-vs-found self-check that GUARANTEES a binary never silently ships with resilience disabled (the catastrophic Layer-0 DCE failure, cross-crate line 149/152)? This binds to the cross-crate composition model (§3) and the conditional strategy (cfg vs codegen vs runtime gate).
- Should the imperative RetryTemplate/ResiliencePipeline primitive be the PRIMARY surface (with the annotation a thin sugar over it) to give a zero-overhead monomorphized path and a container-free testable escape hatch (§2.10), and to dodge the dyn-boxed-future-per-attempt cost — i.e. how much of resilience is usable entirely off the AOP/dyn path? This cross-cuts whether declarative parity (DX) or zero-overhead (#2) wins at the dyn boundary, and the metadata/codegen boundary (§3).

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. The Spring resilience section is short but explicit (spring-boot-4-di-design.md lines 226, 2075-2085) and I read the full surrounding cluster that gives it mechanics: lines 1712 (inert-annotation → @Enable* registers auto-proxy-creator + Advisor + interceptor; Spring Retry's RetryOperationsInterceptor named alongside TransactionInterceptor/CacheInterceptor/etc.), 1701-1708 (the one-creator-wins / Role::ROLE_INFRASTRUCTURE isolation that retry advice rides), 1770-1772 (one-proxy-many-concerns ordering — the correctness-critical, invisible-in-object-identity ordering retry shares with tx/cache), 1716 (self-invocation limitation that bounds @ConcurrencyLimit-as-instance-lock), and 2079-2081 (@ConcurrencyLimit(1) as a declarative instance lock, positioned for unbounded virtual-thread concurrency). The single most load-bearing intent: resilience is BOTH declarative cross-cutting advice (the interceptor) AND an explicit imperative primitive (RetryTemplate/RetryPolicy moved into core.retry, line 226) — I preserved both in every realization.

The three realizations diverge on the axis that defines THIS feature — what the resilience abstraction IS and where its policy lives: R1 = two infrastructure interceptors over a standalone RetryTemplate + task-execution's ConcurrencyGate (most Spring-faithful, independently interleavable with tx/cache); R2 = one composable Tower/axum-style ResilienceLayer pipeline (retry ∘ limit ∘ timeout ∘ fallback), maximal §2.11 unification and forward-positioned for Resilience4j-class policies, but over-generalizes and can't interleave individual layers with tx/cache; R3 = resilience as runtime-bound policy DATA (named config-bound ResilienceProfiles over one dispatcher), purest config-tuning + shared-ceiling/bulkhead semantics but moves intent out of the annotation and fights typed error classification. All three keep the imperative primitive as the zero-overhead, container-free escape hatch; all three contribute role-tagged, order-as-data descriptors (never link-order); all three reuse task-execution's bounded-permit primitive rather than reinventing a limiter; all three treat backoff as the charter-sanctioned cold-path .await-timer poll (§2.4), never a blocking sleep.

Hard constraints held fixed across all three (the rust clashes): cancellation/timeout is COOPERATIVE (no thread interrupt) — limit=1 is a Semaphore-permit serialization at the proxy seam, NOT a Mutex over target state (selling it as a synchronized-replacement would be a correctness lie in Rust); retry needs a REPLAYABLE proceed() (FnMut → fresh future) because Rust futures are single-shot — a real constraint retry imposes on the deferred aop-proxying invocation model; error classification fights type-erasure at the dyn boundary (precise only on the imperative typed path); RAII permit release on guard-drop is a cancellation-safety WIN over JVM throttle counters; no async Drop needed (the permit guard is sync-droppable). The uniquely-async ordering hazard — whether a permit is held across retry backoff sleeps or re-acquired per attempt — has no clean Spring analogue and I surfaced it as a phase2Question rather than resolving it.

Things I deliberately did NOT decide (phase2Questions): the shape (two-interceptors vs layer-pipeline vs policy-data); where error classification lives; whether proceed() must be replayable and its ownership ripple; where the concurrency-ceiling primitive lives (shared with task-execution/async-execution — flagged identically by the task-execution design); permit-hold-across-vs-reacquire-per-attempt and intra-resilience ordering; the framework-wide cancellation/drain contract; the anti-DCE force-link + self-check policy; and how much resilience is usable off the dyn/AOP path. Naming ('RetryTemplate','RetryPolicy','BackoffPolicy','RetryInterceptor','ConcurrencyLimitInterceptor','ConcurrencyGate','ResilienceLayer','ResiliencePipeline','ResilienceProfile','ResilienceDispatcher','ResilienceHandle','AdvisorDescriptor','Role') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files.

Grounding paths (all absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (resilience 2075-2085; inert-annotation→interceptor wiring 1712; one-creator/role-isolation 1701-1708; one-proxy-many-concerns ordering 1770-1772; self-invocation 1716; resilience-moved-to-core 226; immediate-destroy-without-stop 2083-2085); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2.3/§2.4/§2.5/§2.9/§2.10/§2.11, deferred §3, topology §6); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (Layer-0 DCE + mandatory force-link/self-check 149/152, ordering-as-data 170, generics-can't-register 153, runtime-conditions-answer-what-does 209, Tower/axum explicit-composition camp 220, two-source/dynamic-classpath 80, architectures A-D 287-335); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (AFIT/RPITIT not dyn-safe → boxed-future at dyn boundary 162, trait_variant Send-variant 135/260, no async Drop → explicit shutdown 86/273-280, trait upcasting 1.86); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/design/explorations/wasm-modules-for-leaf.md (origin-agnostic Arc<dyn Trait> proxy seam 47-55, static-vs-dynamic-classpath duality 77-80, WASI 0.3 async ready). Upstream Phase-1 designs consumed concretely: auto-proxy-creator (advisor collection/match/order, Role::Infrastructure isolation) and task-execution (ConcurrencyGate/bounded-permit shared primitive). Neighbour designs not yet in docs/design were referenced by catalog intent only.

## Expression Language (SpEL)  `[expression-language]`

Spring's SpEL is a runtime expression layer threaded through many declarative features, exposed as `#{...}` strings. Its INTENT (not its interpreter mechanism) is what leaf must replicate, and that intent splits into four distinct consumer needs that Spring happens to unify behind one string grammar: (1) VALUE PRODUCTION — `@Value("#{systemProperties['x'] ?: 8080}")` evaluates an expression to a value then converts it to the field type (distinct from `${...}` placeholder resolution; the doc is explicit that `@Value` IS SpEL-capable while `@ConfigurationProperties` deliberately is NOT — config is inert data, `@Value` is container expression). (2) BOOLEAN FILTERING — `@EventListener(condition="#root.event.priority > 5")` evaluated per-event at dispatch, and `@ConditionalOnExpression` evaluated at registration; both reduce to a predicate over an evaluation context. (3) CACHE KEYS — `@Cacheable(key="#root.args[0]")` builds a key from method args/target/result. (4) BEAN REFERENCES — `@beanName` resolves a bean from the factory inside an expression, and the `&factoryBean` dereference escapes to the FactoryBean itself rather than its product. The unifying machinery in Spring is an EvaluationContext: a ROOT OBJECT plus named VARIABLES (`#root`, `#this`, args by name `#myEvent` / index `#a0`/`#p0`, `#result`) plus a BeanResolver (`@`/`&`) plus a property/method accessor stack — all evaluated by a reflective interpreter over erased Objects, with a lenient-by-default failure mode (an unresolved value can degrade to a literal). The cross-cutting promise: ONE evaluation engine that conditions/value-injection/caching/events all delegate to, so the context surface (what `#root`/`#args`/`@bean` mean) is learned once. For leaf the central tension is that Rust has no runtime reflective interpreter and no erased-Object dynamism, while each of the four needs has a far more direct, typed rust-native expression — so the question is whether leaf ships a real interpreted mini-language or proves the unified string layer unnecessary by giving each consumer a typed closure/expression that shares one evaluation-context shape.

### Candidate realizations

#### Typed closures over a shared EvalContext — no interpreter, the macro lowers #{...} sites to Rust expressions
Reject the string interpreter entirely as the DEFAULT surface; realize each #{...} site as a monomorphized Rust closure `Fn(&EvalCtx) -> Result<T, ExprError>` (or `-> bool` for conditions, `-> CacheKey` for keys) that the thin attribute macro EMITS by lowering the (small, fixed) SpEL-subset grammar into hand-writable Rust. `EvalCtx<'a>` is a borrowed, stack-built struct carrying exactly the ambient surface Spring's EvaluationContext exposes: `root: &dyn Any` (the event/target), named bindings (`args: &[&dyn Any]`, arg-name lookup, `result`), an `env: &Environment` handle (property-resolution neighbour), and a `beans: &dyn BeanResolver` for `@name`/`&factory`. So `@Value("#{server.port ?: 8080}")` expands to a closure reading `env` and falling back to a literal; `#[event_listener(condition = "event.priority > 5")]` expands to `|cx| Ok(cx.event::<MyEvent>()?.priority > 5)`; `@cacheable(key = "args.0")` expands to a closure hashing arg 0. Heavy logic (the grammar→Rust lowering) lives in leaf-codegen, not the macro body (invariant 10). For genuinely dynamic needs the user writes a plain Rust closure directly (`#[event_listener(condition = expr_fn(my_predicate))]`) — the escape hatch IS ordinary Rust. The shared primitive is the EvalCtx SHAPE and the typed accessor traits (`Root`, `Args`, `Result`, `BeanResolver`), not an interpreter.

**Pros:** Zero interpreter, zero runtime parsing, zero erased-Object dispatch on the value/key path — the expression IS monomorphized Rust, best low-overhead story (invariant 2) and stack-built EvalCtx (invariant 5); DX parity for the COMMON case: users still write a short `#{...}`-shaped attribute; what leaf emits is legible in `cargo expand` (invariant 10), and the escape hatch is just a Rust closure — no bespoke DSL to learn for power users; Rich, early diagnostics (invariant 9): an ill-typed expression (`event.priority` where priority is a String) fails at COMPILE time with a real Rust type error pointing at the attribute span, far better than SpEL's runtime ClassCastException; missing bean `@foo` can be a startup-pass error; Async-clean (invariant 3): closures are sync pure predicates/value-builders by contract; no interpreter that might block, no hidden reflection; condition evaluation on the event hot path is a direct fn call (invariant 4 — reactive, no polling); Fits the typed-conversion neighbour exactly: the closure yields a typed value or yields a ConfigValue/typed result that type-conversion's `FromConfigValue` then coerces, mirroring Spring's evaluate-then-convert without an erased middle

**Cons:** LOWEST dynamism: anything not expressible in the fixed lowered grammar must be a hand-written closure — runtime-authored expressions (e.g. a `condition` string loaded from a config file at runtime, or an admin-supplied cache-key rule) are impossible without falling back to Realization 2/3; Hardest divergence from Spring's mental model: there is no single runtime ExpressionParser/Expression object a user can `parse(str).getValue(ctx)` against; Spring migrants lose the 'it's just SpEL' uniformity; The grammar leaf chooses to lower is now a COMPILE-TIME-frozen subset; extending it (new operators, new accessors) is a leaf-codegen change, not a user-pluggable PropertyAccessor/MethodResolver the way Spring allows; `#root.event` style dynamic member access over `&dyn Any` still needs a typed accessor: the closure must downcast `root` to the concrete event type, so the macro must KNOW that type at the call site (fine for `@EventListener` where the param type is present; awkward for fully generic `#root` navigation); Bean references `@name`/`&factory` inside an expression reintroduce a runtime lookup against the registry that is fallible at runtime (the bean may not exist / wrong type), so even this realization cannot make the bean-reference sub-case fully compile-checked — it lands in a startup/dispatch-time pass

**Mechanisms considered:** Thin attribute macro lowering a fixed SpEL-subset grammar to a Rust closure (primary; heavy parse/lower logic in leaf-codegen, not the macro); Typed EvalCtx struct + accessor traits (Root/Args/Result/BeanResolver) as the shared primitive instead of a shared interpreter; Plain user-written `Fn(&EvalCtx)->...` closures as the escape hatch (bevy/axum-style explicit composition); `&dyn Any` + downcast (stable) for the root/args erased surface, with macro-known concrete types where available; const-eval of literal-only expressions (e.g. `#{8080}`) to plain consts (stable const baseline); trait_variant/boxed closure only if a condition ever needs to be async — kept out of the default sync contract

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** No cross-crate registry is needed for the engine itself — each expression is lowered in the crate that writes the attribute, so it compiles and links wherever that code does, with ZERO link-time/DCE exposure and full wasm/embedded support (a major win over any inventory/linkme-based engine). The ONLY cross-crate surface is the BeanResolver behind `@name`/`&factory`: resolving a bean by name is a runtime query against whatever registry substrate leaf picks (the deferred type-erasure & registry-substrate meta-concept), and that registry's cross-crate discovery (linkme vs inventory vs build-time codegen, per the cross-crate doc) is where Layer-0/DCE hazards live — but that is the registry's problem, not the expression engine's. The EvalCtx accessor traits and the ExprError type must live in leaf-core so emitted closures hard-code `::leaf_core::` paths (proc-macro hygiene, absolute paths). Bean-by-name in an expression is exactly the scoped-discovery concern: an expression referencing `@some_bean` only resolves if that bean's crate is actually linked and registered — so this realization inherits, but does not create, the discovery duality (static link-time registry as default classpath, optional dynamic/WASM-module source) the catalog defers.

**T2 — execution context:** By contract every emitted expression closure is SYNCHRONOUS, pure, CPU-only, non-blocking — it must never `.await` or do I/O (documented invariant; the macro hard-errors on an async body). This is what lets condition evaluation sit DIRECTLY on the event-dispatch hot path (invariant 4: a plain fn call, no polling) and value/key building sit inline during bean construction or a cache-interceptor call without spawning. Execution contexts: (a) @Value/@ConditionalOnExpression closures run during the startup wiring/condition pass (cold path) on the refresh task; (b) @EventListener condition closures run per-dispatch on whatever task the multicaster dispatches on (could be the publisher's task synchronously, or an async listener's task) — being sync+Send-by-value-of-captured-data keeps them safe across thread hops; (c) cache-key closures run inside the caching interceptor synchronously before the (async) method body. Errors propagate as `ExprError` Results folded into the unified diagnostics chain (invariant 9); a condition error is a hard fail-fast at startup but, per Spring's lenient mode, a @Value expression MAY degrade to a default — a policy knob on EvalCtx. Cancellation is a non-issue (bounded pure work); the bean-reference sub-case is the one fallible-at-runtime point and surfaces as a typed not-found/wrong-type ExprError carrying candidates considered.

#### A small interpreted expression engine as a leaf-core service (Spring-faithful) — parse-once Expression objects over a dynamic EvaluationContext
Build a real, small expression interpreter as a leaf-core bean: `trait ExpressionParser { fn parse(&self, src: &str) -> Result<Arc<Expression>, ParseError>; }` and `Expression::evaluate(&self, ctx: &EvaluationContext) -> Result<Value, EvalError>`. `Value` is a leaf-owned dynamic enum (Null/Bool/Int/Float/Str/List/Map/Bean(handle)) — NOT `Box<dyn Any>` — so the interpreter stays self-contained and avoids open-ended downcasting. `EvaluationContext` holds the root object (as a `Value` or an erased handle with a registered accessor), named variables (`#root`,`#args`,`#a0`,`#result`, arg names), a `BeanResolver` for `@`/`&`, and a pluggable `PropertyAccessor`/`MethodResolver` chain (the genuinely-extensible analogue of Spring's SPI). The grammar is a deliberately small subset: literals, property/index access, comparison/logical/arithmetic ops, ternary/Elvis `?:`, `@bean`/`&factory`. Expressions are PARSED ONCE at startup (compiled to a tiny bytecode or an AST) and cached, then evaluated many times. @Value/@Cacheable/@EventListener-condition all delegate to this one engine; the attribute macro emits only the literal source string + a parse-at-init call, holding zero logic (invariant 10).

**Pros:** Highest intent-parity with Spring: a single, introspectable ExpressionParser bean every feature delegates to; gives Spring migrants the exact 'it's one expression engine' mental model and uniform `#root`/`@bean` surface; Only realization that supports RUNTIME-AUTHORED expressions — a condition/key/value string loaded from config, an admin-supplied rule, an expression composed at runtime — because parse is a runtime operation; Genuinely extensible at runtime via the PropertyAccessor/MethodResolver/BeanResolver chain, mirroring Spring's pluggable accessors (the SPI users can extend without a leaf-codegen change); Origin-agnostic by construction: the engine operates over a `Value`/handle abstraction, so a value originating from a WASM module or any dynamic source slots in behind the same accessor interface (honors the origin-agnostic guardrail); Diagnostics can be rich and uniform: a parse error reports column/span in the expression string; an eval error can list the property/bean tried — one place to make all expression diagnostics good (invariant 9)

**Cons:** Reintroduces exactly the dynamic-dispatch / boxed-Value / interpreter overhead Rust would otherwise eliminate (invariant 2): every evaluation walks an AST/bytecode allocating `Value`s — and condition evaluation is on the event HOT path, so this is the worst-overhead realization there (invariant 4 tension: not busy-wait, but heavy per-dispatch work); Failure moves to runtime: an expression that references a nonexistent property or mis-typed comparison is only caught when evaluated (startup for @Value/conditions, but per-event/per-call for some) rather than at compile time — weaker than Realization 1 on the invariant-9 TIMING ladder; The dynamic `Value` enum is a heap-touching, allocation-prone representation (lists/maps/strings) used on hot paths — brushes against stack-over-heap / minimal-Arc discipline (invariant 5); parse caching needs a shared read-mostly store (frozen-after-startup to avoid a global lock); Building even a 'small' correct expression interpreter (precedence, Elvis, type coercions matching Spring's, security of `@bean`/method calls) is substantial, bug-prone surface area that duplicates what Rust's own evaluator does for free — high cost for a feature that, in Rust, most users won't author dynamically; Bridging the dynamic `Value` to the typed target (the type-conversion neighbour) needs an erased step (`Value` -> target T), reintroducing the very erasure the conversion design tries to avoid; and exposing host beans/methods to a string interpreter raises an injection/safety surface Spring has but Rust idioms don't

**Mechanisms considered:** Hand-written recursive-descent parser + small AST/bytecode interpreter in leaf-core (no external dep, full control of grammar/diagnostics); Off-the-shelf expression crate (e.g. an evalexpr/rhai-class engine) as the backend — weighed against pulling a scripting dependency into leaf-core's surface (invariant 6); Leaf-owned dynamic `Value` enum instead of `Box<dyn Any>` to keep the interpreter closed and avoid open downcasting; Parse-once-at-startup with an Arc<Expression> cache, frozen after refresh (no per-eval parse, no global lock at steady state); Pluggable PropertyAccessor/MethodResolver/BeanResolver trait-object chain for runtime extensibility (Spring SPI analogue); BeanResolver delegating to leaf's registry substrate for `@name`/`&factory`

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The engine is ONE leaf-core bean, so the interpreter itself has no cross-crate discovery problem — it is a normal service resolved from the container. Pluggable accessors/resolvers contributed by other crates (a custom PropertyAccessor for a domain type, a MethodResolver) ARE a cross-crate extension-point and would ride whatever SPI/discovery substrate leaf chooses (the cross-crate doc's linkme/inventory/build-time-codegen space) — this is the dual-classpath candidate: a static link-time set of accessors (default) PLUS an optional dynamically-loaded/WASM-module accessor source feeding the same chain; both candidates, not decided here, and both subject to the Layer-0/DCE silent-empty hazard, so accessor discovery must be scoped, not implicitly global. The `@bean`/`&factory` BeanResolver again defers to the registry substrate for cross-crate bean visibility. Because parse/eval are pure runtime operations with no link sections, the engine works identically on wasm/embedded (unlike linkme-based schemes) — a portability advantage if the engine itself, not its accessor discovery, is what matters.

**T2 — execution context:** `parse` runs at startup/first-use (cold path) and its results are cached (Arc<Expression>) and frozen, so steady-state evaluation never parses. `evaluate` is synchronous CPU work and MUST be non-blocking/non-async: it runs (a) during the startup condition/value pass on the refresh task, (b) per-event inside the multicaster on the dispatch task for @EventListener conditions (the hot-path cost concern), and (c) inside the caching interceptor before the async method. The interpreter must be written to never block or allocate unboundedly; `@bean` resolution inside eval is a registry lookup that must itself be non-blocking (the bean is already constructed by the time an expression references it, or it is a fail-fast error). Errors propagate as `ParseError`/`EvalError` Results into the unified diagnostics chain; lenient-vs-strict is a context policy (an unresolved @Value expression may degrade to a default per Spring). Cancellation is a non-issue for bounded eval, but if any accessor were ever allowed to do I/O that would violate invariant 3 — so the accessor contract is sync-pure, and any I/O-backed dynamic source must pre-resolve into a `Value` before eval, never during it. The parse cache, if mutated at runtime (dynamic expressions), needs fine-grained interior mutability, not a global lock (invariant 5).

#### Hybrid: typed closures by default + an optional opt-in interpreter feature, sharing ONE EvalContext primitive
Treat the four consumer needs as the real unit and unify them on a single `EvalContext` SHAPE and a single `Expression<T>` abstraction that has TWO implementations behind one interface. Default impl = Realization 1's monomorphized closure (`Expression<T> = ClosureExpr<T>` lowered by the macro, zero-cost). Opt-in impl (behind a `dynamic-expr` cargo feature / explicit API) = Realization 2's parsed interpreter (`Expression<T> = ParsedExpr`), used ONLY where dynamism is genuinely needed (runtime-authored strings) or where the orphan-of-the-grammar case bites. Consumers (value-injection, conditions, caching, events) program against the `Expression`/`EvalContext` interface and never care which backend produced it; the attribute macro picks the closure backend for literal attributes and exposes a `parse_expression("...")` runtime API for the dynamic backend. `EvalContext` is the shared primitive both backends consume: root + named vars + env + BeanResolver. This makes the engine an internal abstraction (the nightly doc's recommended pattern) so the closure path is the zero-cost default and the interpreter is a contained, optional escape hatch — not the steady-state mechanism.

**Pros:** Best DX/overhead BALANCE (invariants 1+2): the 99% literal-attribute case is monomorphized and compile-checked; the rare runtime-authored case is still possible, so leaf doesn't have to choose between 'fast but rigid' and 'flexible but slow' — it gets both, with the fast path as default; Keeps the interpreter (with all its overhead, allocation, and runtime-failure surface) OFF the hot path and out of the default build entirely (cargo-feature-gated) — conditions on the event hot path use the closure backend; the interpreter never taxes users who don't opt in; Unification win (invariant 11): one `EvalContext` and one `Expression<T>` interface serve all four consumers AND both backends, maximizing shared machinery — exactly the Phase-2-friendly 'shared primitive, swappable substrate' shape the cross-crate and nightly docs both recommend; Clean migration/escape-hatch story (invariant 10): users start with `#{...}` literals (closure backend), drop to a hand-written closure for slightly-more, and only reach for the parsed interpreter for true runtime dynamism — escape hatches at every level; The optional interpreter is where Spring-faithful introspection/runtime-authoring lives, so leaf can claim intent parity WITHOUT paying for it by default; satisfies migrants and the 'config-driven rule' use cases as an additive capability (additive-feature discipline from the cross-crate doc)

**Cons:** Two backends behind one interface is more design/maintenance surface and risks SEMANTIC DRIFT: the closure-lowered grammar and the interpreter grammar must evaluate identically (same Elvis/precedence/coercion), or `#{a ?: b}` means subtly different things depending on backend — a correctness-and-trust hazard; The `Expression<T>` unifying interface must erase the closure's monomorphized identity to share a type with the parsed backend (likely a `Box<dyn Fn>` or an enum), which can claw back some of Realization 1's zero-cost benefit at the seam unless the macro path is specialized to bypass the interface — a real tension with invariant 2; Cargo-feature gating the interpreter interacts with the feature-unification trap (cross-crate doc): a sibling/transitive crate enabling `dynamic-expr` silently pulls the interpreter into a binary that didn't ask for it — must be an additive capability with runtime selection, not an on/off behavior switch; More to explain (two mental models) — though mitigated by 'you only learn the interpreter if you need runtime expressions'; still a larger conceptual surface than either pure realization; Decreed unification here risks pre-empting Phase 2: WHICH backend is default and whether the interpreter exists at all is entangled with the type-erasure/registry-substrate and unified-diagnostics meta-concepts, so this realization is really proposing a SHAPE (one interface, two backends) more than a settled split

**Mechanisms considered:** Single `Expression<T>` trait/abstraction with a monomorphized closure impl (default) and a parsed-interpreter impl (opt-in) — the nightly doc's 'keep the async/engine surface behind an internal abstraction' pattern applied to expressions; Shared `EvalContext` struct consumed by both backends (the genuine unification primitive); Cargo feature `dynamic-expr` gating the interpreter as an ADDITIVE capability (per cross-crate feature-additivity discipline), with backend selection at runtime not via mutually-exclusive features; Macro emits closure backend for literals; `leaf::expr::parse::<T>("...")` runtime API yields the interpreter backend; Enum dispatch `enum Expr<T>{ Closure(...), Parsed(...) }` vs `Box<dyn>` for the unifying type — weighed for the zero-cost-seam tension; Consumers (value-injection/conditions/caching/events) depend only on the `Expression`/`EvalContext` interface, never a concrete backend

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The default closure backend has no cross-crate/link-time exposure (compiles where written, full wasm support) — same clean T1 as Realization 1. The optional interpreter backend is a leaf-core service with the same T1 profile as Realization 2 (one bean; its pluggable accessors are the only cross-crate extension point, riding the deferred SPI/discovery substrate with its Layer-0/DCE caveats). The cargo-feature gating is the sharp T1 edge unique to this realization: per the cross-crate doc, `#[cfg(feature="dynamic-expr")]` gates COMPILATION of the interpreter but feature unification can silently activate it for a binary that didn't ask, and a feature alone won't force-link a registration — so the interpreter must be modeled as an additive capability whose presence is a runtime-discoverable fact, never a behavior switch, and backend selection must be a runtime/profile decision. Both backends share the leaf-core `EvalContext`/`Expression`/`ExprError` types (absolute-path emitted by macros), keeping the ABI stable across the swap. The dual-classpath candidate (static link-time accessor set + optional dynamic/WASM-module accessor source) applies only to the interpreter backend, as a candidate not a decision.

**T2 — execution context:** Identical sync-pure, non-blocking contract for BOTH backends at the eval seam — the `Expression<T>::evaluate(&EvalContext)` interface is synchronous CPU work, never async/blocking, so consumers don't change behavior across the swap. Closure backend: as Realization 1 (direct fn call on the relevant task — refresh for @Value/conditions, dispatch task for event conditions, interceptor task for cache keys). Interpreter backend: as Realization 2 (parse cached at startup/first-use cold path; eval is bounded sync work). The unifying interface guarantees the SAME execution-context discipline regardless of backend, which is itself a benefit: a consumer's hot-path code (event-condition dispatch, invariant 4) is written once against a sync interface and is reactive/non-polling whichever backend is plugged. Errors propagate as one `ExprError` type from both backends into the unified diagnostics chain; lenient/strict and the (rare) dynamic-parse-failure are context/policy concerns. Cancellation is a non-issue (bounded). The only T2 subtlety is ensuring the erasing interface (`Box<dyn Fn>`/enum) preserves `Send`/`Sync` so event-condition closures survive the multicaster's possible thread hops — designed into the `EvalContext`/`Expression` bounds.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's SpEL exists largely to route AROUND JVM limitations Rust does not have: it is a reflective interpreter over erased `Object` values because Java config (annotations) is just strings and the target type/structure is only known at runtime. In Rust the expression site's input types and output type are almost always STATICALLY KNOWN at the attribute (the event param type, the field type, the cache method signature), so a string interpreter solves a problem the language mostly removes — faithfully porting the interpreter reintroduces erasure/boxing/runtime-type-failures (invariant 2/9) the type system would eliminate. This is the keystone tension and ties into the deferred type-erasure & registry-substrate meta-concept.
- `#root.event.property` navigation assumes runtime member access by string name over an erased object — Rust has no field-by-name reflection. Any expression that navigates into the root/args must either (a) downcast a `&dyn Any` to a concrete type the macro knows at the call site (works for typed sites, fails for fully-generic navigation) or (b) require types to register a PropertyAccessor (manual, verbose). There is no rust-native equivalent of SpEL's free `obj.getX()` reflective property read.
- Spring's lenient-by-default resolver silently degrades an unresolved expression/placeholder to a LITERAL STRING (the doc flags this as a classic bug source). That behavior depends on Object-typed leniency and is actively hostile to Rust's typed, fail-fast ethos (invariant 9) — leaf must decide whether to replicate lenient degradation (DX-familiar but bug-prone) or make expressions strict-by-default (rust-native but a parity divergence); the choice differs per consumer (@Value lenient vs @ConditionalOnExpression strict) and cannot be a single global policy.
- `@beanName` / `&factoryBean` inside an expression are a runtime lookup against the container registry, and Spring's `&` factory-bean dereference is a JVM-specific escape for FactoryBean-vs-product disambiguation. In Rust, bean-by-name resolution is fallible at runtime (no compile-time guarantee the named bean exists or has the expected type), so the bean-reference sub-case is the one part of any realization that cannot be made compile-checked — it always lands in a startup/dispatch-time pass, and the FactoryBean `&` semantics depend on whatever the factory-bean neighbour decides leaf's product-vs-factory model even IS.
- Exposing host beans and arbitrary method calls to a STRING interpreter (`@bean.someMethod(#root.x)`) is a code-injection / capability surface in any runtime-authored-expression scenario — Spring lives with it because SpEL is ubiquitous, but a Rust framework that admits runtime-parsed expressions must consciously bound what the interpreter can call/touch (no idiomatic Rust analogue makes this safe for free).
- The dynamic `Value`/erased middle of any interpreter (Realization 2/3) must bridge to the typed target via the type-conversion neighbour, but type-conversion's strongest rust-native realization is itself compile-time-typed (FromConfigValue) precisely to AVOID an erased step — so an interpreter forces an erased `Value -> T` hop that re-creates the very erasure the conversion design eliminates, a clash between the two cross-cutting layers that only the closure realization avoids.
- Event-condition expressions are evaluated PER EVENT on the dispatch hot path (invariant 4: reactive, no polling, minimal per-dispatch work); a tree-walking interpreter is heavier per dispatch than a monomorphized predicate, so the most Spring-faithful realization is also the worst-fit for leaf's hot-path discipline — the typed-closure realization is the only one that makes the hot path a direct fn call.

### Interactions
- **value-injection** — Primary consumer and the canonical `#{...}` site. `@Value` first does `${...}` placeholder resolution (property-resolution), THEN, if a `#{...}` expression is present, evaluates it via the expression engine to a value, THEN type-conversion coerces to the field type — the evaluate-then-convert chain. The doc is explicit that `@Value` IS expression-capable while `@ConfigurationProperties` is NOT, so this feature must agree with value-injection on lenient-vs-strict policy (lenient default, strict mode available) and on where the expression boundary sits versus placeholders.
- **property-resolution** — Upstream + sibling on the @Value path. `${...}` placeholder resolution (property-resolution/property-sources) runs BEFORE `#{...}` expression evaluation; the two are distinct layers Spring composes. The expression EvalContext must expose an `Environment`/property handle so expressions like `#{server.port ?: 8080}` can read resolved properties — so this feature depends on property-resolution as an input source inside the evaluation context, while keeping placeholder syntax separate from expression syntax.
- **type-conversion** — Downstream sibling on the @Value path. The expression evaluates to a value that type-conversion then coerces to the target type (Spring: evaluate, then ConversionService). The clash noted in rustClashes lives here: an interpreter backend yields an erased `Value` needing an erased `Value->T` hop, whereas the closure backend can yield a typed value or a ConfigValue that type-conversion's `FromConfigValue` consumes directly. Must agree on the ConvertCtx / ExprError boundary and on origin propagation for diagnostics.
- **conditions / condition-family** — Primary consumer via `@ConditionalOnExpression` (SpEL evaluated at registration time as a boolean predicate over container/environment state). This feature supplies the boolean-filtering form of expression evaluation; condition-family's OnExpression reduces to evaluating an expression against an EvalContext exposing the Environment/bean state. Note the doc's warning that an OnExpression referencing a bean forces very early init — leaf must decide how bean-reference-in-condition interacts with the refresh-lifecycle ordering.
- **events / event-multicaster** — Primary HOT-PATH consumer via `@EventListener(condition=...)`. The condition expression is evaluated PER EVENT at dispatch; the EvalContext exposes `#root.event`/`event`, `#root.args`/`args`, and each arg by name (`#myEvent`) or index (`#a0`/`#p0`). This is the most overhead-sensitive consumer (invariant 4) and the strongest argument for the monomorphized-closure realization. The multicaster decides the dispatch task, so the condition closure/expression must be Send-safe across possible thread hops.
- **caching** — Primary consumer via `@Cacheable(key=...)`/condition/unless. Cache-key expressions read method args/target/result (`#root.args`, `#result`) and must produce a HASHABLE key, evaluated synchronously inside the caching interceptor before/around the (async) method body. This feature must expose `#result` (post-invocation) for `unless`/key-on-result and agree with caching on the key type (a leaf CacheKey) and on convert-to-hashable semantics.
- **bean-registry / autowiring-resolution** — Resolver dependency for `@beanName` / `&factoryBean` bean references inside expressions. The EvalContext's BeanResolver delegates to the registry substrate to look up a bean by name (and the `&` dereference to obtain the FactoryBean itself). This is the one runtime-fallible part of any realization (the named bean may not exist / wrong type), so it shares the deferred type-erasure & registry-substrate meta-concept and must produce rich not-found/wrong-type diagnostics.
- **factory-bean** — Defines the semantics of the `&` dereference in a bean-reference expression. Spring's `&factoryBean` escapes to the FactoryBean rather than its product; whether and how leaf models product-vs-factory determines what `&name` means inside an expression. This feature must defer the `&` semantics to whatever factory-bean decides leaf's factory/product model is.
- **profiles** — Adjacent expression-grammar sibling, deliberately SEPARATE. `@Profile` uses its OWN tiny expression grammar (`!`/`&`/`|` over profile names, the doc notes Spring keeps it non-ambiguous and separate from SpEL). Profiles is a `@Conditional` and does NOT use the SpEL engine — a clean boundary: profile expressions are their own minimal predicate language, this feature is the general expression layer. Worth noting so leaf does not accidentally merge them.
- **context-propagation / locale-context** — Possible EvalContext input + execution-context concern. If an expression needs ambient context (locale for a locale-sensitive comparison, request attributes for a request-scoped condition), that context must enter via the explicitly-passed EvalContext, NOT an .await-unsafe thread-local — sharing the unresolved async context-propagation meta-concept (T2). The EvalContext is the explicit-passing vehicle that keeps expression evaluation execution-context-disciplined (invariant 3).

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Does leaf ship a real interpreted expression language at all, or does it prove the unified string layer unnecessary by lowering each #{...} site to typed Rust (closures) and giving the rare dynamic case an explicit escape hatch? This is the keystone decision and is entangled with the deferred type-erasure & registry-substrate meta-concept (Spring's SpEL exists mainly to route around JVM erasure that Rust lacks).
- If both a closure backend and an interpreter backend exist (Realization 3), how is grammar/semantic EQUIVALENCE guaranteed between the macro-lowered grammar and the interpreted grammar so `#{a ?: b}` cannot mean two different things depending on backend — and is the unifying `Expression<T>` interface allowed to claw back zero-cost via specialization/enum dispatch, or does the seam force a Box<dyn Fn>? Cross-cuts the metadata/codegen-boundary meta-concept.
- What is leaf's lenient-vs-strict policy for unresolved expressions, given Spring degrades to a literal string by default (a flagged bug source) but Rust is fail-fast-typed — and is that policy per-consumer (@Value lenient, @ConditionalOnExpression strict) rather than one global knob? Shares the unified error/diagnostics meta-concept (invariant 9) and the property-resolution strict-mode question.
- Where does expression evaluation sit on the fail-fast timing ladder (invariant 9): compile-time (closure approach type-checks the expression), a startup condition/value pass, per-event dispatch (conditions), or per-call (cache keys) — and is that boundary consistent or deliberately per-consumer? The bean-reference sub-case (@name/&factory) is irreducibly runtime-fallible regardless; how is that reconciled?
- How does an expression reference a bean (@name) and dereference a factory (&name) given leaf has no global classpath — does the BeanResolver ride the SAME scoped cross-crate discovery substrate chosen for components/auto-config (linkme vs inventory vs build-time codegen), and how is the Layer-0/DCE silent-empty hazard handled when an expression references a bean whose crate might not be force-linked? Couples to the cross-crate composition meta-concept and the 'scope discovery, not global' guardrail.
- If a runtime-authored/interpreted expression is supported, what bounds the capability surface of `@bean.method(...)` calls into host beans (an injection/safety concern Spring tolerates but Rust idioms do not make safe for free), and do user-pluggable PropertyAccessor/MethodResolver extensions ride the static-link-time SPI plus an optional dynamic/WASM-module accessor source (the dual-classpath candidate)?
- How does ambient context an expression might need (locale, request attributes, transaction state) enter the EvalContext without an .await-unsafe thread-local, and is the EvalContext the single explicit-passing vehicle across all four consumers? Shares the async execution & context-propagation meta-concept (T2) with context-propagation and locale-context.
- Should the four consumer needs (value production, boolean filtering, cache keys, bean references) actually be unified behind ONE expression abstraction at all, or are some better served by purpose-built typed primitives (a predicate trait for conditions, a key-builder for caching) that merely SHARE the EvalContext shape — i.e. how far does the unification (invariant 11) go before it forces an erased common type that hurts the hot path?

### Notes
The strongest rust-native fit is Realization 1 (typed closures): it turns Spring's runtime reflective interpreter — which exists mainly because JVM annotations are strings over erased Objects with no compile-time reflection — into monomorphized Rust the compiler type-checks, best serving invariants 1/2/4/9, at the cost of losing runtime-authored expressions and the 'it's one engine you introspect' parity. Realization 2 (small interpreter) is highest Spring-parity and the ONLY one supporting runtime-authored/config-driven expressions and a pluggable accessor SPI, but it reintroduces the boxed-Value/AST-walk overhead, runtime-typed failure, and (worst of all) puts a tree-walker on the event-dispatch HOT path — directly at odds with invariant 4. Realization 3 (hybrid: closure default + opt-in interpreter behind one EvalContext/Expression interface) is the likely Phase-2 synthesis shape — zero-cost common case, contained optional dynamism — but it carries semantic-drift risk between two backends and a feature-unification hazard, and deciding WHICH is default (or whether the interpreter exists) is explicitly entangled with the deferred type-erasure/registry-substrate, metadata/codegen, and unified-diagnostics meta-concepts, so it is not settled here. Recurring structural facts across all three: (a) the unifying primitive that actually matters is the EvalContext SHAPE (root + named vars/args + result + Environment handle + BeanResolver), not an interpreter — every realization shares it, and it is the natural Phase-2 unification point; (b) the four consumers have genuinely different output types (value, bool, hashable key, bean handle) and different execution contexts/timing, so 'one expression engine' may be less unifying than 'one EvalContext + purpose-typed evaluators' — a real invariant-11-vs-hot-path tension; (c) the bean-reference sub-case (@name/&factory) is irreducibly runtime-fallible and defers to the registry substrate and factory-bean model regardless of realization; (d) profiles deliberately keeps its OWN minimal `!/&/|` grammar and must NOT be merged into this engine; (e) the evaluate-then-convert chain ties this feature tightly to type-conversion, and an interpreter's erased `Value->T` hop re-creates exactly the erasure the conversion design works to avoid, which is itself an argument for the closure path. Lenient-vs-strict (Spring's literal-degradation default) is a per-consumer policy decision, not global, and should be surfaced to Phase 2 alongside property-resolution's strict mode.

## Type Conversion & Formatting  `[type-conversion]`

Spring centralizes string-to-typed-value coercion in a single ConversionService/formatter registry shared by @Value injection, @ConfigurationProperties binding, and (rarely) autowiring. The registry is pluggable: built-in converters handle numbers, booleans, enums (case-insensitive), Duration (ISO-8601 or ns/us/ms/s/m/h/d suffixes via @DurationUnit), Period (y/m/w/d), DataSize (B/KB/MB/GB/TB via @DataSizeUnit), and collections (lists/sets/arrays from comma-separated scalars or indexed entries; maps from child keys). Custom converters register as beans (in Boot, qualified @ConfigurationPropertiesBinding, declared static because binding runs very early). The intent is: a uniform, extensible coercion layer so every framework surface that takes external string config turns it into a typed value the same way, with one place to teach new types and fail-fast, descriptive errors on bad input. Hidden Spring concepts to replicate as INTENT (not mechanism): conversion is keyed by (source type, target type) pairs with a graph/fallback search; collections are REPLACED not deep-merged across sources; unit annotations supply a default unit when the string omits one; lenient vs strict coercion is a policy knob; conversion failures must say what value, what target type, and where. For leaf, target types are usually statically known at the call site (the field/param type), which is the crux of the rust-native opportunity and the central design tension.

### Candidate realizations

#### Trait-native conversion (FromConfigValue) — compile-time dispatch, no runtime registry
Make conversion a Rust trait the target type implements, resolved by monomorphization at the call site where the target type is statically known (the @Value field type, the config-properties field type). Define `trait FromConfigValue: Sized { fn from_config_value(raw: &ConfigValue, ctx: &ConvertCtx) -> Result<Self, ConvertError>; }` where ConfigValue is leaf's origin-tracked value type (string scalar, list, map, with provenance from origin-tracking). leaf ships blanket/wrapper impls layered over std + serde: any `T: FromStr` gets a leaf impl; enums via a `#[derive(LeafEnum)]` thin macro emitting a case-insensitive match (THIN macro per invariant 10 — only emits a hand-writable match); newtypes Duration/DataSize/Period are leaf-owned types implementing the trait with the Spring suffix grammar. Unit-defaulting (@DurationUnit) is expressed as a typed wrapper or an attribute the binder macro forwards into ctx. Collections: blanket `impl<T: FromConfigValue> FromConfigValue for Vec<T>` splits comma-separated scalars or iterates indexed children; `HashMap<K,V>` reads child keys. The binder and @Value macros emit `T::from_config_value(raw, ctx)?` — pure compile-time dispatch, zero registry lookup. Custom conversions = user implements the trait (orphan-rule: wrap in a leaf-owned `Conv<T>` or provide a `#[derive]`). ConvCtX carries policy (strict/lenient) and locale.

**Pros:** Zero runtime registry, zero TypeId lookup, zero dyn dispatch on the hot binding path — best low-overhead story (invariant 2), entirely stack/monomorphized; Fully stable Rust: built on FromStr/TryFrom/serde patterns, no nightly (invariant 8); Rich diagnostics for free: target type is statically known so errors name the concrete Rust type (invariant 9); compile error if a field type has no FromConfigValue impl — failure surfaces at compile time with full legibility; Thin macros (invariant 10): enum/derive macros emit only a hand-writable match/impl; the binder emits a plain trait call; Origin-agnostic: the trait takes an opaque ConfigValue, so a future WASM-module config source feeding ConfigValues slots in unchanged; No global lock / no shared mutable registry (invariant 5)

**Cons:** Diverges hardest from Spring's runtime ConversionService intent — there is no central bean a user can introspect/replace at runtime; 'register a converter bean' becomes 'impl a trait', a different mental model; Orphan rule: users cannot impl FromConfigValue for std/foreign types they don't own (e.g. a third-party SocketAddr-like) without a newtype wrapper or a leaf-provided Conv<T> bridge — friction vs Spring's anywhere-register; Cannot do arbitrary (SourceType -> TargetType) conversion between two arbitrary runtime types the way GenericConverter can; only string/ConfigValue -> T is first-class. General object-to-object conversion (rare in config but present in Spring's ConversionService) is not modeled; Per-injection-point target-type knowledge is required; a fully dynamic 'convert this Any to that Any' path (needed if autowiring ever coerces between bean types) is absent; Conversion logic is spread across many impls rather than one inspectable service — harder to enumerate 'all supported conversions' for tooling/diagnostics

**Mechanisms considered:** Trait + monomorphized dispatch at the macro-emitted call site (primary); Blanket impls bridging FromStr/TryFrom and serde::Deserialize into FromConfigValue; #[derive] thin macro for case-insensitive enums and unit-annotated newtypes; Newtype Conv<T> escape hatch for orphan-rule cases; serde Deserializer adapter over ConfigValue as an alternative built-in path

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** No cross-crate registry needed for the core path — conversion is resolved by the compiler wherever the target type is in scope, so it works identically on every target including wasm/embedded with zero linker/DCE exposure. Custom converters defined in downstream crates are just trait impls; they are visible wherever the type and trait are imported (normal Rust coherence), NOT auto-discovered into a global pool — which sidesteps the entire linkme/inventory DCE hazard. The cost: there is no link-time 'classpath' of converters; a converter must be a coherent impl reachable at the call site. If leaf later wants Spring-style 'drop a converter crate in and it's globally active', that would require bolting a runtime registry (Realization 2) on top as an optional fallback — kept as a candidate, not decided here. The leaf-owned newtypes (Duration/DataSize/Period) live in leaf-core so their impls are always coherent for downstream users.

**T2 — execution context:** Conversion is synchronous, CPU-only, non-blocking, no I/O — safe to run inline on any executor including during async bean construction without spawning or blocking. It runs at two cold-path moments: config-properties binding (startup validation pass) and @Value resolution at bean instantiation — both already off the steady-state hot path.
