Cross-cuts transaction-management, scopes, and the security/tenant story; must be one coherent policy, not per-feature.
- How is propagation context marshalled across the origin-agnostic provider boundary (WASM module / alternate-origin provider)? A WASM guest needs the ambient context lowered into the WIT call (the proxy reads task-local/bundle and marshals it). R3's explicit Context marshals cleanest (it's already a value at the call seam); R1/R2's ambient model needs the proxy to read-and-lower at the boundary. Binds to the WASM-module layer and the registry/scope substrate — and to whether an alternate-origin provider can even participate in propagation.
- Cancellation and the restore/teardown contract: confirmed sync restore rides Drop cleanly (a Rust win, no try-finally), but does ANY context concern need async cleanup on scope exit (which can't ride Drop — no async Drop on stable)? And at task cancellation mid-flight, is the cooperative-cancel + RAII-restore guarantee sufficient framework-wide, or do scope-based concerns need explicit container-driven teardown? Cross-cuts runtime-lifecycle, context-close, and task-execution's identical cancellation question — needs one unified answer.

### Notes
Confidence: high on intent. The Spring section is dense and explicit (2089-2164) and I read it in full plus the surrounding threading/holder cluster (the holder/strategy pattern 2093-2112, TransactionSynchronizationManager never-inheritable 2114-2118, the four Micrometer abstractions + capture/restore lifecycle 2120-2141, the Reactor/Observation bridges 2143-2147, ContextPropagatingTaskDecorator wiring 2149-2155, and the v4/v7 PropagationContextElement/coroutine + virtual-thread-hazard deltas 2157-2164). The single most important INTENT to preserve: a COMPOSABLE, NEUTRAL, MULTI-SOURCE capture/restore SPI (one capture moves ALL registered contexts atomically across ANY boundary) that is OPT-IN by default (data-bleed + cost) and LEAK-FREE (restore-not-clear, RAII-enforced). I treated 'do NOT port ThreadLocal' as the fixed constraint, because in async Rust the value belongs to the logical task/future, not the OS thread — the charter §3 keystone.

The three realizations are genuinely divergent on the axis that matters HERE — the rust-native answer to 'ambient context across .await': (R1) the Micrometer-faithful runtime accessor-registry + immutable snapshot + RAII restore-Scope, captured/restored explicitly at task boundaries (most intent-faithful, but only correct if the underlying holder is task-scoped, and a one-shot restore is insufficient for value-based contexts across multi-poll migration — it leans on locale-context for the hard part); (R2) context RIDES THE FUTURE via a poll-wrapping combinator re-installing one framework task-local on EVERY poll (the ThreadContextElement/ScopedValue-analog, the only model structurally correct across work-stealing migration, cheapest cross-hop, but needs a runtime-backed task-local primitive leaf must build); (R3) EXPLICIT injectable Context value as primary with the capture/restore SPI demoted to a seam-only ambient bridge (most rust-native and abuse-resistant, inverts Spring's whole ambient premise, viral signatures, and the AOP/event/schedule seams force the ambient bridge anyway so it doesn't escape R1/R2's hard problems — it relocates them). They span most-Spring-faithful (R1) to most-rust-native (R3), with R2 as the structurally-correct middle. R2 and R3 SHARE the Context-bundle value type; R2 and R3 share the poll-wrapper bridge; all three share the ContextAccessor SPI (as full registry in R1, as capture-into-bundle in R2, as seam-adapter in R3) — so they are composable, not exclusive, which I left for Phase 2/3 to merge.

Three things to carry forward: (1) This feature is NOT self-contained — it sits between locale-context (owns the holders / the values) and task-execution (owns the decorator chain / the hops), and is meaningless without both; its design MUST be co-decided with them and with the deferred async-execution model (charter §3). (2) The deepest clash is that ThreadLocal is actively WRONG across an .await (work-stealing migration), not merely 'breaks at hops' — so the correct unit is the future/task, capture-once-at-submit is insufficient (per-poll reinstall needed for value-based contexts, the PropagationContextElement intent), and leaf must BUILD a task-local-across-await primitive (no std ScopedValue-async; tokio::task_local! is runtime-coupled). (3) The Send/'static (often Sync) bound is a hard compile-time wall with no JVM analogue — some ambient values simply cannot propagate on a multi-worker runtime — and the transaction-resource 'never inheritable' case (2114-2118) inverts the default to exclude-by-default, demanding a per-key propagation POLICY model. The RAII-Drop-replaces-try-with-resources point is a real Rust WIN for sync restore (cancellation can't skip cleanup), but does NOT extend to async teardown (no async Drop on stable).  I surfaced the keystone (the ambient-context-across-.await mechanism) as a phase2Question rather than picking one — picking it here would pre-decide the charter §3 deferred async/context model.

## Locale / Request Context Holders  `[locale-context]`

Spring exposes "the current X" — current locale, current request attributes, current transaction synchronizations/resources — as ambient, per-execution lookups: code reads/writes a value that is a property of *this execution* without threading it through every method signature. The mechanism in Spring is the Holder/strategy pattern: a static facade (LocaleContextHolder, RequestContextHolder, TransactionSynchronizationManager) of pure-static methods that delegate to static ThreadLocal fields, with a deliberate dual-field selection (plain NamedThreadLocal vs. inheritable, the inheritable one off by default because copy-on-create is dangerous with pools) and TX state deliberately *never* inheritable (a transactional Connection must live and die on one thread). The named-threadlocal subclass exists purely for heap-dump debuggability. Rejected alternatives are recorded: explicit parameter passing (correct but viral) and a single global static (wrong — not per-request).

This feature is the *storage/holder* half of the ambient-context story; its sibling `context-propagation` is the *capture/restore-across-boundaries* half (the ThreadLocalAccessor/ContextSnapshot SPI). leaf must realize the same intent — a low-ceremony "the current X" ambient lookup keyed by named holder — but the underlying invariant Spring relies on (work is pinned to one thread for the duration) is FALSE in async-first Rust: a future resumes on an arbitrary worker thread after every `.await`. So the realization is intent-parity, not mechanism-parity: the holder must be bound to the *logical execution* (the task/scope), not the OS thread, and the binding must be a structured, RAII-bounded scope (capture/restore, leave-the-thread-pristine) rather than a free static write. We replicate: named holders, dual visibility policy (inherit-into-child vs. strictly-non-inheritable for TX), debuggable names, an origin-agnostic holder registry, and the explicit escape hatch (always a parameter-threading fallback). We do NOT replicate ThreadLocal-as-mechanism on hot async paths.

### Candidate realizations

#### Task-scoped RAII holders over runtime task-local storage (async-native, hot-path)
Each holder is a typed handle `Holder<T>` (e.g. `LOCALE: Holder<Locale>`, `REQUEST: Holder<RequestAttributes>`) declared once in the crate that owns the concept. The ambient value is bound to the *logical task*, not the OS thread, via runtime task-local storage abstracted behind a leaf `AmbientStore` trait (default impl over tokio `task_local!`; a smol/std fallback impl can slot in). API feel: `LOCALE.scope(de_DE, async { ... }).await` enters an RAII scope that sets-on-open and restores-on-close (so a borrowed worker thread is left pristine), and `LOCALE.get()` / `LOCALE.try_get()` reads the current value (`Copy`/clone-out or `with(|v| ...)` for borrow). Holders carry a human-readable name (the rust-native analogue of NamedThreadLocal) surfaced in diagnostics. Dual-visibility policy is a property of the holder declaration: `Visibility::Inherited` means the value is captured into spawned child tasks at spawn time, `Visibility::TaskLocal` (default for TX) means it is NOT — enforced by leaf's own spawn wrapper, which consults the holder registry. The holder registry is origin-agnostic: a holder is just `{ name, TypeId, visibility, accessor-fns }`, so values can come from native code or a future WASM layer. Cross-thread movement (the `.await`-hop and explicit `spawn`) is delegated to the `context-propagation` neighbour, which iterates registered holders and capture/restores them as one snapshot.

**Pros:** Async-correct by construction: bound to the task, so it survives `.await` resumption on a different worker thread — the thing std thread_local cannot do.; Hot-path friendly: reads are a task-local lookup (no global lock, no Arc churn for Copy values); honors the reactive/no-global-lock invariants.; RAII scope = leave-the-thread-pristine capture/restore, directly mirroring Spring's two-phase entry/exit (setValue/restore) and avoiding pooled-worker contamination.; Dual-visibility (inherited vs strictly-task-local) is expressed declaratively per holder, faithfully replicating Spring's dual-field pattern and the TX-never-inheritable rule.; DX: `HOLDER.scope(v, fut).await` and `HOLDER.get()` are minimal-ceremony and hand-writable; a thin `#[holder]` attribute is pure sugar emitting the same static + registration.; Origin-agnostic registry lets context-propagation move *all* holders atomically across one seam (Spring's captureAll/setThreadLocals intent).

**Cons:** Couples the default impl to a runtime's task-local primitive; runtime-agnosticism requires the `AmbientStore` abstraction (extra trait, mild indirection) and a non-tokio impl is non-trivial (std has no task concept).; `scope` wraps a future, so the ambient value is only live inside that future — code that escapes the scope (detached spawn without propagation, manual poll) silently loses it; needs lints/diagnostics, not just runtime nulls.; Reading by borrow (`with`) ties the borrow to the closure; ergonomic friction vs Spring's bare `getLocale()` returning a value.; Spawn-time inheritance requires leaf to own/wrap the spawn seam; a raw `tokio::spawn` bypasses it (the Rust analogue of Spring's pool hazard) — must be documented and ideally lint-guarded.; Task-local set/get is not free on every read for non-Copy types (clone or closure).

**Mechanisms considered:** tokio `task_local!` as the default backing store; a leaf `AmbientStore` trait to keep it runtime-agnostic (tokio / smol / single-thread fallback); RAII `Scope` guard mirroring Micrometer's AutoCloseable Scope (set-on-open, restore-on-close); thin `#[holder]` attribute macro emitting a `static HOLDER: Holder<T>` + a `linkme`/`inventory` registration row (so context-propagation can enumerate holders) — registry mechanism deferred to Phase 2; holder registry as origin-agnostic descriptor `{name, TypeId, visibility, fns}`; delegation of cross-boundary capture/restore to the context-propagation neighbour

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Holder *declarations* live in whichever crate owns the concept (locale in i18n crate, request attrs in a web integration crate, TX sync in the tx crate) — the framework core must NOT hardcode them, so there must be a registry the holders self-register into for context-propagation to enumerate. That registration is the classic cross-crate discovery problem: a `linkme` distributed-slice row (zero-cost, server-targets) or `inventory` (DCE-robust, wasm) — CANDIDATES, decided in Phase 2. Critically, a holder declared in a crate the binary never path-references will silently not register (Layer-0: crate must be linked), and link-time rows can be DCE'd — so the macro must emit a const, data-only descriptor and the substrate must use the force-link / sentinel discipline the cross-crate doc mandates. The macro crate (`leaf-macros`) emits only the thin row via absolute paths; the `Holder<T>` type, registry, and `AmbientStore` trait live in a normal kernel crate. Each holder MUST be declared exactly once across the whole dependency graph (workspace dependency unification prevents two copies of a holder's TypeId/key); a duplicate must be a loud error, not silent shadowing (mirrors bean-naming's collision discipline).

**T2 — execution context:** Reads (`get`/`with`) run in the caller's execution context with no blocking and no await — pure lookups, safe on hot paths. `scope(v, fut)` runs entirely within the caller's task; the value is live for the duration of `fut`'s polling, set on poll-entry and torn down on completion/drop (RAII), so cancellation (future dropped) correctly restores the previous value — no leak. The value never blocks; storing a non-Send value in a holder that is declared `Inherited` is a compile-time/registration error since inheritance crosses to a possibly-Send-requiring spawn. Errors: `get()` when no value is bound returns `None`/a rich 'no current X bound — were you inside HOLDER.scope(...)?' diagnostic naming the holder, never a panic by default. Crossing an explicit thread/task hop is NOT automatic here — it is the context-propagation neighbour's job; a detached spawn without propagation deliberately sees no value (Spring's isolate-by-default async stance), and TX-visibility holders are *forbidden* from inheriting at all.

#### Explicitly-threaded ambient context object (`&Ctx` / `RequestContext` passed by reference)
Reject hidden ambient storage on the hot path entirely; make 'the current X' an explicit, borrow-checked parameter — the rust-native answer to Spring's own 'rejected alternative' (explicit parameter passing), reframed as *not* viral because leaf threads one opaque context handle, not N parameters. A single `Context<'a>` (or `&Ctx`) carries a typed, append-only stack of ambient values: `ctx.get::<Locale>()`, `ctx.with::<Locale>(de_DE)` returns a child `Ctx<'_>` borrowing the parent (locale shadows parent — same one-directional shadow as container-hierarchy). Holders become typed keys (`struct Locale; impl AmbientKey for Locale { type Value = ... }`) rather than static storage. Framework-managed entry points (request handlers, scheduled tasks, event listeners, bean lifecycle hooks) receive `&Ctx` from leaf; user code that wants 'the current X' takes `&Ctx` (or leaf injects it). Crossing a thread boundary is trivial and explicit: the owned `OwnedCtx` (clone of the relevant entries) is `Send` and moved into the spawned task — no capture/restore SPI needed for the common case. The holder registry still exists (origin-agnostic typed keys + names) so generic code and context-propagation can enumerate entries, but storage is the context object, not ambient TLS.

**Pros:** Borrow checker enforces correctness: you cannot read 'the current X' where none is in scope — it's a compile error, not a runtime null; richest-possible diagnostic timing (charter invariant 9).; No thread-vs-task hazard at all — the context is data you move; crossing a boundary is `OwnedCtx` move into `spawn`, inherently Send-checked, no hidden propagation magic.; No global state, no TLS, no life-before-main, no link-time DCE hazard for the *storage* (only the optional key registry); maximal memory discipline (stack-passed borrow).; Trivially testable: construct a `Ctx`, pass it — no container, no runtime, mirrors injection-styles' POJO-testability ethos.; Shadowing semantics (`ctx.with(...)`) reuse the exact one-directional visibility model of container-hierarchy — conceptual unification.; Visibility policy is explicit: you choose what you clone into `OwnedCtx` for a child task; TX-resources simply aren't put there (never-inheritable becomes the default, not a special case).

**Cons:** Viral in exactly the way Spring rejected: every function that *might* need the current X must take `&Ctx` — high ceremony, the opposite of Spring's 'free ambient lookup' DX (charter invariant 1: DX is #1).; Deep call stacks must thread `&Ctx` through layers that don't themselves use it — friction leaf can only partly hide with macros.; Doesn't match the Spring *feel* at all; a library author used to `LocaleContextHolder.getLocale()` will find this alien — intent-parity is weakened even if correctness is strongest.; Generic/third-party code that wasn't written to take `&Ctx` (e.g. a logging formatter wanting current locale) cannot reach it — the very gap ambient holders exist to close.; Macro sugar to inject `&Ctx` risks hiding logic in macros (tension with thin-macro invariant).

**Mechanisms considered:** explicit `&Ctx`/`Context<'a>` parameter threading (Spring's named rejected alternative, reframed); typed `AmbientKey` keys instead of static holders; borrow-based child contexts with shadowing (mirrors container-hierarchy); `OwnedCtx: Send` clone for explicit cross-task hand-off (no capture/restore SPI for the common path); optional thin macro to inject `&Ctx` into framework-entry-point handler signatures; an origin-agnostic key registry kept only for generic enumeration / context-propagation interop

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The storage has no cross-crate discovery problem — it's a passed object — which is its biggest T1 advantage: no linkme/inventory, no DCE, no Layer-0 linking hazard for ambient values. The only cross-crate surface is the optional typed-key registry (so generic code / diagnostics can name keys); that can be a small per-key descriptor or omitted entirely if all access is statically typed. Keys are ordinary types with trait impls — fully cross-crate via the trait system, no link-time tricks, works identically on wasm/embedded. The cost is that there is no way for a crate that *only* reads 'the current X' generically (without a `&Ctx` in hand) to participate — there is no ambient fallback, by design.

**T2 — execution context:** Maximally explicit, which is exactly what charter invariant 3 wants. Reads are borrows — zero blocking, zero await, hot-path safe. Crossing a thread/task boundary is an explicit `OwnedCtx` move; the compiler enforces `Send`, so a non-Send ambient value simply cannot cross (TX resources naturally stay put). Cancellation is a non-issue: the `Ctx` is owned by the future's stack frame and dropped with it. Errors are compile-time (missing `&Ctx`) wherever possible, else a typed `None` from `ctx.get::<K>()` with a key-named diagnostic. No hidden execution context anywhere — what runs where is visible in every signature.

#### Hybrid: ambient task-scope as the DX default, with a thread-local sync-bridge and an explicit-context escape hatch
Treat the two prior realizations as one layered system rather than alternatives — the holder is an abstraction with pluggable backing, chosen by execution context. Primary backing = task-scoped store (Realization 1) for async code. A second backing = a `std::thread_local!` *bridge* used only on synchronous, thread-pinned bridge points (e.g. inside a `spawn_blocking` body, or a sync FFI/JDBC-analogue callback), so legacy/sync code can still do `LOCALE.get()` while leaf's seam keeps the thread-local in sync with the task value for the duration of the blocking call (set-on-enter, restore-on-exit, exactly mirroring Spring's holder but scoped to the blocking island). A third, always-available layer is the explicit `&Ctx` escape hatch (Realization 2) for code that wants zero ambient magic. All three share ONE origin-agnostic holder registry and ONE capture/restore SPI (the context-propagation neighbour): `Holder<T>` exposes `get/scope` regardless of which backing is active, and a per-task 'active backing' selector decides where a read resolves. API feel: identical `LOCALE.scope(...)`/`LOCALE.get()` everywhere; the difference is invisible to the user, which is the DX win — Spring's 'the current X just works' across the async/sync seam. The dual-visibility policy and named-holder debuggability live on the shared `Holder` descriptor.

**Pros:** Best DX-parity with Spring: `the current X` works the same in async tasks, inside spawn_blocking, and in sync bridge callbacks — the user doesn't pick a mechanism (charter invariant 1).; Meets sync code where it is (the thread-local bridge) without poisoning the async hot path with thread-local semantics — async stays task-scoped.; One shared holder registry + one capture/restore SPI = maximal unification (charter invariant 11); context-propagation moves everything uniformly across both task-hops and the sync island boundary.; Provides the explicit `&Ctx` escape hatch the charter requires at every level (invariant 10/§6) while keeping the ambient default ergonomic.; Thread-local bridge confines TLS hazards to a known, bounded blocking island (where the thread *is* pinned, so TLS is actually correct) — Spring's invariant restored exactly where it holds.

**Cons:** Most complex: three backings + a selector is more machinery than either pure option; risks violating 'lock with intent / minimal moving parts' if the selector logic grows.; Two sources of truth (task value vs thread-local bridge) must be kept coherent across every blocking-island boundary; a missed restore on the bridge is the classic Spring leak, reintroduced in the one place we allow TLS.; Diagnosing 'why is the current X wrong here' spans two mechanisms — harder mental model than either pure approach (some tension with rich-diagnostics intent: the *cause* can be the seam, not the user code).; More for Phase 2 to unify; the selector and bridge lifetimes interact with the scope/lifetime model and the async-context model, both deferred.; Thread-local bridge is still a `Send`/pooled-thread hazard if a blocking island itself spawns — must forbid inheritance through the bridge (re-deriving the TX-never-inheritable rule a second time).

**Mechanisms considered:** layered backing: task-local primary + thread_local sync-bridge + explicit &Ctx escape hatch; per-task 'active backing' selector resolving a read to the right store; set-on-enter/restore-on-exit thread-local bridge scoped to spawn_blocking / sync-FFI islands; one shared origin-agnostic holder registry across all backings; unifying capture/restore through the context-propagation SPI for both task-hops and sync-island seams; thin `#[holder]` macro emitting the shared descriptor regardless of backing

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same holder-registry discovery question as Realization 1 (a linkme distributed-slice vs inventory row per holder, force-link/sentinel discipline, declare-once-or-loud-error) — CANDIDATES for Phase 2. The added T1 surface is that the thread-local bridge must be available on every target including wasm (where there are no OS threads / spawn_blocking) — so on wasm the bridge layer is simply absent and only the task-scope + explicit-&Ctx layers exist, which is fine because the runtime API is identical (the cross-crate doc's 'keep the runtime API identical across substrates' principle). The registry must be enumerable by context-propagation across crates regardless of which backings a given target compiles in.

**T2 — execution context:** This realization is *about* execution context: reads resolve against whichever backing matches the current context (async task -> task-local; inside a blocking island -> thread-local bridge; with an explicit `&Ctx` -> that). Async reads never block. The thread-local bridge is set only on entry to a synchronous, thread-pinned island and torn down on exit (RAII), so it is correct precisely because the thread *is* pinned there; it must refuse to inherit into any sub-spawn. Cancellation: dropping the task tears down the task-local scope; dropping/returning from the blocking island tears down the bridge — both RAII. Errors: a read with no value bound in *any* active backing yields a holder-named diagnostic that also reports which backing was consulted (so 'lost across the spawn_blocking seam' is a distinguishable, explained failure, honoring invariant 9). Send discipline: values crossing the async task-hop go through context-propagation (Send-checked); values living only in the thread-local bridge never cross a thread by construction.

### Rust clashes
- std::thread_local! is the WRONG mechanism here: Spring's holders rest on the invariant that a unit of work stays on one OS thread, but in async-first Rust a future resumes on an arbitrary worker thread after every `.await`. A thread-local set before an await is silently invisible after it (and may leak onto a pooled worker for the next task). The whole point of the Spring holder breaks — so leaf must bind to the *task/logical execution*, not the thread.
- Rust has no static-method facade over hidden mutable global state in the Spring sense, and the charter forbids global locks / coarse shared-mutable state. A holder cannot be a freely-writable global; it must be a structured, RAII-bounded *scope* (set-on-open, restore-on-close) so the borrowed worker is left pristine — closer to Micrometer's AutoCloseable Scope than to Spring's bare `setLocale()`.
- No InheritableThreadLocal equivalent: child-task inheritance must be done explicitly by leaf's spawn seam capturing and re-binding the value, and it forces a `Send` bound. Spring's 'off by default' becomes 'opt-in per holder AND Send-checked', and the TX-never-inheritable rule is enforced by simply never registering TX holders for inheritance.
- No global classpath: Spring's holders are just static fields in known framework classes; leaf's holders are declared in whatever crate owns the concept and must self-register into a registry for context-propagation to enumerate — reintroducing the cross-crate discovery/DCE/force-link hazards (a holder in an unreferenced crate silently doesn't register).
- NamedThreadLocal exists purely for JVM heap-dump debuggability; Rust has no heap-dump-by-thread-local concept, so the 'name' must be repurposed into leaf's own startup/diagnostic surface (naming the holder in 'no current X bound' errors) rather than a GC-introspection aid.
- Spring leans on GC: a leaked thread-local value is merely a memory smell. In Rust, a value bound in a scope that outlives its intended lifetime is a correctness/borrow problem; RAII scope guards make leakage structurally impossible on the happy path but make 'escaping the scope' (detached spawn) a real, visible failure rather than a silent stale read.
- No async Drop: tearing down a holder scope is synchronous (Drop), which is fine for the value binding itself, but any *async* cleanup tied to 'the current X' (e.g. closing a TX resource) cannot ride Drop and must be an explicit container-driven shutdown — consistent with the framework-wide async-Drop-absence constraint.
- Borrow vs value: Spring returns the value by reference freely (GC-backed); a Rust holder over non-Copy data must offer either clone-out or a `with(|&v|...)` closure, a small but real ergonomic divergence from `getLocale()`.

### Interactions
- **context-propagation** — The defining relationship: this feature is the per-execution *storage/holder* half; context-propagation is the *capture-and-restore-across-boundaries* half (the ThreadLocalAccessor/ContextSnapshot SPI). Holders self-register into one origin-agnostic registry; context-propagation iterates it to snapshot all current values and re-bind them after a thread/task hop. They MUST share one registry and one capture/restore Scope abstraction — this is the prime unification target.
- **messages-i18n** — The current Locale holder is the canonical input to MessageSource resolution: `resolve(code, args)` reads the current locale from this feature's LOCALE holder when no explicit locale is passed — exactly Spring's LocaleContextHolder feeding MessageSource. messages-i18n is the most important consumer of the locale holder.
- **transaction-management** — TransactionSynchronizationManager is realized via this feature's holders, but with the strictly-non-inheritable visibility policy: TX resources (the connection/EntityManager-analogue) and synchronizations are bound per-execution and MUST NOT be captured into spawned child tasks (a transactional resource must live and die on one logical execution). This feature must make 'never inherit' a first-class holder policy, and the async-Drop absence means TX teardown is explicit, not Drop-driven.
- **scopes** — Request/session scoping resolves 'the current request/session' via a request-attributes holder; scoped-bean lookup keys off the ambient request identity this feature provides. Holder lifetime and scope lifetime are coupled — the holder scope likely opens/closes at the same seam the request scope does.
- **scoped-proxies** — A scoped proxy resolves its live target per call by consulting 'the current X' (request/session) — i.e. it reads this feature's holder at invocation time. The holder is the lookup substrate that lets a singleton hold a stable proxy over a shorter-lived target.
- **task-execution** — Every TaskExecutor thread/task hop is exactly where ambient context is lost; leaf's executor seam is where context-propagation captures this feature's holders. The decision of whether to propagate by default (Spring: opt-in, isolate-by-default) is a shared policy between this feature, context-propagation, and task-execution.
- **async-execution** — @Async-style method offloading crosses a task boundary; whether 'the current X' follows depends on the propagate-context policy. Self-invocation/void-return caveats from async-execution interact with whether a holder scope is entered around the offloaded work.
- **event-multicaster** — Synchronous (default) event dispatch runs on the caller's execution and so preserves the current holders automatically (mirroring Spring's 'event behaves like a method call'); async dispatch loses them unless context-propagation re-binds — the same opt-in propagation seam. transactional-events specifically read the TX-sync holder to defer to a commit phase.
- **container-hierarchy** — Realization 2's `ctx.with(...)` child-context shadowing reuses container-hierarchy's one-directional visibility model (child sees/overrides parent, parent never sees child). The conceptual shape of nested ambient scopes mirrors nested container scopes — a unification opportunity.
- **bean-naming** — Holder names must be unique across the dependency graph and collisions must be a loud error rather than silent shadowing — the same collision-discipline bean-naming establishes. 'Declare each holder exactly once' is the analogue of unique bean names.
- **aware-callbacks** — Like Aware callbacks inject container infrastructure into beans that need it, leaf may inject 'the current X' accessors (or the &Ctx) into beans/handlers that declare a need — the ambient holder is infrastructure a bean can be made aware of at a framework entry point.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- The async execution & context-propagation model (charter §5): what is leaf's canonical task-local primitive — tokio task_local, a runtime-agnostic AmbientStore trait, or a structured-concurrency scope — and is leaf willing to take a soft tokio dependency for the default backing? This decides Realization 1/3's foundation and is shared with context-propagation and task-execution.
- Propagate-by-default vs isolate-by-default across thread/task hops (Spring chose opt-in for data-bleed/CPU reasons): is that the right default for an async-first Rust framework where hops are pervasive, or should leaf invert it? A cross-cutting policy spanning this feature, context-propagation, task-execution, async-execution, and event-multicaster.
- Ambient-everywhere (DX-parity, Realization 1/3) vs explicit-&Ctx (correctness-max, Realization 2): this is a DX-vs-explicitness call that touches charter invariants 1 (DX #1) and 3 (explicit execution context). Is hidden ambient storage acceptable in an async-first framework, or does it violate 'what runs where is explicit everywhere'? Likely resolved by the hybrid, but the hybrid's cost must be weighed.
- The holder registry's cross-crate substrate (linkme distributed-slice vs inventory vs build-time codegen vs explicit registration) and the static-vs-dynamic-classpath duality (link-time default + optional WASM/dynamic directory source) — shared with every discovery-based feature (component-scanning, auto-configuration, post-processor-spi, candidate-resolver). Must a holder declared in an unreferenced crate force-link, and how is 'declare-once' enforced cross-crate?
- The scope/lifetime/ownership model (the keystone deferred decision): how does a holder scope's lifetime relate to bean scopes (request/session) and to borrow lifetimes? Does `with(|&v|...)` borrow or must values be Arc/clone? This determines the holder's value-vs-borrow ergonomics and its memory discipline.
- Send/Sync discipline for ambient values: should leaf require all inheritable holder values to be `Send + Sync`, and how does that interact with the trait_variant/dyn+Send decisions already flagged in the nightly research? Strictly-task-local (non-inherited) values could avoid Send — is that distinction worth encoding in the type system?
- Unified capture/restore Scope primitive: can ONE RAII Scope abstraction serve this feature's holder scoping, context-propagation's snapshot restore, and the thread-local sync-bridge (Realization 3) — and how does it behave under cancellation given no async Drop? Shared with transaction-management's resource teardown.

### Notes
Realization 1 (task-scoped RAII holders) is the closest async-native intent-parity to Spring and the natural hot-path default; Realization 2 (explicit &Ctx) is the borrow-checked correctness extreme and the always-present escape hatch the charter mandates; Realization 3 (hybrid) is the DX-maximizing superset and most likely the Phase 3 convergence target, but its multi-backing complexity must be justified against the minimal-machinery invariant. All three deliberately share ONE origin-agnostic holder registry and ONE capture/restore Scope so context-propagation can move everything uniformly — that shared substrate is the real unification payload, not the per-feature holder.

Note the deliberate scoping split: this feature OWNS the holder abstraction, the named-holder descriptor, the dual-visibility policy (inherited vs strictly-task-local/TX), and the read/scope API; it DELEGATES all cross-boundary movement to context-propagation. Keep that boundary crisp so Phase 2/3 don't duplicate the SPI.

The framework core must NOT hardcode which holders exist — locale lives in i18n, request attributes in a web integration crate (out of core scope per charter §1), TX sync in the tx crate. Core provides only `Holder<T>` + registry + Scope. This keeps the core integration-agnostic (charter invariant 6) and lets dynamic/WASM-sourced ambient values slot in later via the same origin-agnostic registry.

A holder-name field is retained from Spring's NamedThreadLocal but repurposed: not for heap dumps (no Rust analogue) but for leaf's own 'no current X bound' diagnostics and startup-instrumentation, satisfying the rich-diagnostics invariant.

std::thread_local! survives in this design only as a *bounded sync-bridge* inside thread-pinned blocking islands (Realization 3), never as the async hot-path mechanism — a point worth carrying loudly into Phase 2 to prevent a naive ThreadLocal-as-holder reading of Spring parity.

## Container Concurrency Contract  `[concurrency-contract]`

Spring's concurrency contract is a DOCTRINE, not a mechanism, and it is the single most-misunderstood guarantee in the framework. Read precisely (spring doc 1924-1952, themes line 69): the container draws ONE clean division of labor — "the container owns assembly, the developer owns runtime concurrency." It guarantees exactly two things and explicitly disclaims a third. (1) SAFE PUBLICATION / VISIBILITY: a fully-built singleton's post-construction state (constructor/setter-injected config, anything set during init/@PostConstruct) acquires "final-like visibility" to every thread that later observes the bean — Spring earns this via the JMM happens-before edge of the singleton creation lock release → later acquisition, and the doc is emphatic that config-only fields set solely during init need NO `volatile` because safe publication already gives them effectively-final semantics (1942-1944). (2) It does NOT make your methods thread-safe: a shared singleton is invoked concurrently by every request thread, and any post-publication mutation of shared state is a data race the container will not prevent — the `volatile`/lock requirement applies ONLY to fields mutated AFTER publication. The design intent (1940): making every method `synchronized` would annihilate the whole rationale for sharing one stateless instance and impose needless contention on the overwhelmingly common stateless case; the container CANNOT know your invariants, so a "thread-safe-by-default" container is both impossible and pessimal. Spring therefore solves the generic JMM problem it can solve (safe publication) and hands the application-specific problem (mutable runtime state) back to the developer with well-shaped tools, NOT abandonment. (3) SCOPE IS THE PRIMARY CONCURRENCY DESIGN LEVER (1948-1952): "prototype for stateful, singleton for stateless" is not a perf tip but THE concurrency lever — statefulness in a singleton is the #1 source of subtle data races, so the container reframes "reason about locks" into a SCOPING decision it can mechanically honor (delegated to the scopes feature). The mental model to instill: "the container guarantees you safely SEE a fully-built bean; it guarantees nothing about what happens when many threads CALL it." leaf must replicate this INTENT — a precise, teachable contract that (a) guarantees safe publication/visibility of finished singletons across threads, (b) explicitly delegates per-instance mutable-state safety to the developer, (c) frames scope choice as the first-class concurrency tool, (d) refuses to globally lock or synchronize method calls — WITHOUT importing Spring's mechanism (a JMM happens-before edge bought by a singleton MUTEX, the `volatile`-on-mutable-fields advice, or the runtime-lock-as-the-publication-primitive). The deep rust-native twist: in Rust this contract is LARGELY ENFORCED BY THE TYPE SYSTEM rather than promised at runtime. `Send`/`Sync` are the compiler's safe-publication-and-shared-access proof: a singleton shared as `Arc<dyn Trait + Send + Sync>` is provably safely publishable AND its concurrent-call safety is checked, not hoped — so "the container guarantees safe publication" becomes "the type bound on the shared handle IS the guarantee," and "mutating shared state is your problem" becomes "the borrow checker forbids it unless you reach for `Mutex`/atomics/concurrent collections, exactly as Spring points at volatile/locks." This feature must FRAME and ANCHOR that doctrine across the container — it does NOT pick the singleton-handle type (the §3 ownership keystone), the Send/Sync bound placement, the registry substrate, or the async-execution model; it states the CONTRACT those choices must collectively satisfy.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — The contract as a TYPE BOUND
Realize the contract not as a runtime promise but as a compile-time INVARIANT carried by the shared-handle type, and document/diagnose it as the framework's stated concurrency doctrine. The core claim: in Rust, `Send` + `Sync` on the type a singleton is shared as are NOT incidental bounds — they ARE the rust-native encoding of Spring's two-part contract. (a) SAFE PUBLICATION: the container builds a singleton on the bootstrap task, then PUBLISHES it by moving the finished value into the shared store as `Arc<dyn Trait + Send + Sync>` (handle type is bean-instantiation's/the §3 keystone's call — this feature only fixes the BOUND). `Send + Sync` is precisely the compiler's proof that the value can be observed by other threads soundly; the `Arc` clone/atomic-refcount that hands it out establishes the happens-before edge for free (the publish-side write is sequenced before the consumer's atomic load of the Arc) — so leaf gets Spring's 'final-like visibility' WITHOUT a singleton mutex, because the move-then-share is itself the publication. Config-only fields need no `volatile` for the same reason Spring's don't, only now it's enforced: an immutable field of a `Sync` type is provably safe to share. (b) NOT RUNTIME-THREAD-SAFE, BY THE TYPE SYSTEM: because a singleton is shared as `&self` behind an `Arc`, the borrow checker FORBIDS mutating a plain field through it — to mutate shared state you MUST reach for `Mutex`/`RwLock`/atomics/`Mutex`-free concurrent structures, exactly the tools Spring points developers at, except the compiler refuses to let you forget. This is Spring's contract made UN-FORGETTABLE: the footgun (mutate-without-guarding) is a compile error, not a latent race. The feature's deliverables are therefore: (1) a fixed, documented CONTRACT statement (the doctrine) that every shared-scope handle is `Send + Sync + 'static` and that this is the safe-publication guarantee; (2) the diagnostic surface — when a user's bean type is NOT `Send + Sync`, the failure must be a rich, doctrine-citing error ('singleton beans are shared across executor threads and must be Send + Sync; if this type holds mutable per-interaction state, make it a prototype/request-scoped bean instead — see scope-as-concurrency-lever') rather than a raw trait-bound error; (3) the explicit carve-out that non-Send/non-Sync types are legal at NARROWER scopes (prototype hand-off-and-forget needs only `Send` to cross a spawn, or not even that if used inline; request-scoped needs whatever its store demands). No new runtime machinery — the contract rides the bounds the registry and instantiation already must choose.

**Pros:** Strictly STRONGER than Spring: the 'you must guard post-publication mutation' half of the contract is COMPILE-ENFORCED by the borrow checker rather than left to developer vigilance — the #1 latent-race footgun Spring can only warn about becomes impossible to ship (charter §2.9 'fail as early as the richest diagnostic allows', here pushed all the way to compile time where the error IS legible).; Zero new runtime mechanism and zero overhead (charter §2.5): the guarantee is a type bound the singleton handle must carry anyway to be shareable on a work-stealing async executor — no global lock, no singleton mutex, no per-method synchronization; the Arc handoff that already publishes the value IS the happens-before edge, so safe publication is free.; Highest intent fidelity to the DOCTRINE: it preserves Spring's exact mental model ('safely SEE a built bean; nothing guaranteed about concurrent CALLS') and its exact delegation ('reach for the standard concurrency tools for your own mutable state') — only the enforcement moves from runtime/JMM to the type system, which is the textbook intent-not-mechanism translation (charter §2.2).; Async-first by construction (§2.3): `Send + Sync + 'static` is exactly what tokio's multi-thread work-stealing runtime demands of anything shared across tasks, so the contract and the async-execution requirement are the SAME bound — the doctrine falls directly out of the runtime discipline rather than being bolted on.; Makes scope-as-concurrency-lever concrete and type-checked: 'if it has mutable per-interaction state it should not be a singleton' becomes 'a type that isn't cheaply shareable/Sync wants a narrower scope' — the lever Spring states in prose, leaf can guide via the type system and diagnostics.

**Cons:** The contract's enforcement strength is HOSTAGE to the §3 ownership-keystone and registry-substrate decisions this feature must NOT make: IF the shared handle is `Arc<dyn Trait + Send + Sync>` the story is clean, but if Phase 2 picks an arena/borrow model or a different erasure substrate, WHERE the `Send + Sync` bound lives (on the trait, the handle, the registry value) shifts — so this realization states the bound as a CONTRACT the keystone must honor, not a settled type, and is only as strong as that downstream choice.; `Send + Sync` is necessary but NOT sufficient for Spring's full visibility story in one edge case: a singleton built on the bootstrap task and only ever shared via Arc is safely published, but a bean whose factory internally spawns a thread that fills a field AFTER the Arc is published reintroduces the exact post-publication-mutation hazard — the type system catches the mutation (needs interior mutability) but the 'is it fully built at publish' boundary is bean-instantiation's safe-publication-at-handoff job, not a bound; the two features must compose (the bound guarantees safe SHARING; instantiation guarantees the value is FINISHED before sharing).; Over-constraining the common case is a real risk: forcing `Send + Sync` on EVERY bean (including ones a user knows are single-threaded) is stricter than Spring, which lets a non-thread-safe singleton exist (and race). leaf must offer the narrower-scope escape (prototype/request, possibly `!Send` local) and a documented single-threaded-runtime carve-out, or DX (§2.1) suffers for users who genuinely don't share across threads.; The diagnostic quality is the whole DX value here, and a good 'your bean isn't Send+Sync, here's why and the scope fix' message is non-trivial to produce from a raw trait-solver bound error — it likely needs a dedicated check/where-clause-with-diagnostic-attribute or a startup-validation pass, coupling this feature to the unified-diagnostics model (§2.9, deferred).; States a doctrine but adds little NEW code, so its 'realization' is mostly a contract + diagnostic spec layered onto bean-instantiation/scopes/registry — a reviewer could fairly ask whether it's a feature or an invariant; the answer (it's the load-bearing CONTRACT those features must satisfy) must be made explicit so it isn't dissolved into its neighbours and lost.

**Mechanisms considered:** `Send + Sync + 'static` as the mandatory bound on the shared singleton handle = the rust-native encoding of safe publication + shared-access safety (the Arc atomic handoff supplies the happens-before edge, replacing Spring's singleton-lock JMM edge); borrow-checker-as-delegation: `&self`-through-`Arc` forbids plain-field mutation, FORCING interior mutability (Mutex/RwLock/atomics) for post-publication state — Spring's 'use volatile/locks' made un-forgettable; a doctrine-citing diagnostic (custom on_unimplemented / dedicated validation message) when a bean type isn't Send+Sync, steering to the scope-as-lever fix rather than a raw trait error; scope-graded bounds as the carve-out: narrower scopes (prototype/request) relax to the minimal bound their store/handoff needs (Send-to-spawn, or none if inline), so non-shareable stateful types are legal where they belong; safe-publication-at-handoff is delegated to bean-instantiation (value is finished BEFORE it is shared); this feature owns only the SHARING bound, not the finished-ness boundary

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** This realization is almost entirely a CROSS-CRATE CONTRACT rather than a registry of contributions, which is its T1 strength: the `Send + Sync + 'static` bound is a property of the bean TYPE and is checked at the contributing crate's own compile, in that crate, with no link-time aggregation and no DCE exposure — a downstream crate's `#[component]` bean that isn't `Send + Sync` fails to compile WHERE IT IS DEFINED, the ideal locality (charter §2.9). The thin macro (leaf-macros) emits the bean's descriptor/factory via absolute paths exactly as the registry features require; this feature adds only that the emitted shared-handle type carries the contract bound, so the macro stays logic-free (§2.10). The contract bound must be stated in ONE ultra-stable place in leaf-core (the shared-handle / registry-value type alias or trait), since every contributing crate's emitted code hard-codes a path to it (cross-crate doc §99-102) — if the bound is part of that type, it propagates to every contribution automatically with zero per-crate restating. The origin-agnostic mandate is honored cleanly: a future WASM-module provider or a test-double publishes through the SAME shared handle and therefore inherits the SAME contract bound — the container cannot tell native from dynamic, and the safe-publication contract is identical because it lives on the handle type, not on the construction path. No anti-DCE self-check is owed by THIS feature (it registers nothing iterable); the registry/discovery features own that. The static link-time registry (default 'classpath') and the optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-module source are both just producers of contract-bound handles — CANDIDATES, not a decision. Generics-cannot-register is inherited (a generic bean has no 'static TypeId until instantiated; the macro hard-errors), and is actually relevant here because Send/Sync auto-traits on a generic depend on its type parameters — so the contract is checkable only on concrete instantiations.

**T2 — execution context:** The contract is fundamentally a STATEMENT ABOUT execution context, so T2 is its substance. SAFE PUBLICATION: a singleton is built on the bootstrap task (cold path) and PUBLISHED by moving the finished value into the shared store as a `Send + Sync` handle; the atomic Arc-clone handed to each consumer establishes the happens-before edge (publish-write sequenced-before consumer's atomic load) — so every executor worker thread that later resolves the bean observes the fully-built state, with NO singleton mutex and NO global lock (§2.5). This is the rust-native replacement for Spring's JMM-via-singleton-lock edge: the move-then-atomically-share IS the publication. RUNTIME CALLS: once published, the singleton is invoked `&self` concurrently from arbitrary executor tasks on arbitrary worker threads; the `Sync` bound is the compiler's proof those concurrent `&self` calls are sound, and the borrow checker forbids un-guarded shared mutation — so 'the container guarantees nothing about concurrent calls' becomes 'the type system guarantees concurrent calls are at least memory-safe, and forces you to choose a guard for any shared mutation.' BLOCKING/CANCELLATION are NOT this feature's concern at call time (a bean method that blocks the executor is execution-context discipline owned elsewhere); but the doctrine explicitly notes the analogue of Spring's @PostConstruct-deadlock-trap: heavy/blocking init must not run under any creation guard, and the publication boundary (finished-before-shared) is bean-instantiation's. SCOPE-AS-LEVER in T2 terms: a type carrying mutable per-interaction state should be resolved per-request/per-resolution (narrower scope) so each concurrent execution gets its own instance and the shared-mutation question never arises — the lever steers state OUT of the shared-across-threads context entirely. Errors propagate as rich, doctrine-citing diagnostics (compile-time where legible, else startup validation) per §2.9; no runtime error path is added by the contract itself.

#### R2 — Scope-graded SharingPolicy table
Reframe the contract NOT as one universal bound but as the column of a small, declarative SHARING-POLICY table indexed by scope — making 'scope is the primary concurrency lever' literally the structure of the feature. Each scope (singleton, prototype, request/session/custom — owned by the scopes feature) carries a `SharingPolicy { required_bound, publication, mutation_doctrine }`: SINGLETON = { bound: Send + Sync + 'static, publication: SafePublishOnceAtBuild (move finished value into shared store, share via atomic handle — the happens-before edge), mutation: SharedAcrossThreads → developer must guard post-publication mutation with interior mutability }; PROTOTYPE = { bound: Send-if-it-crosses-a-spawn else none, publication: HandOffOwned (no sharing, owned move to caller), mutation: ExclusivelyOwned → no guarding needed, the caller has &mut by ownership }; REQUEST/SESSION = { bound: whatever the per-context store demands, publication: PerContextKey (one instance per ambient key, shared only within that execution's lifetime), mutation: SharedWithinContext → guard only if the context spans concurrent tasks }. The feature's job is to DEFINE this table as the canonical concurrency-contract surface and to make the engine consult it at publish time: when bean-instantiation finishes a bean, it asks the scope's SharingPolicy how to publish and which bound to assert. This turns Spring's prose doctrine ('singleton for stateless, prototype for stateful') into a MECHANICAL policy the container honors — and crucially explains WHY each scope has the concurrency properties it does, in one place, rather than scattering 'singletons are Send+Sync' across the codebase. The 'NOT runtime-thread-safe' half is the `mutation_doctrine` column: it is documentation + diagnostics, not enforcement code, except where the bound (Sync) makes un-guarded mutation a compile error. Custom scopes (via the Scope SPI) supply their own SharingPolicy row, so a custom 'tenant' scope declares its own sharing/bound/mutation semantics — the contract is extensible alongside the scope mechanism.

**Pros:** Makes scope-as-concurrency-lever the literal SHAPE of the feature (charter intent fidelity to 1948-1952): the doctrine 'stateful→narrower scope' is encoded as a policy table where each scope's row explains its exact sharing/bound/mutation contract — the single most teachable realization of WHY scope is the concurrency tool, in one auditable place.; Unifies the contract across ALL scopes (charter §2.11): instead of 'singletons happen to be Send+Sync' as a scattered fact, every scope (incl. custom) declares its SharingPolicy uniformly, so the concurrency semantics of the whole scope family are one consistent, reviewable table — and a new scope cannot be added without stating its concurrency contract.; Correctly graduates the bound (a real DX win over R1's blanket Send+Sync): a prototype stateful bean legitimately need NOT be Sync, a request-scoped bean's bound is tied to its store — so leaf doesn't over-constrain the narrow-scope cases the way a universal singleton-style bound would, fixing R1's main con (§2.1).; Gives the publication discipline a precise per-scope home: SafePublishOnce (singleton) vs HandOffOwned (prototype) vs PerContextKey (request) are exactly the three publication shapes the upstream bean-instantiation/scopes designs already identified — this feature names them as the CONCURRENCY-relevant facet and ties each to its bound, composing cleanly with those neighbours.; Origin-agnostic and extensible: a WASM-module or test-double provider publishes through whatever SharingPolicy its scope dictates (identical to a native bean of that scope), and custom scopes extend the table — the contract scales with the scope SPI rather than being singleton-special-cased.

**Cons:** Risks OVER-ENGINEERING a doctrine into machinery: Spring's contract is essentially 'singletons are safely published, guard your own mutation, choose scope wisely' — formalizing it as a per-scope policy table with three columns may be more apparatus than the intent needs, and borders on encoding decisions (which bound per scope, how publication works) that belong to scopes/bean-instantiation/the §3 keystone, not to this framing feature (charter Phase-1 'implementation-agnostic').; Heavy overlap/boundary-ambiguity with scopes and bean-instantiation: the publication column IS bean-instantiation's safe-publication-at-handoff work, and the required_bound is entangled with scopes' multiplicity + the §3 ownership keystone — so the SharingPolicy table could end up RESTATING those features' decisions rather than adding a distinct concurrency contract, a coherence/duplication hazard that must be policed (this feature should own only the CONCURRENCY READING of each row, deferring the mechanism to its neighbours).; The 'mutation_doctrine' column is mostly non-enforced prose (except where Sync makes it a compile error), so two of the three columns are documentation — a table that is 1/3 mechanism and 2/3 doctrine may not justify being a runtime-consulted structure; it might be better as a documented contract (R1) than a consulted policy object.; Adds a per-publish indirection (consult the scope's SharingPolicy) that, while cold for singletons, sits near the prototype/request hot path — must be made allocation-free and ideally compile-time-resolved so it doesn't regress §2.5; if SharingPolicy is a `dyn` lookup it adds cost exactly where the narrow-scope hot path can least afford it.; Closed-vs-open tension: a fixed table of built-in scope rows is clean but adding a custom scope's row through the SPI means the contract surface is now extensible by downstream crates — widening what must stay stable/coherent and reintroducing the predictability concern (a transitive dep's custom scope declaring a surprising sharing policy).

**Mechanisms considered:** a per-scope `SharingPolicy { required_bound, publication, mutation_doctrine }` table as the canonical concurrency-contract surface, consulted by bean-instantiation at publish time; three named publication shapes tied to scope: SafePublishOnce (singleton, finished-value-moved-then-atomically-shared) / HandOffOwned (prototype, owned move) / PerContextKey (request/session, shared within ambient lifetime); graded bounds per scope (singleton: Send+Sync+'static; prototype: Send-if-spawned-else-none; request: store-dependent) replacing R1's blanket bound; mutation_doctrine as the per-scope statement of what the developer must guard (and where the Sync bound makes un-guarded mutation a compile error); custom-scope SharingPolicy rows via the Scope SPI so the concurrency contract is extensible alongside the scope mechanism (CANDIDATE — borders on scopes' territory)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Built-in scope rows (singleton/prototype/request) live in leaf-core, so the common-case concurrency contract needs NO cross-crate discovery and carries NO DCE risk — the lowest-risk surface. The per-bean fact a contributing crate must satisfy is 'my type meets my scope's required_bound', checked at that crate's own compile (locality as in R1). Only CUSTOM-scope SharingPolicy rows ride the discovery substrate (the same linkme/codegen/explicit CANDIDATES as the scope mechanism itself), and only those owe the anti-DCE self-check (an expected custom-scope crate producing no SharingPolicy → loud error, so beans don't resolve under a scope with a missing concurrency contract). The thin macro emits the bean descriptor + a reference to its scope identity (absolute paths, §2.10); the SharingPolicy is looked up by scope identity in leaf-core, NOT emitted per-bean, keeping macros logic-free. Because the policy is keyed by scope and the bound rides the shared-handle type, a WASM-module/test-double provider slots in identically (it publishes via its scope's row). The contract surface being scope-keyed means the predictability warning (donsz.nl, cross-crate doc §36) applies to custom-scope rows: a transitive dep silently introducing a scope whose SharingPolicy relaxes the bound is exactly the surprise to avoid — so custom rows must be opt-in/non-global, same posture as scope discovery. Generics-cannot-register inherited; auto-trait bounds checkable only on concrete instantiations.

**T2 — execution context:** The SharingPolicy's publication column IS the execution-context story, graded by scope. SINGLETON (SafePublishOnce): built on the bootstrap cold path, finished value MOVED into the shared store, shared via an atomic handle whose first observation establishes happens-before — safe publication with no global lock; thereafter invoked `&self` concurrently across executor worker threads under the Send+Sync proof. PROTOTYPE (HandOffOwned): built on whatever task calls resolve (often a hot path), then MOVED OWNED to the caller — there is no sharing, so there is no publication-visibility question and no guarding needed (the caller has exclusive ownership); cancellation drops a half-built value cleanly, nothing shared to leave inconsistent. REQUEST/SESSION (PerContextKey): one instance per ambient context key, shared only within that execution's lifetime and bound to an async-propagated context (NOT a ThreadLocal — same §2.3/§2.4 reason as scopes), so its concurrency contract is 'safe within the context's task(s); guard only if the context fans out to concurrent tasks'. The mutation_doctrine in T2 terms: a singleton mutating shared state races across executor threads (compile-forbidden without a guard); a prototype/request bean mutating its own state is safe because it isn't shared across concurrent executions — which is precisely scope-as-lever steering mutable state into a non-shared execution context. No blocking/cancellation machinery is added by the contract; it states the CONDITIONS (safe-publish-before-share for singletons; owned-handoff for prototypes; context-bound sharing for request) the engine must meet. Errors are rich/doctrine-citing per §2.9.

#### R3 — Doctrine + diagnostic + audit layer
Take the most minimal, most honest reading: the concurrency contract is NOT a feature with runtime state at all — it is an INVARIANT the rest of the container must satisfy plus a DX/diagnostic surface that makes the contract legible and steers users when they violate it. The realization is three thin things, no new runtime object. (1) The CONTRACT STATEMENT as a first-class, documented invariant threaded into the charter-level guarantees: 'leaf guarantees safe publication and concurrent-call memory-safety of shared-scope beans via the Send+Sync bound on the shared handle and the move-then-atomically-share publication; leaf does NOT make your bean logic thread-safe — guard post-publication shared mutation yourself, or (preferred) choose a narrower scope.' This is the canonical doctrine other features cite; it fixes the CONTRACT without owning the mechanism (handle type, bound placement, publication — all deferred to §3/registry/bean-instantiation). (2) A GUIDANCE surface (the rust-native analogue of Spring's FailureAnalyzer for this concern): when a bean fails the contract — not Send+Sync at a shared scope, or a detected post-publication shared-mutation pattern — leaf emits a doctrine-citing message naming the bean, the scope, why the contract requires the bound, and the two sanctioned fixes (guard it / narrow the scope), rather than a raw trait-solver error. This may be a custom diagnostic on the shared-handle bound (#[diagnostic::on_unimplemented]-style), a startup-validation check, or both — the timing follows §2.9 (compile-time where legible, else startup pass). (3) An optional AUDIT/observability hook: a 'leaf doctor'-style report (cf. cross-crate doc §269) listing each singleton's sharing posture (Send+Sync confirmed, interior-mutability fields detected, etc.) and a lint/heuristic flagging singletons that hold obvious un-guarded mutable state as scope-choice smells — accountability for the convention, mirroring condition-report's 'make the heavy convention debuggable'. Nothing in (1)-(3) adds a runtime lock, a per-bean concurrency object, or any hot-path cost; the contract is enforced by the bounds the registry/instantiation already carry, and this feature's value is making that enforcement EXPLICIT, LEGIBLE, and GUIDED.

**Pros:** Most honest to Phase-1's implementation-agnostic mandate: it adds essentially NO mechanism and pre-decides nothing (handle type, bound placement, publication, scope policy all stay with their owners) — it states the CONTRACT and supplies the DX surface, which is exactly what a 'doctrine' feature should be, avoiding R2's over-engineering and R1's risk of being read as a bound-placement decision.; Best DX leverage for the least code (§2.1): the entire felt value of this contract in Spring is the MENTAL MODEL and the moment a developer learns it — a doctrine-citing diagnostic + an audit report deliver that teaching directly at the point of confusion, which is where Spring's contract actually pays off (it's 'the most misunderstood guarantee', so guidance IS the feature).; Zero overhead, zero global locks, no hot-path touch (§2.5): purely compile-time/startup/observability; the contract rides bounds that must exist anyway, so it cannot regress performance and cannot introduce a lock — the cleanest possible posture for a cross-cutting invariant.; Composes with EVERY realization of its neighbours: because it owns only the contract statement + diagnostics + audit, it is compatible with R1's blanket bound, R2's per-scope table, any §3 ownership keystone, and any registry substrate — it is the stable doctrine layer the variable mechanisms slot under, which is robust across Phase-2's open decisions.; The audit/lint operationalizes scope-as-lever (§2.11 accountability, cf. condition-report): flagging 'this singleton holds un-guarded mutable state — consider prototype/request scope' turns the prose doctrine into actionable, debuggable guidance, the same way condition-report makes conditions accountable — a genuine value-add beyond Spring's purely-prose contract.

**Cons:** Thinnest 'realization' — a reviewer may rightly ask whether this is a feature or just an invariant + good error messages; its distinct deliverables (contract statement, guidance surface, audit) must be sharply scoped or it dissolves entirely into bean-instantiation/scopes/the diagnostics model and contributes nothing reviewable of its own.; The diagnostic + audit quality is the ENTIRE value, and producing a precise 'your singleton holds un-guarded mutable state, narrow the scope' signal requires detecting post-publication shared-mutation patterns — which is genuinely hard in Rust (interior-mutability fields like Mutex are LEGITIMATE, so the heuristic must distinguish 'guarded mutable state' from 'un-guarded smell' without false positives that erode trust); a weak audit is worse than none.; Defers nearly everything to neighbours and to the deferred §2.9 diagnostics model and §3 keystone, so on its own it cannot guarantee the contract is actually MET — it can only describe and guide; if a Phase-2 handle choice fails to carry Send+Sync, this feature surfaces the problem but does not prevent it (it is a spotlight, not a wall), which may be too passive for the 'single most important guarantee'.; An audit/lint that flags scope-choice smells risks NAGGING (DX cost) if tuned wrong, and a 'leaf doctor' report is build/observability tooling whose home (a separate diagnostics/tooling crate?) and timing are themselves Phase-2/Phase-3 questions — so part of this realization's deliverable is itself deferred, weakening it as a standalone Phase-1 answer.; Provides the weakest ENFORCEMENT of the three (R1 makes violation a compile error via the bound; R3 mostly describes and guides) — for the contract the doc calls 'the single most important guarantee', leaning on documentation+guidance rather than the type system may under-deliver, unless paired with R1's bound (which suggests R3 is really a layer ON R1, not an alternative).

**Mechanisms considered:** a first-class documented CONTRACT/INVARIANT statement (safe-publication + concurrent-call-safety via Send+Sync; not-runtime-thread-safe; scope-as-lever) cited by neighbour features — no runtime object; a doctrine-citing GUIDANCE diagnostic (FailureAnalyzer-analogue) at the contract-violation point, via custom on_unimplemented and/or a startup-validation pass (timing per §2.9); a 'leaf doctor'-style AUDIT report (cf. cross-crate §269) listing each singleton's sharing posture and flagging un-guarded-mutable-state scope-choice smells (accountability, cf. condition-report); an optional lint/heuristic distinguishing guarded interior mutability (legitimate) from un-guarded shared mutable fields (a scope smell) — hard to do without false positives; explicit positioning as a DOCTRINE LAYER that slots under any neighbour realization (R1 bound, R2 table, any §3 keystone) — owns contract+diagnostics+audit, defers all mechanism

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Purest cross-crate posture: a doctrine + diagnostic layer registers NOTHING iterable, so it has NO link-time aggregation, NO DCE exposure, and owes NO anti-DCE self-check of its own — it is immune to the cross-crate failure modes that dog discovery features. The contract is checked per-contributing-crate at that crate's own compile (the bean's Send+Sync at its scope), the ideal locality. The diagnostic surface lives in leaf-core (the custom on_unimplemented message attached to the shared-handle bound, hard-coded by absolute path like every other emitted reference, §99-102) and propagates to every contribution automatically because it rides the shared-handle type. The AUDIT/doctor report, however, DOES need whole-graph awareness (it lists every discovered singleton's posture), so it must run at the final binary / startup (where whole-graph awareness is anchored, cross-crate §77) and reuse whatever the registry/discovery features collect — it does not build its own registry. The lint, if compile-time, is per-crate; if startup, whole-graph. Origin-agnostic: a WASM-module/test-double bean is audited and contract-checked identically because the contract is on the handle/scope, not the construction path. The static link-time registry + optional dynamic WASM source are, again, just producers the audit reports over — CANDIDATES. The donsz.nl predictability concern barely applies (this feature introduces no global side effect; it only observes/guides). Generics-cannot-register inherited; the audit reports over concrete instantiations only.

**T2 — execution context:** As a doctrine+diagnostic layer it adds NO execution-context machinery and touches NO hot path — its T2 is the CONTRACT it states about other features' execution contexts, identical in substance to R1's: safe publication = finished value moved then atomically shared on the bootstrap task (happens-before via the atomic handle, no global lock); concurrent calls = `&self` across executor worker threads under the Send+Sync proof, with un-guarded shared mutation compile-forbidden; scope-as-lever = steer mutable per-interaction state into per-request/per-resolution instances so the shared-across-threads question never arises. The only ACTIVE execution contexts this feature introduces are cold: (a) the optional STARTUP-VALIDATION check (if the diagnostic is realized as a startup pass rather than a compile-time bound) runs once on the bootstrap task after the graph is known, all-or-nothing-ish, surfacing a rich doctrine-citing error and aborting if a shared bean violates the contract; (b) the AUDIT/doctor report runs on demand / at startup as a cold observability pass, read-only over the resolved graph, never on a hot path and never holding a lock. Cancellation/errors: the startup check propagates a rich ResolveError (§2.9) and aborts refresh; the audit is read-only and side-effect-free. No blocking, no per-call cost, no global lock — the contract's enforcement is the type system's (compile-time) and its guidance is cold-path tooling.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's safe-publication MECHANISM (a JMM happens-before edge bought by the singleton creation MUTEX) is exactly what the charter forbids (§2.5 no global locks) — but the rust-native realization is STRONGER, not a workaround: moving a finished value into a shared store and handing it out via an atomic `Arc` clone establishes the same publish-write-sequenced-before-consumer-load happens-before edge WITHOUT any lock, because the atomic refcount operation IS the synchronization point. So leaf replicates the GUARANTEE (final-like visibility of post-construction state) by the move-then-atomically-share, dissolving Spring's lock-as-publication-primitive rather than inheriting it. The clash is only that this requires the singleton be FINISHED before it is shared (bean-instantiation's safe-publication-at-handoff), which is the boundary this contract depends on but does not own.
- Spring's `volatile`-on-post-init-mutable-fields advice has NO direct analogue and is largely OBVIATED by the type system — a rust ADVANTAGE that changes the contract's character. In Spring a developer can mutate a plain field of a published singleton and the container won't stop them (a latent data race); the doc explicitly warns practitioners 'under-protect genuine runtime state.' In Rust, a singleton shared as `&self` behind `Arc` CANNOT have a plain field mutated through it — the borrow checker forbids it — so to mutate post-publication state you are FORCED to interior mutability (`Mutex`/`RwLock`/atomics), which is exactly Spring's 'use volatile/locks' but compile-enforced. The 'most misunderstood guarantee' becomes un-forgettable; the under-protection footgun is eliminated. The clash: leaf must NOT carry over the 'remember to add volatile' guidance (meaningless and impossible to forget in Rust) and instead frame the doctrine as 'the compiler makes you choose a guard.'
- 'Thread-safe-by-default is impossible and pessimal' (Spring's reason for NOT synchronizing methods) lands differently in Rust: `Send + Sync` is NOT method-level synchronization (zero runtime cost, it's a static proof of memory-safety-under-sharing, not mutual exclusion), so requiring `Send + Sync` on shared singletons does NOT 'annihilate the performance rationale' the way Spring's hypothetical method-`synchronized` would. BUT it IS stricter than Spring in a different way: Spring lets a non-thread-safe singleton EXIST (and race at runtime); leaf's bound makes a non-`Sync` type a compile error AT THE SHARED SCOPE, refusing the program rather than allowing the race. This is more correct but over-constrains users who genuinely never share across threads — so leaf must offer the narrower-scope escape (prototype/request, possibly a single-threaded-runtime carve-out) or it's stricter-than-Spring in a way that hurts DX (§2.1).
- WHERE the `Send + Sync` bound lives is hostage to the deferred §3 ownership keystone and the registry substrate — this feature can state the CONTRACT but cannot place the bound. If the shared handle is `Arc<dyn Trait + Send + Sync>` the contract is clean and propagates via the handle type; if Phase 2 picks an arena/index or a borrow-based model, 'the bound on the shared handle' must be re-expressed (on the registry value, on the trait, on a marker). The clash is that the single most important guarantee in the framework depends on a decision this feature is forbidden to make — so it must be written as a CONTRACT the keystone MUST satisfy ('whatever the shared-singleton handle is, it carries the safe-publication-and-shared-access bound'), surfaced as a phase2Question, not a settled type.
- async execution INTENSIFIES the contract rather than complicating it: tokio's multi-thread work-stealing runtime migrates a task across worker threads at every `.await`, so a shared singleton is GUARANTEED to be observed from multiple threads — making `Send + Sync` not optional-nicety but mandatory for any shared bean, and making Spring's 'shared across all request threads' the DEFAULT rather than a servlet-container detail. The clash: there is no single-threaded escape hatch on a multi-thread runtime, so the contract's bound is MORE load-bearing in async Rust than in Spring; the only relief is a single-threaded-runtime build (a §6 pluggable-runtime question) or narrower scope.
- No async Drop means the contract's 'fully-built before published' boundary has a teardown mirror this feature must not silently assume: Spring publishes after @PostConstruct returns and relies on GC for the failure path. In Rust a singleton that is contract-bound (Send+Sync, safely published) but holds async-cleanup resources cannot self-clean on Drop — but that is context-close/bean-instantiation's concern; the clash for THIS feature is only that 'safe publication of a fully-built singleton' must mean the value is COMPLETE (all init done) before the Send+Sync handle is shared, so the publication boundary and the finished-ness boundary must coincide — a property this contract DEPENDS ON and must state, even though it doesn't implement it.
- Scope-as-concurrency-lever maps onto Rust ownership BETTER than onto Spring's GC model, sharpening the doctrine: Spring's prototype 'fresh stateful instance, then forget' relies on GC; Rust's prototype is an OWNED MOVE to the caller, so a stateful prototype bean is exclusively owned (it has `&mut` by ownership, needs no guarding at all) — which is the strongest possible realization of 'push mutable state into a narrower scope to sidestep synchronization.' The clash is pedagogical: leaf's contract should TEACH that the rust-native lever is even cleaner than Spring's (owned-move prototype = zero synchronization, statically), but must be careful that the narrower-scope bound differs from the singleton bound (prototype needs only Send-if-spawned, request needs whatever its store demands), so 'the contract' is scope-graded, not one universal bound — entangling this feature with scopes' per-scope semantics.

### Interactions
- **scopes** — DEFINING SIBLING. scopes provides the multiplicities/lifetimes; THIS feature provides the doctrine that SELECTS among them ('stateful→narrower scope, stateless→singleton' is THE concurrency lever, spring doc 1948-1952). The two are two faces of one coin: scopes is the mechanism, concurrency-contract is the WHY. In R2 this becomes literal — each scope carries a SharingPolicy row stating its required bound + publication + mutation doctrine. The required-bound-per-scope (singleton: Send+Sync; prototype: Send-if-spawned; request: store-dependent) is shared territory; this feature owns the CONCURRENCY reading of each scope, scopes owns the multiplicity/teardown.
- **bean-instantiation** — PRIMARY ENABLER. bean-instantiation's PUBLISH step (move the finished singleton into the ready cache only after init returns) IS the concrete site where safe publication is honored — this feature states the CONTRACT (finished-before-shared + the shared handle carries Send+Sync, so the atomic handoff is the happens-before edge) that that publish step must satisfy. bean-instantiation owns the construct→init→publish sequence and the no-global-lock creation guard; concurrency-contract owns the guarantee about what the published handle means across threads. The 'fully-built before shared' boundary is bean-instantiation's; the 'safely shareable' bound is this feature's.
- **bean-registry** — UPSTREAM SUBSTRATE. The registry stores the shared singleton handle (e.g. Arc<dyn Any + Send + Sync> per the nightly-doc backbone) and hands out clones — the Send+Sync bound on that stored value IS where this contract physically lives, and the atomic clone on lookup IS the publication edge. This feature states the bound the registry value must carry; bean-registry (and the §3 ownership keystone) picks the concrete handle type. The 'where does the bound live' is a shared phase2Question.
- **task-execution** — COMPLEMENTARY HALF of Spring's division of labor (spring doc line 69): the container guarantees safe publication (this feature); the developer 'talks to the TaskExecutor abstraction rather than to threads' for running work. task-execution is the 'well-shaped tool' Spring hands back alongside the delegation — when a bean wants concurrent work it declares it to an executor abstraction (which on a multi-thread runtime forces Send on the work), reinforcing that shared beans are Send+Sync.
