The contract explains WHY task-execution exists: the container won't make your logic thread-safe, so it gives you a tuned, monitored way to run concurrent work explicitly.
- **context-propagation** — COMPLEMENTARY TOOL. Spring frames context-propagation as the other 'well-shaped tool' (line 1920) handed back when work crosses threads — the rust-native answer to ambient context surviving `.await`. This feature's safe-publication contract covers SHARED SINGLETONS; context-propagation covers PER-EXECUTION ambient state that must move across task hops. Together they cover both halves of 'what is shared vs what is per-execution' — the contract says singletons are safely shared, context-propagation says ambient context must be explicitly carried, never assumed thread-local.
- **smart-initializing** — THE ESCAPE HATCH the contract points to. Spring's @PostConstruct-under-the-singleton-lock is a deadlock trap (spring doc 1956-1970); the sanctioned lock-free phase for heavy cross-bean work is SmartInitializingSingleton.afterSingletonsInstantiated(), run after all singletons exist and OUTSIDE any lock. This feature's doctrine (safe publication via finished-before-shared, no work-under-a-held-guard) names smart-initializing as the place real cross-bean work belongs — the rust-native dissolution of the deadlock trap. smart-initializing owns the callback; concurrency-contract explains why it's the safe place.
- **injection-styles** — REINFORCING. Constructor injection encodes collaborators as `final` (immutable) fields handed in fully-built (spring doc 769) — which is exactly the config-only-state-needs-no-volatile case (1942-1944): an immutable, safely-published field needs no guarding. In Rust this is even cleaner: a constructor-injected field of a Sync type, never mutated after build, is provably safe to share with zero ceremony. This feature's contract leans on injection-styles' immutability to deliver the 'config doesn't need volatile' half of the doctrine.
- **deferral-primitives** — SUPPORTING the scope lever. When a longer-lived (shared) bean needs a shorter-lived (per-interaction, possibly non-Sync) collaborator, injecting it directly would freeze a non-shareable instance into a shared one — the singleton-holds-shorter gotcha. Deferral handles (ObjectProvider-analogue) re-resolve the live per-interaction instance per call, keeping mutable per-interaction state OUT of the shared singleton — a sanctioned realization of 'narrow the scope of stateful collaborators.' This feature's doctrine makes deferral-primitives load-bearing for keeping shared beans contract-compliant.
- **scoped-proxies** — SUPPORTING the scope lever (sibling of deferral-primitives). A scoped proxy lets a shared singleton hold a stable handle that resolves the live shorter-lived target per call — the other sanctioned fix for keeping mutable per-interaction state out of the shared/Send+Sync singleton. This feature explains the concurrency REASON scoped proxies exist: they preserve the singleton's stateless/shareable contract while still letting it collaborate with per-interaction state.
- **context-close** — TEARDOWN MIRROR of the publication boundary. The contract says a singleton is published only when fully-built; context-close destroys singletons in reverse dependency order via explicit shutdown().await (no async Drop). The shared dependency: a contract-bound singleton (Send+Sync, safely published) that holds async-cleanup resources needs container-driven teardown — this feature's 'finished-before-shared' boundary and context-close's 'explicit-teardown' boundary together bracket the singleton's safely-shared lifetime.
- **background-bootstrap** — STRESS-TESTS the contract. Background/parallel singleton init means a bean may be built on a background executor thread and observed by the main bootstrap thread — exactly the multi-thread publication the contract must cover. The Send+Sync bound + atomic handoff guarantees the main thread safely sees a background-built singleton; this feature's no-global-lock safe-publication is what MAKES concurrent background init sound. background-bootstrap owns the concurrent-init mechanism; concurrency-contract owns the guarantee that the result is safely visible.
- **runtime-lifecycle** — CONTRACT EXTENDS to running state. The static singleton graph is contract-bound (safely published, Send+Sync); runtime-lifecycle layers a start/stop running-state machine on top whose phase transitions are themselves concurrent operations on shared beans. This feature's 'concurrent &self calls are memory-safe under Sync' contract is what lets the running-state machine drive start/stop across threads without the container synchronizing every method — the doctrine covers steady-state running, not just startup.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- WHERE does the safe-publication/shared-access bound physically live, and what IS it? This is the §3 ownership-keystone viewed through the concurrency contract: if the shared singleton handle is `Arc<dyn Trait + Send + Sync>` the bound rides the handle and propagates for free; if it's an arena/index, a borrow model, or a different erasure substrate, the Send+Sync (and 'static) requirement must be re-expressed (on the registry value, the trait, a marker). The single most important guarantee in the framework depends on this decision, which this feature is forbidden to make — it states only the CONTRACT the keystone must satisfy. Shared with bean-registry, bean-instantiation, scopes, and the type-erasure substrate.
- Is the contract a UNIVERSAL bound (R1: every shared bean is Send+Sync+'static) or SCOPE-GRADED (R2: a per-scope SharingPolicy where singleton=Send+Sync, prototype=Send-if-spawned, request=store-dependent)? The universal bound is simplest and matches the multi-thread-async reality but over-constrains genuinely-non-shared narrow-scope beans; the graded model is more precise but borders on encoding scopes'/bean-instantiation's territory and adds a per-publish policy consultation. A unification call (§2.11) needing the whole scope family and the async-execution model to judge. Shared with scopes.
- Does leaf offer a SINGLE-THREADED-RUNTIME carve-out from the Send+Sync requirement? On a multi-thread work-stealing runtime, shared singletons MUST be Send+Sync (a task migrates threads at every .await); but a single-threaded runtime (a §6 pluggable-runtime choice) could legitimately relax this for users who never share across threads — at the cost of bimodality the charter resists (§2.3 'no sync/async bimodality' — does that extend to threading modes?). Whether to support this, and how to keep it from fracturing the contract, is open. Shared with the async-execution model and §6 runtime-agnosticism.
- HOW and WHEN is a contract violation diagnosed, and how good can the message be? The whole DX value of this 'most misunderstood guarantee' is the moment a user learns it — a raw 'X: Send is not satisfied' trait error fails the doctrine. Is enforcement a compile-time bound with a custom #[diagnostic::on_unimplemented] doctrine-citing message, a dedicated startup-validation pass, or both? Timing follows §2.9 (compile-time where legible, else startup), but the realization couples this feature to the deferred unified-diagnostics model. Shared with the §2.9 diagnostics keystone.
- Can leaf AUDIT/LINT for the scope-choice smell (a singleton holding un-guarded mutable state) without false positives? Interior mutability (Mutex/RwLock/atomics) in a singleton is LEGITIMATE (it's exactly the guarding Spring prescribes), so an audit must distinguish 'guarded mutable state' from 'un-guarded smell' — genuinely hard in Rust. Whether a 'leaf doctor'-style report (cf. cross-crate §269) and/or a lint is feasible, where it lives (a diagnostics/tooling crate?), and its timing (compile vs startup vs on-demand) is open. Interacts with condition-report's accountability pattern and the whole-graph-awareness-at-final-binary constraint.
- Does the contract's 'fully-built before shared' boundary need an explicit primitive, or does it fall out of bean-instantiation's publish-after-init? Safe publication REQUIRES the value be complete (all init done, all injected, any proxy attached) before the Send+Sync handle is shared — but with no async Drop and a cancellation/partial-construction unwind path, a half-built bean must never be shared. Whether this is purely bean-instantiation's responsibility (publish step) or needs a contract-level assertion is open. Shared with bean-instantiation and context-close.

### Notes
Confidence: HIGH on intent. I read the precise feature section in full — 'The container's concurrency contract: safe publication, NOT runtime thread-safety' (spring doc 1924-1944), plus the two sibling sections that are part of this doctrine: 'Scope as the primary concurrency design tool' (1948-1952) and the @PostConstruct-deadlock-trap + singleton-lock-internals (1956-1988) — and the charter-level statement of the same doctrine (line 69: 'the container owns assembly, the developer owns runtime concurrency'; 1920: 'an assembler, not a runtime concurrency manager'). I grounded the rust-native translation against the nightly doc (the Arc<dyn Any + Send + Sync> registry backbone §17/§81/§481, trait upcasting 1.86 §80, async Drop absent-as-first-class-constraint §86) and the charter invariants (§2.5 no global locks / minimal Arc, §2.3 async-first execution discipline, §2.9 rich-early diagnostics, §2.11 unification) and the cross-crate doc (whole-graph awareness at the final binary §77, self-check mandate §152, donsz.nl predictability §36, thin-macro/absolute-path ABI §99-102).\n\nCRITICAL SCOPING NOTE: this is a DOCTRINE/CONTRACT feature, NOT a heavy mechanism — and that shapes everything. Spring's concurrency-contract owns almost no runtime code; it is the STATEMENT of a guarantee (safe publication) + a disclaimer (not runtime-thread-safe) + a design lever (scope choice). Its value is the mental model, which the doc calls 'the single most important — and most misunderstood — guarantee.' My three realizations therefore diverge on HOW MUCH MECHANISM the contract should be, not on a creation algorithm: R1 = the contract IS a type bound (Send+Sync on the shared handle = the rust-native safe-publication-and-shared-access proof; the borrow checker = the 'guard your own mutation' delegation, made compile-enforced) — strongest enforcement, leans hardest on the §3 keystone; R2 = a scope-graded SharingPolicy table making 'scope is the concurrency lever' the literal shape of the feature — most teachable/unified but borders on encoding scopes'/bean-instantiation's decisions; R3 = a thin doctrine + diagnostic + audit layer with zero runtime mechanism — most honest to Phase-1's agnostic mandate and best DX-per-line, but weakest enforcement and arguably a layer ON R1 rather than an alternative. The genuine divergence is the mechanism/doctrine spectrum: R1 (type system enforces) ↔ R2 (per-scope policy structure) ↔ R3 (documentation + guidance). They are Phase-2-composable: R3's diagnostics layer sits naturally on top of R1's bound, and R2's table is R1's bound graded by scope — so Phase 3 may well MERGE them (bound + scope-grading + diagnostic surface).\n\nThree things to carry forward. (1) The deepest rust-native insight: this contract is LARGELY ENFORCED BY THE TYPE SYSTEM, making it STRONGER than Spring's. Spring buys safe publication with a singleton MUTEX (a JMM happens-before edge) and asks developers to remember `volatile` on post-init mutable fields — the doc explicitly notes practitioners 'under-protect genuine runtime state' (a latent-race footgun). In Rust: moving a finished value into a shared store and handing it out via an atomic Arc clone establishes the SAME happens-before edge with NO lock (the atomic refcount IS the synchronization), AND the borrow checker FORBIDS un-guarded mutation of a shared `&self` field — so the 'remember to guard' half is un-forgettable, not advisory. Spring's two-part contract (you safely SEE a built bean; nothing guaranteed about concurrent CALLS) maps to (Send+Sync = safe publication + concurrent-call memory-safety) and (borrow checker = forced choice of a guard for shared mutation). This is the textbook intent-not-mechanism win (§2.2). (2) Scope-as-concurrency-lever maps onto Rust OWNERSHIP even better than onto Spring's GC: a prototype is an OWNED MOVE, so a stateful prototype has &mut by exclusive ownership and needs ZERO synchronization, statically — the cleanest possible 'push mutable state into a narrower scope.' The contract is therefore SCOPE-GRADED (singleton needs Send+Sync; prototype needs only Send-if-spawned; request needs whatever its store demands), which entangles it with scopes' per-scope semantics. (3) The contract STATES a guarantee it does not IMPLEMENT — safe publication is realized at bean-instantiation's publish step (finished-before-shared) and the bound lives on the §3-keystone handle type. So this feature must be written as a CONTRACT its neighbours satisfy, not as standalone machinery, or it dissolves; I sharpened the boundary so it owns the CONCURRENCY DOCTRINE + the bound-as-contract + the diagnostic/audit surface, deferring the handle type, bound placement, publication step, and scope policy to bean-registry/bean-instantiation/scopes/§3.\n\nI refused to pick the meta-architecture: every realization keeps the shared-handle type abstract (states the bound as a contract the §3 keystone must honor, surfaced as the lead phase2Question), keeps the diagnostic timing per §2.9 (compile where legible else startup), and is origin-agnostic (a WASM-module/test-double publishes through the same handle and inherits the same contract because it rides the handle/scope, not the construction path). The two-source duality (static link-time registry + optional dynamic WASM source) barely touches this feature — it registers nothing iterable, so it is immune to the DCE/silent-failure hazards; the audit merely REPORTS over whatever the discovery features collect. Naming ('SharingPolicy', 'SafePublishOnce', 'HandOffOwned', 'PerContextKey', 'leaf doctor') is Phase-1 placeholder. I wrote no files. Grounded source paths (absolute): /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (line 69 + 1920 the division-of-labor theme; 1924-1944 THE contract — safe publication, not-runtime-thread-safe, config-needs-no-volatile; 1948-1952 scope-as-concurrency-lever; 1956-1988 @PostConstruct deadlock trap + singleton-lock internals + benign-BeanCurrentlyInCreation + strict/lenient locking; 2089-2118 ThreadLocal-ambient-context vs thread hops); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md (invariants §2 esp. 2.2/2.3/2.5/2.9/2.11, deferred keystones §3 ownership + erasure + async-context, §6 pluggable runtime); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (Arc<dyn Any + Send + Sync> registry backbone §17/§81/§481, trait upcasting 1.86 §80, async Drop absent-first-class-constraint §86, dyn+Send the most-important call §260); /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (whole-graph-awareness-at-final-binary §77, self-check mandate §152, donsz.nl predictability §36, thin-macro/absolute-path ABI §99-102, leaf doctor self-check §269).

## Area coherence notes

- SELF-INVOCATION INCONSISTENCY across the advice family: caching's R2/R3 realizations explicitly ELIMINATE the self-invocation caveat (a self-call DOES cache), while transaction-management is locked to a proxy/interceptor model that KEEPS it (self-call bypasses @Transactional). caching's own phase2Question #5 flags this ('how is the resulting INCONSISTENCY with transaction-management reconciled, given users expect cache and tx to behave the same?'), and validation/exception-translation/the-missing-async-execution all sit on the same fence. This is a real area-wide coherence hazard: leaf must decide ONE interception-consistency policy (mirroring Spring 7's deliberate @Proxyable/global-default consistency move) rather than letting each advice feature pick independently. Currently four designs defer to aop-proxying without asserting they must all resolve identically.
- DUPLICATED TIMER / DURATION / CRON PRIMITIVES: scheduling needs a reactive timer-wheel (sleep-until-next-fire) and a cron calculator; retry-resilience needs a backoff timer; type-conversion owns Duration/Period parsing (ISO-8601 + suffix grammar); and scheduling+value-injection both parse '5s'/'PT1M' trigger durations. scheduling and retry-resilience BOTH independently note they 'may share the timer-wheel primitive' (§2.4 cold-path), and scheduling defers duration parsing to type-conversion — but there is no single owner for the shared reactive-timer primitive. Three designs gesture at the same unification candidate ('await on a reactive timer, cold path') without one of them owning it. Recommend a single leaf timer primitive consumed by scheduling, retry backoff, caching TTL, and any periodic-refresh.
- THREE INDEPENDENT RE-DERIVATIONS OF THE SILENT-DCE SELF-CHECK: exception-translation ('an empty translator chain yields untranslated errors that look like success — worse than a loud missing-bean'), scheduling ('a dropped @Scheduled descriptor silently never fires'), retry-resilience ('a binary that silently ships with resilience disabled'), task-execution ('a binary never silently lacks its execution facility'), and context-propagation ('a dropped accessor is a SILENT correctness bug') ALL independently invent an 'expected-vs-found manifest / leaf-doctor self-check' obligation against the deferred cross-crate substrate. This is correctly recognized as cross-cutting, but it is duplicated five times with no single owner; the self-check mechanism should be ONE shared substrate concern, not re-specified per feature with subtly different severity framings (fatal for executor, degraded-not-fatal for accessor, silently-wrong for translator).
- ORDERING-CORRECTNESS IS ASSERTED BY EACH ADVICE FEATURE BUT OWNED BY NONE IN THIS AREA: transaction-management (cache-outside-vs-inside-tx; validate-before-tx), caching (validate-before-cache), validation (validate-before-retry/tx/cache), retry-resilience (retry-outside-tx), and exception-translation (translation-innermost) each declare a REQUIRED relative order against their siblings and all defer the actual sort to auto-proxy-creator (which is NOT in this area). The designs are mutually consistent in their stated orderings, but because the arbiter is out-of-area, there is no in-area validation that the union of all these pairwise constraints is satisfiable/acyclic (e.g. validation says 'outside tx', retry says 'outside tx', translation says 'innermost/below tx' — the total order across validate+retry+tx+cache+translate+async is never assembled in one place). The inspectable-chain-order diagnostic (§2.9) that each design wants is also unowned here.
- CONTEXT-PROPAGATION KEYSTONE IS THE SHARED BLOCKER BUT RESPONSIBILITY IS DIFFUSE: locale-context, context-propagation, task-execution, transaction-management, validation (locale for messages), messages-i18n, expression-language, caching, scheduling, and exception-translation R3 ALL depend on the unresolved 'rust-native ThreadLocal-across-.await' primitive, and each correctly defers it. This is coherent (everyone points at the same keystone) but the designs disagree subtly on the DEFAULT: context-propagation and task-execution say propagate is OPT-IN/isolate-by-default (Spring-faithful), while transaction-management needs the tx-resource EXCLUDED-by-default even when propagation is on (inverted), and messages-i18n/validation IMPLICITLY ASSUME the locale DOES propagate across hops (else a post-.await resolution sees the wrong locale). So 'locale propagates by default, tx-resource never, everything else opt-in' is a three-tier per-key policy that no single design fully owns — context-propagation gets closest with its 'per-key allow/deny' question but does not commit, and messages-i18n's assumption that locale survives the hop is in tension with context-propagation's isolate-by-default stance.
- ENGINE-VS-FAÇADE 'INERT IN BARE ENGINE' PARITY IS CLAIMED CONSISTENTLY: caching, transaction-management, exception-translation, and messages-i18n/resource-loading all correctly state their capability is absent in the bare engine and installed by the façade/auto-config (matching Spring's BeanFactory-vs-ApplicationContext split and the @Enable*/conditional-auto-config opt-in). This is a GOOD coherence point — the designs agree the magic-named-bean + install-default-if-absent pattern (messageSource, applicationEventMulticaster, applicationTaskExecutor) is one shared façade mechanism. The only gap: the (missing) event-system design is the third member of that magic-named-service trio and would need to share the identical assembly mechanism, but it is not present to confirm the unification.
- EXPRESSION-LANGUAGE'S HOT-PATH CONSUMER IS UNOWNED: expression-language designs its #{...} engine around four consumers and names @EventListener(condition=...) as the most overhead-sensitive (per-event dispatch) — but the event system that would exercise that condition is not a design in this area. So expression-language's keystone decision (typed closures vs interpreter) is being made partly to satisfy a consumer (event conditions) whose own design does not exist to state its real requirements (Send-safety of the condition closure across the dispatch task hop, exposure of #root.event/#args). Caching's key/condition/unless is the only in-area hot-path consumer present to validate the EvalContext shape; the event-condition requirements are asserted by expression-language on behalf of an absent feature.
